# The Holdout Set

# Chapter 1

The basement lab smelled the way it always had: burnt coffee, hot dust, old upholstery, and the dry animal heat of the server closet breathing through its cracked door. For one relieved second, before she saw what had changed, Leela loved it again. The blue couch was still there with one arm lower than the other because nobody had ever fixed the internal spring. The long table was still ringed with chairs stolen over several years from conference rooms that did not miss them enough to ask questions. One whiteboard still held a ghost of June's looping handwriting under months of uncapped marker haze. The place looked like what Mohit had once promised it was going to be, which was not prestige exactly, not yet, but a corner of machine learning where people fed one another and passed around chargers and covered each other's classes and went to June's apartment after seminar to eat rice and whatever could be made with cabbage.

Then the second board came into focus.

Somebody had scrubbed it clean and left only four printed sheets taped in a neat column: fall milestones, conference deadlines, sponsor visit dates, and a calendar for one-on-one check-ins with Mohit. The shelf by the microwave, which usually held soy sauce packets, stale protein bars, tampons, and the ibuprofen bottle everybody denied owning, had been half-cleared for a row of black binders with fresh spine labels. The room had not become nicer. It had become legible to somebody outside it.

June was at her desk with her backpack open, digging through a side pocket for nicotine gum hard enough to make the zipper rasp. Her hair had the blunt unevenness of a cut she'd done herself in a bathroom mirror, and she was wearing the same paint-flecked chore coat she wore when she needed to make it through a whole day without pretending to be polished. Omar sat across from her in a pale button-down so crisp it made the basement look rude. He had rolled his sleeves once, exactly evenly, and was aligning his notebook with the edge of the desk as if order might spread by contact. At the coffee machine Ben Haber was reading the laminated instruction card June had taped up last spring after it flooded twice. He wore careful white sneakers no basement deserved and a dark jacket expensive enough that Leela could not stop seeing the price of it.

"You made it," June said. She popped the gum in, chewed once, and looked at Leela's face more closely. "You look like summer didn't heal you."

"It took a crack at me," Leela said.

Ben turned too quickly, bright with first-year energy and the fear of getting the tone wrong. "Morning. I was just trying not to destroy the machine on day one."

"The machine destroys itself," June said. "You only have to stay out of its way."

Omar gave Leela a small nod. "Good morning."

He made morning sound salvageable, even in a basement under fluorescent lights. It was one of the things about him that irritated her and steadied her at the same time.

Leela dropped her bag by her desk. Her wrist brace caught on the strap, and for an instant the old embarrassment flashed through her: thirty years old, sixth year, body already filing workplace complaints her mouth had not. On her monitor a yellow sticky note from May still clung to the bezel. `Ask Mohit about committee timing.` She had asked twice by email over the summer and gotten paragraphs back about vision, positioning, and the need to align the lab's external story after a difficult funding season. No answer had contained a date.

Mohit's office door was open. That alone made him seem generous.

"I'm going to grab him before he disappears," she said.

June leaned back in her chair. "If he says 'big picture' before minute three, you owe me coffee."

"That bet is rigged."

"Then win it."

Mohit was at his desk in shirtsleeves, blue oxford, cuffs immaculate, the kind of softness that looked accidental until you noticed how completely he controlled it. His expensive glasses were pushed halfway down his nose while he typed, and behind him the office shelves had been rearranged. The framed group photo from last year's retreat had been moved aside for a stack of white banker boxes and a new acrylic plaque from some accelerator program Leela had never heard of. He looked up and smiled with immediate warmth, as if he had been hoping she would interrupt.

"Leela. There you are." He stood, because he had learned long ago that standing to greet people made them feel met rather than managed. "How was the end of summer?"

"Short," she said. "Do you have five minutes?"

"For you, yes."

He said it like a favor and a truth.

She sat in the chair opposite him. The fabric still had a cigarette burn on one arm from whoever had owned it before the lab inherited it. Mohit lowered himself back down, hands folded loosely, listening face already in place.

"I need to lock my year," she said. "I sent the revised chapter and the methods memo. If we're treating this as my dissertation year, I need a defense timeline, committee names, the normal approvals. If we're not, I need to know that now rather than in November."

He gave a tiny sympathetic exhale, the sound of a man encountering a perfectly reasonable concern he intended to reroute. "You're not wrong."

It was not yes.

"But I think the frame has shifted over the summer in a way that makes the old sequence less helpful." He leaned back. "The center bid not landing was clarifying. Painful, but clarifying. We have to stop imagining that public-interest branding alone is going to protect the work. Reviewers are harsher. Sponsors are harsher. The field is harsher. If I send you into that environment with the same lab habits we've had, I'm not serving you."

Leela kept her face still. "I'm asking a smaller question than that."

"I know." His smile deepened, gentle enough to make interruption feel rude. "And I'm answering the larger one because it determines the smaller one."

There it was. Not `big picture`, but close enough that June would claim the coffee anyway.

"Three o'clock," he said. "Mandatory group meeting. I want everybody hearing the same strategic reset at the same time. Then you and I can talk about how your path fits inside it."

"I don't really need a strategic reset to know whether you will approve a final dissertation year."

He watched her for a beat. When he was cornered he did not get louder. He became tidier.

"I don't want to give you a false answer," he said. "You're close enough that the form of your final year matters. Close is not the same as market-ready. You understand that better than most people. Come to the meeting. Let me show you where the lab is going. Then we'll calibrate."

Calibrate. As if her life were a model drifting off distribution.

"Okay," she said, because he had made no other word available.

"Good." He brightened again at once. "And Leela?"

She looked up.

"I want you helping set the tone. Ben is new. Omar is still finding his footing. June has had a rough few months. You're the senior person in the room."

It was flattering if you did not look directly at the use it was being put to.

"Right," she said.

"We'll make this a strong year."

He meant it, probably. The problem with Mohit was that the lie and the plan so often lived in the same sentence.

When she came back out, June looked up immediately. "Well?"

"You owe me coffee," Leela said.

June barked out one laugh. "He got there before minute three?"

"He used `clarifying` instead."

"That's spiritually the same word."

Omar glanced between them. "Is there a problem?"

Leela was about to say no when the clean white calendar on the board pulled her eye again. Sponsor visit dates. Check-ins. Milestones. The black binders waiting on the shelf like a row of shut mouths.

"Not yet," she said.

The morning spread thin after that. Ben asked where they kept extra HDMI cables and thanked her too earnestly when she pointed to the mislabeled drawer. June muttered at a permissions error under her breath with the bored obscenity of long practice. Omar took one phone call in the hallway in Arabic, low and quick, then came back with his jaw set a fraction too hard. Every fifteen minutes Leela found herself checking the time as if the meeting might become smaller if she caught it early enough.

At one point she opened the dissertation chapter she had revised in July and read the first page without absorbing a word. Her own prose looked like evidence from somebody else's stalled life. The Hudson Federal card bill was due on the eighteenth. Graduate housing renewed by academic year, not by dignity. If Mohit decided to delay her again, it would not be an emotional inconvenience. It would be rent, insurance, another winter in a studio the size of a held breath, another season of telling her mother that the department's timeline was unusually complicated this year.

At two fifty-three Mohit sent a lab-wide message with no subject line. `Downstairs conference corner now, please. Bring notebooks.` Ben was already standing before the notification finished loading.

The conference corner was the far end of the lab by the dead window that looked into a cinderblock shaft. Someone had tried to civilize it years ago with a rug and two standing lamps rescued from a faculty office purge. One lamp still worked if you kicked the base. Mohit had added a tray of coffees from the bakery near the lake, the one none of them bought from unless parents were visiting, and a pastry box bleeding butter through the cardboard. The gesture was so practiced it almost worked on Leela. By reflex she was hungry.

"Please eat," Mohit said as they settled around the table. "I don't want this feeling like a punishment."

June, reaching for a croissant anyway, said, "Comforting opener."

He smiled at her as if she'd offered useful texture. Ben laughed a second too late.

Mohit remained standing. He did not use slides. That made everything feel more intimate and less appealable. He had a yellow pad in front of him and one of his expensive pens uncapped beside it, though Leela never saw him write a note he could not later use.

"I spent a lot of the summer thinking about what this lab has done well and what it has hidden from itself," he said. "We have been generous with one another. That matters to me. I mean that. But generosity can become a methodology of avoidance. We have a culture of softening our critique because we live in one another's pockets. We share food, code, panic, backup TA coverage, moving boxes, all of it. It's humane. It also means too much of our work reaches the world under-tested."

Leela hated the accuracy of `share food`. It made the next part harder to reject cleanly.

"The external environment is not getting kinder," he went on. "Reviewers are more adversarial. Industry is absorbing methods faster than academia can explain them. If we want your dissertations to survive contact with reality, we need to do our hardest stress-testing before anyone else does it to us and enjoys the blood."

Omar had his notebook open already. June had not uncrossed her arms. Ben was looking at Mohit the way first-years looked at any professor fluent enough to sound like a future.

"So this semester," Mohit said, "we're formalizing internal adversarial evaluation."

The room stayed quiet, waiting for the joke to arrive and declare itself one.

"I do not mean hostile culture," he said. "I mean disciplined red-teaming. Each of you will take a confidential brief attached to another lab member's core project. Your job will be to identify brittleness, leakage, invalid assumptions, and evaluation theater before a reviewer, competitor, or sponsor does it for us. Secrecy matters here. If you coordinate too early, you protect one another. That's human. It's also bad science."

June tilted her head. "Confidential from who?"

"From the lab, except through me, until first findings are on paper."

"So confidential from the person whose work we're supposed to break."

"Stress-test," Mohit said, not correcting her so much as laundering the word. "And yes. Otherwise we drift into politeness."

Leela could hear the fluorescent hum over his voice. The room felt already entered into evidence.

"There are three reasons to do this," Mohit said, counting lightly on his fingers. "One: the work gets stronger. Two: each of you learns how other people's claims fail, which will sharpen your own. Three: in a constrained funding environment, I need deliverables that make this lab legible to people with choices."

There it was: the center bid translated into lab weather, pressure dressed up as professional realism.

"How constrained," Omar asked.

He asked it in the courteous tone that most often meant he was frightened.

Mohit gave him a sympathetic look. "Constrained enough that I'm not interested in wasting your time on work that can't survive scrutiny."

Omar nodded anyway.

Mohit turned the yellow pad around. He had drawn four names with arrows between them.

"High level only," he said. "You'll each get a specific brief after this."

His pen tapped once beside Leela's name. "Leela, you'll be stress-testing Omar's calibration and transfer claims on the incident-response model. He's been building the case that it carries cleanly from Lakebridge dispatch data into the Phoenix and Cleveland feeds. If that transfer breaks, I want the break before a sponsor visit turns it into a promise."

Omar's jaw moved once. The incident-response model was the thing he'd spent the summer dragging across borrowed city datasets, the one outside people had actually asked about by name.

"Omar, you'll audit June's benchmark splits and baseline assumptions. If her train-test boundary is leaking duplicates or the baseline is easier than it looks, she needs that pain now, not after she builds a paper around it."

June made a short noise through her nose, not yet a laugh. She had spent most of the spring scraping duplicate incident reports out of inherited junk data, and Mohit had just recast all that labor as a place for Omar to stick a knife.

"June, you'll red-team Ben's provenance tracker and annotation reliability pipeline. If the chain-of-custody logging falls apart on contact or the annotators only agree because the labels are too blunt to disagree with, I'd rather kill the trust story here than watch him sell it outside."

Ben sat up straighter. The provenance tracker was his whole grown-up costume so far, a clean story about making messy data respectable, and June now had formal permission to prove it was mostly packaging.

"Ben, you'll audit the label history and data provenance on Leela's intake corpus. I want every hand relabel, undocumented import, and inherited spreadsheet accounted for."

For a second Leela only heard the words `Ben` and `Leela` in that order, absurd and insulting enough to be almost funny. Then the rest of it arrived. He was giving a first-year sanctioned access to the years she had spent cleaning the ugliest parts of the intake data: volunteer triage sheets with names half-scrubbed, old batches relabeled after the rubric changed, shortcuts survivable as lab memory and lethal as formal provenance holes.

"I'm sorry," she said, before she could make her tone nicer. "Is this on top of our existing work, or are you redefining dissertation progress through somebody else's failure modes?"

Ben looked down at the table. June looked at her with sudden alertness.

Mohit took the question like a man accepting the chance to clarify. "Those are not opposites."

"They are if the semester is finite."

"The semester is finite," he said. "Exactly. Which is why duplication, sentimentality, and under-ambitious evaluation have to end."

June chewed slowly. "Cool. Love being called sentimental by a man who bought us croissants."

Ben gave another uncertain laugh, then killed it when nobody joined him.

Mohit did not so much as glance in his direction. "June, if Omar can break your benchmark in-house, I would rather know that in October than watch someone else do it from a conference microphone in November."

The sentence landed and kept traveling. Leela saw June register that too.

"And if he can't?" June asked.

"Then your claims become much stronger."

"Lucky me."

Mohit turned back to Leela. "For you, the answer is a little more direct. You're at the stage where I need evidence not just of productivity but of defensibility. I don't think I can responsibly move your final-year approval forward until you've completed the Omar evaluation at a serious level."

He said it in the same voice another advisor might have used to recommend extra citations.

The table went still. Even Ben looked up.

Leela felt the room narrow around the cheap black chair under her. "My approval is contingent on this."

"Your approval depends on me being able to make a credible case for your dissertation's maturity," he said. "Right now, the strongest path is for you to lead with rigorous adversarial evaluation rather than one more inward-facing chapter. That's not punishment. That's strategy."

One more inward-facing chapter. As if the last year of her life were a decorative indulgence she kept trying to sneak past him.

"That's a major change," she said.

"It's an adaptation to reality."

There were versions of this conversation she could have had if she had been twenty-four, if she had parents paying her rent, if she had a different advisor already informally lined up, if she had not spent six years letting Mohit define what counted as almost finished. In none of those versions did she say what she said next.

"What does serious level mean."

June turned to look at her fully. The humiliation in that was smaller than the relief. Questions were work. Anger was expensive.

Mohit nodded once, as if pleased she had chosen adulthood. "Good. Specifics. For you it means a clean evaluation design, a workshop submission we can point to, and enough technical depth that nobody on a committee can call the work superficial. I'm not asking you to sabotage Omar. I'm asking you to show you can think like someone defending a dissertation in the current field."

Omar cleared his throat. "And if the evaluation damages the original paper?"

"Then we learn something before reviewers do. Better from friends than enemies."

The word `friends` sat in the middle of the table like something dropped there by mistake.

June spoke around her gum. "And we're not supposed to compare notes, because that would what, corrupt the scientific process with basic social life?"

"I'm asking for independence until first findings," Mohit said. "After that, I expect collaboration. But not before. If you want honest pressure, you have to tolerate some temporary discomfort."

Temporary. As if he were asking them to hold a stretch.

He reached into a slim stack of folders and slid one to each of them. Heavy paper. Their names printed on labels. The folders made the whole thing feel more official than most of the university's actual paperwork.

"Please don't circulate these," he said. "These are working documents. I tailored them."

Leela opened hers despite herself. The first page listed Omar's current model components, target failure modes, suggested ablations. The second page was shorter.

`Milestone for dissertation-year approval: successful completion of adversarial evaluation brief and associated external-facing submission.`

No signature. No form number. Nothing she could appeal to except his mouth.

Around the table paper whispered under fingers. June had stopped chewing. Omar was reading with such fixed attention that it looked almost devout. Ben flipped too fast, eager enough to seem grateful, then slowed when he realized no one else was moving.

"Questions?" Mohit asked.

June looked up. "Yeah. Did you always want to run a lab like this, or is this a fun new side effect of losing money?"

Leela felt the shot of admiration before the fear.

Mohit's expression barely changed. "I want to run a lab that tells the truth about what the world will do to weak work."

"The world or you."

"If you can't tell the difference yet, this will help."

That shut the room more effectively than if he had snapped at her.

He checked his watch. "I know this is a shift. Sit with it. Send me initial scopes by Friday. Leela, stay two minutes after if you can. Everyone else, you're free."

Chairs scraped. Ben grabbed his folder and then the pastry box, hesitated, and set the box back down when he saw nobody else was taking anything. Omar slipped his notebook under his arm with the care of a man handling medical paperwork. June stood last, shoving the folder into her bag as if it might stain the table if she left it out.

Leela stayed because he had not left the possibility of not staying available either.

When the others were out of earshot, Mohit sat on the table edge instead of across from her. The pose was meant to feel less administrative.

"I know that landed hard," he said.

"You changed my year in front of everyone."

"I made the logic transparent in front of everyone. Different thing."

"Not especially."

He let that pass. "Leela, you're not a second-year. I can't keep protecting you with vague encouragement. You need a case I can defend to other people, not just to you."

Her fingers pressed into the folder so hard the paper ridge marked her skin. "I didn't ask for encouragement. I asked for approval."

"And I'm telling you how to get it."

Outside the conference corner she could hear June's voice in the hallway, low and furious, then Ben's thinner answer. The ordinary rhythm would have been all of them around the table translating Mohit's euphemisms into English, stealing three extra minutes before anyone pretended to get back to work. Now even hearing the others felt slightly illicit, as if the room had been fitted with microphones while they were not looking.

"You're asking me to spend time attacking Omar's work instead of finishing my own," she said.

"I'm asking you to produce the kind of work that makes your own finishable."

"Those are not the same sentence."

"In this market, they are."

He smiled after he said it, neat and bloodless. He'd set a policy on the table and expected her to initial it.

"Friday," he said. "I trust you to scope it well."

Trust. Another word he only seemed to use when he meant obligation.

She stood because the meeting was over only when she stood up and left.

The hallway outside the basement lab was painted the same defeated beige as every corridor in Keating. Someone had propped the fire door open with a recycling bin, and the smell of old coffee had thinned into cinderblock damp. June was by the vending machine, coat already on despite the heat, tearing another piece of nicotine gum out of its foil with her teeth. Omar stood beside her with his folder held flat against his chest. Ben was hovering near both of them and neither, one hand on the strap of his laptop bag, still trying to look like the sort of person who found all this stimulating.

The old version of the afternoon would have been automatic. They would have drifted to this exact patch of hallway and said what he really means is, what he wants is, what he's scared of is, then gone back in mildly repaired. The body remembered the movement even after the permission was gone.

June looked at Leela. "So that's psychotic."

"That's one word," Leela said.

Ben moved first, unable to stand inside silence without decorating it. "I mean, red-teaming is real, though. At least as a methodology. People do blind challenge tracks all the time."

June turned her head slowly toward him. "Thank God. I was worried we might miss the nineteen-year-old VP of Product perspective."

"I'm twenty-four," he said before he could stop himself.

"That's not the part of the sentence I was mocking."

He flushed hard enough to show his age all at once. Under the fluorescent lights he looked abruptly younger than his jacket, a kid who had overpacked for weather and status at the same time.

"I just meant," he said, faster now, "it doesn't have to be, like, interpersonal. If the point is stronger work."

Omar spoke before June could answer. "He told me not to discuss my brief."

June looked back at him. The joke dropped out of her face. "He told all of us not to circulate the folders."

"No." Omar's voice stayed even, which was how she knew he was straining to keep it that way. "He told me specifically not to discuss the assignment details with anyone in the lab. Especially the target. He said comparison would contaminate independence."

There it was. The methodology version of isolation.

Leela said, "He used almost the exact same language with me."

June gave a short, ugly laugh. "Great. So we're apparently all in a hostage situation sponsored by peer review."

Ben shifted his weight. "Maybe he just doesn't want groupthink."

June stared at him. "Ben, if you say `groupthink` in this hallway one more time, I'm going to put you through the drywall."

The threat was too specific to feel theatrical. Ben nodded as if receiving useful feedback.

Omar looked down at the edge of his folder. His thumb had started worrying the paper soft. "I don't think refusing is possible."

Leela heard the sentence for what it was and for what he had chosen not to attach to it. One unsigned funding certification, one missed deadline with the Office of International Students, and Omar's file could flip to noncompliant before the month was out. Mohit did not have to say `visa` to make refusal dangerous. He only had to let a form sit in his inbox.

"No," she said. "Probably not."

June shoved the spent gum wrapper into her pocket. "I can't decide what's worse, that he thinks this is science or that he knows it isn't and doesn't care."

"Both can be true," Omar said.

It was the driest thing Leela had ever heard him say. June almost smiled.

Behind the lab door Mohit's laugh carried out briefly, warm and social. Ben's head turned toward it before he could keep himself from doing that. Mohit called his name a second later, asking if he could help move a couple of boxes from the office. Ben looked at the three of them, caught between relief and embarrassment, and then back toward the door.

"Yeah," he said. "Sure. One second."

June watched him go. "Jesus."

The word did not contain contempt by itself. Mostly fatigue.

Omar adjusted his grip on the folder. "I should get back upstairs."

Leela knew he did not work upstairs. None of them did. But she understood the lie and let him have it.

"Okay," she said.

He nodded to both of them. "If anything becomes less impossible, let me know."

"That's not how impossibility works," June said, but softly now.

Omar's mouth moved at one corner, almost a smile, and then he was gone up the stairs with the careful, upright pace he used whenever fear was trying to make him rush.

June leaned back against the cinderblock wall and shut her eyes for a second. Without the jokes her face looked younger and harsher at once.

"You all right?" Leela asked.

"No. You?"

"Not especially."

June opened her eyes. "Did he say anything extra to you because you're the dissertation emergency?"

Leela considered lying and found she was too tired to build the sentence elegantly. "He said he won't move my final-year approval until I do the Omar brief at what he calls a serious level."

June's expression changed, not to pity exactly. To recognition. "Fucking hell."

"Yeah."

"And if you tell him no?"

Leela looked past her through the little wired-glass pane in the lab door. Ben was inside now, stacking banker boxes while Mohit stood too close, talking with one hand in his pocket like a man mentoring rather than deploying. The black binders on the shelf by the microwave looked stupidly official. The blue couch sagged under nobody.

"Then I don't know what the year is," she said. "Which is sort of the point."

June pushed off the wall. "Come to dinner tomorrow. Tuesday. Usual thing."

It was June refusing to let the afternoon define the whole room.

"Okay," Leela said.

For a second the week remembered how to be ordinary.

June nodded once and went back inside for her bag. Leela stayed in the hallway long enough to feel the building's air push weakly through the vent above her, all noise and no cooling. She had imagined, stupidly, that the first day of the semester might give her a shape again. Instead it had taken the old shape apart and relabeled the pieces.

When she returned to her desk, Mohit's folder was where she'd left it, square on the keyboard like a bill. She opened it again because not opening it would have been a form of superstition. The technical brief was specific enough to be real: calibration curves, transfer failures, error clusters across city datasets. Work she could do. Work she might even do well. That was part of the obscenity. The assignment was useful.

The second page looked meaner in the lab's ordinary light than it had in his hand.

`Milestone for dissertation-year approval.`

Official paperwork would have buried the threat under policy nouns. Mohit's control lived in the territory just outside paperwork, where everything consequential could still be described later as mentorship, misunderstanding, a conversation about standards.

Leela sat down slowly. Her chair gave the familiar squeal at the left wheel. On June's desk a mug with a chipped cartoon shrimp stared sideways at the room. Omar had left one pen uncapped, lined up perfectly beside his notebook. Somewhere behind the server closet door a fan rattled and corrected itself. All the proof of the lab's old life was still physically present. None of it had helped.

She could have walked across campus and asked Gloria Fisk what counted as normal advisor behavior. She could have called her mother and said the year was slipping again, this time for a reason ugly enough to sound fake. She could have refused the brief on principle, which was another way of saying she could have risked paying the principle in rent and time and the slow bureaucratic death of a sixth year with no schedule.

Instead she pulled her laptop closer and opened a blank email.

`Subject: adversarial scope`

Her fingers stayed above the keys for a moment. The wrist brace pressed warm into her skin. Around her the lab kept making its old sounds, fluorescent hum and fan whine and the far-off clatter of someone dropping a spoon in the sink. The place went on naming itself after the people in it had already agreed to become something else.

She typed that she understood the brief and would send a first-pass plan by Friday. She typed that Omar's calibration claims were a sensible place to start. She did not type that she needed the approval, that the card payment was due on the eighteenth, that another year of drift would mean another year of explaining her life in smaller and smaller lies. She did not type that he had built the choice so cleanly that agreeing could still pass for reason.

She hit send because refusing had no immediate grammar and because wanting to finish and agreeing to this had become, in one afternoon, the same sentence.


# Chapter 2

By the second flight of June's stairs, the beer cans in Leela's tote were knocking together hard enough to bruise her leg through her jeans. The hallway held the usual Alder Street mixture of old oil, damp plaster, and whatever somebody downstairs had decided could count as incense. Outside, the immigrant strip was still loud in the late-summer heat: buses gasping at the curb, somebody dragging metal shutters halfway down, bass leaking from a car that had been parked under the no-standing sign for most of the year. June had texted an hour earlier: `beer if possible. something acidic if you're feeling rich.` Leela had bought six cheap lagers, two limes, and a bunch of cilantro that already smelled overcommitted.

Tuesday dinner had begun two years earlier because everybody kept ending seminar hungry and Mohit had once made a whole performance out of community before he got bored of paying for dumplings. June's apartment took over after that. It was smaller than the lab but warmer in all the ways that mattered. People sat on the floor. Somebody always chopped something. If the evening went well, the room let them be stupid for an hour without penalty.

Leela wanted the room to do that again. She wanted its folding table and dented rice cooker and the absurd confidence of June's little kitchen to tell her Mohit's new brief had not, in fact, changed the species of their lives overnight.

June opened the door with her shoulder because both hands were occupied: one with a wooden spoon, one with a packet of nicotine gum she'd bitten open with her teeth. Her blunt black hair was clipped back with a binder clip she must have stolen from the lab, and she had on an old diner T-shirt with a cartoon coffee cup on the chest and grease marks that no wash cycle had fully defeated. Heat rolled out behind her carrying soy, garlic, onion, and the red pepper sting of something cooked past politeness.

"You're late," she said.

"I'm six minutes late."

"Exactly. Shoes off."

Leela kicked off her sneakers into the pile by the door and squeezed past the folding table, already unfolded and already in everybody's way. The apartment was the same cramped second-floor geometry it always was: one front room pretending to be living room and dining room together, one bedroom half blocked by a curtain instead of a door, a kitchen so narrow the oven and the opposite counter nearly touched knees. A box fan rattled in the window more for morale than airflow. June had wedged cookbooks under one leg of the table, and the fridge wore its usual collage of takeout menus, expired coupons, and one postcard of Lakebridge in winter so ugly it had become permanent.

Ben was already there, perched at the edge of the counter as if the apartment were a place he had not yet earned the right to lean on. His expensive navy jacket was folded very neatly over the back of June's desk chair, and his careful white sneakers sat by the door heel to heel, cleaner than anything else in the room. He had brought a bottle of orange wine and a tub of marinated olives from the expensive grocery up the hill, the kind of contribution that showed both effort and a childhood in which stores like that had not been a dare.

"I also brought bread," he said quickly, as if she might dock him points. "But June said the olives were, quote, rent marbles."

June pointed her spoon at the stove. "Because they are. Put the beer in the sink. The fridge is full of condiments pretending to be groceries."

Leela dropped the cans into a bath of cold tap water and melting ice. On the stove, chicken thighs and potatoes were collapsing together in a Dutch oven stained brick red with pepper paste. A pan of cabbage sweated down beside it with enough black pepper to make the air feel textured. The rice cooker on the floor gave off small patient puffs, like a thing refusing panic on principle.

"I brought limes," Leela said.

"Jesus. Rockefeller." June took them anyway. "Cut one. Cucumber salad needs help."

Leela found the cucumbers in the sink and the only sharp knife in the apartment. The familiar work steadied her more than the room itself had. June cooked the way she talked: with brisk contempt for unnecessary elegance. Smash, salt, vinegar, sesame oil, done. Ben had already set out mismatched bowls and was pretending not to notice that June rearranged them after he stepped back.

"Where's Omar?" Leela asked.

"Texted that the bakery line was obscene," June said. "Which is what happens when you insist on buying dessert on a Tuesday in a neighborhood with six bakeries and no self-respect."

Ben laughed too loudly, then caught himself and reached for the opener. He held the wine bottle at the angle of somebody performing competence for an audience that might or might not be hostile. Leela saw, not for the first time, how young he really was when nobody rewarded him fast enough. Twenty-four in a room built on thirty-year-old damage.

June slapped a bowl onto the table. "Ground rules. Nobody says 'adversarial robustness' before the second beer."

"What about 'deliverable'?" Ben asked.

"Immediate ejection."

"Cross-functional alignment?"

"Now you're just flirting."

Leela laughed. It came a beat late but still landed, and for a minute the room found its old rhythm. Ben tried the wine and said it tasted like cider somebody had resented. June told a story about the downstairs tenant stealing her laundry detergent one capful at a time, which she claimed was a worse crime than grand theft because it required patience. Leela described the departmental seminar speaker from that afternoon, a political scientist from Cornell who had said "trust stack" six times in fifty minutes and clicked through slides like he was apologizing to them for not having gone to business school. June did an imitation so precise it was almost mean. Ben nearly choked on a laugh. When Omar finally arrived, pink from the stairs and still neat in a blue button-down that looked ironed because he probably ironed it, he came in holding a white bakery box tied with red string and apologized for the lateness before he'd even shut the door.

"I bought plum pastries," he said. "This was apparently a municipal emergency."

"You came with dessert. You're forgiven," June said. "Shoes."

Omar set the bakery box down with the care of a person placing evidence on a table. Then he took off his loafers and lined them beside the others with a quick little adjustment until both toes matched the wall exactly. He smelled faintly of starch and street heat. When he passed Leela the stack of bowls, his cuffs were rolled to identical heights, not a millimeter off. Even exhausted, he looked as if he had decided on his own behalf that sloppiness was a luxury for citizens.

They ate shoulder to shoulder because the table was too small to permit any other social arrangement. June at the head because it was her apartment and because nobody challenged her there. Leela on the side with the bad table leg. Ben tucked between the fan and the window, his knee bumping the radiator cover whenever he forgot where he was. Omar opposite Leela, cutting his chicken carefully before mixing it into the rice.

For the first ten minutes they held the line. The food helped. June's chicken was spicy enough to bully speech into honesty and then, perversely, keep everyone quiet while they chewed. Sweat gathered at Leela's hairline. The cucumbers were cold and sharp. Outside, somebody yelled in Spanish and got an answer from half a block away. The fan chopped their voices into smaller pieces and sent them back around the table.

Ben talked about orientation week, how one of the new robotics students had already called the engineering complex "brutalist but kind of iconic" and gotten nearly booed. Omar described the undergraduates in his recitation section with the exhausted respect of a man who had already learned two names for every one of his own students: the official one and the one on the roster. June said the only correct thing to teach first-years about academia was where to cry without being found. Leela told them the vending machine on Keating three had started dispensing two peanut-butter crackers for the price of one, then ruined the joke by adding that it was probably a calibration error. June said, "See, this is why you need us. Any normal person would stop at free crackers."

Nobody had run out of things to say. Ben kept worrying his fork through the potatoes, June kept tossing jokes into the middle and yanking them back by the handle, and Omar kept drinking before he finished a thought. Mohit sat between them anyway, upholstered into every skipped clause. Even the jokes swerved around him.

June stood to get more rice, used the movement to reset the mood, and failed by half an inch. "All right," she said. "We're twenty-three minutes in. That's basically Olympic level. Who's breaking first?"

Ben looked up too fast. "Breaking what?"

"The fake normal," June said. "It's doing its best."

"I thought we weren't doing work talk."

"We're not. We're doing gossip, which is the only form of peer review left to the people."

Omar gave a small smile into his glass. Leela watched his jaw tighten and release before he drank. He had said almost nothing about the meeting in the hallway after it, only that Mohit had separately told him not to discuss certain details. At the time that had felt ominous enough. Tonight it felt like a door somebody had locked from the inside.

Ben set his fork down. "I don't know if it helps, but I think he was trying to make roles clearer."

June leaned back. "Whose roles."

"Everybody's. I mean, not like in a punitive way." He winced at his own word choice. "He just said the lab had gotten kind of... diffuse. That people were doing too much invisible labor and it was making authorship blurry."

Leela heard Mohit's syntax in the sentence, thinned out and made younger in Ben's mouth. Invisible labor. Clear roles. Authorship blurry. Mohit had a way of offering a grievance to the exact person most likely to feel flattered by it. To Ben, a first-year standing at the edge of everybody else's history, he had offered clarity as a gift.

"Did he," June said.

"He said this could be good for me, actually. Because I get exposure across projects instead of being stuck in the first-year corner forever. Like I can see how the senior work is structured."

"Exposure," June said. "Terrific. Very useful after death."

Ben smiled because he understood he was supposed to, then stopped smiling because June had not made it easy enough.

Omar wiped his fingers with a paper napkin folded twice before use. "That's interesting," he said. "Because he told me almost the opposite."

Leela's thumb stuck to the sweating beer can. The fan kept knocking a spoon against the serving bowl. June went so still that Leela set her bottle onto the old conference-badge coaster one careful inch at a time, listening to the cardboard catch and release.

"Opposite how?" she asked.

Omar looked at her, then at June, then at the chicken as if it might issue procedural guidance. "He said the problem with the lab was blur itself. That we'd become too sentimental about boundaries. If the work mattered, overlap shouldn't frighten anyone."

"That sounds like him," June said. Her tone made it impossible to tell whether she meant last week or last year.

Ben frowned. "But that still sounds like the same pitch from a different angle."

"A sentence only first-years say," June muttered.

Leela could have left it there. She felt the option in her hand like a bead of sweat sliding around the beer can. Instead she heard herself say, "He told me this was part of proving I was ready for a final year. That if I handled it maturely, it would make my timeline easier to formalize."

There. A partial truth. She had left out the ugliest part, that her approval to keep existing in the program had been hung on Omar's model like a coat on a nail. But it was enough to make June's eyes sharpen and Ben forget his fork.

June's eyes cut to her and stayed there a second too long. "Maturely," she said. "God, of course he did."

Ben looked startled, then almost guilty, as if Leela's sixth year had only now arrived in his imagination as a real calendar. Omar's face did something smaller and worse. It grew more careful.

"So for Ben it's exposure," June said, counting off on greasy fingers. "For Leela it's maturity theater. For Omar it's apparently anti-sentimental overlap. Great. Everybody gets their own little horoscope."

"It wasn't a horoscope," Ben said, then seemed to hear himself. "Sorry. That sounded stupid."

"No, keep going," June said. "I'm always interested in how my professional death looks from freshman orientation."

"I didn't mean that." He pushed his bowl away an inch, appetite shrinking in public. "I just mean maybe he uses different language with different people. Faculty do that."

"Sure," June said. "Usually to lie."

Leela could feel herself beginning to overexplain, the impulse arriving with the old panic that if she didn't sort the terms cleanly enough the emotional version would win by default. "Different language can mean targeted language," she said. "He tells each of us the version that'll go down easiest. I hate it. I'm saying I don't yet know whether this is ordinary faculty manipulation or something worse, and the difference matters."

June looked at her as if weighing whether to rescue or wound. "You hear yourself, right?"

Leela did. She hated it before the sentence even finished leaving her. She heard herself replacing the word lie with targeted language and the difference matters, stacking qualifiers until the real point disappeared. She stabbed a potato with more force than it needed.

Omar cleared his throat. June's fork stopped halfway to her mouth. "There's one more thing," he said. "I wasn't planning to mention it because I may have misunderstood. But since we're doing horoscopes."

June sat back down.

Omar's fingers worried the edge of his napkin into a tighter square. "He said the new robustness line would create room for one person to lead the first paper if they could demonstrate serious range. Not just implementation. Judgment. He said the person who identified a real failure mode cleanly would not be treated as support labor. They would own the correction."

Nobody moved.

Leela heard the word own as if it had dropped onto the table and cracked. One person. The first paper. Own the correction. Mohit had wrapped a future with only one chair in it in the language of a task.

June spoke first. "One person where."

"Lead author," Omar said. "Possibly the talk as well, if it moved quickly enough. He made it sound..." He stopped, and for a moment the carefulness broke. "He made it sound like the sort of thing that changes how people see you."

Ben stared at him. "Like an internal paper?"

"There's no such thing as an internal paper when your advisor is shopping it to everyone he has ever met," June said.

Omar's mouth tightened. "I understand that."

"Do you?" June asked, not loudly.

The spoon settled against the serving bowl with one last thin click. Leela felt the bad table leg stop jittering under her wrist and understood, a second late, that nobody was moving. Omar had not meant to show the card whole. June had not meant to react before deciding how. Ben was trying to shrink without physically moving. The chicken on Leela's plate had gone cold in the sauce.

She saw the structure all at once and not at all. If Omar had been promised leadership for finding a weakness, then weakness was not a technical fact waiting in the data. Weakness was now a door. Somebody got through; somebody became the wall.

"Did he say whose failure mode?" Leela asked.

"No."

"Then whose work are you supposed to own the correction of?" June said.

"He didn't phrase it that way."

"That's because the way I phrased it's ugly."

Ben jumped in too quickly. "Maybe he just meant there'd be one natural point person. Like for coordination. That doesn't automatically mean somebody loses."

June turned to him. "Ben, I need you to hear the sentence you just said and ask yourself whether you've ever seen a lab in this building coordinate anything without blood on the floor."

He opened his mouth, shut it, and reached for his beer instead. His ears had gone red. Leela had the brief, unwelcome impression of him at fourteen, trying to talk his way back into a lunch table.

Omar rubbed at a streak of sauce on the edge of his plate with his thumb. "I'm not saying yes to anything," he said. "I'm saying he said it."

"Those aren't separate things anymore," June said.

The cruelty of it was that June was right and wrong at once. Leela could feel both truths scraping against each other. Omar had not made the offer. He had still heard it. He had carried it here in his mouth like a stone and now set it on the table between the rice bowl and the salt.

June stood up abruptly and started stacking plates that were not yet empty. "Dessert," she said. "Because apparently we deserve plum pastries before the republic collapses."

The line was funny enough that everybody let it be funny. That was the last generosity the room managed. Omar opened the bakery box. Ben asked whether the plums were local, a question so stupid with nerves that June actually laughed for real. Leela said the limes had been criminally priced and June called that the most important economic indicator of the quarter. Omar said the bakery was selling cardamom knots now and they were an offense against both cardamom and bread. They all played along because dinner required an ending and none of them knew how else to get to one.

Ben left first, insisting on taking the trash out as if volunteer labor might rebalance the evening. At the door he looked around with a hopeful, battered expression and said, "Next week I'll bring normal olives."

"Bring cheaper wine," June said. "This one tastes like a candle confessed."

He laughed, relieved to have been permitted a path out, and was gone in a rush of night air and stair noise.

Omar stayed long enough to help carry plates to the sink. He did it with his sleeves rolled higher now and with the concentrated dignity of a man refusing to seem either ashamed or defensive. At the door he turned back once, bakery box in hand.

"For what it's worth," he said, to no one person exactly, "I may have overstated it. He speaks in futures. Sometimes he means only atmosphere."

"That's not better," June said.

Omar accepted that. "No," he said. "It isn't." Then he left too.

With both of them gone, the table showed its gaps all at once. Ben's chair sat crooked by the window, Omar's napkin square remained beside the salt, and the fan kept chopping air nobody needed. The stove ticked as it cooled. Grease hardened on the plates in streaks the color of dried rust.

June picked up the sponge. Leela took the dish towel without asking. They had done this often enough that the motions did not require coordination. Wash, rinse, dry, stack. The sink was too small for the pot, so June attacked it in circles while Leela stood hip to hip with her at the counter, catching whatever could be saved from slipping back into the basin.

For a while they said nothing. The quiet had weight to it, but not the clean kind. It felt like a storage problem.

Leela dried a bowl and said, carefully, "Did he tell you anything else?"

June did not look up. "Mohit? Besides turning Tuesday dinner into a hostage video?"

"June."

"Yeah. He told me things." She rinsed, set the pot down harder than necessary, and bit into another piece of nicotine gum. "He always tells me things."

Leela waited. Outside, a siren moved up Alder and away again. The towel in her hands had gone damp and useless.

June exhaled through her nose. "After the meeting he stopped me in the hall and did the soft voice. The one he uses when he wants you to feel selected instead of cornered."

Leela knew the voice exactly. So did everybody in that lab, probably. It changed almost nothing in the sentence and everything in the balance of power.

"He said," June went on, scrubbing at the Dutch oven as if the enamel had personally betrayed her, "'I don't want you blindsided later if someone is sitting on a benchmark weakness and hoping you won't catch it before external review.'"

Leela set the bowl down so she would not drop it.

June laughed once, without joy. "That was the phrasing. 'Sitting on.' As if the data were a fucking tack somebody could hide under a cushion."

"What did you say?"

"What do you think I said? I said, 'Interesting, thanks,' because he's a faculty member and I enjoy housing." She finally looked at Leela. Her face had flattened into the expression it wore when fear was trying to pass as annoyance. "Then I spent the rest of the walk home doing inventory in my head. Who has my benchmark scripts, who touched the preprocessing, who I'd forgotten to cc on what, whether I left anything sloppy enough for somebody to turn into a morality play."

Leela could feel the damp hem of her T-shirt sticking to the small of her back. June kept grinding the sponge over one patch of enamel until it squealed. Mohit had planted a social hallucination and left June to live inside it.

"He told Ben the lab needed clearer roles," Leela said.

"Of course he did."

"And Omar that overlap was maturity, basically. Or professionalism. Something adjacent."

"And you?"

Leela folded the towel once, then again. There were only so many ways to delay a plain sentence before the delay became its own answer. "That if I wanted him to formalize my last year, I should show I could do hard things without making them interpersonal."

June barked a laugh. "So that's your version. Don't make them interpersonal. Incredible."

"I know."

"Do you? Because from where I'm standing it sounds like he told you to keep everything at frames and degrees until nobody has to say who's getting cut."

The line landed because it was aimed well. Leela felt it in the wet ceramic under her fingers. She had, after all, nearly done exactly that at the table. Degrees. Frames. Ordinary faculty manipulation. The sort of sentence people built tenure cases out of while somebody else bled quietly under it.

"I'm aware," she said. "It sounded bad."

"It sounded like you."

That hurt more because June said it without relish. No joke on top, no cushioning profanity. Just a clean placement of the blade.

Leela set the towel down. "What do you want me to say?"

June turned off the tap. Water ticked from the faucet into the quiet. "I don't know yet. That's sort of the problem."

They stood there facing the sink like people waiting for an x-ray to develop. Leela could smell the vinegar from the cucumber bowl, the detergent June bought in bulk because the laundromat upcharged for soap, the faint sweet burn of the gum in June's mouth. She wanted to ask the direct question. Do you think he's trying to turn us on each other? The sentence rose, found every possible cost attached to it, and would not cross.

June broke first, but only by a fraction. "If you hear anything about my benchmark," she said, eyes still on the rack of drying plates, "I need you to tell me before he does."

June kept slotting plates into the rack as she said it, one after another, without looking over. Each plate hit the metal tines with a neat little click. That ordinary rhythm made the request land harder.

Leela thought of Omar's careful jaw at the table, of Ben borrowing Mohit's diction because he did not yet own his own, of the way Mohit had made every offer sound private and therefore flattering. She thought of her own year hanging on a signature. She thought of how quickly the room had frozen around the phrase one person.

"Okay," she said.

June nodded once, then went to the counter and started spooning leftovers into reused yogurt tubs. Halfway through, she wiped her hand on the dish towel, dug a black Sharpie out of the junk drawer, and wrote directly on the lids: JUNE on one, LEELA on the other. The marker squeaked against the plastic.

When Leela finally left, June handed her the tub with her name on it and said, "Text me when you get home." It was what she always said after dinner. Her thumb pressed once across the lid before she let go.

On the stairs, the tub sweated in Leela's palm. Her name sat in June's black block letters across the lid, catching the stairwell light each time she turned. By the time Alder Street opened below, the ink had started to feather at the edges, but the name was still easy to read. So was June's request.


# Chapter 3

The basement smelled like hot dust and old coffee, that sealed Keating air that made every season feel faintly mechanical. Leela had been pretending to read a benchmark paper in the outer lab, one eye on the PDF and one eye on the hallway, because it still felt possible that if she was visibly present enough Mohit might remember he owed her a real answer about her year. When he appeared in his doorway and said, "Do you have a minute?" the question landed in the tone that implied a favor she should be grateful to provide.

His office door clicked shut behind her. He did not usually close it for small things.

"What's up?" she said.

Mohit stayed standing for another beat, looking at the screen on his desk as if the difficulty lived there and not in him. He had the same immaculate shirtsleeves he always had, pale blue today, cuffs clean, expensive glasses catching the fluorescence. In public that softness read as youth or calm. In a closed room it read more like the discipline of somebody who had learned never to give away the wrong thing for free.

"We may have had some repository irregularity over the weekend," he said. "Probably noise. Possibly not. I don't want to make it larger than it is before we have a clean read."

Leela waited. He liked these little ramps, the part where everybody else was supposed to prove they understood the seriousness without forcing him to say it too plainly.

"What kind of irregularity?"

"Bulk clone behavior. A few pulls that don't map neatly to the usual workflow. It could be a backup job misfiring. It could be someone getting overcurious about another student's code. It could be nothing except bad hygiene, which frankly would be on brand for this building." He gave her a small private smile, inviting her into a wearily competent we. "I need somebody careful on it."

The ugly thing was how quickly that worked. Careful. Not brilliant, not fast, not visionary. Careful was the compliment he gave when he wanted unpaid labor and a low chance of public drama, and still some part of her leaned toward it like a plant toward a window.

"You want me to check Hades?" she said.

"Just the access trail for now. Quietly."

He pulled open a drawer, took out a yellow sticky note, and slid it across the desk blotter toward her. On it, in his neat squared-off handwriting, were the old admin username and a temporary password. Not even temporary, really. A variation on one she had used two years ago, before Mohit said the cluster needed cleaner governance and took most of the permissions back into a folder of his own.

She looked down at the note and then back at him. "I thought that account was disabled."

"Disabled is a strong word." He sat now, finally, one wrist resting on the lip of the desk. "Dormant. It's still the least messy way in. If I send this through central IT, I create a ticket, and then we have a record before we even know if there's a problem. I'd rather not invite process into the room before we have to."

He said process the way other people said raccoons.

Leela turned the sticky note over once with her thumbnail. The paper had already softened a little from his hand. "Do you know which repos?"

"Some student directories. I didn't do a deep pass. I saw enough to know I didn't want Ben freelancing an interpretation." He gave a brief exhale through his nose. "June would turn it into a constitutional crisis, Omar is underwater, and you actually know the system's history."

There it was, the inventory of why she mattered and why everybody else was unusable. Even June got deployed here as a warning. Leela heard herself ask, "Do they know something happened?"

"No, and let's keep it that way unless we have to widen the circle." His voice went warmer, more mentor than manager. "Leela, this is exactly the kind of thing that reminds me why I trusted you with infrastructure in the first place. You see the shape of a mess without needing to perform your feelings about it."

He always sounded most paternal when he was asking someone to help him lie about scale.

She should have asked why he had noticed the irregularity himself if he had not done a deep pass. She should have asked why he still had her old account alive. She should have asked why a faculty director who had spent the last two weeks making everybody compete for scraps suddenly wanted discretion more than compliance. Instead she said, "Okay. I'll take a look tonight."

Mohit nodded as if she had restored something orderly in the world. "Good. Send me a short readout when you have one. And keep this bounded. If it's student curiosity, I want it handled as mentoring, not scandal."

"Sure."

"And once this is off the table, let's sit down properly about your timeline. I know that uncertainty has been hanging over you."

The sentence landed exactly where he meant it to. Not a promise, not even a date, just a little ration of future. He knew how little it took now.

She put the sticky note in her notebook and stood. Mohit had already turned back to his screen, the conversation filed. On the wall behind him, a framed printout from some old award still thanked him for leadership in data-for-good research, the kind of phrase that sounded noble until you had watched the machinery that fed it.

At the door he said, without looking up, "I appreciate this."

Outside, the lab kept humming as if nothing had happened. Ben was at the far whiteboard drawing boxes around boxes with a blue marker, his expensive jacket slung over the back of a chair like he expected nobody would ever steal from engineers. He was mouthing some sentence to himself as he erased and rewrote a heading. June was not there. Omar was not there. The room felt briefly simpler because the people who would have read something into Mohit's closed door were gone.

Leela went to the bathroom, took the sticky note back out, and read it again in the stall. The password was one extra symbol added to the old one, a lazy mutation. Mohit was always preaching attack surfaces and threat models to first-years and then doing things like this inside his own lab. The practical explanation was that he trusted obscurity more than hygiene when the hygiene inconvenienced him. The less practical explanation sat next to it and would not stop breathing.

She washed her hands longer than necessary, pressing her thumb into the sore ridge at the edge of her wrist brace. Then she went upstairs to the graduate office and stayed there until dusk answering email she did not care about, because if she logged into the cluster at four in the afternoon it would feel too much like obedience. At six thirty she bought peanut-butter crackers and a sweating bottle of Diet Coke from the machine near the loading dock, carried both back down to the basement, and waited until even Ben finally left, backpack bulging, cheerful enough to wave.

The lab after hours never became quiet. It only lost the frequencies made by people. Once the talking stopped you could hear what the building had been saying all along: the fluorescent ballast whine, the thin rattle in the vent by the server closet, the old refrigerator by the sink clicking into another doomed attempt at cold. The salvaged couches looked more embarrassing empty. Somebody had left half a seminar cookie on a napkin by the printer, one bite gone and the rest hardening into department archaeology.

Hades lived in a closet off the main room behind a louvered metal door that never fully latched. The cluster had been named by a previous generation of graduate men who found mythology hilarious when applied to purchased hardware. Nobody had renamed it after the joke stopped being funny. The closet was too warm all year and carried a linty current of air that smelled faintly toasted, like circuitry and old carpet. When Leela pulled over the rolling chair, the fake leather seat gave a small ripping sound under her thigh where it had split last spring.

She logged in from the battered workstation outside the closet because remote access from her apartment liked to fail exactly when she needed it, and because if something went wrong she wanted to be physically next to the noise. Her old username still worked. The password worked on the first try. She hated that most of all, the confirmation that this piece of buried infrastructure had been sitting there all this time, waiting to be useful to him again.

For a minute she just looked at the prompt.

Then she started with the unglamorous things. Authentication logs. Git service logs. Cron jobs. Access histories on the shared storage mount. The work was not detective cinema. It was waiting for text to unspool, narrowing time windows, copying one ugly line into a scratch buffer and then another, realizing she had the wrong date format, backing out, trying again. She kept a legal pad on her knee because if she trusted herself to remember the timestamps she'd be there until dawn inventing patterns that were not there. Her own handwriting slanted worse the more tired she got.

At 8:14 she found the weekend spike Mohit had been talking about. It was real enough: a burst of authenticated access late Saturday night, then several large clone operations in close succession. The traffic moved with purpose, one mirror firing after the next. Several repositories under the lab group had been duplicated almost back to back, the kind of sequence no normal student workflow produced because nobody sane copied six private repos on a Saturday unless they were leaving the lab or stripping it for parts.

Leela sat back and ate two crackers without tasting them.

The first clean possibility was a bad backup. She wanted that badly enough to spend forty minutes proving it false. The scheduled backup jobs ran to a different path on a different timetable. These pulls had touched live student repositories directly, including private branches. Worse, the destination did not look like any of the old archive locations she had helped set up. The path name surfaced in fragments at first, just enough to be annoying: `/srv/ruderal/ingest/`, then later `ruderal_sync`, then a permissions entry on a staging directory called `ruderal_private`.

Ruderal.

She wrote it on the legal pad and stared at the word. It sounded botanical, or maybe pharmaceutical, one of those names that tried to imply resilience by borrowing from weeds. No folder she had ever seen on Hades carried a name like that; it was too slick for a university storage convention and too self-conscious for a benchmark suite. She went back through the logs and found it again, this time in a service-account creation command from August, buried between more ordinary admin noise. The creating user was `msaran`.

Her body did not give her a dramatic reaction. No cinematic drop in the stomach, no clear bell of revelation. She only became aware that the brace on her wrist had soaked through underneath, the skin there damp and itchy, and that the server fan was blowing hot air directly at her shins through the closet slats like a small mean animal.

She checked the line again.

User added service account `ruderal_sync`.

User `msaran`.

August date, before the semester started.

She copied the line into a local file. Then she checked whether `msaran` could be somebody else, some inherited service name from the bad old days. Every trail led back to Mohit. Hades was a mess, but it usually attached actions to actual people. Mohit had his own faculty account, same as everyone. He had created something on the cluster in August and named it after a word that kept reappearing around mirrored student repositories.

Leela rubbed the heel of her hand against one eye until lights burst under the lid. When she looked again, the text had not changed.

She kept digging because the alternative was getting up and deciding what this meant, and she was not ready for the deciding part. She pulled the detailed access history on the Saturday session. The source IP belonged to Haviland's VPN range, which was irritatingly broad until she cross-referenced a second log and saw the device label attached to the key exchange. `msaran-mbp`. Not subtle. Not even especially competent. Just protected by the ordinary fact that almost nobody below faculty rank spent their nights reading auth logs unless somebody asked them to.

The repository list made it intimate: June's benchmark codebase, Omar's theory experiments, her own dissertation repo, including the embarrassing side branch where she kept half-finished methods notes because she could not bear to look at them in the main tree, and a smaller private repository that held lab meeting notes and old figure drafts. This went past paper outputs. He had lifted the rooms inside the house.

She opened the destination tree only far enough to confirm structure. The mirrors lived inside an active working directory. The folders had fresh timestamps after the initial clone. Somebody had been pulling updates. There were subdirectories labeled for demo assets and comparative plots. One folder held exported images with filenames that preserved student initials. Another held a markdown inventory of datasets and claims, half finished, as if whoever wrote it expected to come back later and turn the mess into something presentable.

Ruderal was a workspace.

Leela put both feet flat on the floor. The chair wobbled because one wheel had started sticking again, and she had to brace herself against the metal rack to stop the little skid. Her fingers smelled like salt from the crackers and dust from the keyboard. Somewhere down the hall a bathroom hand dryer started up, ran for too long, and quit. Somebody else was still in the building after all, though the floor felt deserted enough that the sound only sharpened the solitude instead of softening it.

She read the markdown inventory once, then again. It carried the chill of a planning document, stripped of even the little false nobility Mohit liked to apply in meetings. Repo names. Candidate figures. Notes on generalization claims. A line about "student-facing provenance clean-up" that made her sit perfectly still for three seconds because there was no benign reading of it and her mind still tried to produce one.

Maybe he was preparing a sponsor deck and planned to loop them in later.

Maybe Haviland's intellectual-property policy was broad enough that this was disgusting but not technically theft.

Maybe Ruderal was some stupid temporary internal codename for the lab's industry push and he had simply not told them because everything this semester now arrived as theater.

Maybe, maybe, maybe. The mind was a pathetic machine when it wanted a softer fact.

She pulled the access logs on her own repo and saw the mirrored private branch names appear one after another, including `chapter2_failures` and `defense_timeline_notes`, and the maybe machine finally lost a little power. There was no professional reason Mohit needed the folder where she had written, in two separate ugly files, what happened if the final year did not materialize. There was no startup hygiene explanation for June's rebuttal notes or Omar's draft theorem appendix sitting under a directory none of them knew existed.

She imagined June seeing this. June would go flat first, voice gone efficient, and then mean. Not theatrical, Mohit's favorite accusation. Specific. She would say, I knew it, and then she would say, How long have you known? The second question mattered more. It always had.

Leela checked the time. 10:52.

The cleanest thing would be to text June now. Are you awake. Come downstairs. There is a thing you need to see. June lived fifteen minutes away; she would put on shoes, she would come, and by midnight at least two people in the lab would understand what Mohit had done. By tomorrow morning it might be everyone.

June was the deeper problem. She would understand, immediately and correctly, that Mohit had chosen Leela for this errand because Leela still came when called. He could close a door, hand her an old credential, promise to discuss her timeline later, and she would say okay in under a minute. The shame in that ran deeper than the ordinary shame of graduate school. Naive faith had already curdled into appetite. She still wanted to be the one he trusted to handle the ugly parts. She still wanted to matter to him in the special administrative way that often preceded getting fed.

She put the phone down without unlocking it.

The next clean thing would be to create a record outside him. Email the logs to herself at Gmail. Forward them to Gloria Fisk. File a dry little ticket with central IT: old dormant admin account active, unauthorized service account, mirrored student repositories under unknown directory. That version required less courage than a confrontation and more honesty than silence. It also required her to admit, in writing, that she had used inherited admin credentials off the books at Mohit's request. Once it entered a system it would not stay her story. It would become timestamps, accounts, scope of access. Mohit would say he had asked for a limited audit and she had exceeded her remit. He would say she had longstanding access habits he had been trying to regularize. He would say this was exactly why informal technical stewardship by students created governance problems. He would not need to invent much. The ingredients already existed.

She could also simply confront him in person tomorrow, shut his office door and lay the printout down between them and ask what Ruderal was. But even in fantasy the scene bent toward him. He would fold his hands, look saddened, and ask why she had dug beyond the breach question. He would tell her lab materials had to be prepared for partnership conversations in aggregate. He would say she was collapsing administrative roughness into moral accusation because she was stressed about her own timeline. Then he would ask who else had seen the logs, and if the answer was nobody, she would be alone in the room with a man who had just proved how quietly he could move student work out the back door.

Then the practical part of her went to work.

If she told nobody tonight, the fact would remain a fact tomorrow.

If she told nobody tonight, she could think.

If she told nobody tonight, she would not yet have detonated the last flimsy version of her year in a burst of righteous clarity that nobody at Haviland would reward.

She kept arranging the reasons as if order changed what they cost. Saying the sentence aloud would make the whole structure real: Mohit was stealing from them, and he had felt safe enough asking her to find the proof.

She copied the relevant logs into an encrypted archive on her laptop. The progress bar moved with insulting leisure. While it ran she pared back the local shell history on the workstation, not enough to beat a real audit, only enough that anyone glancing tomorrow would have to work to see the shape of the night.

When the archive finished, she named it `notes_fall_misc.zip` and dropped it among old dissertation folders.

She opened a new email to Mohit and stared at the blank body until the cursor settled into its patient blink. Then she typed:

No sign of an external breach. There was some internal bulk mirroring over the weekend into a staging directory I don't recognize, and the access appears to have come through valid credentials from inside Haviland's VPN. I can clean up the stale account issue tomorrow if you want. My read is that this stays in the lane of internal permissions drift.

She read it twice. The lie lived in the compression, in the way the email turned theft into housekeeping. Internal bulk mirroring. Valid credentials. Internal permissions drift. By morning he could forward it to whoever he liked and let those phrases do the narrowing for him.

Her thumb hovered over the trackpad.

She pictured June seeing the thread later, her voice going flat on the words internal permissions drift. Omar would go to the timestamp first, then the stale admin account, then everything Leela had chosen not to say.

The cursor kept blinking. In the server-closet heat, the workstation fan and her own pulse found the same thin pitch.

Leela sent the email.

The whoosh sound was tiny, almost comic. Years of training and debt and deferred adulthood, and the decisive noise was the same one that announced seminar reminders and potluck sign-ups.

Afterward she sat still long enough for the workstation screen to dim. In the blackened reflection she could see the pale stripe of fluorescence across her face, her braid loosened at the neck, the legal pad on her knee with RUDERAL written too hard into the paper. She tore off that page, folded it into quarters, and tucked it into the back pocket of her notebook instead of throwing it away.

When she finally stood, her legs tingled from the bad angle and the chair gave another small skin-peeling rip under her. She shut the server closet door though it would never stay fully shut. At the sink she rinsed the cracker salt from her fingers and watched the water go cloudy from dust. The half cookie by the printer was still there. Ben's blue marker cap was still off on the whiteboard ledge. Ordinary stupid lab life had survived the evening intact, which felt like an accusation too.

Mohit's reply came before she left the building.

Thanks. I suspected as much. Let's discuss tomorrow.

That was all. No surprise, no request for the directory name, no curiosity about scope. Only confirmation that whatever needed managing had been managed for the night.

Leela put the phone face down in her bag. She switched off the desk lamp by the sink, checked once that the encrypted file sat where she had hidden it among old dissertation folders, and walked out into the hallway with the notebook held flat against her ribs as if she were carrying something breakable. The basement door shut behind her with the soft padded seal of a well-funded building. Upstairs, somewhere above the concrete and ducts and humming pipes, the rest of the university kept moving toward morning without knowing what had already been taken.


# Chapter 4

By Wednesday morning, after Mohit's reply had flattened everything into tomorrow, the Civic Signals Lab had split into three temperatures. The hallway outside the basement stayed damp and cold from the building's overcommitted air conditioning. The lab itself was fluorescent and stale, all old carpet and takeout salt. The server closet breathed through its slatted door like something alive, pushing a sour animal heat over the nearest desks. Leela had moved her chair there on purpose. If she stayed close enough to the machines she could pretend the sweat at the back of her neck belonged to work and not to nerves.

The whiteboard by the sink held a cluster schedule in blue marker, written over months of ghosted equations and abandoned grant timelines.

`hades`

`Wed 08:00 - Thu 23:59    LEELA`

The block looked vulgar in capital letters. It looked even worse because June had added a question mark beside it sometime before dawn.

Leela refreshed the queue again. Four GPU jobs running, one preprocessing job stuck in `CG` because the node had decided not to die all the way. Her coffee had gone cold an hour ago and developed that skin on top she always wanted to call crema just to make the cup feel less humiliating. She took a swallow anyway. The bitterness sat on her tongue like aspirin dust.

She had started with one clean question on Monday. Could Omar's model survive adversarial corruption on the civic complaint data, or was robustness mostly a brochure word Mohit used because it sounded more expensive than accuracy? By Wednesday the question had developed branches and disclaimers and a separate file of filenames Mohit had told her not to use anymore. The model failed on heavy OCR noise. It failed on transliterated street names. It failed worst on the smallest municipalities, which would have been the actual interesting result if interesting results had ever paid anyone's rent.
