On one narrowed slice, moderate perturbations, English-dominant complaints, no cross-municipality transfer, the degradation curve bent in a way that almost looked publishable. She had been trying to make almost hold still for thirty-six hours.

The lab door banged once against the stopper. June came in carrying a laptop under one arm and a paper cup under the other, blunt-cut hair clipped behind one ear with a bobby pin that had lost its black paint. She wore the same dark work pants she wore for everything from seminar to hauling old monitors out to surplus. A flattened pack of nicotine gum made a square in her jacket pocket. For one stupid beat Leela thought June might not have seen the whiteboard yet.

June read it anyway, and the hope went bad in Leela's stomach before June even looked at her.

"Tell me you didn't reserve the whole rack."

Leela pulled her wrist brace tighter. The Velcro had started to fuzz at the edges, and the brace bit into the damp skin of her wrist. "I requested through tomorrow night. Mohit said the deadline paper takes priority." Requested sounded bureaucratic the second it left her mouth, like the queue had happened by weather instead of by choice.

June set her cup down hard enough to make it slosh. "My benchmark reruns take priority too. It turns out the laws of physics didn't get the memo."

"I know."

"Do you?" June tapped the question mark she had drawn beside Leela's name. "Because this looks less like 'I know' and more like hostage notation."

Leela turned the monitor a few degrees, as if the queue would look more defensible from another angle. "I can probably free node C this afternoon if the current sweep finishes." Probably was a cheap word, shaped like borrowed time.

"Probably is not a time."

"I know that too."

June stared at the screen. On a better day she would have made the joke bigger, put on the fake customer-service smile she used when she wanted to make fury look recreational. This morning the humor arrived thin and late, which scared Leela more than if June had started yelling.

"My baseline is wandering again," she said. "Same commit, same data manifest, different numbers every run. I need the machines and I need to know whether it's the cache, the seed handling, or whether the cluster has finally achieved sentience and hates me personally."

Leela kept looking at the job IDs because if she looked at June she would remember all the Tuesdays when compute had been a collective problem instead of a ration card. June made extra rice because somebody always stayed late. June moved jobs around for everyone as if queueing were a form of manners. June had once slept on the lab couch with a hoodie over her face because Leela's camera-ready deadline had turned into an all-night figure crisis, and at four in the morning she had still gotten up to rewrite a broken plotting script without making martyrdom noises about it.

Before Mohit turned everybody's projects into secret adversarial assignments, June would have been standing beside her already, one hand on the desk, asking to see the failure cases. Now even saying whose model she was red-teaming felt like a disclosure, and the prohibition made every ordinary request sound tactical.

"If I release the block now," Leela said, "I lose the ablation table."

"If you keep it, I lose tomorrow's methods lunch."

The door opened again. Mohit came in with a faculty-lounge espresso in a real ceramic cup, which already irritated Leela before he said anything. He had on a pale blue shirt with the sleeves folded once at the forearm, exact as if someone had measured the folds, and his expensive glasses had gone faintly opaque from the temperature change. He took in the whiteboard, June's posture, Leela's screen, and smiled with the tired patience of a man arriving at an obstacle he had privately predicted.

"Morning," he said. "How bad is it?"

June answered first. "Bad enough that I'd like access to the machines I also use to do my job."

Mohit nodded as if she had made a reasonable contribution to an agenda he already controlled. "Right now our nearest external deadline is Leela's conference submission. So for the next thirty-six hours, contiguous access goes to that. June, can you debug locally and then queue around her?"

June let out one laugh. It had no amusement in it. "Queue around her meaning after the conference is over, or after the sun burns out?"

"Meaning we do resource triage like adults." He set his cup down on the nearest desk and looked at Leela, not June. "This paper needs a complete pass before tomorrow night. Once that lands, we can redistribute."

Leela heard the we and hated it. It made the decision sound communal, like the machines had voted.

"I just need one node tonight," June said. Her voice had gone flat, which was worse than if she had started swearing. "I've got figures that won't reproduce. If I walk into methods lunch tomorrow with numbers I can't defend, that's my face in the room, not yours."

Mohit leaned against the edge of the conference table. "Then don't walk in with numbers you can't defend. Walk in with the structure of the result and name the replication work as ongoing."

"That's not how hostile rooms work and you know it."

"Hostility is partly a framing problem."

June looked at him for a second, then at Leela. The look was not yet betrayal. It was worse. It was the beginning of inventory.

"Fine," she said. "If that's the system, say it's the system."

"It is the system," Mohit said mildly.

June picked up her cup. "Great. Love transparency."

She left without slamming the door. The restraint made the room feel smaller.

Mohit waited until her steps had faded into the hallway. Then he took a sip of espresso and looked back at Leela's monitor.

"Can you make this worth the disruption?" he asked.

There was no anger in it, no overt warning. He asked it like a practical question about whether a purchase had been justified.

"I'm trying to get the moderate-noise slice stable."

"Trying is not the same as getting there."

He picked up his cup again. "Come by my office in ten. Bring the current figures, not the graveyard version."

He left the cup ring on June's desk pad.

Leela sat still long enough for the scheduler to refresh twice. On the second refresh, one of June's jobs appeared in the pending queue with an estimated start time sometime after midnight Thursday. It looked like a small cruelty because it was one. The machine had translated the morning into a timestamp.

She opened the paper draft. The abstract still said the evaluation "identified severe brittleness under realistic perturbation." That had been true on Monday, when truth and usefulness had not yet fully separated. She highlighted the sentence and did nothing with it.

Her phone shivered against the desk with a text from June: `if you get node c before noon, i need two hours. not kidding`

Leela watched the message preview vanish back into the black screen.

Mohit's office was on the second floor, one short corridor up from the labs, far enough from the basement that the air changed. The faculty hall always smelled faintly of somebody else's lunch, toasted cumin or reheated salmon or coffee grounds in a ceramic mug that had never been washed quite enough. His office door was open. Through it she could see the neat public version of his life: books arranged by topic and spine height, one framed conference photo, one plant that somehow never crisped at the edges, though everyone else's office plants died by November.

When the room was full of students, Mohit looked younger than he was. The warmth sat on him well in groups. Alone, with the hall noise cut down and the overhead light showing what the basement fluorescents blurred, the skin under his eyes had gone sallow. He motioned her in and closed the door with two fingers.

"Show me where it breaks," he said.

Leela opened her laptop on the small round meeting table. The chair stuck slightly to the backs of her thighs. She pulled up the plots and rotated the screen toward him. The top row showed the first run, graceful degradation under low and moderate corruption, enough of a drop to sound serious and enough retained performance to sound like progress. The lower rows showed the reruns, the line flattening where it should not, one seed dipping below baseline hard enough to make the story ridiculous.

"The full perturbation suite is dead," she said. "Heavy noise kills it. Cross-municipality transfer kills it. Even the moderate band only looks good on the original split. When I rerun with fresh shuffles the confidence interval gets wide enough that the claim is basically decorative."

Mohit folded his hands. "You're combining several different questions."

"No, I'm combining the questions the paper was supposed to answer."

"On Monday," he said, still calm, "the paper was supposed to identify failure modes. It did. That is useful. The conference submission is a separate question. The conference needs a tractable statement about where the model degrades gracefully and how we measured that."

Leela had written enough committee emails in her life to hear the trick in the nouns. Failure modes had become useful. Selective reporting had become tractable. The conference needed things. The people in the room had apparently turned optional.

"If I cut everything except the moderate-noise band," she said, "it reads stronger than the evidence."

"No. It reads scoped."

"It reads cleaner than the data is."

"The data is what it is. We decide what question to ask of it."

He reached over and tapped the upper-left plot with one fingernail. "This is the paper. Moderate perturbation, worst-group calibration, stress test across realistic corruption rather than cartoon corruption. The rest can go in a paragraph on limitations and future work."

Leela stared at the screen long enough to see her own face reflected in the black border around it. "The multilingual slice is not cartoon corruption."

"For this conference, it is."

"That isn't a scientific category."

"Neither is 'I would have liked a cleaner week,' and yet here we are."

He leaned back in his chair. The shirt sleeves stayed immaculate. "Listen. You're senior enough to know that a submission is not a deposition. Nobody is asking for the final moral truth of the dataset by tomorrow. They're asking whether there is a coherent contribution here."

Leela felt something hot move through her chest, not noble anger, just acid and no sleep. Coherent contribution. In the lab server logs he had cloned whole repositories into a private Ruderal directory and named the folder after a weed that took over vacant lots. Now he was explaining coherence to her like a favor.

"If I say the model is robust under realistic perturbation," she said carefully, "that is false on any broad reading of realistic."

"Then don't say broad. Say what you mean. Moderate perturbation. Structured corruption. Narrow domain transfer. Use adult nouns." He gave her the small soft smile he used when he wanted to cast stubbornness as immaturity. "You don't get points for dying on a hill of methodological purity in September."

"This isn't purity."

"What is it?"

She should have said theft. She should have said that she was tired of being lectured about scope by a man with a startup folder full of stolen student work. She should have said that every week under him involved a new vocabulary word for the same underlying demand, which was Give me the version that serves me and I will call you professional for producing it. Instead she heard herself say the safer thing.

"Variance."

Mohit nodded as if she had proved his point. "Variance becomes a story only if you insist on making it one. Right now the stronger story is that you found a robust region and built an evaluation frame around it."

"One run found that region."

"Then contextualize the computational budget. Nobody in this literature is doing saintly exhaustive evaluation on civic data. They're not. You know they aren't."

He paused, and when he spoke again the warmth thinned just enough to expose the steel behind it.

"Leela, I need to be able to point to motion when I talk about your dissertation trajectory. A visible submission helps me do that. Another week in the lab without external movement does not."

There it was, the soft-voiced price tag. He did not say approval or defense or sixth year because he did not have to. He had trained all of them to hear the unstated nouns.

Leela rubbed at the edge of her notebook until the paper pilled under her thumb. "If I scope it that hard, June loses the machines for nothing."

"June isn't losing the machines for nothing. She's losing them so a senior student can put a paper into review."

"That's the same sentence with better branding."

For the first time he looked faintly tired of her. It only lasted a second.

"You're very close," he said. "Don't romanticize failure because it lets you feel cleaner than everyone else."

The line landed because it had enough truth folded into the insult. She had not exposed the Ruderal logs. She had not even told June. Cleanliness was not available to her, only gradations of filth.

Mohit turned the laptop back toward her and began listing edits in the voice he used after grant meetings, procedural and almost kind.

"Abstract: move the contribution sentence up. Results: lead with the moderate-noise band, not the collapse cases. Table two should report the strongest scoped comparison, not the omnibus average. Discussion: one paragraph on limitations, short. Do not indulge."

He glanced toward her wrist brace. "And sleep a little before submission, if possible. Exhaustion makes people melodramatic about ordinary tradeoffs."

When she left his office June was in the hall by the vending machines, feeding a dollar into the slot for pretzels. She hit the selection button twice because the machine lagged in a way everyone knew and hated. The packet dropped with a thick plastic thunk.

"Well?" June asked.

Leela shifted her laptop higher against her hip. "He wants the moderate-noise slice in front."

June chewed a piece of nicotine gum before answering. "Of course he does."

"It's not insane. That band is the only place the degradation curve is directional."

"Directional is one of his words for 'please stop making me look at the part where the thing is on fire.'"

Leela looked at the pretzel packet in June's hand because June's face had gone too readable. "I still have to see if it holds."

"Does it?"

"Not cleanly."

June gave a short nod, the kind people gave after receiving lab test results they had already guessed. "Right."

The vending machine hummed. Somewhere below them the elevator shuddered its way up from the basement.

"I need node C tonight," June said. "Not in principle. Literally tonight. My baseline is moving around and I can't tell whether it's seed drift or whether that preprocessing cache from August got rebuilt wrong. I need to know before methods lunch tomorrow, and I've got the deck meeting for the October seminar at five. If you have ten minutes, I also need your eyes on the hash logs, because you're still the only person in this building who can read old Hades output without making it sound mystical."

Leela should have said yes to one of those requests. Even one would have preserved something.

"Let me get through the afternoon sweep," she said. "Then I'll look."

June's mouth twitched as if a joke had tried to form and died on the way out.

"Sure," she said. "After your sweep, after methods lunch, after my deck meeting, after capitalism. Text me a real time when you have one."

She took the pretzels and went down the stairs instead of waiting for the elevator.

Leela spent the rest of the afternoon turning plots into smaller plots, then into captions that made the plots sound more settled than they were. She moved to the shared graduate office on the sixth floor because the basement had become too hot to think in. The office had one long window facing the lake. In daylight it could pass for relief. By evening it turned into a mirror and gave everyone back their own bad skin.

She ran the moderate-noise slice on three fresh shuffles, then five. One run came back close to the first result, not identical but near enough to tempt her. The next two sagged. The fifth returned a number that made the whole story look as if it had been assembled by a defendant's cousin. She ate crackers from the department seminar leftovers and licked the salt off her thumb because there was nothing else in the office and she did not want to lose the queue position walking to the cafe.

At 6:12 p.m. June's name lit the phone with `status on node c`. Seven minutes later another banner shoved over it: `also if you have the old hash-check script send it. mine is pulling different cache keys for no reason`. At 7:03 the third message arrived before Leela had answered either of the first two: `not joking, lee. if the august cache got overwritten i need to know now`.

Leela opened the thread and watched the gray bubbles stack over the blank half reserved for her. Then she set the phone face down on a stack of printouts. A few seconds later it buzzed again hard enough to make the paper clip tray chatter against the desk.

She pulled up the old hash-check script anyway. It sat in her utilities directory with a filename she had given it at two in the morning two years earlier, `please_god_work.py`. She could have sent it in five seconds. Instead she started a sixth run.

At 8:41 p.m. Mohit dropped two comments into the draft in quick succession: `Lead with strongest result. The point is evaluative framing, not total collapse.` and `Let's not overplay instability. Workshop audience will read that as lack of contribution.` He was home by then, or at least somewhere with lamplight warm enough to make the track-change colors look expensive. The comments had that composed end-of-day tone people used when someone else was still in the office doing the part that turned output into copy.

Leela typed back before she could stop herself.

`The strongest result is not replicating across fresh shuffles.`

The reply came four minutes later: `Then report the observed result on the scoped slice and name broader replication as future work. This is normal.` Normal had become another one of his laundering words. She stared at it until the blue message bubble blurred.

At 9:17 June called. The phone vibrated itself half an inch across the desk. Leela watched it move and did not pick up. When the ringing stopped, voicemail transcription threw up a mangled preview that was clear enough anyway: `Lee, pick up if you're there. I just need the stupid script. Call me back.` She swiped the banner away without listening to the audio.

She went down to the basement because the office had become too quiet. In the lab, the server closet heat wrapped around her like damp fabric. The fluorescents had entered their late-night phase, a faint intermittent buzz that made even clean objects look greasy. Somebody had thrown out the noodle tray but left the sauce streak on the table. Leela sat cross-legged in her chair, shoes off, one foot tucked under the opposite thigh until it went numb. She reran the sweep with the same parameters and a new seed. Negative again.

She opened the table in the paper and moved the strongest single run into the main column.

The run existed: timestamp, seed, output folder, a number the cluster had actually produced. The PDF could not contain every failed shuffle, every queue hour, every unanswered message from June glowing on her phone, and still remain the kind of document Mohit meant when he said submission. People scoped papers all the time. People decided which failures belonged in the room. If she wrote the full story there would be no paper, only a small respectable obituary for three days of compute and one more item on the list of reasons Mohit could present her as the senior student who did not finish things.

She changed the sentence one clause at a time. `In one initial evaluation, the model exhibited resilience under moderate corruption.` Then she cut `In one initial evaluation,` and watched the claim stand up straighter on the page. The shorter version bought her what the longer one did not: something Mohit could defend, something a committee might count as motion. She kept typing.

At 11:06 June appeared in the doorway.

She had changed into an old gray sweatshirt, the one with a food-service logo half peeled off the chest from too many washes. A nicotine gum wrapper was stuck to the cuff. Her laptop charger hung out of one pocket like an IV line.

"I called," she said.

"I saw."

"And?"

Leela turned back to the screen, hating herself for making that choice in such a visible way. "I'm in the middle of something."

"Obviously."

June came in and set her own laptop on the neighboring desk. The screen showed a spreadsheet of benchmark runs, green and yellow cells, comments in the margins, one column circled in red trackpad ink.

"Look," she said. She was not joking now. "This row was stable last week. Seventy-one point four, seventy-one point two, seventy-one point five. Now it is sixty-eight point nine, seventy-two point one, sixty-nine point two. Same branch. Same seed setting, as far as I can tell. If the cache is corrupt or the scheduler is reusing stale artifacts, I need to know before methods lunch tomorrow or I walk in there and get skinned alive in public."

Leela leaned over because reflex was older than strategy. The familiar code, the familiar naming conventions, June's comments in the margin written like she talked, `this one is bullshit`, `why is god like this`, `check manifest?` The problem might have been a cache mismatch. It might have been the August rebuild. It might have been nothing more dramatic than nondeterminism surfacing in a benchmark June had been too busy to babysit. Ten minutes with the logs would at least narrow it.

"I can look after this run finishes," Leela said.

June looked at the monitor on Leela's desk. "What run? The one that still doesn't hold, or the one after that?"

Leela straightened. "I don't have time for this."

"You mean you don't have time for me."

"I've got a deadline tomorrow."

"So do I, just with less branding."

The words should have escalated. Instead June's voice dropped lower.

"Did he tell you to take the pretty slice and call the rest future work?"

Leela did not answer fast enough.

"Jesus Christ," June said. Not loud. Almost tired. "He did."

"It's more complicated than that."

"No, it isn't. He found a cleaner noun for cherry-picking and now everybody gets to act civilized about it."

Leela felt the old instinct to explain, to produce enough clauses that the moral shape of the thing disappeared inside method. "The moderate band is real. The signal is there on the original split, and one rerun is close, and if I can stabilize the preprocessing"

"If you can wait long enough for the number you want."

Leela looked back at June's spreadsheet because June's face had turned too bare. "I said I'll help after this run."

June stood there another second. Then she shut her laptop.

"Don't do me the favor later," she said. "Either help now or don't."

She picked up the machine by one corner and left. The charger cable dragged behind her for a second like a tail, then snapped free of the chair leg.

Leela sat with both hands flat on the desk until the skin of her palms stuck to the laminate. The run finished. The result was still bad.

At 11:48 Mohit texted `Need draft by 8 so I can do a final pass before portal close.` Then he followed with `Do not flood the paper with caveats. Caveats are not integrity if they obscure the contribution.` Leela laughed once, a dry private sound that made nothing better. She forwarded herself the PDF figures, revised the discussion section, and cut the paragraph on the multilingual failures from six sentences to two. By 1:30 in the morning the paper had become recognizably professional. For a few seconds the trimmed paragraphs and clean table borders let her breathe. It looked like something a serious lab might submit.

She went home at 2:07, not because the work was done but because her right hand had started dropping characters while she typed. In the studio the cheap blinds leaked orange parking-lot light through the slats. She did not bother undressing all the way, only kicked off her jeans and lay down in a T-shirt and underwear on top of the sheet with her phone beside her head. The radiator knocked once and then did nothing useful. She slept for fifty-three minutes and dreamed in progress bars.

When she woke the lock screen held four unread texts from June, timestamps marching from 12:14 to 1:08 as if the night had been orderly: `forget node c. i found cpu time`, `still need the hash script`, `are you actually not answering me`, and finally, at 1:08 a.m., `never mind`.

Never mind was the angriest thing June had sent all week.

Leela showered because she could smell the server closet on her skin. The hot water ran out while she still had shampoo in her hair. She rinsed under a final burst of cold, swore once into the tile, and got dressed in yesterday's black jeans and a sweater that had gone shiny at the cuffs. On the walk back to Keating the early air was almost kind, lake wind and wet leaves and the false September suggestion that a person could restart anything.

The feeling lasted until she opened the draft.

Mohit had already left comments on the PDF she'd sent at 3:11 a.m.: `Title stronger if we foreground robustness rather than brittleness.`, `Table 2: remove variance column. It distracts from main comparison and is underpowered anyway.`, and `Abstract: "demonstrates" is fine here.`

There were no question marks anywhere. He edited like a man touching up a slide deck before a sponsor visit.

Leela put the paper side by side with the raw results. The variance column did distract. It distracted by making the result honest. She deleted it.

At 9:26 June sent a screenshot from the scheduler. Her jobs had finally started on slow shared nodes, estimated completion after 4 p.m. Beneath it she wrote `october seminar deck meeting at 5. if these numbers still drift i'm fucked`

Leela typed `I can look at the cache after submission` and then erased it because the sentence was already an insult.

At 10:02 Mohit called.

"How close are we?"

He sounded as if he were walking somewhere carpeted.

"Close enough to submit," Leela said. "Not close enough to believe."

"Belief is not the threshold."

"It should be."

"For a dissertation, perhaps. For a conference, the threshold is whether the contribution is intelligible and true within the stated scope."

"One run is not scope."

"Then stop saying one run. You did multiple evaluations. One of them supports the framed claim. The paper is about that frame."

Leela leaned against the office window. The glass held the previous night's grease from someone's forehead. Below, the lake looked metallic and uninterested.

"If someone asks for the broader failures in review"

"Then you answer the question in review. That is what review is for."

"And if June asks me why I held the cluster for a result I don't trust?"

There was a brief silence. When he spoke again his voice had gone quieter.

"June's frustrations with infrastructure aren't the governing issue here. Your submission is. Don't confuse side turbulence with your actual priorities."

Side turbulence. She pictured June in the basement at midnight with her sweatshirt cuff sticky from old gum wrappers, watching nondeterministic benchmark numbers wobble across a spreadsheet because the machines were busy generating another version of Leela's alibi.

"Send me the final PDF at noon," Mohit said. "Don't miss the window because you're feeling conflicted. Conflict is cheap. Deadlines are not."

He hung up before she could answer.

At noon she sent him the PDF. At 12:17 he returned it with two edits in the abstract and a single-line note: `Good. Submit this version.`

June did not text again until 2:04, when two messages landed almost on top of each other: `cache hashes don't match august` and, thirty seconds later, `was going to ask if you knew why but apparently i know why`.

Leela read both messages twice. The second one was unfair in the narrow technical sense. She had not caused the cache mismatch. Maybe a half-forgotten rebuild had caused it, or a storage sync glitch, or June's own deadline-cut corner from August. But unfairness was not the point. June had asked for time, for script help, for two hours on a node, for the courtesy of an answer. Leela had spent all of that currency already.

At 3:30 the conference portal opened for final upload. The system had the neutral bureaucratic look all portals shared, soft blue buttons, too much white space, a font that assumed the act underway was clean. She uploaded the anonymized PDF, then the supplemental appendix, then deleted the appendix and replaced it with a shorter one that omitted the worst collapse plots. The portal asked whether the paper included limitations. Yes. It asked whether computational resources might have constrained the breadth of evaluation. Yes. It asked whether all authors had reviewed the final submission. Yes, because Mohit had done more than review it.

At 4:11 June called again. Leela watched the screen light up with her name and a photo from last winter, both of them in June's kitchen with paper crowns from Christmas crackers somebody's cousin had mailed. June was making a face because the crown had torn in the middle. Leela let the phone ring out.

This time June did not leave even the formality of a voicemail.

Leela reopened Table 2. She could still restore the variance column. She could still change `demonstrates robust performance under moderate corruption` back to `shows promising performance in an initial moderate-corruption evaluation.` She could still make the paper small and embarrassing and probably accurate enough that Mohit would stop calling it a paper at all.

Instead she changed one more sentence in the discussion from `the model may retain utility under constrained perturbation settings` to `the model retains utility under constrained perturbation settings.`

The revision took less than two seconds. The firmer verb settled the paragraph into the shape Mohit wanted, blunt enough for a skim, stable enough to pass as confidence. One more line he could point to when he talked about trajectory.

At 4:43 Mohit sent one final text: `Submitted?` Leela typed `Uploading now.` He replied immediately: `Good. Keep the jobs live through tonight in case we need an extra sensitivity figure for rebuttal prep.`

She looked over to the scheduler window still open on the second monitor. Her reservations ran until midnight. June's latest job had crashed on node D with an out-of-memory error and requeued itself behind hers.

Leela could have canceled her block. Submission was enough for the deadline. The paper existed now. Whatever else she did to it after 5 p.m. would be greed or fear or habit, some mix so ordinary it barely needed a name. She rested her cursor over `scancel` and thought of June saying Either help now or don't.

Then June's name surfaced once more on the lock screen: `are you going to answer me at all`

The question sat on the screen while the portal generated its progress wheel. A thin blue circle filling itself with borrowed confidence.

Leela clicked `Submit`.

The confirmation page loaded with indecent speed. `Thank you for your submission.` A paper ID. A receipt. No siren, no lock on the door, no warning that the thing she had just done would remain true after she closed the browser.

She did not answer June.

On the scheduler, the jobs kept running. Her name held the machines in a clean rectangular block all the way to midnight. Below it, June's requeued job waited in yellow.

Leela turned the phone face down and left it vibrating against the desk until it stopped, as if delay were still a smaller thing than choice.


# Chapter 5

The basement seminar room already smelled wrong for October: hot projector dust, grocery-store fruit that had gone mealy in its plastic tray, and coffee cooked down to a dark medicinal syrup on the warmer plate. The Civic Signals Lab called this lunch. When faculty were coming, Mohit upgraded the humiliation budget. There were real plates today, thin white ones borrowed from somewhere upstairs, and a pink box from the bakery near the lake with cardamom knots nobody in the lab ever bought for themselves.

Leela stood in the doorway for a second longer than she needed to, balancing her laptop against her hip and watching June test the clicker at the podium. June had cut her hair again since September, blunt and uneven in the back where she'd clearly done it in her bathroom mirror, and she was already working through a pack of nicotine gum hard enough to show in her jaw. She had on gray work pants, black boots still dusted at the welt with sidewalk salt left over from last winter, and the faded navy overshirt she wore when she wanted to look like a person who fixed things instead of defended them.

June clicked forward, back, forward again. "If this remote dies in front of outsiders," she said without looking up, "I'm setting Keating on fire."

Her tone was light enough for anybody passing in the hall. Leela heard the steel under it anyway.

"I brought my laptop too," Leela said. "We can mirror from mine if the adapter gets weird."

"Great. Love redundancy. Love a field where we can build multimillion-dollar models but not reliable HDMI."

Leela set her bag against the side wall near the extra folding chairs. The room had no windows, only a strip of dusty transom glass over the hall door that admitted a weak institutional brightness. Someone had wiped the table and left damp arcs behind. The whiteboard still carried three months of ghost equations and a half-erased shopping list from some lab dinner long enough ago to feel archaeological: cilantro, onions, sparkling water, batteries.

June was running on speed and contempt, which for her counted as composure.

"Did Mohit say who was coming?" Leela asked.

"Two faculty from stats, one from policy, a postdoc from Data Futures, three first-years who heard there'd be food, and whatever bored souls drift in if the smell gets under the door." June finally looked at her. "Also Omar sent me an email at one fourteen asking if my thread IDs were stable across scrape versions, which isn't a thing a man asks at one fourteen unless God has shown him a knife."

The line should have made Leela smile. It almost did. Instead she looked toward the back of the room where Omar had put his messenger bag on a chair and was lining up a printed copy of June's slides with the straight, careful movements he used when he was trying not to show that his hands wanted to do something else. He was neatly dressed as ever, navy button-down, dark trousers, beard line exact enough to look freshly repaired. One cuff was damp where his coffee had sloshed over the lid seam. He kept smoothing the wet crescent with his thumb, trying to erase the spill by friction alone.

He didn't look at June. He didn't look at Leela either.

"What did you tell him?" Leela asked.

"That if he wanted stable IDs he could pray for them like the rest of us." June clicked to her title slide, frowned, adjusted one line break, clicked back. "He sent 'Thanks' with a period. That's never good."

Leela had been having versions of the same feeling since she woke up: not fear exactly, because fear had edges, but a broad low pressure spreading under the day. Mohit had sent three messages since eight. June had sent none since the night before, which felt worse. Need a strong room. Faculty may join early. Keep this as a working seminar. He had never once in six years used the phrase working seminar when he meant anything kind.

"You can still tell him no questions till the end," Leela said.

June laughed once through her nose. "Sure. I'll tell him no, and then he'll tell me yes in a more professional font."

The door opened behind them and Ben came in sideways, shoulder first, because he was carrying both the short HDMI cable and the long one with the grim ceremony of somebody returning from a rescue mission. His puffer jacket was still on despite the basement heat, expensive enough to shine a little at the seams. He had a sleeve of paper cups tucked under his chin and the pink bakery box balanced on top of them. A crescent of dry skin showed where he'd worried one cuticle bloody.

"I got backup cables too," he said. "And napkins, because the department kitchen only had the weird compostable forks that snap if you look at them."

"Our infrastructure emperor," June said.

Ben grinned, relieved by the joke before he'd fully earned it. "I also grabbed the extension cord from 312 because theirs is literally industrial grade, so if anything fails now it isn't on me."

"Noted," Leela said.

He set the bakery box down with too much ceremony, centered it, then nudged it again until the label faced outward. The little act of hospitality made Leela feel worse. Ben always looked happiest when he got to assist in a scene that mattered. He mistook proximity for innocence.

Mohit arrived a minute later carrying his leather folder and a stainless travel mug he never left anywhere long enough for someone else to touch. His sleeves were rolled to identical heights below the elbow. He wore the expensive glasses that made him look, from a distance, like a man whose taste had simply improved with age rather than a man who had started making money somewhere he had not mentioned. He took in the room in one sweep: food table, projector, faculty chairs, students. Then he smiled the smile that let everyone choose their own explanation for why he was smiling.

"Excellent," he said. "This looks good."

June clicked once more through her opening slide and said, "I'd like to keep questions to the end if we can. I can move faster that way."

Mohit's smile held. "Let's see how the room develops. We have a fuller audience than usual, and I want to make sure the discussion is useful."

"It'll be useful at the end too."

"Of course." He set down the folder, not the mug. "I just don't want us performing the usual artificial distinction between presentation and feedback when the whole point is to pressure-test. Omar has done some adjacent analysis that may be helpful to surface in real time."

There it was, almost polite enough to miss if you had not spent years watching him rename force as process.

June stilled with one hand on the clicker. "Adjacent how?"

Mohit looked at Omar for half a second and then back to June. "Related failure modes in benchmark construction. It may sharpen the conversation."

June's face did a small hard thing and then smoothed over it. "Great. Love sharpening."

The first faculty arrived while nobody answered that. A statistics professor Leela knew only as Professor Adler came in with a bike helmet clipped to her tote and one reflective ankle strap still fastened over her slacks; she'd clearly biked in too fast to finish becoming office-shaped. Behind her was a policy professor who always smelled faintly of clove cigarettes though Leela had never seen him smoke, and a postdoc from another lab with a baby sticker on her water bottle and the raw red eyes of somebody losing an argument with sleep. They greeted Mohit. They nodded at June. They took plates and did what invited faculty always did in basement rooms: accepted food first, so later they could tell themselves they had made the event feel collegial.

By noon every chair was taken except the one by the humming A/V rack that nobody wanted because the fan blasted warm dust at face height. Two first-years leaned against the back wall with cookies in napkins. Ben had claimed the side table nearest Mohit and opened his laptop at an angle that looked like attentiveness from every direction. Omar sat in the second row, not beside Leela, not beside June's empty backpack in the front, but exactly far enough away from both to imply accident.

Leela took a seat on the aisle where June could see her. She told herself the seat meant solidarity. It also gave Mohit a clean line to her if he decided to bring her in, which she knew he might, which she should have taken as a reason to sit in the back and did not.

Mohit made the introduction himself. He used the full lab title, the grant language, the line about civic applications. He praised June's rigor, her systems intelligence, the unusual ambition of her benchmark suite. By the end of thirty seconds of praise Leela could feel the room already being arranged. Mohit only became lavish in public when the lavishness could later be converted into plausible innocence.

"June has been building an evaluation benchmark for policy-evasion detection in local civic forums," he said. "The problem, as many of you know, is that models often look robust until users become even slightly inventive. So today you'll hear about a benchmark designed to capture that drift under realistic linguistic pressure."

He stepped aside. "June."

June stood, took one sip from her paper cup, and put it down too hard. "Hi. Thanks for coming to our basement while the weather is still pretending not to hate us."

A small, grateful laugh moved through the room.

"Short version," she said, clicking to her first content slide. "A moderation model can score beautifully on a polished benchmark and still eat shit the second a real person decides to misspell on purpose. My project is a benchmark for that gap. Local Facebook groups, school-board forums, municipal chat boards, places where people talk ugly and sloppy and like they know exactly how much the platform can and can't catch."

Leela had seen the slides twice already, once at June's apartment in fragments over takeout containers, once alone as a PDF June had sent after midnight with the subject line if you have ten minutes please tell me slide 9 doesn't make me look illiterate. In the room they played differently. June was good at this. Not warm, exactly, but alive in a way the rest of them rarely were once a projector turned on. She moved with the clicker like she was cutting wire. She did not inflate the technical language or apologize for it. When she said thread-level annotations, she meant it. When she said civic euphemism, she had examples from real moderation failures that made the faculty shift forward in their chairs despite themselves.

She took them through the data collection pipeline, the annotation scheme, the taxonomy of evasive language. She showed screenshots with usernames blacked out and little red boxes around phrases that had slipped standard moderation. school boad. clean-up operation. just asking questions about vote math. She had built the examples well enough that even the policy professor looked briefly embarrassed by how easy the games were.

"The benchmark matters," June said, "because people don't announce they're gaming your model in the nice clean syntax your training set prefers. They talk like their cousin's in the thread, and they steal each other's phrasing, and they half-joke their way around policy language. If your system can't survive that, it doesn't matter how elegant the ROC curve looks in a paper."

Leela watched the room lean in and felt, against her will, a flare of pride that made everything worse. June should have been allowed the ordinary vanity of being good at her own seminar. It was not much to ask from academia, which was why it had become impossible.

June clicked to a results table and began walking through the core finding: every standard model degraded under paraphrase and local euphemism, but the degradation was sharpest exactly where deployment rhetoric was most confident. The benchmark did not only lower scores. It reordered the leaderboard. Models that bragged about safety turned out to be brittle as sugar glass.

She was three slides from her limitations section when Mohit lifted two fingers from his mug.

"Can we pause here?" he asked. The question was pure theater. He did not wait for permission. "I think this is the right moment to widen the lens slightly. Omar, do you want to share what you found when you stress-tested the split assumptions?"

No one moved. The postdoc with the baby-sticker bottle stopped halfway through unscrewing the cap. Professor Adler's reflective ankle strap flashed once under the table as she crossed and uncrossed her feet. Two of the first-years lowered their cookies without seeming to know they were doing it.

June stayed facing the screen for a beat too long. When she turned back, her expression was still almost neutral. "I'd rather finish the main result first."

"It is the main result," Mohit said, smiling just enough to make the sentence sound generous. "Or at least one important framing question around it."

Papers shifted. Somebody's compostable fork snapped against a plate near the back. The postdoc finally recapped her bottle without drinking. Ben sat very still in a posture that managed to look eager and frightened at once.

Omar had already taken out his printout. He did not rustle the pages. He held them with the same care he used at the grocery store, the bruised-peach care of a man who handled damaged things gently even when it changed nothing.

"Sure," he said.

His voice was even. That made it crueler, though Leela knew cruelty was not the whole truth of it.

"I reran the benchmark using thread-level grouping and near-duplicate hashing across scrape versions," he said. "The issue isn't the premise. The issue is that the current split lets the model see too much of the same social fabric on both sides."

June crossed one arm over her waist. "Define too much."

Omar looked at the page, then up. "Posts from the same neighborhood thread appear in train and test. In several cases, posts from the same user do as well under variant usernames. When I grouped by source thread and removed character-level near duplicates, the headline robustness result contracted substantially."

"Substantially" was a courthouse word in his mouth. Leela felt it land around the room.

June said, "Numbers."

Omar turned the printout so the nearest row could see. "Your best condition falls from an eleven-point improvement over the baseline to under two. The gap is inside the confidence interval once the thread holdout is clean. More importantly, the benchmark may be measuring recognition of recurring local idiom and author signature rather than generalization to unseen evasive forms."

He paused. He was trying, Leela realized, to keep his language from sounding like a knife. The attempt only highlighted where the blade already was.

"For example," he said, "the model isn't necessarily learning civic policy evasion in the abstract. It may simply be learning that one account in Nassau County always writes school board as skool boad with the same missing letters. That's a real social pattern. It's not the same claim as robust transfer."

Somebody at the back gave a low involuntary whistle and then pretended it had been a cough.

June's laugh this time was one dry exhale. "Okay. Several things. One, yes, people in local groups repeat themselves. That's why they're local groups and not weather systems. Two, the benchmark is supposed to preserve community texture. If you sterilize it into isolated single posts, you break the object."

"I agree," Omar said. "That is why I grouped by thread, not by post. The community texture stays. The leakage changes."

"Leakage is your word."

"No," Mohit said softly from his chair. "I think it's a fair methodological word."

There it was: not only allowed, but ratified.

June looked at Mohit then, properly looked at him, and something went bare in her face before she got hold of it.

"Great," she said. "Then let's use the fair methodological word. The thread-held-out reruns were already in progress. They aren't in the deck because the cluster has been backed up for weeks."

Leela felt the sentence hit her like a thrown object. June did not look at her when she said it. She did not need to.

Mohit spread one hand. "And that's useful context. At the same time, if the current headline depends on an uncleared split assumption, then I think the room deserves to understand that now rather than later."

The whole trick sat inside deserved. The room became the injured party; June became the one who owed it an explanation. Public embarrassment passed for a responsible release schedule.

Professor Adler cleared her throat. "Was the thread-level grouping planned from the start, or is that a response to this critique?"

June answered before Omar could. "Planned. I've got notes from August on it. The random split was a first pass to get error surfaces and annotation sanity."

"But the slides show the random split as the primary result," Adler said.

"Because I don't, in fact, control the laws of time."

That got a laugh from one of the first-years, quick and guilty. Nobody else joined.

Omar leaned forward, elbows on knees now, the pages hanging between his hands. "June, I'm not saying the work fails. I'm saying the central claim needs to narrow. If you present this as robust transfer to unseen euphemism, it overstates what the current experiment shows."

June snapped her gum once and took it out of her mouth, folding it into a napkin with two sharp fingers. The little domestic motion in the middle of the bloodletting did something awful to Leela. It made June look less like a speaker and more like a worker being asked to clean up while she was still being hit.

"You could've sent me that in an email," June said.

Omar's jaw tightened. Fear showed there first on him, exactly where the continuity of his face started to break. "I did ask about the thread IDs."

"At one in the morning, like a man leaving a bomb under a cake."

"June," Mohit said, still soft. "Let's not dramatize. This is what an internal seminar is for."

Ben typed something with intense concentration, then stopped typing when he seemed to realize his screen could be seen from the side.

Leela could hear the server closet through the wall, a constant fan-whine rising and falling like breath through teeth. She knew, all at once and with a clarity so complete it felt stupid that she had not known it earlier, exactly how Mohit had built the room. He had told Omar his adversarial work was anonymized and ordinary. He had told June this was her seminar. He had told the faculty they were coming to see strong work under honest pressure. He had told Leela, by omission, that if she sat in the second row and behaved, she might make it through the hour without being used.

He had not needed to lie perfectly. He only needed each person to arrive with a different script.

June clicked back to the results table, reclaiming the slide the only way she had left: by putting her hand on it. "Fine. Here's the context the room deserves, then. The benchmark still shows that local euphemism and thread familiarity break standard moderation models. The cleaner split hurts the pretty number. It doesn't reverse the failure mode. If you want a benchmark that ignores community carryover entirely, that's a different benchmark, and maybe a cleaner one for some papers, but it's not this one."

She was good even now. Too good, maybe; the room could use her composure against her. The fact that she was still capable of a sharp answer let everybody keep pretending the terms were fair.

Mohit tipped his head toward Leela.

"Leela, you've been closest to the infrastructure side and to the reproducibility bottlenecks this month," he said. "How would you characterize Omar's concern? Is this a limitation to flag, or does it materially alter the claim?"

He might as well have stood up and put his hand between her shoulder blades.

Every bad option arrived at once. If she backed June cleanly, Mohit could later say she was defending a result she knew was unstable. If she backed Omar, she helped nail June to the wall with work June had not been given time to finish. If she tried to split the difference, the split itself would be the wound, because a room like this always heard nuance in the direction of power.

June turned to look at her. Not pleading. That would have been easier. It was the opposite of pleading. It was the look you gave somebody whose intelligence you had already counted on.

Leela became intensely aware of the wrist brace under her sleeve, the edge of the Velcro rough against the thin skin near her thumb. She could feel the heat from the A/V fan across the aisle on one cheek. A drop of coffee had dried tacky on the side of her paper cup and glued itself to two fingers.

She knew what she could say. She could say June had been rerunning the clean split already. She could say compute allocation had been a mess and the room was treating an intermediate version like a final claim. She could say the critique was valid and the ambush was not. She could say any one of those things.

Instead she heard herself choosing the sentence that sounded most defensible from a future committee room.

"I think," she said, and hated how careful she sounded, "if the thread-level overlap is doing what Omar says, then the transfer claim probably needs to be narrower right now. I don't think it kills the benchmark, but it isn't a footnote either."

Silence.

Not a dramatic silence. Worse. The ordinary, administrative silence of people hearing a question settle.

June looked back at the screen. The side of her face had gone flat in a way that made her look older. Not wiser. Just more expensive to damage.

"Great," she said after a second. "That's useful. Anybody else want a turn with the knife before I keep going?"

Nobody spoke.

Mohit folded his hands. "Let's keep the tone productive."

June gave him a smile so brief it barely counted as visible. "Oh, absolutely."

The policy professor raised two fingers, cookie still in hand. "If the cleaner split is the one you'd trust in deployment, what survives of the claim now?"

June kept one hand on the podium. "The part where the models still fail on local euphemism. What gets smaller is the transfer claim. I stop saying robust transfer to unseen evasive language and say the benchmark is stronger for within-community failure under a clean thread holdout."

"And the baseline?" Professor Adler asked, already writing.

"It looks less embarrassing once you stop letting it peek at the same PTA mothers twice."

One of the first-years laughed before he could stop himself. Nobody else joined him. Professor Adler's pen kept moving.

"Would you submit it that way," she asked, "with the headline narrowed?"

"I'd submit the narrower claim with the reruns attached," June said. "I'd prefer not to workshop the distinction over pastries, but here we are."

Omar leaned forward a fraction. "I think that would be a stronger paper."

June nodded once without looking at him. "Thank you for the live developmental edit."

The policy professor said, "So the annotation scheme still holds, but the generalization claim has to be fenced."

"Yes," June said. Her voice had gone lower each time she'd answered, until what remained was a service-worker briskness so stripped down it sounded almost polite. "The label distribution doesn't collapse. The benchmark doesn't collapse. The bragging rights collapse."

That got a quick, guilty sound from the back that might have been a laugh and might have been somebody choking on coffee. Leela wanted to stand up and throw something at all of them. They were grateful to June for making the violence manageable. A funny victim reduced paperwork.

Mohit looked at the clock and said, "Why don't we take ninety seconds while June pulls up the appendix with the split notes. Then she can finish the architecture section and we can return at the end if needed."

It sounded gracious. It also sounded like a man granting a bathroom break.

June set the clicker down and unplugged her laptop with movements so precise they had gone past calm into something else. "Sure," she said. "Ninety whole seconds. Luxury."

She stepped out into the hallway carrying the laptop against her chest. The hall outside the seminar room was colder and smelled of floor cleaner and old radiator metal. Somebody had left a rolling cart of recycling bins near the vending machine. One fluorescent tube above the copy room door buzzed louder than the others, then dimmed, then came back.

Leela followed before she'd decided what she meant to say.

June had already put the laptop on the wide windowsill beside the stairwell door, though the window only faced another interior hall. She was tapping through folders too hard, missing once, swearing under her breath.

"June," Leela said.

"Don't."

Her voice was quiet now. That was worse than if she'd shouted. June only got this quiet when the joke budget was gone.

Leela stopped two feet away. "He's making it bigger than it is."

June laughed once, a sound with no amusement in it at all. "You helped."

"I wasn't trying to."

"I know. That's the amazing part."

From the room behind them came the soft surge of people standing, the paper-plate noise of second cookies being taken under cover of discussion. Mohit's voice floated through the door, genial, already reframing. Wonderful energy in the room. Exactly the kind of honest exchange we want.

Leela said, "The issue is real. I couldn't pretend it wasn't."

June turned then. Up close, she looked less angry than Leela had expected and more injured, which was harder to meet. There was a faint wet mark on the front of her overshirt where the paper cup had touched it. Her left hand still held the wad of napkin with the dead piece of gum inside.

"You had one sentence," June said. "Not a speech. One sentence. 'She's already rerunning the clean split.' That's all. Then it's a seminar and not an execution."

Leela opened her mouth and found nothing in it she could stand behind.

June shook her head, not dramatically, almost tired. "I know what the flaw is. I know Omar's not making it up. I know the slide should've been cleaner. What I didn't know, till just now, was that you'd sit there and help him make me look dishonest because that version was safer for you."

"That's not fair."

June's eyes went wide for the first time. Not in outrage. In disbelief. "No? What's the fair version, Leela? That you froze in exactly the direction that protects you most? That's not unfair. That's basically a campus tradition."

The sentence should have hit like sarcasm. It landed like a report.

Leela could feel the old impulse rising, the one that always made her start adding clauses until maybe one of them contained a shelter. "I was trying to say the benchmark still holds, just more narrowly. I thought if I overstated the defense he'd push harder, and then it would get worse, and I didn't know if the room would believe me if I said the reruns were underway because-"

"Because you had the machines."

That one she did not deny.

June looked down at the laptop, clicked into the appendix folder, found the file she wanted. When she spoke again her voice had flattened almost completely.

"I asked for two nights on Hades," she said. "Not your kidney. Two nights. You left me on read and then you told the room the flaw wasn't a footnote."

The vending machine compressor kicked on beside them with a shudder. Somebody laughed inside the seminar room, too loud, the laugh of a person performing that the event remained normal.

Leela said, "I'm sorry."

June tucked the napkin into her pocket. "That's nice. It would have been nicer two minutes ago."

Mohit opened the seminar-room door halfway and leaned out with his mug still in hand, wearing the mild face of a man checking on late catering.

"You good?" he asked June. "No rush, but let's land the rest while we have the faculty."

June looked at him long enough that Leela thought, absurdly, maybe she would throw the laptop at his face. Instead she smiled with every part of her mouth that wasn't feeling.

"Absolutely," she said. "Wouldn't want to waste the faculty."

Mohit nodded once, satisfied by the word and not the tone, and disappeared back into the room.

June picked up the laptop. For a second she held it low against her stomach the way somebody might hold an ice pack to a bruise they didn't want to show. Then she straightened.

Leela said her name again, softly this time, because she had not earned anything louder.

June did not look at her. "Watch and learn," she said.

Then she went back in.

The room had resettled itself while she was out. People were back in their chairs with the obscene efficiency of academics who believed sitting down again restored innocence. Someone had opened the bakery box all the way. Half the cardamom knots were gone. Professor Adler had her pen ready. Ben had angled his laptop farther from the aisle.

June plugged in, pulled up the appendix, and stood at the podium under the fluorescent panels that flattened everyone's skin to the color of old paper. Leela stayed by the door for a beat before forcing herself back to her seat. The chair scraped louder than it should have. Nobody turned.

June put both hands on either side of the podium, not gripping, just placing them there like measurements.

"Okay," she said.

The word came out lower than before, stripped clean.

"Appendix first, since we're apparently doing surgery before triage. These are the thread-grouping notes from August. This is the author-linking mess. This is where the cleaner split changes the score, and this is where it doesn't. After that I'll finish the architecture and the deployment cases, because those still exist."

She clicked to the appendix and brought up the dated notes, the hashed user clusters, the ugly yellow cells where the held-out numbers sank. Nobody interrupted now. They watched her narrow her own claim in public. Professor Adler wrote without looking up. The policy professor had turned his plate upside down on his folder so the grease wouldn't get on the paper. Omar sat bent over his printout, staring at it hard enough to suggest the sheet itself might clear him.

"When the split is clean," June said, touching the laser dot to the table, "the improvement is modest and local. Fine. Then the honest claim is local. The benchmark still tells you something useful. It just doesn't get to swagger."

A chair leg screeched at the back. One of the first-years slipped out with half a cardamom knot wrapped in a napkin, trying for stealth and failing. Ben had started typing again, no longer pretending not to.

June clicked back to the architecture slide. "Model stack is the same. Thread context. Author-link heuristics. Error slices by euphemism family. None of that vanishes because the cleaner split takes a bat to the pretty number."

Halfway through the next sentence her voice caught. She cleared it and kept going. On the second catch, the postdoc with the baby-sticker bottle stood, crossed the aisle, and set the bottle beside June's paper cup without a word.

The whole room saw it.

June stopped for exactly one beat, nodded once without looking up, unscrewed the stranger's bottle with her free hand, and drank. Then she braced her left hand on the lectern and kept explaining the architecture while Professor Adler wrote down the smaller claim as June's headline all along.


# Chapter 6

By the time Leela left Keating, the seminar had been over for nearly two hours, but the room still seemed to exist under her skin. She could still see June at the screen with one hand braced on the lectern, doing that impossible thing of continuing to speak while her authority leaked out into the fluorescent air. Leela had tried texting once from the sixth-floor bathroom, something cowardly and small, `You home?`, and June had not answered. She did not blame her. She kept seeing the point where Mohit turned in his chair and said, almost pleasantly, Leela, do you want to weigh in here, as if he were offering her the floor instead of asking her to finish the job.

The engineering building was on night mode now, lobby lights reduced, glass doors black with reflected hallways. Outside, the cold had sharpened since late afternoon. Not winter yet, not the knife of it, but the first weather that made your teeth feel overexposed. Omar was standing beside the loading-dock ramp with his backpack on one shoulder and a flattened paper coffee cup in his hand. He looked as neat as he had looked at noon, button-down still tucked, beard line still exact, which only made the day feel more deranged. Leela thought briefly, stupidly, about turning back into the building and taking the long way out through the library.

Instead he said, "Have you eaten?"

She laughed once through her nose. "Is that a trick question?"

"I don't know. You looked like you were going to walk home and have coffee for dinner out of spite."

It was annoying that he was right. She had seven dollars in her checking account until Friday, half a sleeve of saltines in her studio, and enough anger to mistake that for a plan. He crushed the paper cup smaller, then dropped it into the trash beside the loading dock.

"The buffet under the tax office stays open till eleven," he said. "If we get there now, they won't hate us until we're already sitting down."

She should have said no. She should have gone home and sat with what she had done to June, or failed to do, which was the same thing from the only vantage point that mattered. Instead she said, "Fine," because she was hungry enough that the word came out before principle could organize itself.

They walked downhill through the strip between campus and Alder Street, past the beauty supply place with mannequins fading in the window, past the halal butcher hosing pink water toward the curb, past a laundromat that always looked overlit enough to expose sin. The town below Haviland had its own hours, its own smells, its own patience with graduate students in bad shoes. Keating, with its sealed corridors and server heat, always made the rest of Lakebridge feel invented until Leela came back into it and heard men stacking fruit crates in Spanish behind the grocery or saw a delivery driver smoking beneath a neon OPEN sign that had lost the O. Omar kept his hands in his coat pockets and walked quickly, not talking. Once he reached up and touched the side of his jaw, checking, maybe, whether his face was still under control.

The buffet was still open in the technical sense. Half the overhead bulbs had been turned off, which made the steam trays glow harder. At the register a woman in a gray fleece vest was painting her left thumbnail dark green while a dubbed soap opera ran above her head on mute. Without looking up she said, "Nine-fifty-five. Kitchen closes at ten. You can stay after, but no refills after ten-fifteen."

"We're scholars, not animals," Omar said.

The woman finally glanced at him, took in the campus badges clipped to both their bags, and snorted. "That's not what I said."

Leela paid cash because the handwritten card minimum sign made the decision for her. The bills left her hand with the humiliating lightness of money that already had another use. Then they were inside the smell of fryer oil and cardamom and bleach, carrying plates still warm from the stack.

The food was bad in several distinct ways and exactly what she wanted. Paneer gone rubbery at the edges. Green beans cooked past apology. Tandoori chicken lacquered red enough to stain a napkin. A tray of lo mein in the middle of the Indian food for reasons nobody here respected enough to explain. She took too much on the first pass because appetite had arrived late and angry. When she sat down, Omar was arranging his plate with the unconscious fussiness of someone who hated having sauce touch anything before he had chosen it. He had somehow built a first round that looked sensible, lentils, rice, cucumber salad, one piece of fish, as if he were auditioning for the role of man who had not just detonated a colleague in public. Then, as soon as he saw Leela looking, he said, "This is the respectable plate. The second plate is where the moral collapse happens."

Against her will, she smiled.

"Good," he said. "I was worried I'd have to do all the work."

It was the first thing all day that had felt remotely like relief, not because it was funny enough to matter, but because it let her chew for a minute without also having to perform righteous clarity. Oil and salt hit her bloodstream so fast she could have cried from it. Her body had apparently made its own administrative decision and classified the seminar as something to survive after calories.

For a while they only ate. Spoons touched china. The soda fountain hissed and reset itself with nobody using it. Somewhere near the kitchen a child was whining in Spanish while an adult voice kept answering with the flat, invulnerable patience of someone who had done fourteen hours on their feet. Leela kept seeing June's face anyway, and what stayed with her was the recovery: June forcing her voice lower and flatter and talking through the bruise of it, which in June always meant the jokes were over and the damage would be itemized later, in private.

Leela put down her fork. "Did he tell you to do that?"

Omar did not pretend not to understand. "Yes."

She had wanted him to lie first. A lie she could kick open. This made her angrier.

"That's it?"

"No," he said. "That's just the shortest truthful answer."

She waited.

He folded his paper napkin once, then again, into a thinner rectangle. "He told me there was an internal robustness review attached to one of the benchmark papers. He said the paper would be anonymized to keep it clean. He said whoever it was had agreed to the process in advance, because otherwise it would become personal and useless."

"And you believed that?"

"Enough to proceed."

"That's not the same sentence."

"No." He took a drink of water. "It's still the one I had."

Leela leaned back against the booth. The vinyl gave under her shoulders with a sticky sigh. "Anonymized. Jesus Christ."

"I know how it sounds now."

"No, I don't think you do."

That landed. He let it land. Outside the plate-glass window, a bus shuddered past and splashed old rainwater against the curb. Omar looked at the table, not at her.

"He said the point was to simulate conference conditions," he said. "He used the phrase 'adversarial pressure test' at least three times, so I assume he believed repetition would turn it into ethics."

"He made you say it in seminar?"

"He said it needed to happen in the room, yes."

"Why?"

Omar gave a short, humorless laugh. "Because in private June could argue back and in public she had to keep being June."

The sentence was ugly enough that Leela stared at him. Its precision made it worse; falseness would have been easier to swat away. Omar saw that and rubbed the bridge of his nose with thumb and forefinger, as if he could erase the phrasing after it was already in the air.

"I'm not trying to sound insightful," he said. "He all but said it. He said she needed to learn how the field would treat that claim if she took it outside. He said it'd be kinder for the correction to come from us."

"Us."

"Yes. That was the word."

She thought of Mohit's face in meetings when he said our students, our work, our standards, each pronoun padded and soft and impossible to prove harmful. She stabbed a piece of chicken and found she had no interest in swallowing it now.

"When did you know it was her?" she asked.

He was quiet long enough that the answer arrived before he gave it.

"The night before," he said. "He sent the slide deck after midnight. He said not to circulate it and not to overprepare because spontaneity mattered."

"So you knew?"

"Yes."

"And you still did it?"

"Yes."

She wanted the pleasure of calling him a coward and could not quite have it, because the word would have slid too easily over the place where her own silence sat. Instead she said, "Why?"

Omar pushed rice into a neat mound with the side of his fork, then stopped when he saw himself doing it. "Because by that point refusing wouldn't have made me innocent. It would've made me difficult. There's a difference in this department."

"That's not an answer either."

"It's closer." He looked up. "He's been telling me for weeks that I need to be more visible, less careful, that the theory work alone is not enough if I want anyone to think I can lead a room. He said there might be a workshop paper out of the robustness audits. He said he could maybe put my name forward for the winter visit at Meridian if I showed I was useful beyond my narrow piece. He said many things. None of them were promises in the legal sense. All of them were promises in the only sense that matters when someone controls your funding."

Meridian. Leela knew the name. Fancy private lab, absurd salaries, the kind of visit that got repeated in bios for years after. She had not realized Mohit was dangling that kind of bait already, though of course he was. He had learned early that his best manipulations were the ones ambitious people could help narrate for him.

"So you sold her out for a maybe?"

"Yes," Omar said, and for the first time there was the faintest edge in his voice, not anger exactly, something more exhausted and therefore less manageable. "And I'd appreciate it if you wouldn't talk as if I haven't been doing the arithmetic myself."

That shut her up for a second. He ate two bites in quick succession, less from hunger than from the need to have his mouth occupied.

When he spoke again, he was quieter. "Also, if I'm confessing properly, I was angry with her."

Leela blinked. "With June?"

"Not in some grand tragic way. In the ordinary petty way people in labs hate each other for three days at a time and then call it weather. She's got a habit of talking about theory as if it's where results go to die. She doesn't mean it as cruelly as it sounds, but she says it anyway. He knew that. He used it."

That, too, felt true enough to offend. June did talk that way when she was tired, with the brisk contempt of someone who had spent a week fixing infrastructure and had no patience left for elegance. Omar had the kind of dignity that made mockery slide off until, eventually, it didn't.

"So there was that," he said. "It doesn't justify anything; it just made the lie easier to step into."

Leela looked down at her plate. She had made a small wreck of it without noticing, sauces dragged together, one torn naan corner hardened where it had cooled. This was the part nobody ever wrote into faculty statements about collegiality, the fact that exploitation recruited through whatever was already bruised. Nobody needed to invent a wound if a lesser one was available.

"He asked me to weigh in," she said finally. "Today. In the room. Like I was some neutral fucking authority."

Omar's expression changed, not softer, but less defended. "I know."

"I could've said it needed to wait. I could've said the benchmark issue wasn't settled. I could've said almost anything except what I said."

"Which was almost nothing."

"Yes."

"That's not the same as nothing."

"Don't do that."

"Do what?"

"Make me feel better because you're embarrassed."

He held up one hand, surrender or correction. "Fair."

The woman at the register called that the kitchen was closing. Omar rose for his second plate with a grimness that made the whole act funny again, and when he came back he was carrying exactly what he had promised: the shameful buffet truth. Two pakoras gone soft, lo mein, an alarming square of pizza from the children's tray, and a scoop of gulab jamun shining in syrup beside everything else like a bad decision he'd decided to commit to fully.

"That can't all be on the same plate," Leela said.

"I think you'll find that it can."

"This is why empires collapse."

"Empires collapse for many reasons," he said. "Tonight one of them is sugar syrup on institutional pizza."

She laughed again, actually this time, and hated him a little for making it possible.

But the relief only opened enough space for June to come back sharper. Leela imagined June in her apartment, shoes kicked under the radiator, nicotine gum chewed flat to bitterness, replaying the room with the precision she usually reserved for code regression. She had wanted Leela to do one small, visible thing in public. Leela had not done it. Sitting here with Omar, building an account of how Mohit had engineered the blow, did not alter the fact that June had paid the cost alone in the room. On impulse Leela pulled out her phone and typed, `I'm with Omar. Can I come by later?` She hated the sentence for how quickly it tried to sound useful. She sent it anyway. Nothing came back.

"Do you think he planned all of it," she asked, setting the phone facedown, "or is this just the ordinary version? Regular academic bad faith. The kind everybody explains away because tenure exists and feelings don't."

Omar chewed, swallowed. "I think 'planned' gives him too much theater. He doesn't need a lair. He needs to know which person to corner in public and which one will call it rigor afterward."

"That still sounds like planning."

"Fine," he said. "Then yes. But it's the kind that gets rewarded."

That was the most useful thing anyone had said to her since lunch. She felt it click into place with a depressing neatness. The point did not absolve Omar; it pinned his choice back inside a structure where choice still counted. Mohit had not hypnotized anyone. He had only made every refusal expensive and every compromise narratable.

They stayed until the register woman began stacking chairs onto the empty tables in the far section with blunt, warning noise. Leela was full in the graceless way buffet fullness always was, her stomach hot and overcommitted, her brain briefly slowed by grease. Outside, the cold hit the sweat at the back of her neck and turned it mean. The storefronts on the block had mostly gone dark. A tax-refund banner snapped against a chain-link fence. Somewhere farther down Alder, a couple were fighting in low furious voices while a car idled with music shaking faintly through its doors.

They started uphill toward campus. Without the table between them the conversation felt riskier, as if whatever either of them said next would have to survive in thinner air. Omar zipped his coat all the way up and then tugged the zipper tab twice, a little checking gesture Leela had seen him do before major presentations, as if fastening something could count as preparation.

"There's one other thing," she said.

He looked over, wary at once. "That's not an encouraging opening."

"No."

Her hands were cold enough that she tucked them under her arms. She had not planned to tell him anything tonight. She had planned, if she was honest, to let him carry the whole moral burden of the seminar for at least a week, maybe longer, because anger was simpler than inventory. But now he had given her something she could use, and the cheapness of taking it without returning anything showed too clearly.

"Earlier this semester," she said, "Mohit asked me to look into what he called a repo breach. He said somebody might have been copying work off the lab server."

Omar slowed. "And?"

"And it wasn't clean." She felt herself immediately start to sand the sentence down and hated that, too. "The logs showed mirrors being made. Whole directories. Not random pulls, not somebody poking around where they shouldn't. It looked like top-level access."
