He stopped walking. They were under the dead white spill of a parking-lot lamp outside the closed pharmacy, bright enough for her to see the hard line that formed along his mouth when fear showed up before the rest of his face had decided what to do with it.

"Top-level as in you?"

"Inherited admin. Still my credentials on paper. Yes."

"Top-level as in Mohit?"

She looked at the pavement. Rain had dried in dirty crescents around old leaves. "Somebody who did not need to steal a password," she said.

That was enough. It was also a lie by reduction. She did not say Ruderal. She did not say private folder. She did not say that she had seen Mohit's own account move student repositories into a place no student had been told existed, then gone home and decided not to become the kind of person who reported it. The name of the folder sat in her head with terrible clarity anyway, eight letters and a little underscore of corporate ambition. Saying only this much made her feel both clever and dirty.

Omar stared at her for a long second. The cold moved around them in clean knives.

"When," he said.

"September."

His laugh came out short and disbelieving. "September."

"Yes."

"And you're telling me now?"

"Yes."

"Why now?"

There it was. She could have said because tonight made the pattern undeniable, because his confession demanded one, because I need you useful. All of them were true. None of them were the whole thing.

"Because if he's moving work around at that level," she said, "then today wasn't just him being an asshole in seminar. It was operational. And because if I tell you everything the first time I say any of it, you'd be an idiot to trust me and I'd be an idiot to trust you."

He let that sit. His breath showed white between them.

"That's almost honest," he said.

"We're having an almost honest conversation."

"Apparently."

They resumed walking, slower now. The campus lights above the hill looked expensive and remote, a brochure version of knowledge waiting to resume in the morning. Omar shoved his hands deeper into his pockets.

"He told me something else," he said after a while. "About why the seminar had to happen that way."

Leela waited.

"He said if I kept acting grateful all the time, people would mistake that for weakness. Those weren't his exact words, but close enough. He said international students get trapped performing reliability. He said I needed to show that I could make a hard call in a room and survive being disliked for it. He made it sound almost protective."

Leela felt a pulse of disgust so specific it was nearly admiration. That was Mohit's favorite register, the one where exploitation arrived dressed as preparation for a cruel world that only he was honest enough to name.

"He says versions of that to all of us," she said. "He picks the sentence you've almost already told yourself."

Omar nodded once. "Yes."

They reached the footpath behind the library where the wind shifted and came cleaner off the lake. Fallen leaves had pasted themselves against the fence in dark wet fans. Leela's stomach was beginning to resent the buffet, but the solid weight of it still felt preferable to the airy sickness from earlier. Beside her Omar walked with his head bent slightly into the cold, shoulders tight, not elegant now, simply tired.

"So what are we saying," he asked. "That he's strategic or that he's merely like every advisor with enough money and not enough supervision?"

"Those aren't different categories."

"No," he said. "But one is something June can name and one is just weather."

"June doesn't need a vocabulary lesson. She needs to know what he said to you."

He turned to her. "Tonight?"

"Why not tonight?"

"Because I already stood up in front of her and did it once," he said. "Showing up at her door with an explanation of my own behavior isn't confession. It's vanity."

"It's not vanity if it's true."

"Are you about to tell her everything you know?"

The answer moved through her before she could dress it up. "No."

"Then don't ask me to arrive with my partial truth while you keep yours in your pocket."

That shut her up. He was right, but that was not even the part that stung. He had found the exact shape of the wrongness faster than she had.

Her phone vibrated in her coat. She pulled it out with fingers gone clumsy from cold.

June's reply sat on the screen in a single hard block: `Not with him. Don't call tonight.`

Omar watched her face and then looked away. "She answered?"

"Yes."

"And?"

"She doesn't want either of us near her."

He nodded once. "Fair."

They walked another few steps with the message still burning against her palm.

"Maybe the useful question isn't which one," she said. "Maybe it's whether he keeps each of us in a separate room long enough to make us accept the smaller version."

Omar looked at her. "Smaller version."

"The one where you're not being used, just corrected. The one where I'm not covering for him, just being practical. The one where June didn't get set up, she just had a rough seminar. The one where tonight's answer is that she's overreacting instead of that we earned this."

He was quiet for several steps. Then he said, "That does sound like him."

She almost said the folder name then. Almost. It sat at the back of her tongue like a pill she might still decide not to swallow. But if she gave him that, everything changed at once. He would want proof. He would want dates. He would want to know why she had hidden it. He would be right to want all of it. Tonight she did not have the stomach for a full moral accounting, not with grease cooling in her blood and June's refusal still warm on her screen and the week ahead not even pretending to be survivable.

So she said, "If he asks you to review my work again, you tell me first."

Omar answered immediately. "Yes."

"And if he asks me anything about your models, your benchmarks, your paper, I tell you."

"Even if it makes you look bad."

"Especially then."

He considered her in profile for a second, as if testing the sentence for hidden conditions. "All right," he said. "And if he asks about tonight."

"We got food because we were hungry."

"Which is true."

"Yes."

"And we complained about him like graduate students since the beginning of time."

"Also true."

He gave the faintest smile. "You see. We're finally building a rigorous method."

By the time they reached the edge of campus, Keating was visible again above the trees, all lit rectangles and sealed doors, the building that had taught them to call theft collaboration if the server cluster stayed on. June's text had gone dark with the rest of the screen, but Leela could still feel it there. Under it she could feel, almost physically, the two larger omissions still walking between them: the part of Omar's ambition he had not fully named, the part of her evidence she had still kept for herself. Neither omission cancelled the other. Neither balanced anything. But the conversation had produced a narrow strip of ground that had not existed at dinner, a place to stand without Mohit in the middle of it translating everyone's motives for them.

At the crosswalk Omar stopped and waited for the signal even though no cars were coming. He had the tired, overcorrect habit of someone who obeyed empty streets on principle. When the light changed, he said, "I'm not asking you to forgive me."

"Good."

"I'm asking you not to let him narrate me to you without my participation."

That was such an academic way to put a plea that she would have laughed if June's message had not still felt like a shut door in her hand.

"Same," she said.

He nodded. Then they crossed.

Whatever this was, June's answer had already stripped the word trust out of it. It was leaner and meaner, built out of hunger, shared usefulness, and two edited confessions offered in the cold. June had paid for the lesson in public and, when Leela finally reached toward her, refused to make herself available as the cleanup crew for it. Still, when Leela headed toward graduate housing and Omar kept on toward the international student apartments, they were no longer carrying only Mohit's version of the day. They had made a smaller truth between them, damaged and usable, and it was nowhere near enough. It did nothing to shrink the September logs still sitting behind everything else, admin-level and ugly and hers on paper. For the moment, it was all they had.


# Chapter 7

At her desk in Keating, Leela was trying to coax one dead benchmark run into becoming a paragraph she could still someday call a dissertation when the email from Research Integrity arrived. Outside the sixth-floor window, Lakebridge had gone the color of dishwater, the wet part of cold rather than snow, a steady damp that climbed into coat sleeves and stayed there. The message carried a cheerful blue flag beside it, as if someone had taken the time to mark it important by hand.

Subject: Required Attendance - Research Integrity Intake

Dear Ms. Krishnan,

Please report to Baines Hall, Room 414, at 2:30 p.m. today regarding a data-handling concern associated with privileged access credentials under your stewardship.

The message was signed by Mara Sloane, Associate Director, and copied an Information Security address that looked automated enough to absolve everybody in advance.

Leela read it twice, then a third time with her hand flat on the desk so the tendons in her wrist would stop twitching under the brace. The phrase privileged access credentials under your stewardship sat in her head like a stain. Nobody in Keating said things like stewardship unless they were already arranging blame into folders.

Across the cubicle wall Omar said, "You made the face."

He had a pharmacy bag open on his desk and was eating pistachios one by one, setting the empty shells in a neat line along the edge of a conference flyer. Even hungry, even frightened, he organized his mess before it existed. He leaned back enough to see her over the partition, his beard line still too careful for a man who had slept in the department three nights the week before.

"What face?"

"The one you make when the machine has decided it knows more than you do."

She turned her laptop so he could read the email. He was silent longer than she liked. Outside, somebody in the hallway laughed too loudly at something faculty-adjacent, the sound carrying the false confidence that came from not being the one in trouble.

"This isn't routine," Omar said.

"Everything here calls itself routine until somebody loses a year."

He looked at the subject line again. "Do you know what it is?"

She did. The words themselves were vague, but her body had already sorted them into the correct directory: root access, audit trail, the cloned repositories from September, the ones she had found under the private namespace and then hidden from everybody because the clean version of that discovery had lasted maybe twelve seconds before it turned into the dirty version, the one where she still needed Mohit for money, timing, recommendations, permission to exist in the building as something other than an error.

"Probably the cluster," she said.

"Probably," Omar repeated, in the tone of a man laying a cracked dish gently back on the shelf.

Her phone buzzed before either of them spoke again.

Mohit: Heard RI may reach out today about some inherited access-governance issues. Annoying but manageable. Keep your answers tightly factual and don't speculate beyond what you can document.

He sent it like weather. He sent it three minutes after the summons as if he had merely sensed atmospheric pressure changing over Baines Hall. Leela stared at the screen until it dimmed. Omar did not ask to see the message. He only watched her face become something he could already interpret.

"He knows," he said.

"Apparently."

"Did he say why?"

"No. He said not to speculate." She gave a small laugh that had nothing in it. "Which is generous, because speculation is currently all I own."

Omar pushed the pistachio bag toward her. She took one and hated him a little for the kindness of it.

"Will you tell them he had access?" he asked.

The question was clean enough that she could not answer it cleanly. She had configured Mohit's laptop herself in August, kneeling on the thin rug in his office while he stood over her with one shoe off because he'd stepped in something outside and didn't want it on the carpet. He had talked the whole time about maturing the lab's infrastructure, about not being bottlenecked by central IT, about how much he appreciated that she understood urgency at a systems level. She had pasted his public key where he wanted it because he had said appreciated in the tone that really meant remember who decides your timeline.

"I don't know what I'll tell them yet."

Omar cracked another shell with his thumb. "That usually means you know exactly what the choices are and hate all of them."

"Useful."

"I can walk over with you."

She looked at him. He kept his expression neutral, but his left hand had started flattening and refolding the corner of the conference flyer until the paper went soft. There was always some tiny object taking the damage on his behalf.

"You have class at three."

"I can be late."

"And then if they ask why you came, what do I say? That the lab brought a witness because we run our misconduct reviews communally?"

"That's not what I meant."

"I know."

He let that sit. He was one of the few people in the department who could let silence stand without rushing in to explain himself. Under pressure it read less like calm than like expensive discipline, something he had built because being visibly scrambled in institutional rooms cost him more than it cost everybody else.

"Text me when you come out," he said at last.

"I will."

"And Leela."

"What."

"If they're asking yes-or-no questions about how the lab actually works, don't let them make you sound stupid just because the truth sounds embarrassing."

She almost said that's not the embarrassing part. Instead she nodded, and Omar looked away first, giving her back the privacy of her panic.

She did not work between eleven and two-thirty. She opened the policy page on privileged systems access and closed it. She opened the log screenshots she'd hidden in a folder called tax_receipts and closed those too. She drafted three versions of an explanation in her head, each one stable until it reached Mohit. Past him, language turned to paste: advisor, supervisor, PI, mentor. None of the nouns held the right ratio of dependence to fear. None of them explained why she had known enough to be culpable and not enough to be safe.

At two-ten she left Keating. The air across the quad smelled of wet wool and leaf rot and the campus coffee carts grinding through beans they charged too much for. Baines Hall sat ahead of her in its black-glass self-importance, near enough to the engineering complex that a summons felt immediate, far enough that the walk still gave her time to imagine running. She did not run. She kept going because most academic disasters were built exactly this way, from people continuing to walk toward rooms they should have set on fire.

Omar matched her at the curb without comment. He had put on the charcoal coat he wore to anything that smelled faintly official, and the neatness of him made her feel both steadier and more doomed.

"You didn't have to," she said.

"No," he said. "But I wanted the exercise of crossing campus with an indictment in the weather."

"It's not an indictment."

"I know. It's an intake. Much more relaxing."

The automatic doors opened before they touched them. The lobby smelled unmistakably of artificial citrus, the kind that never occurred near actual fruit. Somebody had polished the stone floor recently enough that the overhead lights lay across it in obedient rectangles. A student worker at the front desk wore a pumpkin-patterned cardigan and looked seventeen and terminally bored.

"Fourth floor," she said without looking up. "Sign in on the tablet."

Leela typed her name with the plastic stylus chained to the stand. Visitor reason flashed in a drop-down menu. Procurement. Sponsored Programs. Appeals. Research Integrity Intake. She chose it and watched the words settle under her own name as if they had always belonged there.

The elevator was lined in brushed metal that gave everyone the complexion of bad fish. Omar kept his eyes on the floor numbers.

"If they suspend your access," he said, "that's not neutral."

"You have a gift for comfort."

"I'm serious."

"So am I."

The doors opened on four. The carpet began immediately, a sound-eating neutral gray that made her wet shoes seem obscene. Room 414 was already open. Omar stopped outside it.

"I shouldn't be visible in there," he said. "Not for my sake. For yours."

That was true and ugly and not worth pretending otherwise. Anybody in Baines could convert a student's need for company into evidence of instability in two sentences flat.

"Text me," he said again.

"Go teach."

"I am not teaching. I'm pretending first-years can derive asymptotic notation before caffeine."

She glanced at him. The uncontracted form landed as deliberate precision, his way of holding himself together when the edge came close. Then he gave her a brief, crooked look, the private sardonic one he never used in seminar rooms.

"Fine," he said. "I'm going to stand in front of eighteen undergrads and lie about recurrence relations. Text me after."

Then he left, one hand already in his pocket feeling for the cheap click pen he always carried to class like a talisman.

The conference room had no windows, only a frosted glass panel beside the door and a long table too shiny for the light above it. A speakerphone sat in the middle with the mute button lit red, performatively inactive. Mara Sloane stood when Leela came in. She was maybe in her late forties, wearing a navy sweater set under a blazer and the expression of a woman who had once been pretty and still resented administrative lighting for taking liberties. A Sunday crossword half-finished in blue ink lay tucked under her yellow legal pad, nine-down abandoned at the edge. Next to her sat Brendan Yu from Information Security, damp at the cuffs from the weather and with a bicycle helmet clipped to his messenger bag like he had come straight from the real world and been trapped here by accident.

"Ms. Krishnan," Mara said. "Thank you for coming over on short notice."

"I got the sense it wasn't optional."

Mara gave the kind of polite almost-smile administrators used when they wanted credit for hearing tone without engaging content. "Please. Have a seat. This is Brendan Yu from Information Security. We're doing a preliminary intake regarding privileged systems usage on the Hades cluster and related repository replication events. This is not, at present, a formal misconduct hearing."

At present, related, hearing: each word arrived scrubbed and dry.

Leela sat. The chair cushion hissed under her with trapped air. Somebody had left the room too cold, or maybe buildings like Baines believed temperature itself could count as professionalism.

"Do I need a lawyer?" she asked.

Brendan blinked. Mara folded her hands.

"You're welcome to seek advice at any point," Mara said. "For today's purposes we're collecting factual information about account stewardship and access control. If that changes, you would be notified."

"Okay."

"Would you like water?"

"No."

"All right." Mara clicked her pen. "Can you confirm that from August 2024 through the present you have been the designated custodian of the `hades-admin` credential set for Civic Signals Lab infrastructure?"

There it was. Not her name, not exactly. Something older, shared, deniable in the way only longstanding disasters were deniable.

"Designated by who?" Leela asked.

"By the lab's internal access documentation and by Professor Saran's annual systems attestation."

The phrase made her picture Mohit signing some web form with his expensive glasses halfway down his nose, annoyed by the interruption, writing her into responsibility because she was the senior student and because she said yes too often in the years when yes still looked like progress.

"I had the credential set," she said. "Custodian makes it sound more orderly than it was."

Brendan opened a folder and slid three printed pages toward her. They were log excerpts, timestamped and annotated in yellow highlighter. She recognized the paths immediately. `/srv/projects/ruderal_sync/`. Mirror clones of June's benchmark repo, Omar's baseline code, her own work, all hauled sideways into a private directory tree under the authority of `hades-admin`.

The room seemed to contract around the yellow marks. She had seen these lines on a screen in September at two in the morning, with server fans screaming on the other side of the closet door and a vending machine burrito turning in her stomach. On paper they looked cleaner, almost reasonable. Paper removed the heat from a crime.

"These replication events occurred under that credential set," Brendan said. "Do you dispute that?"

"No. I dispute that that tells you who did it."

"We'll get there," Mara said. "First I need to establish baseline governance. Were these credentials shared with anyone else?"

Leela looked at the pages again, buying time. The truthful answer depended on which part of the system anyone wanted to claim was normal. There was official policy, under which the answer was supposed to be no. Then there was the lab, which had run for years on a more embarrassing answer: yes, but only when urgent, yes, but everybody knew, yes, but don't write it down, yes, but never in a way a university office could quote back to you later.

"Informally, yes," she said.

Brendan wrote something. Mara's face changed almost imperceptibly, not surprise exactly, more like the small settling motion of a shelf receiving the object it had expected.

"Shared with whom?"

"Professor Saran had access."

"Direct credential access?" Brendan asked.

"He had enough access to use the systems when he wanted something done quickly."

"I'm asking whether he knew the password, possessed the key material, or otherwise had the ability to authenticate as `hades-admin`."

"Yes."

That felt good for half a second. Then Mara asked, "Was that approved through central IT?"

Leela laughed before she could stop herself. Nobody else did.

"No," she said.

"Was it documented anywhere?"

"Only if you count the lab wiki and a lot of people pretending not to notice how the lab wiki worked."

"I don't think we can count that," Mara said.

"That's convenient."

Mara let the sentence pass as if she were being generous rather than tactical. "Can you describe how Professor Saran came to have access?"

Leela rubbed the edge of the first log page with her thumb until the paper began to fuzz. She could tell them the clean version. Professor Saran needed emergency weekend access during a server migration, and she provisioned it under time pressure. That version made her sound merely sloppy. The dirtier version was closer to true: Mohit had wanted direct access because waiting made him feel subordinate to infrastructure, and she had been too close to graduation, too dependent, too flattered by being treated as the grown-up in the room to tell him no.

"He asked me to set up access on his machine last summer," she said. "He said central IT was slow, and if we wanted the lab to function like a real research group we couldn't open tickets every time something broke."

"Did you raise concerns about policy compliance at that time?"

"To him?"

"To anyone."

"No."

"Why not?"

Because she was thirty and still living in graduate housing with cheap blinds and a credit card balance she fed minimum payments to like a sick stray. Because Mohit could delay her defense by three months without ever phrasing it as punishment. Because he could call her mature when he wanted labor and unstable when he wanted leverage. Because by the time you had words for coercion in academia you had usually already helped build the machine that used it.

"Because saying no to your advisor isn't the same as saying no to a colleague," she said. "It doesn't fit the formal vocabulary, but it's real."

Mara's expression softened in a trained way that made Leela want to flip the table. "I understand there may have been supervisory pressure."

"No," Leela said. "You translated it into something smaller."

"What are you saying?"

Leela heard the trap open under the question. If she said control, then Mara could recommend a different office, a different form, a different useless corridor. If she said fear, it would become affect. If she said he made it functionally impossible to refuse without threatening my funding and my degree timeline, she would sound like every graduate student cliche faculty rolled their eyes at in closed meetings.

"I'm saying the lab doesn't operate as a place where students freely decline requests from the PI," she said. "Not if the PI controls compute, authorship, letters, travel, defense timing. You can't separate system access from that."

Mara nodded once, already translating. "I can note that you experienced pressure within the supervisory environment."

The sentence was so thin Leela could see daylight through it.

"That vocabulary is too small for what I mean," she said.

"It may be the most precise language available to this office."

"Then your office is useless."

Brendan shifted in his chair, not offended, just wary of splash damage. Mara put her pen down very carefully on top of the crossword.

"Ms. Krishnan, I understand that you're frustrated."

"No, you don't."

The room went still. Even the HVAC seemed to pause to hear whether she would keep going. Her anger had arrived too early, before it could be made useful. She knew that even while it was in her mouth.

Mara's voice stayed level. "Would you like five minutes?"

"No. I'd like a vocabulary that doesn't turn my dissertation committee into an abstract weather pattern."

"I'm trying to determine who used privileged credentials to duplicate restricted repositories into an unsanctioned project namespace. If you want to make a broader complaint about your advisor, there are channels for that. They aren't the same channel."

There. The architecture of the whole place, laid out with the quiet pride of competent bureaucracy. If she wanted to describe the way Mohit occupied every future tense in her life, she needed one office. If she wanted to describe the way he had put a startup directory on university hardware and routed it through access she nominally controlled, she needed another. If she wanted any of those descriptions to matter, she needed documents she had not created when the truth was still only dangerous, not yet expensive.

"Fine," she said. "Then ask the narrow question."

Mara did.

"Did you personally initiate the repository replications shown in front of you?"

"No."

"Do you know who did?"

Leela looked at the highlighted path where the private directory name sat in plain text: `ruderal_sync`. The word itself was almost funny, startup-anxious and vaguely botanical, the sort of brand name a man like Mohit would think meant resilience because it sounded scrappy in a well-funded way. She had the screenshots from September. She had the memory of seeing his laptop connected. She had enough to start something and not enough to control where it would spread.

"I know who had motive and access," she said.

"That isn't what I asked."

"Then yes and no."

"Ms. Krishnan."

"I can't prove it in the format you want."

Brendan tapped the log pages. "Do you have documentary evidence that someone else authenticated with the credential set?"

"Not in a way that would make you happy."

"Do you have documentary evidence, full stop?"

This was the point. The room had been moving toward it since she sat down. She could put the screenshots on the table. She could say Mohit's name without softening it and watch the whole thing widen immediately, not into justice but into exposure. They would ask when she discovered the activity. They would ask why she didn't report it. They would ask why the same credential set remained active afterward, why she continued acting as senior student, why she had not preserved the chain of custody cleanly, why her own compromised paper had later used the same compute environment. She could feel those future questions lined up behind the current one like faculty outside a seminar door.

"No," she said.

The lie was technical, which made it academic enough to survive a minute in the room.

Brendan wrote again. Mara's tone stayed patient. "Were you aware, prior to today's meeting, that these replications had occurred?"

Leela looked at her.

Here it was again, the second trap under the first. The truthful answer would move time itself against her. If she said yes, she became the student who sat on a known access violation. If she said no, she became either incompetent or naive. Mohit had built the options so elegantly over months that even now, absent from the room, he was choosing the furniture.

"I was aware there had been unusual replication activity on the cluster," she said. "I did not understand the full purpose of it."

Mara took that in. "Did you report the unusual activity to anyone?"

"No."

"Why not?"

Because she had seen catastrophe and called it maybe, then called it later, then called it after the deadline, after the seminar, after she had some other piece that would keep the first piece from costing her everything. Because cowardice in graduate school rarely felt dramatic. It felt like scheduling. It felt like I need one more week.

"Because I thought I could resolve it internally," she said.

"How?"

"By understanding whether it was backup behavior, export behavior, or something else."

"Did you resolve it?"

"Obviously not."

Brendan cleared his throat. "Just to confirm: you allowed shared use of privileged credentials, you did not document that sharing through approved channels, you became aware of unusual replication activity, and you did not report it."

He read it back in a tone almost free of accusation, which made it land harder. It sounded like a case summary already. It sounded like the version of events a committee would believe because committees loved lists.

"You can also confirm," Leela said, "that my advisor had direct systems access because he wanted it, that the lab's governance was effectively whatever he said it was, and that students don't get to decline that without consequences you people will later call interpersonal."

"I can record your statement regarding lab culture and supervisory pressure," Mara said. "I can't independently verify those conditions from today's materials."

"Of course you can't."

"Can you provide any emails, messages, or written instructions documenting that Professor Saran asked for or used the `hades-admin` credentials?"

Leela thought of Mohit's text from three hours earlier. Keep your answers tightly factual. It was almost artful, how a warning could arrive dressed as advice. But the message did not admit what mattered. He would say access-governance issues because he knew there were access-governance issues. He would say don't speculate because mentors always told students not to spiral. He had spent years building sentences with emergency exits already installed.

"No," she said.

"All right." Mara folded the log pages back into the folder, the yellow highlights vanishing under her hand. "Based on what we have today, I'm not opening a formal misconduct action against you at this time."

Relief came so fast it embarrassed her. It lasted less than a breath.

"However," Mara continued, "Information Security will place an interim restriction on your privileged access to Hades while we review the broader access-governance failure. This means admin rights, shared-key authority, credential rotation authority, and direct repository replication permissions will be suspended effective today."

Leela stared at her. "Suspended for how long?"

"Pending review."

"Pending review isn't a timeline."

"No," Mara said. "It isn't."

Brendan slid a different paper toward her. It was one page, university letterhead, checkbox language. The notice called itself an interim access restriction, not disciplinary, not a finding, an administrative safeguard. There was a place for her signature acknowledging receipt. At the bottom, in smaller text, distribution: Research Integrity, Information Security, Department Chair, Faculty Supervisor, Graduate Programs.

"You're copying Mohit?" she asked.

"Professor Saran is the faculty supervisor for the affected infrastructure."

"He's the reason any of this happened."

"That's your position," Mara said. "It's not established by today's intake."

"You don't need it established to give him control back over my work."

"If you need research materials retrieved during the interim period, those requests can be coordinated through your PI or, if necessary, through your chair."

"My PI is the person I just told you I couldn't safely refuse."

"And I noted that."

"In what words."

Mara did not answer immediately. Then she turned the legal pad half an inch so Leela could see the line she had written in compact blue script: Student reports feeling pressured by supervisory environment; no documentary support provided at intake.

It was obscene in its tidiness.

"That's your life in there?" Leela asked.

Mara's expression changed at last, not to guilt, nothing so dramatic, but to the brief weariness of someone who had long ago decided not to feel individually responsible for structural cruelty. "It's what I can defend on the record."

Leela picked up the pen. The university used the cheap kind with a translucent barrel and a cap that never stayed on. Her wrist brace caught on the cuff of her sweater as she bent to sign. For a wild second she considered refusing. Then she imagined the record that would produce too, the note that she had declined acknowledgment, the tiny extra proof that she was difficult when handled administratively. She signed.

Brendan took the page back. "Your access change should propagate within the hour."

"Should."

"Usually faster."

"Great."

Mara gathered her folder. The crossword corner peeked out again, nine-down still unsolved, something four letters long. "You'll receive a summary email before end of day," she said. "If you later locate supporting documentation relevant to your concerns about Professor Saran's involvement, you may submit it as an addendum."

"And if I do, what happens?"

"We evaluate it."

"No," Leela said. "What happens to me."

For the first time all meeting Mara looked directly at her without administrative cushioning. "I can't answer that in advance."

"Because it depends who believes him."

"Because it depends on the evidence."

Which was another way of saying the same thing. Evidence was never just evidence here. It was timing, hierarchy, polish, who had an office, who had already been entered once into the system as a concern.

Mara stood. The meeting was over because the office had decided it was over.

In the hallway, Leela took out her phone before she had even cleared the door.

No formal case, but I lost admin. They copied the chair and Mohit.

She sent it to Omar and immediately hated the telegraphic ugliness of it. Her entire afternoon reduced to plus signs because complete sentences would have made it feel more real.

He replied while she waited for the elevator.

I'm sorry. That's a weapon with committee stationery.

She almost laughed. Then the elevator opened and she stepped into her own distorted reflection again.

Outside, the rain had thinned to mist, the kind that got into your hair without admitting it. She walked back toward Keating too fast, carrying the folder they had let her keep. The paper had already gone soft at one corner from her grip. Every time she loosened her hand, the distribution line flashed back at her: Department Chair, Faculty Supervisor, Graduate Programs. By the time she crossed the quad, her access problem had become everybody else's careful copy.

When she pushed into the basement lab the familiar air hit her all at once: burnt coffee, hot dust, old couch fabric, server heat. The fluorescent hum felt almost intimate after Baines. June was at the long table near the whiteboard, in a washed-out green work jacket with nicotine gum wrappers tucked under her keyboard because she never seemed to find a trash can at the exact moment trash existed. She looked up, took one read of Leela's face, and pulled a fresh piece of gum from the packet in her coat pocket.

"Well?" she said.

Leela set the folder down. "No formal finding."

"Which means there was definitely a punishment."

"They suspended admin on Hades."

June stopped with the gum halfway to her mouth. "Jesus."

"Yeah."

"For how long?"

"Pending review."

June put the gum in, chewed once, hard. "Pending review means whenever they feel like it. That's a hostage note."

Leela sat at her machine and woke the monitor. Her inbox was already filling with perfectly normal department garbage, colloquium reminders, an undergrad asking for extension mercy, a mailing-list message about pumpkin-carving in student affairs. The ordinary world had continued with such commitment it bordered on insult.

"Did you tell them it was him?" June asked.

Leela did not answer quickly enough.

June's chewing slowed. "You didn't."

"I told them he had access."

"That's you ducking his name."

"I know."

June looked back at her screen, not out of delicacy, more because contempt was easier to manage at a forty-five-degree angle. "They make you sign something?"

"Acknowledgment."

"Which they absolutely won't treat like guilt later."

Leela let out a short breath. "Can you not do that right now."

June was quiet. When she spoke again the meanness had gone flatter, more useful. "What do you need off Hades before the lock hits?"

The question landed deeper than comfort would have. Leela typed `ssh hades.cs.haviland.edu` with hands that felt suddenly too large for the keys. Passwordless auth attempted. The terminal thought for a second, as if politeness still applied.

Permission denied (publickey).

She stared at the line. It was not dramatic. No alarm sounded. The server closet kept humming ten feet away, heat breathing through the slats. Somewhere a long-running experiment of hers was probably still alive on a GPU node, counting through the afternoon without her, no longer hers in any way that mattered.

"Already propagated," she said.

June muttered, "Motherfuckers," so quietly it almost counted as prayer.

"I can maybe grab some of your stuff from my side," she said after a moment. "User-level, not admin. Assuming they haven't decided we're all diseased."

"Thanks."

"Don't thank me. Just make a list before they get clever."

Leela opened a blank text file and found she couldn't immediately remember the names of the directories that mattered most. Not because she had forgotten them, exactly. Because every project she had touched in the lab was braided through with somebody else's timelines, somebody else's permissions, somebody else's informal promise that it would all sort itself out once the paper was in, once the review came back, once the defense got scheduled. She was not losing a password. She was losing the fiction that competence had ever translated into ownership.

June stood. "I'm getting coffee before I commit a misdemeanor. Want one?"

"No."

"Suit yourself."

At the door she paused without turning around. "For what it's worth," she said, "that's not a small thing."

Then she left, jacket rubbing the frame, leaving behind the faint medicinal mint smell of her gum.

Leela remained at the desk with the dead terminal window. The lab sounded exactly as it had that morning, fans and fluorescent buzz and an undergrad somewhere down the hall laughing too hard. She opened the interim notice again. Her signature sat at the bottom like a confession written by someone else. Three lines of retrieval instructions told her what the dead terminal already had: every figure, checkpoint, and half-trained model she needed now had to come back through Mohit first.

Her phone buzzed with the summary email from Research Integrity.

Dear Ms. Krishnan,

Thank you for meeting with us this afternoon. This message confirms that no formal misconduct proceeding is being initiated at this time. As discussed, privileged access associated with the `hades-admin` credential set has been temporarily suspended pending review of lab-wide access governance practices. This interim measure is administrative in nature and should not be construed as a finding regarding intent.

Relevant parties have been notified to ensure continuity of research operations.

Relevant parties. She did not need to scroll to know the cc line would include Gloria Fisk and Mohit Saran. She scrolled anyway.

It did.

Thirty seconds later another email appeared in the thread.

From: Mohit Saran

Thanks, Mara. I appreciate the quick clarification.

Leela, let's use this as a chance to simplify process and protect your momentum. For any materials you need during the review window, send requests through me and I'll help coordinate with the team. We should be able to keep dissertation work on track if we're disciplined about scope.

Best,
Mohit

She read it twice. Protect your momentum. On track. Disciplined about scope. He had already converted the afternoon into mentorship.

The server closet kept humming behind its locked metal door, the same pitch it always held when the GPUs were hot. For years that sound had meant she was still central, still useful, still the one who knew where things lived and how to restart them when they failed. Now it was just a machine continuing without witness. On paper she had lost nothing worse than a credential set. In practice Mohit had just been copied onto the terms of her dependency.


# Chapter 8

The front step at Edison was already half claimed by rangoli, the outline feathered at one corner where somebody had opened the storm door too hard. The chalk loops were Meera's, neat enough to look premeditated and a little too symmetrical for holiness. A string of orange lights had been tested and abandoned in a coil by the planter. Through the glass Leela could hear the pressure cooker and the television from next door and her mother's voice talking to herself in the kitchen in the firm, efficient tone she used for recipes and incompetent doctors.

She set her duffel down on the crowded shoe rack and stood there for a second in the familiar crush of the entryway, coat half unzipped, train stink still in her hair. The townhouse smelled like hot oil, toasted cashews, sandalwood, and the damp wool of too many winter things drying in one place. Framed school photos climbed the wall by the stairs in a chronology that had once felt flattering and now mostly felt like evidence. One showed Meera in her white coat. Another caught Leela at seventeen with an overachieving smile she wanted to slap off her own face.

From the kitchen Anita called, "Shoes off. Wash your hands. Don't bring Penn Station onto my clean floor."

Leela laughed before she meant to. "Hi to you too, Amma."

Anita turned just long enough to look at her over reading glasses that had slid halfway down her nose. She was frying ribbon pakoda in a dented kadai and keeping the good roasted cashews in an old Danish butter-cookie tin because every storage container in the house had lived at least two lives already. "If I say hi first, you think the rules have changed. They haven't. Go."

It was a pleasure, instantly and embarrassingly deep, to be told what to do in language that had nothing to do with deliverables or remediation or whether a committee might someday decide that her future qualified as real. She took off her shoes, washed the train grime off her hands, and came back to the counter where Anita had already poured tea into a steel tumbler and slid it to the one open patch of space between the curry leaves and the box of matches.

Anita looked her over with quick billing-clerk accuracy, the same glance that could find a missing ICD code in a doctor's dictation and now found the wrist brace, the under-eye shadows, the sweater that had once been black. "You've lost weight."

"I've been eating."

"I didn't ask that question."

The tea was too hot. Leela burned her tongue and felt absurdly grateful for it. She stood at the counter drinking while Anita turned strips of dough in the oil with a slotted spoon, shook water off more curry leaves into the sink, and kept up a running commentary on the cardiology practice's latest software disaster.

"They changed the patient portal again," Anita said. "Now nobody can find where to pay and they all call us as if I designed the website myself. One man this morning told me his blood pressure went up because of the login screen. I said, good, then at least you know why you're coming to cardiology."

"You said that?"

"I thought it. Same thing."

Leela smiled into the rim of the tumbler. The house was full of small competent noises: oil ticking in the pan, the cabinet hinge that always clicked before shutting, the hum of the old refrigerator, the faint pop-pop-pop of neighborhood firecrackers starting too early. At Haviland every sound lately had seemed to carry obligation. Here even the pressure felt older and more decent. It asked only that she bring the steel plate from the second shelf, not that she defend her adulthood in the language of institutional process.

Her phone buzzed in her coat pocket on the chair.

She ignored it.

Anita did not. "Take it out, at least. If it keeps vibrating on the wood, I can hear it."

Leela pulled the phone free and saw Mohit's name before she could stop herself.

Hope you made it home safely. When you have a quiet ten minutes tonight, send me a short chronology of how the legacy root access passed to you. The office will ask again Monday, and it's better if we're clean and consistent.

For a second the black glass administrative conference room in Baines Hall rose inside her with such clarity that she could smell the fake citrus cleaner again. She turned the phone facedown on the counter hard enough that Anita looked up.

"Work?" Anita asked.

"Lab stuff."

Anita made a small dismissive sound. "Today of all days."

That was all. Anita gave no speech about balance, asked no questions about whether professors had the right to text on Diwali, and offered no helpful escalation. The dismissal itself was a form of protection. In this kitchen Mohit was merely rude, which was not the same as powerless but came close enough to be briefly soothing.

Anita lifted a piece of fresh pakoda onto a paper towel and then, because she had never believed in serving food at a safe temperature, tore one piece in half and put it straight into Leela's hand. Oil hit her skin, and chili, ajwain, and salt bloomed across her tongue. The burn on her fingertips matched the one on her tongue. It was perfect.

"Too much spice?" Anita asked.

"No."

"Good. Your sister said I made it old-people mild last year."

"I said it was edible by cardiologists," Meera called from the doorway, arriving with the exact timing of someone who had always expected to be quoted in absentia.

She came in carrying a bakery box against one hip and a reusable grocery bag full of tea lights and spare batteries, still wearing charcoal slacks under a navy scrub top with the elastic marks of an N95 stamped faintly into her cheeks. When she shrugged out of her coat a handful of individually wrapped floss picks spilled from the pocket and skittered across the floor. She looked down at them with ordinary annoyance, as if her job was physically incapable of ending at the office.

"Those are clean," she said. "Don't make that face."

"I wasn't making a face," Leela said.

"You were. Move, let me put this down. Amma, Mrs. Bhatia sent another sweets box to the practice. If we eat all this, every one of us deserves our A1C."

Anita took the bakery box without gratitude. "People bring sweets to a dentist like they have never once thought a thought through to the end."

"Exactly. Also, one patient bit through his temporary crown because apparently ice is a civil right." Meera bent to gather the floss picks, stuck them back in her pocket, and nudged the bag of tea lights toward Leela with her foot. "Can you check which of the diyas are cracked? Last year half of them leaked oil all over the tray."

Again she felt that bodily drop of relief, the sweetness of being conscripted into something finite. Leela took the bag, sat at the table, and sorted clay lamps onto newspaper while Anita and Meera moved around her in the kitchen's narrow practiced choreography, never colliding because they had been not colliding in this room for decades. Meera washed grapes and talked about a hygienist who wanted Thursdays off now that the middle-school pickup time had changed. Anita complained about the HOA newsletter's latest war on front-yard clutter. They spent a full minute on whether the cardiology office holiday potluck could survive another quinoa salad. Every complaint came with a fix already attached.

Leela checked each diya for hairline cracks and lined the unbroken ones in rows. Her own head was full of benchmark contamination, compute bottlenecks, and faculty who used the word community only when donors were present. Meera had autoclave maintenance contracts and payroll-tax deadlines. Anita knew exactly which doctor in the practice would forget to sign a form on a Friday and which insurance company would pretend not to receive a fax until the third attempt. The envy landed low in Leela's stomach: all that ordinary inconvenience, and every bit of it came with a next step.

Her phone buzzed again on the counter.

Meera glanced at it. "You can just turn it off, you know."

"I know."

"Then that's a personality issue."

"Thank you, doctor."

"Different kind."

She smiled, but she still picked the phone up. A second text had arrived.

Also, let's not widen this unnecessarily. These reviews tend to become narrative very fast. A simple factual chronology from you will help.

He had a gift for making the administrative voice sound less like bureaucracy and more like a private warning. The word narrative sat on the screen with a smugness that made her want to throw the phone into the sink. Instead she slid it into her back pocket, where it felt warm and invasive as a hand she had not invited.

Outside, the light had thinned to the blue-gray that made the whole block look briefly provisional. Anita sent them both out with the tray of diyas and a steel bowl of oil, then remained inside to rescue something from the stove. Meera crouched on the concrete in her work clothes and adjusted the rangoli border with the concentration she probably gave a difficult bite wing.

"You made this?" Leela asked.

"Part of it. The rest looked childish, so I redid it."

"Whose?"

"Mine."

Leela laughed, genuinely enough that the sound surprised her. She knelt beside the tray and pinched cotton wicks between oily fingers. The front step was cold through her jeans. Across the street two boys were trying to light sparklers from the wrong end while their father shouted uselessly from the porch. Somewhere farther down the block someone had started frying onions. Home, it turned out, was partly smell and partly being insulted by people who would still hand you a shawl if the wind picked up.

Meera placed the last pinch of magenta powder with the tip of her little finger and sat back on her heels to inspect it. "How bad is the wrist?"

Leela flexed the brace. "Fine."

"That means it's not fine."

"It means I type too much."

"Then stop typing like you're digging a trench."

"Extremely actionable advice."

"I've got more if you want it. Most of it involves posture and accepting that your spine is attached to the rest of you."

This, too, had continued while Leela had been elsewhere: Meera becoming the kind of person who carried her competence around lightly enough to make it look innate. She had not become smug. That would have been easier to hate. She still squinted in the wind and cursed under her breath when a diya tilted. She still had the old family habit of treating care as a form of correction. But there was a settledness to her that made Leela feel not judged exactly, more like she had stayed too long at a bus stop after everybody else had admitted the route was wrong.

They lit the lamps one by one. Oil darkened the clay. The little flames steadied faster than they had any right to in the cold. Anita came out wiping her hands on a dish towel and made a dissatisfied circuit of the steps, moving two diyas half an inch apart because symmetry, apparently, was also a moral value.

"Don't put that one so close to the door," she told Leela. "You'll kick it coming in."

"You have a lot of faith in me."

"I remember."

The block brightened in pieces. Orange lights glowed in hedges, blue LEDs sagged across a garage, and actual flames burned at thresholds that made the houses look, for once, as if someone lived inside them on purpose. For ten whole minutes Leela's phone stayed silent. She stood on the step with oil on her fingers and watched the rangoli catch the low light, the chalk colors deepening as the concrete dampened underneath. Her stomach was warm from tea and fried dough. Her mother's shoulder pressed briefly against hers as Anita reached past her to straighten the tray. It was enough, more than enough, and because it was enough it made her almost immediately wary.

Dinner happened in the old family way, with no one admitting there had been a decision to sit down. One moment they were still moving through tasks, and the next there was rice, cauliflower roast, chole, raita, the bakery sweets ignored in favor of homemade ones, and Anita telling Meera to stop serving herself like a guest. The dining table was the same one from high school, small enough that knees negotiated with each other under it. The crowdedness felt merciful. It meant nobody could retreat into a graceful posture.

Meera talked about the practice because practice was what had happened to her all day and because she had never learned the academic habit of pretending work was an embarrassment in domestic space.

"I had a man today insist that if flossing matters so much, why don't we give it away free. I said, we do. It's literally in my pocket right now. He still acted like I'd revealed a secret menu."

"Did you at least shame him properly?" Anita asked.

"Of course. I'm a professional."

"You enjoy that too much," Leela said.

"I enjoy being right in a chair that reclines."

Anita snorted. "One day somebody will punch you."

"Then I'll bill it as oral trauma."

The rhythm of them was so old it did not need performance. Leela found herself adding little comments at the correct places, not because she was especially present but because the pattern still knew her shape. Anita asked whether graduate housing had fixed the heat. Meera reminded her, without looking up, to book an actual dental cleaning and stop waiting until something hurt. Anita described a patient who had screamed at the practice because his copay existed. Meera described the office landlord's new obsession with changing the parking lot striping right before winter. Somewhere between the raita and the second round of chole, time took on the blunt domestic quality Leela had come home for. It moved by serving spoon and interruption, not by countdown.

Then Anita said, "Tell me which month the defense is."

It was a clean sentence, neither suspicious nor theatrical. Anita said it while spooning more rice onto Leela's plate, as if exact months belonged in the same category as who needed seconds and whether there was enough yogurt left.

Leela looked up too slowly.

"What?"

"Which month," Anita repeated. "If it's March, I need to put my time off in early. If it's April, I can wait a little. Meera also needs to know if she has to move patients."

Meera nodded with her mouth full. "If it's a Friday, tell me soon. I can shift fillings. Sedation cases are a whole drama."

The shame arrived so fast that for a second it felt physiological, like missing a stair. It came not because the question was cruel but because it was ordinary, and ordinary people asked for dates when events were real.

"It should be spring," Leela said, and heard at once how useless that sounded. She kept going because stopping was worse. "I mean, the committee part is mostly calendar coordination, and there's this annoying compliance review because of some inherited server permissions, but that's separate, it's not really about the dissertation as such, so it depends on when they want all the paperwork lined up."

Meera swallowed. "That was a lot of words for no month."

"We're still figuring it out."

Anita's face did not change. She had the kind of self-command that made stronger emotions look like a narrowing of practical focus. She set down the rice spoon. "Did your advisor give you a season or not?"

Leela drank water to give herself a second. Her own voice, when it came, had the careful collaborative softness she hated in herself. "He thinks spring is realistic."

That was perhaps not yet a lie, or not fully one. Mohit thought many things were realistic when realism cost him nothing.

Meera said, "Okay. Fine. Text me when you have an actual date. I need notice."

Meera went back to her plate already doing the math on moved fillings and sedation cases, as if the defense were a real thing that would eventually occupy a Friday. Anita picked up the rice spoon again. Dates in this house went straight onto calendars; at Haviland they dissolved into committee moods and forwarded email. Leela pressed her thumbnail into the damp ring her water glass had left on the table until the paper coaster went soft.

Her phone vibrated in her pocket against the chair.

She did not have to look to know.

Anita was saying something about whether the cardiology office was closing early on Christmas Eve. Meera was describing, with professional disgust, a patient who used her front teeth to open pistachios. The room continued around Leela with insulting competence. She put down her fork and went to the kitchen on the excuse of getting more water.

It was Mohit again.

If there's any chance you've described the review more broadly, let me know before Monday. These things escalate through misunderstanding.

Beneath it, as if he had earned casualness by writing two procedural messages first, he added:

Enjoy the holiday.

The obscenity of that almost made her laugh. She stood at the sink with the faucet running too long, reading the text twice as if the second pass might reveal a version less intimately irritating. He had never been inside this house. He had no claim on the crowded shoe rack or the cheap orange lights or the old table with the water ring no one could remove. Yet his language had entered anyway, with its aligned facts and misunderstandings and cozy little threats.

From the dining room Meera called, "You drown in there?"

"Coming."

She put the phone away and went back. The rest of dinner held. That was almost the worst part. No catastrophe arrived to justify the pulse of dread she carried back to the table. Anita cut the milk sweet into exact squares. Meera took a call from the office because a patient was panicking about post-procedure bleeding and came back rolling her eyes. "He spit after the extraction even though I told him not to, so now the clot's gone and suddenly I'm a witch." Anita said, "You are, but that's separate," and for a moment all three of them laughed so hard Leela had to wipe her eyes.

The laugh died. Anita kept cutting the milk sweet into exact squares, Meera settled back into her chair, and the room went on being warm enough to make the lie feel heavier.

By nine thirty Meera had packed leftovers into two containers, checked tomorrow's schedule on her phone, and stood in the entryway zipping her boots.

"Text me when you get the defense date," she said. Then, with the affectionate impatience she used when she wanted concern to look like task management, "And book a cleaning before I drag you in by your hair."

"You say the nicest things."

"I save the really nice things for patients with insurance."

She hugged Anita first, then Leela in the quick solid way siblings do when they have spent too much of life pretending not to need ceremony. During the hug she slipped three floss picks into the outer pocket of Leela's duffel without comment. When she pulled back, she said, "Those are for your own good. Don't act persecuted."

"I haven't said anything."

"Your face has." She leaned down to fix one of the diyas at the threshold, because even leaving required an adjustment, then straightened. "Drive safe doesn't apply, so just text when you get back Sunday."

After she left, the house went quieter by a whole dimension. Anita put away the extra food, wiped the table, and turned the television down to a murmur. Outside, intermittent firecrackers kept cracking open the dark. Leela carried plates to the sink and rinsed them because it was easier than sitting still. Her mother did not send her to bed. That itself was a form of notice.

The kitchen after guests or near-guests was always smaller. Steam had dried off the windows. The floor was sticky in one patch by the stove where sugar syrup had splashed earlier. Anita stood at the sink in her old house T-shirt, bangles pushed up her forearm, scrubbing the kadai with more force than the job required. The reading glasses were gone now; without them her face looked less official and more tired.

Leela dried plates and stacked them. Neither of them spoke for long enough that the silence became specific.

Then Anita said, "How bad is it?"

Leela kept her eyes on the plate in her hand. "What?"

"Your professor texting on a holiday. Your face all evening. The answer at dinner that went around the block and came back with no address." Anita turned off the faucet and looked at her directly. "How bad is it?"

Here it was, the point in the evening where truth might still be chosen. She could say: my admin access is suspended because he routed his own repository theft through credentials that technically used to be mine. She could say: I sat in a conference room that smelled like disinfected fruit and watched an officer take notes as if coercion were a workflow preference. She could say: I'm thirty years old and still vulnerable to a man who can hurt me by sounding reasonable. She could say: I don't actually know if spring means spring or if it means never.

And then what would she do with the truth once it was in the room?

Anita would get practical. She would ask whether the stipend was safe, whether housing depended on enrollment, whether there was somebody above Mohit who could sign anything if he turned vindictive, whether this was racism or ordinary professor evil or the usual braid. Then she would ask how much was on the card and reach for a pen. By the third question the whole arrangement would be sitting on the counter in parts: money, housing, one man's name, how little control Leela actually had.

Leela put the dry plate into the cabinet and heard her own answer arrive in the falsely calm tone she used in meetings when she was trying to preempt other people's alarm.

"It's not that bad. It's stupid and procedural and I should've just said that better at dinner. The review is about old permissions, not research fraud or anything dramatic. It doesn't change the defense. We're still on track."

Anita watched her. "On track for what month."

There was a final chance even then to step sideways into honesty or at least vagueness. Leela missed it deliberately.

"Probably March," she said. "Maybe early April, depending on calendars. But this academic year. That's still the plan."

The lie entered the room without fanfare. There was no thunderclap, no maternal intuition sharp enough to cut it in half. It simply sat down between them with the dish soap and the damp steel bowls and became, for the moment, another household fact.

Anita turned back to the sink. "Okay."

Relief came first, hot and ugly.

Then her mother said, "How much is on the card now?"

Leela stared. "What card."

"Don't be insulting."

"Amma."

"How much."

This was another one of her mother's gifts, if that was the word: the refusal to let shame select the nouns. The card was the card. Debt was not an atmosphere. It was a number that could be named or ducked, and ducking it only made Anita more exact.

Leela set the dish towel down. "It's fine."

"That's not a number either."

"I'm making the minimums."

"That also isn't a number."

The kitchen felt suddenly overlit. She could hear the refrigerator motor kick on, the neighbor's television through the wall, a child outside begging for one more sparkler. The whole block seemed to be practicing ordinary life at high volume while she tried not to tell her mother the balance on a credit card she had run up buying groceries and train tickets and the occasional decent meal on weeks when the lab ate every available hour.

"Around five," she said finally.

Anita's hands stopped moving in the sink. "Five hundred?"

Leela laughed once, without humor. "No."

"Five thousand?"

"A little under."

Anita closed her eyes, not theatrically, just for one second as if filing that number somewhere she did not want it loose. When she opened them she did not scold. Of course she did not. Scolding was for correctable mistakes. She reached for the dish towel, dried her hands, and picked up her phone from beside the fruit bowl.

"Amma, no."

"I didn't ask your opinion yet."

"You don't have to do that."

"Apparently I do."

She tapped into her banking app with the same unromantic speed she used to pay utility bills. Leela could see the hairline crack in the corner of the phone case, the one Anita had been meaning to replace for months and had not because the old one still held. That, almost more than the number, made the next minute hard to stand upright through.

"Take this and pay the card down," Anita said. "Not all the way. Keep some for groceries and whatever else you've been pretending doesn't cost money."

"Amma, seriously, keep it."

"For what. A nicer funeral?" She kept tapping. "You're close enough to the end that I'm not going to watch you do something stupid because of pride."

To Anita, she was close enough to the end. The lie thickened in Leela's throat.

"I can manage," she said, and heard how weak it sounded even to her. It wasn't false; she had managed, technically, for years. But the sentence belonged to the version of herself that was still trying to keep family love from becoming family accounting.

Anita looked up. "Can you?"

It was not cruel. That made it worse.

Leela said nothing.

Anita's voice gentled but did not soften. "Listen to me. I know the difference between needing help once and building a whole life out of other people's rescue. I'm not confused. This is the last push, that's all. Finish properly. Then we'll talk about the rest."

Finish properly, her mother had said. The words made the degree seem like a road instead of weather, as if March existed because they had now said it aloud twice.

Leela's phone buzzed on the counter before she could answer.

For one stupid second she thought it was Mohit again and felt a flash of murderous clarity so clean it was almost pleasurable. Then she saw the bank notification.

ANITA KRISHNAN sent you $1,200.00.

The amount sat there with brutal legibility. It was not festival money in an envelope, not a vague promise, not dinner pressed into her hand on the way out the door. It was twelve hundred dollars, enough to knock the card down, enough to breathe through next month, enough to make the lie feel functional.

"Amma."

"Pay it tonight before you decide to become noble," Anita said. She turned back to the sink and picked up the last steel bowl. "And don't send it back. I will only send it again and be irritated."

Leela did not say thank you right away because thank you would have made the whole transaction sound cleaner than it was. She stood at the counter with the notification still bright on the screen, the digits reflected faintly in the dark window over the sink. Behind her, the diyas at the front step burned low and steady. In the sink Anita rinsed soap from the bowl, set it on the rack, reached automatically for the next thing that needed doing.

Leela opened her banking app. Relief moved through her first, plain as hunger. After that came humiliation, and after that something meaner: the knowledge that her body could not entirely distinguish between being loved and being bailed out. She did not return the money. She did not even hover over the option. She looked at the new balance, thought of the minimum due on the eighteenth, thought of groceries, train fare back, the little strip of life that twelve hundred dollars could hold in place, and let the transfer settle.

On the counter her phone lit once more with Mohit's name, another text arriving into the house as if he owned some corridor there. Leela turned the screen facedown without reading it.

"Put the laddus away before they dry out," Anita said.

Leela slid the sweets back into the steel tin with her oiled fingers and kept the money.


# Chapter 9

By nine in the morning the conference hotel had taken everybody down to badges.

That was the first thing Leela noticed about Baltimore, or at least the part of Baltimore the Harbor East Conference Hotel had been willing to admit existed. Men who would have looked merely tired on campus now looked senior because their lanyards were printed in a darker color. Women in black blazers moved through the lobby carrying tote bags with sponsor logos and cups of coffee that cost more than a decent lunch in Lakebridge. The carpet had the thick, muffling softness of money spent to keep panic respectable. The air-conditioning was set for an audience of menopausal donors and had no interest in November.

June stood at the registration table with one hand on her rolling suitcase and the other digging through her backpack for the confirmation email she had already pulled up twice. She had cut her hair again sometime after Diwali; the blunt edge sat sharper at her jaw now, as if she had decided even her face needed less excess. She wore black trousers, a white button-down, and the dark green blazer Leela had only ever seen hanging on the back of June's apartment door like a threat of adulthood neither of them trusted. The blazer fit, technically. It still looked like June was trying it on for irony and had run out of time to change.

The volunteer at the table had a Ravens scarf looped three times around her neck and a fountain-pen stain on the side of her hand. "Professor Saran already checked in," she said, sliding over two folders and not looking up from the alphabetized stack. "He asked if his students got the reduced parking voucher."

"Of course he did," June said.

The volunteer gave her a quick, tired grin, the kind conference workers saved for people who were clearly not the problem at the current moment. "It's clipped inside."

Leela found her own badge, Haviland University printed under her name in a font that made the institution look cleaner than it had ever been in life. She clipped it on. June did the same. Instantly they were no longer two women who had taken a too-early train with bad coffee and laptop chargers tangled together in a tote bag. They were attendees. Presenters. People who could be judged before speaking.

Mohit had texted at 8:11.

Running slightly behind. Please rehearse without me. I have a breakfast conversation with external folks that may be useful to all of us later.

Useful to all of us later had become one of his favorite phrases that semester. It usually meant useful to him immediately and harmful to somebody else on a delay.

June looked over Leela's shoulder at the message and started chewing one of her nicotine gums with the clipped violence she usually reserved for people speaking too slowly at her. "He doesn't even bother to lie attractively anymore."

"He might actually be with a sponsor."

"Then he can sponsor his own advisee's fucking talk."

Leela should have laughed. On campus she probably would have. The hotel had changed the proportions of June's anger. It looked less like a joke here and more like something she was trying to keep inside the lines of her face.

They took the escalator up to the meeting floor, past a glass case full of fake sailboats and a bar that would not open for another hour but was already displaying oranges pierced with cocktail picks. The room reserved for their prep slot, Patapsco B, was colder than the hallway and smelled faintly of dust baked out of the projector. Someone had left a half bowl of peppermints at the lectern from a breakfast panel on grant partnerships. June set her laptop down, plugged in the HDMI cable, and the first slide appeared: title, institutional logos, the clean set of fonts she used when she wanted the work to look more finished than her life felt.

"Do you want me to time it?" Leela asked.

"I know how long my own talk is."

"I know."

June rubbed at the red mark the lanyard had already made on the side of her neck. "Sorry. Yes. Time it."

Leela sat in the front row with her phone in her lap and watched June begin. This, more than almost anything else in the lab, had always made Leela feel briefly superstitious about competence. June at dinner was funny because she could not bear an earnest silence. June in the lab was funny because contempt saved time. June in front of slides became something tighter and cleaner. She didn't waste words. She didn't need to perform how clever she was because the structure of the talk already assumed she had done the reading and the work and the stupid, necessary cleanup no one praised until it failed.

She moved through the opening framing quickly, one hand in her pocket, the other tapping the clicker against her thigh. Evaluation drift in public-service NLP systems. Benchmark contamination. Error propagation when institutions reused supposedly robust models in places where the human cost of a miss was not abstract. Practical, bodily, anti-ornamental. When June trusted the material she almost sounded gentle.

Leela checked the time. Good pace.

Then June hit the middle section and stalled for half a beat at the slide with the main benchmark comparison, the one Omar had gone after in the seminar. It was barely visible if you didn't know her. Leela knew her. The stall lived in the room like a skipped stair.

She finished the rehearsal anyway. Eleven minutes forty seconds.

"You're fine," Leela said. "If anything, you can slow down on the third section."

"That's reassuring. I love being told I can savor my own public damage."

The joke should have put things back where they belonged. Instead it landed between them with its edges exposed.

Leela said, "June."

June unplugged the laptop and wound the cable too tightly around her hand. "No. It's fine. I'm not doing this before the talk."

What Leela heard under it was not dismissal but triage. June could not afford to open the wrong thing at 9:20 in the morning in a hotel ballroom whose walls could be moved with hidden latches. Leela let it go.

At 10:03 Mohit still was not there.

At 10:09 he sent another message.

May need to miss the session start. Unexpectedly productive conversation. You know the material. Trust the room.

June read that one directly on her own phone and gave a little breath of laughter that had no humor in it. "Trust the room. He talks like a man who has never once in his life trusted a room."

"Do you want me to call him?"

"For what? So he can tell me this is good practice?"

Leela looked at the rows of empty chairs, the draped table for panelists, the sweating pitcher of water already collecting a ring on the linen. On campus the damage between them had a basement, a seminar room, doors, a geography. Here it had followed them into a generic hotel that had no memory of them and therefore gave nothing back.

"If anybody asks a bullshit version of the seminar question," Leela said, "I'll step in."

June's eyes lifted to her face. They were very dark when she was tired enough. "Do that because you think it'll help now," she said. "Not because you suddenly figured out what one sentence costs."

Then she picked up her laptop and headed for the ballroom.

* * *

The session chair wore silver running shoes under a navy dress and kept unwrapping the same cough drop, then deciding against it and rewrapping it again. Her badge said ELENA RITCHIE, Carnegie Mellon. When she introduced June she pronounced Haviland correctly and the project title correctly and still managed to make the whole thing sound like a provisional inconvenience before lunch.

The room filled to a little over half. Enough bodies to hurt. Not enough to disappear in.

Leela sat near the aisle with her notebook open and the deck title written at the top of the page in case June needed a visible ally in the audience, something as stupid and ancient as eye contact. The panel before June's had gone long on a healthcare triage model that nobody trusted and everybody clapped for anyway. People were hungry. Two faculty in the back were already packing their bags on their laps so they could leave quickly for whatever sponsored buffet waited downstairs. A man in a red quarter-zip opened his laptop before June reached slide two and left it open the entire talk like a private insult.

None of that would have mattered if June had caught the room early.

She didn't.

Bad talks flailed, sweated apologies, and gave in to the terrible impulse to re-explain every slide as if volume were clarity. June stayed sharp, fast, entirely herself. The problem was that she walked into the room carrying the shape of the earlier injury, and some part of the room could smell it even without knowing the story. Her voice tightened on the benchmark section. One transition came half a beat too soon. She made a joke about everyone in evaluation quietly praying their datasets were less haunted than they were, and only one person laughed, a little too late.

When she reached the central result, Leela could feel the audience deciding whether this was a talk about infrastructure or a talk about a flaw the speaker was trying to outtalk.

Then the question came.

It came from a woman in the second row with athletic tape around one thumb and a legal pad covered in tiny, square handwriting. She waited until Q and A, smiled as if she were about to ask for a citation, and said, "Could you say more about how you're separating adversarial brittleness from benchmark construction error here? Because if the perturbation set is partly surfacing annotation mismatch, then the system may not be failing in the way you're claiming. It may just be inheriting noise your benchmark invited."

The wording was not Omar's. The wound was.

June answered immediately, which was one of the reasons she survived as well as she did in academia: speed could look like confidence to people who had not seen you break. She explained the held-out slices, the ablation checks, the reason they had included exactly the noisy subsets the questioner was worried about. She was right more often than not. But the question had done its work. It had pulled the seminar back into the room and laid it over her slides like cheap acetate.
