The woman nodded in a way that meant thank you for confirming I should remain unconvinced.

Leela lifted her hand before she had fully planned the words. Elena Ritchie pointed to her with visible relief, the way chairs did when they thought a friendly question might rescue timing.

"Could you say a little more," Leela asked, keeping her voice level, "about why the failure mode matters even if some of the noise is benchmark-level? My understanding is that the institutional use case here is exactly that nobody deploying these systems gets to pretend the mess is elsewhere."

For one beat June held on Leela with an unreadable stillness, as if she were sorting rescue from trespass. Then she turned back to the audience and answered the version of the question Leela had handed her instead of the accusation underneath it. She was good there. Brief, specific, unsentimental. For thirty seconds the talk was the talk it should have been.

It still ended to polite applause.

Not warm. Not interested enough to become hostile. Just the sound a regional conference made when it believed it had behaved decently.

In the hallway outside the ballroom, people did the soft, awful thing conference people did when they thought they were being kind.

"Tough room."

"Important problem space."

"I liked your point about deployment assumptions."

A doctoral student from Maryland stopped to tell June she had appreciated the slides' color palette. She wore a pearl barrette shaped like a fish skeleton and kept pushing it back into place with the side of her wrist because both her hands were full of boxed salad and a charger brick. June thanked her with perfect public politeness. As soon as the student was gone she said, "If one more person tells me the problem space is important, I'm going to put my head through the ice machine."

Leela said, "You answered well."

June turned on her. Not loudly. That would have been easier. "You don't get to grade the recovery."

Leela felt the point land because it was accurate. The conference folder dug a paper edge into the crook of her elbow. "I wasn't trying to."

"You asked the question you should've asked in October."

"I know."

"Do you?"

The hallway kept moving around them. A sponsor banner for responsible AI bent slightly where somebody had assembled it wrong, so the word responsible had a permanent wave through the middle. Two men walked past arguing cheerfully about compute budgets as if those were weather systems that happened to everyone equally.

June looked exhausted suddenly, not theatrical about it, just used up along the edges. "Where is he?"

Leela did not need to ask who.

She checked her phone. No message from Mohit asking how the talk had gone. No Are you okay. No strategic interpretation of the room. Nothing.

"I'll call."

He sent the new text before she could.

Need a favor. I left a print packet at the business center under my name. Could one of you grab it and bring it to Harbor C by 3:15? I'm tied up in a conversation that's running long. Will regroup afterward.

June read it over her shoulder and went very still. It was different from anger. Anger at least had movement in it.

"He wants us to courier his little side hustle," she said.

"He didn't say what it is."

"He didn't ask how it went."

There wasn't a defense for that. Leela did what she had spent years doing around Mohit and looked automatically for a version of the situation that would cost the least if spoken aloud. External folks. Productive conversation. Something useful later. Every interpretation came dressed as management and ended in degradation.

"Let's get the packet," June said.

"You don't have to."

"No. I really fucking do."

* * *

The business center sat off the lobby behind a frosted glass wall that made everybody at the computers look guilty regardless of what they were printing. A man in a clipped vest staffed the counter. His tie clip was crooked by almost an inch, and every few minutes he pressed the pad of his thumb against the bare pale groove where a wedding ring had once sat, as if checking whether the skin had fixed itself. He looked up when Leela gave Mohit's name.

"Professor Saran?" he said. "Yeah. He's been in and out all morning with those decks. Said two students might pick up the last set."

June's mouth flattened. "Decks."

"Color double-sided, nicer stock." The man reached below the counter and brought up a rubber-banded packet with a print receipt paper-clipped to the top. "He was pretty specific about the paper weight."

Of course he had been.

Leela thanked him and took the packet. The cover sheet showed a hotel logo, print charges, and the file name.

`ruderal_pitch_v3.pdf`

User: `msaran@haviland.edu`

11:07 AM

24 copies

June saw it at the same time she did. "Give me that."

They moved out into the hall before June tore off the rubber band. The packet was still warm from printing. It had that faint sweet-plastic smell of toner and thick paper. On top sat a title slide:

RUDERAL ANALYTICS  
Decision Support Under Adversarial Conditions

No Haviland logo. No lab name. Just a clean green sprig of a logo that made the whole thing look like a climate-tech company until you actually read it.

June flipped to the next slide, then the next.

On slide six she stopped.

Leela knew the figure before she consciously understood it. A clustered bar chart, blue against rust, error rates across perturbation categories. June had shown a version of it in the seminar. June had shown a cleaner version of it ten minutes ago. This one still carried the old typo in the x-axis label, the one June had corrected in October after swearing for five straight minutes at matplotlib.

`sythetic code-switch`

The missing n sat there like a fingerprint.

Slide seven was worse. A heat map from June's internal benchmark slices, not the public draft. The tiny title at the top still read `eval_holdout_oct3b`, because Mohit had apparently taken the screenshot directly from the version June had exported for lab feedback and never meant to circulate outside Keating. Slide nine had one of Leela's robustness curves with the ugly orange confidence band she had used only in a late-night internal draft because the prettier palette broke on the department printer. The caption underneath called it a proprietary generalization stress test.

Leela touched the edge of the page and felt the slick tooth of the paper against her fingertip. Her own bad September choice looked strangely expensive in color.

"No," June said.

It was almost quiet. That made it worse.

She flipped farther.

Slide twelve: pilot partnerships.

Slide fourteen: market opportunity in municipal compliance and triage systems.

Slide sixteen: defensible moat through exclusive benchmark access.

Slide eighteen: timeline to seed round.

In the footer of the final slide, smaller than the thank-you line, sat a general contact address and a web domain: `contact@ruderal.ai` and `ruderal.ai`

June gave a short laugh that sounded like something breaking inside a wall. "He made a fucking moat out of my benchmark."

Leela took the packet and forced herself to look again, page by page, because the first shock wanted to turn everything abstract and she could not afford abstraction. Their figures were here. Their labels. Their phrasing. June's benchmark, which had just been publicly reopened as maybe flawed, was being sold downstairs as proprietary infrastructure. Leela's robustness curve, built partly on a result she still did not trust enough to speak about cleanly, had been pulled out of the lab and dropped into a deck with market sizing and a clean little plant logo.

The evidence was stupidly physical: a print receipt, a filename, toner still warm on the page.

"We should photograph all of it," Leela said.

June was already taking out her phone. "I know."

She shot the receipt first, then each slide, her thumb steady now in the practical way it became when panic got too expensive. They worked through the packet leaned against the wallpaper between the business center and the elevators, two women in conference badges doing documentary labor in public while a dentist from somewhere in Connecticut wheeled a suitcase past them and did not look twice.

At slide nine June paused again. "Did you give him this?"

Leela looked at the figure. Not the version from the submitted paper. Earlier. Internal. The orange band. She had forgotten it existed outside the folder structure in her own head.

"No," she said, which was true and not enough.

June studied her face for a second too long, then went back to photographing.

When they finished, she shoved the papers back into order with a violence that left one corner bent. "Harbor C," she said. "Let's see what the productive conversation is."

Harbor C was at the far end of the meeting floor, past the sponsor lunch buffet with its little silver labels and its trays of food sweating under heat lamps. The door to the room was closed. On the easel outside, somebody had slid a plain white sheet into the holder where conference signage usually went.

PRIVATE MEETING

No logo. No program listing.

From inside came the softened hum of a projector and Mohit's voice, unmistakable even through hotel wood because he had a way of making every sentence sound like a calm solution to a problem he had not caused. Leela couldn't make out the words, only the rhythm: pause, reassurance, controlled enthusiasm.

June stood with the packet tucked under her arm and looked at the door.

If she went in now, Leela thought, the scene would belong to him within ten seconds. He would smile, introduce them as brilliant students, say there had been an unfortunate misunderstanding about draft materials, make them look unstable in a room full of people who had already arrived prepared to invest in his competence.

June knew it too. You could see the knowledge hardening in the way she shifted her weight and did not move closer.

"He used my worst month to do a sales pitch," she said.

"Yes."

"And your figure's in there too."

"Yes."

"Do you know what the clean version of this is going to be? University policy, broad rights, collaborative assets, everybody's confused, nobody remembers where a slide came from."

Leela did know. The wording was practically already on Gloria Fisk's paper folder.

June pressed the heel of her hand against her mouth once, hard enough to flatten it, then dropped it. "I want five minutes where I don't have to hear him explaining my own work back to me."

"Come upstairs," Leela said.

June's jaw flexed once. Then her attention slid from Leela to the closed door and on toward the elevators. "My room."

That was all.

Leela followed her.

* * *

June's room looked exactly like Leela's room would have looked if a committee had designed loneliness by consensus. One king bed with a white duvet too thick for the season, a desk bolted under the television, a print over the bed of boats in a harbor painted in colors no actual harbor had ever admitted. The window showed a slice of gray water and three office buildings that could have been in any city willing to finance itself with glass.

June dropped the packet on the desk so hard the cheap wood shuddered. She took off her blazer, missed the back of the chair with it, and let it fall. Then she stood with both hands braced on the desk and her head down for so long that Leela thought, stupidly, of the night in the third chapter of her dissertation when her code had corrupted and she had spent forty minutes simply staring at the monitor because rage required calories she did not have.

"Say something useful," June said.

Leela shut the door behind her. The room clicked sealed. "The receipt matters."

"Yes."

"The typo matters. The draft labels matter. The fact that he's using your benchmark as proprietary access matters."

"I know the evidence matters." June straightened and turned around. Without the blazer she looked younger and more tired, the white shirt wrinkled at the waist from sitting through other people's talks. "Say something useful about him. About us. About why I gave that talk while he was downstairs trying to sell the blood still in it."

Leela felt the dry hotel air catch at the back of her throat. Not a generic panic. The room's climate control had been pushing cold through a vent above the window all afternoon, and now it made every breath feel borrowed.

"He thinks," she said carefully, "that if he keeps the language professional enough, then the actual theft becomes a matter of interpretation."

"That's about him."

"June."

"No." June shook her head. "Not this thing where you give me the institutional version first because it's neater. I'm not asking for neat."

Leela set her conference folder on the nightstand and looked at the packet spread open on the desk. June's work. Her own work. Mohit's fonts. Ruderal's stupid sprig logo. Everything in the room had edges suddenly. The stitched hem of the duvet. The plastic seam on the ice bucket. The bend in the bent slide corner June had made in the hall.

"Useful," Leela said, making herself keep going before caution could dress it up. "I should've backed you harder in the room today, and I know why that sounded disgusting coming from me after October."

June gave a single nod, not kind and not cruel either, and Leela understood it as permission to continue.

"Useful is that this isn't him being careless," Leela said. "This is work. He printed twenty-four copies. He paid extra for paper weight. He made a plan. And useful is that if he planned it, he thinks he can survive it."

"He probably can."

"Not if we have enough."

June laughed once. "We. That's ambitious."

The question came out before Leela could revise it. "Do you want me to leave?"

June went quiet.

From somewhere down the hall came the brief thud of an ice machine emptying itself. Someone laughed in the neighboring room and then coughed for a solid ten seconds in the bathroom, the kind of ugly hotel cough that told you conferences were just trade shows for overwork.

June reached into her pocket, took out a stick of nicotine gum in a crumpled wrapper, then looked at it and put it back. "I don't know what I want you to do," she said. "That's part of the problem."

It was the plainest thing she'd said all day. In the lab or at dinner June usually hid feeling inside velocity. Here the velocity was gone.

Leela moved closer, slowly enough that June could have stopped her without difficulty. She could smell June now: cold air, train wool, the faint medicinal mint of gum.

"I know I missed the sentence that mattered," Leela said.

June's eyes flicked up. "Yes."

"I keep hearing it anyway."

"That doesn't help me retroactively."

"I know."

Another step. They were close enough now that Leela could see the shallow red groove the lanyard had rubbed into June's neck. She wanted, absurdly, to press her mouth to that exact irritated strip of skin. The wanting had been in her for longer than she had language she trusted for it. That was part of what made the seminar so awful: not just that she had failed June publicly, but that she had failed her while knowing too well how June's face changed when she was trying not to show damage.

June said, "Why are you still here?"

The question had more than one right answer and at least three wrong ones.

Leela did not let herself choose the prettiest version. "Because I didn't want to leave you alone with that deck," she said. "Because he spent all day treating us like labor and I'd like one hour that isn't organized around him. Because I wanted to come upstairs with you before I had a good reason."

June kept one hand closed around the crumpled gum wrapper and took a full breath before answering, as if belief itself might expose her.

"That's more useful," she said softly.

Leela could have stopped there. She knew that. Could have turned it into a conversation about evidence handling, tomorrow's train, the version of solidarity that lived comfortably on paper. Instead she lifted a hand and touched the mark on June's neck with the backs of her fingers. June didn't move away.

The contact was so slight it should not have changed anything. It did.

June caught Leela's wrist, not hard, just enough to keep the hand there. "If this is guilt," she said, "go back to your room and write me a memo."

The line was mean in structure and wrecked by how low her voice had gone.

"It isn't."

"Not even a little?"

Leela thought about lying. Decided against it. "I feel guilty about plenty. This isn't that."

June's mouth twitched. "Good. I'd hate to get pity all over the duvet."

Then she let go of Leela's wrist and kissed her.

June kissed with decision. Tentative would have sounded sarcastic on her. Her hand came up to the back of Leela's neck and the first kiss landed almost angry, hard enough that Leela's teeth clicked lightly against her own. The second was slower. By the third June had stepped them backward until the backs of Leela's knees hit the bed.

Leela kissed her back with the relief of finally being allowed the obvious. The room, the deck, the day, the conference floor below them with its buffet labels and donor sentences, all of it stayed present, which was part of why this did not feel like escape. June's shirt was cool from the air-conditioning. The skin at her waist, when Leela got her hand under it, was warm.

June broke the kiss just long enough to tug the lanyard over Leela's head and throw it onto the lamp. It caught there, swinging. "That thing was making me homicidal."

Leela laughed into June's mouth. June made a sound that could have been a laugh too and then it was teeth and breath again, June's blunt-cut hair against Leela's cheek, June's palm flattening over the small of her back as if she had been meaning to place it there for months.

Leela got one hand under the white shirt, then both, and June let her push it up. The conference camisole underneath had a tiny snag near the strap, ordinary and specific enough that it made Leela want her more, not less. She kissed down the side of June's throat and felt June swallow.

"Tell me if you want me to stop," Leela said against her skin.

"I won't."

That could have been arrogance. From June it was trust with the paint stripped off.

Leela pulled the shirt over her head. June stood still to help, then immediately reached for the hem of Leela's sweater and dragged it up harder than necessary. The soft lining of it caught briefly on Leela's wrist brace. June stopped at once.

"Shit. Sorry."

"It's fine."

June looked at the brace, then at her. "Does it hurt?"

The question was so practical it nearly undid her.

"Sometimes."

June sat her on the edge of the bed and peeled the brace open carefully, undoing the Velcro one strip at a time so it wouldn't rip loud through the room. Underneath, the skin of Leela's wrist was indented and pale where the fabric had pressed all day. June put her thumb there, lightly, then bent and kissed the mark.

Tenderness, Leela thought wildly, had always been the more dangerous thing.

She touched June's face, the blunt line of haircut, the warm shell of her ear, then leaned in and kissed her again. June pushed her back onto the bed this time, climbing over her with a competence that made Leela's stomach go tight in a way no theoretical language had ever helped. The duvet was too smooth; they slid on it until June swore and shoved the whole top layer half off the mattress.

"God, I hate conference hotels."

"You don't have to say that like a warning label."

"I'm setting expectations."

Leela reached for her and June's answer came all at once: mouth on hers, knee between her thighs, one hand working the button of Leela's jeans with impatient concentration. The room's cheap art knocked once against the wall when the headboard shifted. Both of them froze, then listened. Nothing. The people next door were still talking too loudly about a grant review panel. June grinned against Leela's mouth, sudden and young and a little feral.

"Good," she murmured. "I wanted one thing in this building to belong to us."

Leela pulled June down harder by the waist. There wasn't anything neat about the way it went after that. June had to spit the nicotine gum, finally, into the empty conference coffee cup by the bed. Leela's jeans stuck at one ankle because she had kept her shoes on too long. June's bra clasp took two tries in the bad lamp light. None of it made the wanting smaller. If anything it made it more exact.

When June slid her hand between Leela's legs, Leela grabbed at the fitted sheet so hard it snapped free from one corner. The exposed mattress pad rasped against her calf. June watched her face with a seriousness that was almost stern, then kissed her once, once more, and pushed two fingers inside her with the same direct competence she brought to every task she had decided was worth doing. Leela bit down on the heel of her own hand to keep quiet.

June pulled the hand away. "No."

"The walls are thin."

"Then bite me instead."

Leela did, lightly, at the slope where shoulder became neck. June's breath broke. The sound of it went through Leela like a current. She had spent months, maybe years, not letting herself imagine specifics because specifics asked to be answered for. Now June was above her in a hotel room with the deck on the desk and the afternoon going gray at the window, and the answer turned out to be not abstract at all. It was June's wrist flexing. It was the scratch of the blanket at Leela's thigh. It was the little involuntary curse June said when Leela got a hand under her underwear and found her already wet enough to make June jerk and laugh at once.

"Fuck," June said, forehead dropping briefly to Leela's shoulder. "Okay. Fine."

Leela rolled them partly over, wanting to see her. June let her, then didn't let her stay in charge for long. That was one of the things that made this feel less like a soft collapse and more like an event: leverage kept changing. June went rough when she wanted the day out of her body. She went careful the second Leela winced on the bad wrist. Leela, who lived so much of her life translating herself into acceptable precision, found she did not have precision left. She had hands, mouth, the stunned pleasure of June making room for her and then asking for more.

By the time Leela went down between June's thighs the room smelled like hotel detergent, sweat, and the citrus soap the housekeepers used on every bathroom surface in the country. June had one arm flung over her eyes and the other hand in Leela's hair, not pushing, just holding. When Leela brought her to the edge June's whole body tightened under the thin conference-hotel light, then came apart with a sound she buried in the pillow too late to matter.

Afterward they stayed half tangled on the stripped bed, both breathing too hard for conversation. The deck still sat on the desk in a crooked stack. One of June's socks had landed on slide fourteen, market opportunity obscured by black cotton.

June turned her head on the pillow and looked at Leela with an expression Leela had not seen on her before: not armored, not exactly soft, but unhidden. "Come here."

Leela already was. Still she moved closer until June's bare leg slid over hers and the cool part of the room no longer mattered.

For a minute it might have passed for peace if you did not know anything about them.

Then June said, "This doesn't fix October."

Leela stared at the ceiling print of fake harbor boats. "No. October was me freezing in real time."

"And it definitely doesn't fix that deck."

"No," Leela said. "But the deck leaves paper. October was just you getting hit and me standing there."

June traced the indentation from the wrist brace again, lightly, as if testing whether the skin had memory. "Okay," she said.

She didn't say what okay meant. Leela did not ask.

When June finally got up to pee, she took the packet with her to the desk on the way back and photographed every page again under the lamp, this time slower, making sure the slide numbers showed. Leela watched from the bed, still naked under the twisted sheet, and felt how little of the day had actually left them. Desire had clarified something. It had not simplified anything.

Leela said, "We should make backups."

June snorted softly. "There you are."

"That wasn't a retreat."

"I know. It was you."

The difference mattered enough that Leela let it stand.

They made the backups sitting on the carpet in front of the desk, Leela with the twisted sheet around her waist and the rough hotel weave rubbing the outside of her hip while both laptops stayed open on the desk above them. June pulled `ruderal_pitch_v3.pdf` onto her machine. Leela forwarded the slide photos to three different accounts. By the time both batteries died, June's screen was still frozen on the deck's thumbnail pane, blue-orange plots gone dim in miniature.

June set the packet on the nightstand instead of the desk and got back under the twisted sheet without turning off the lamp. The yellow hotel light stayed on the neat green sprig logo and the glossy theft beneath it, as if desire and evidence had to remain visible in the same room or neither of them would survive the night.

When June's bare foot found Leela's ankle again, it felt intimate and provisional in exactly the same measure. Leela lay awake listening to the vent work at the window and understood that wanting June had changed the room without making either of them safer.


# Chapter 10

The room had gotten too cold to stay fully asleep in. June had kicked the duvet halfway off sometime before dawn, and the strip of hotel sheet across Leela's thigh felt damp where sweat had dried after the sex and then cooled under the vent. When she moved, the skin along the outside of her hip reminded her about the carpet. From the bathroom came toothbrush noise, then running water, then June clearing her throat like she resented even that.

On the desk, both laptops were open where they'd gone dead in the middle of the night. June's still displayed the thumbnail pane of `ruderal_pitch_v3.pdf`, frozen at a slide with a blue-orange benchmark plot that Leela knew too well now to look at casually. It had felt almost cinematic yesterday, in the bad fluorescent hallway outside Ballroom C, to find the deck at all. This morning it looked uglier and more ordinary. It was a PDF with a date stamp, a thing that would either survive contact with policy or get rinsed down to misunderstanding.

The bathroom door opened. June came out in yesterday's T-shirt and hotel socks, her blunt-cut hair shoved back with wet fingers. There was a bright scrape on her collarbone that Leela knew she had made. June saw her see it and did not pull the shirt up.

"You're awake," June said.

Leela pushed herself up against the headboard. "Barely."

"Good." June picked up the room-service notepad from the nightstand, tore off the page with the breakfast menu printed on the back, and sat cross-legged at the foot of the bed. "Let's figure out whether this is real or whether he's just such a fucking parasite that everything feels criminal by default."

That was June's version of not mentioning last night.

Leela reached for her laptop. The plastic was cold. "I thought we already knew it was real."

"We know it feels real." June clicked the trackpad hard enough to make the loose hotel desk shiver. "I want the part that would still be real if Gloria looked at it with that dean face she does. The calm one. The one that means you're about to be turned into process."

Leela almost smiled, then didn't. The phrase had the right cruelty in it.

June enlarged slide eight until the caption fuzzed. The plot compared four model variants across municipal complaint datasets, the exact chart June had spent most of October trying to stabilize after the seminar disaster. Mohit had put it on a white background with Ruderal's green logo in the corner and a title that read `Reliability Under Distribution Shift for Public-Sector Decision Pipelines`. He had shaved off the caveats and the entire point of the work with one line of copy.

"This y-axis," June said. She tapped the screen with her nail. "I stopped calling it failure rate on October third. After the seminar. He said failure sounded defensive."

"You changed it to worst-case transfer error."

"Yeah." June opened Finder, then a folder called `baltimore_talk`, then a graveyard of versions. `v2`, `v2_fix`, `v4_REAL`, `v4_REAL2`, `v5_use_this`, `v5_use_this_final`, `v5_use_this_finalforreal`. The naming alone made Leela's chest hurt with recognition. June clicked through them until an earlier export appeared. Same plot, same colors, same heavy blue line. Same y-axis label as the deck.

"There." June rotated the screen toward her. "October second, 11:48 p.m. The old label. And see the gray band? I took that out after Omar said the bootstrap intervals made the variance look cleaner than it was."

Leela leaned in. June smelled like hotel soap over sweat and nicotine gum. The gray band was in Mohit's deck too, translucent enough to miss if you were not looking for it.

"He didn't recreate the figure," Leela said. "He lifted this version or something exported from this version."

"From my laptop?"

"Maybe from the shared drive. Maybe from an attachment. Maybe from somewhere older."

June looked at her. "Older how?"

Leela held the look for half a beat too long and turned back to the deck. She knew older how. She knew there was a private directory on Hades with cloned repositories and Ruderal nested under it like rot under paint. She also knew she had kept that knowledge to herself in September, then only handed Omar the softest corner of it in October, and that last night had not made her cleaner.

"Older than Baltimore," she said. "This didn't start at the conference."

June kept looking. Leela could feel it. Then June let it go, or parked it.

"Fine," June said. "Let's keep making him a timeline."

They moved slide by slide.

Slide eleven used one of Leela's robustness plots from the paper she had submitted in September. Mohit had retitled it `Adversarial Resilience With Minimal Retraining Cost`, which would have been funny if it were not her work and not a lie. The tell there was smaller: the legend still said `budgeted perturbation`, a phrase Mohit himself had later crossed out in red on a printout because he thought it sounded "too defensive, too academic." In the submitted paper the legend read `structured shift budget`. In the deck, the old wording remained.

June noticed it a second after Leela did. "That yours?"

"Yeah."

"Can you date it?"

Leela clicked into the folder she'd synced locally before Research Integrity had frozen her access. Her cache was incomplete. A few filenames had the small cloud icon that meant they now lived somewhere Haviland controlled and she did not. Others had actually made it onto the laptop. She found the September draft, then an August export she had forgotten existed. There it was: the same legend, and below it the same small asymmetry in the orange line where a failed rerun had left one point lower than the rest. She had flattened that later. Mohit's deck had the unflattened point.

"August twenty-ninth," she said.

June let out a short, incredulous breath. "He didn't even steal the cleaned-up version."

"No. He stole the one before it would've survived questions."

"That is so on brand it almost counts as a signature."

Leela clicked `Get Info` on the PDF itself. She had not done it yesterday because by the time they'd found the deck they had both been too angry to manage the parts of anger that required menus. The properties window opened.

Created: November 12, 2025, 2:14 a.m.  
Modified: November 12, 2025, 7:06 a.m.  
Author: Mohit Saran.

June read over her shoulder. "He skipped my prep slot because he was up till two building this thing."

"Or editing it."

"He left me out there to take that panel question by myself because he was polishing seed-copy."

The hotel's vent hit the same register as the panel-room mic, and the room snapped back into Leela's ears: the squeal, the polite throat-clearing before the question, June saying thank you with her teeth set hard enough to count as a bite. Yesterday she had thought the worst part of Baltimore was the talk landing flat after all the work June had put into recovering from October. This was somehow worse. It converted rudeness into allocation. Mohit had been elsewhere on purpose.

June reached past her and scrolled to the title slide. `RUDERAL ANALYTICS`. White type. Green weed-shoot logo, tasteful enough to look expensive. Underneath, smaller: `Decision infrastructure that survives the real world.`

"He thinks he's clever," June said. "Ruderal. Weed that grows through concrete. Real fucking subtle."

Leela clicked properties again, searching for something more than the author field. There was nothing useful beyond the creation data: Keynote, Mohit's name, and not enough.

"It's not enough by itself," she said.

June turned. "What isn't?"

"This." Leela touched the deck, then June's version folders, then her own August plot. "It proves he used earlier drafts. It proves he presented unpublished figures. It doesn't yet prove what he says the deck was for."

"It's a startup deck."

"Yes. To us."

June held her gaze until Leela felt her shoulders tighten against the headboard, then laughed once without humor. "Jesus. You sound like a judge."

"I sound like somebody who's already been in a room where the logs were real and it still didn't matter because the institution had a nicer noun for what happened."

She had not said the worst of it, but the sentence stripped the flesh off the rest.

June's face changed a little. The meaner edge of her posture loosened without softening. "Okay," she said. "Then say it clean."

Leela made herself do it. "If we send one PDF around today, he says he was socializing translational possibilities for lab work. He says Haviland owns the IP. He says faculty show unpublished results to potential partners all the time. He says the labels are early because he grabbed draft slides in a rush, which is sloppy but not misconduct. And if Ruderal isn't visibly his, he gets to make us sound hysterical for the part we know is true."

"It's visibly his. His name is in the file."

"On the file metadata."

"Which is his file."

"On his laptop. That part helps. It doesn't finish it."

June threw the pen onto the notepad. It bounced once. "You always want the evidence one document cleaner than the life we're actually in."

The sentence landed where June meant it to. Leela looked down at the deck because looking at June would mean picking one injury to answer and there were several lined up now, some older than Baltimore, some from last night, some from the seminar in October when one sentence from her might have changed the room and she had let Mohit's arrangement stand because her own paper was already dirty.

"I want something that doesn't die the second he talks over it," she said.

"Survivable for who?"

Leela opened her mouth, shut it, and then chose accuracy over dignity. "For me too, obviously. I'm not pretending otherwise. But also for you. If this turns into us forwarding a deck around because we're furious, he'll fold it into the story he already tells about you being unstable after the seminar."

June's jaw locked. Color climbed under the scrape on her collarbone. Leela hated herself immediately. The hit had been too clean, too accurate, too mean.

June said, very flat, "I know what story he tells."

Silence held for a moment with the vent whirring over it and distant elevator chimes moving up the hotel shaft. It would have been easy to let the conversation curdle there into the private thing, into whose body had done what on the carpet and whether last night had been desire or anger or both and whether either of them could afford one more relationship that doubled as evidence against judgment. But the deck sat between them, visible, stupid, undeniable.

Leela said, "I think he stole them."

June looked back at her.

"I think he stole them," Leela repeated. "I just think we need the part that says from where, and when, and as what."

June breathed out through her nose. "Okay." She dragged the notepad back. "Then we make a list."

She wrote in block letters that looked almost architectural when she was pissed:

`1. SOURCE FILES / EXPORT DATES`  
`2. PDF METADATA / VERSION HISTORY`  
`3. WHAT THE FUCK RUDERAL ACTUALLY IS`  
`4. KEEP OFF Haviland systems`

Under the fourth line she drew so hard the pen scored the paper.

Leela said, "We should probably not talk about it on lab Slack."

"We should probably not say the word out loud anywhere in Keating," June said. "Honestly we should maybe not say anything in that building that we don't want attached to a faculty meeting agenda."

The room had gotten brighter without warming. November Baltimore light, thin and metallic, sat on the dresser mirror and the empty plastic water cups and the heap of June's clothes by the desk chair. June reached for the coffee Leela had let go cold, made a face, and drank it anyway.

"Do we tell Omar?" she asked.

Leela thought of Omar's careful face, of how badly he needed any information that might explain Mohit's shifting cruelty, of how clean it would feel to widen the circle, and of how every widened circle in this lab had become leverage for someone else.

"Not yet," she said. "Not until we know what we're saying."

June nodded once, not agreeing so much as storing the decision for later argument. Then she stood, stretched, and winced very slightly when her shoulder rolled back.

Leela had left a bruise there too.

June caught her looking and said, "Don't start being gentle now. We have checkout in forty minutes."

The line came barbed, but it sounded more like June than the silence had. Leela took it.

By the time they left the room, the notepad page was folded into June's back pocket and the deck existed in three places that were not Mohit's laptop. The outline of a plan had started to harden. Panic had finally been forced into sequence.

* * *

Lakebridge had turned from bright November to damp November while they were gone. The sidewalks between the station and campus held a skin of old rain that kept trying to become ice but had not committed. By Monday afternoon, Keating carried damp coats, radiator heat, and the basement's usual dust-baked breath. Students had lined boots under radiators that never quite dried them. Somewhere below, the server closet whined through the floor.

Leela found June in the ground-floor coffee alcove, under the bulletin board where half the flyers had curled off the cork from humidity. Ben was already there.

He stood by the industrial urn in a cream parka that probably cost half a month of Leela's rent, stirring cinnamon into a jar of overnight oats gone gray from chia seeds. He had managed to snap the bowl off the plastic spoon and was using the handle like a dowel, concentrating on the task with the earnest seriousness of someone who still believed adulthood might be a series of correct purchases.

When he saw them, he brightened too fast. "Hey. Welcome back. I was actually going to email, which is annoying, I know, but Mohit wanted everybody from Baltimore to dump materials into the shared drive today just so the external versions are aligned. Not, like, urgent urgent. Just cleaner."

June took a paper cup from the stack without looking at him. "Aligned with what?"

Ben's eyes flickered once. "Just, you know, if anybody has handouts or sponsor-facing things or slide exports. There was some confusion after the side meetings."

"The side meetings you weren't at?" June said.

He laughed a little, the sound of someone checking whether laughter still counted as charm. "No, right, obviously. I just mean that's what he said. About avoiding version noise."

Leela poured coffee. The coffee was burnt, bitter, and thin. Ben always made her feel the full age gap in strange directions: sometimes older than he was, sometimes much younger, like he had skipped straight from being the kind of undergraduate professors remembered fondly into performing the grad student he thought faculty wanted. His hair was still damp at the temples from the cold outside. A pharmacy receipt stuck out of his pocket with the top line folded over and over by his thumb.

June said, "We'll manage our own files."

"Totally." Ben nodded too many times. "Totally. I just figured I should flag it because he sounded pretty specific about provenance, and if there are, like, multiple unofficial exports floating around, people can get weird."

There was that word again: `Provenance`. Mohit had it now too.

Ben went back to his oats with furious concentration, stirring the gray chia sludge until the spoon handle clicked the jar. June blew across the coffee and said, quietly enough that only Leela heard, "That's new."

Leela did not answer until they were in the stairwell, the concrete walls amplifying their footsteps. "The word?"

"The whole sentence. He sounds like a junior admin memo."

"He always sounded like that."

"Not at eight in the morning with breakfast slime in a jar."

On the sixth floor the graduate office was half full and therefore unusable. Two first-years from another group were arguing about package versions at the long table. Someone had opened the lake-facing window a crack and then regretted it, so the room held both radiator heat and hard outside air at once. Leela led June past it to the narrower alcove by the printer, where the lake came through the glass in flat steel strips between buildings.

"This is still too public," June said.

"It's two minutes."

"That's how people get caught in documentaries."

"We're not in a documentary."

June looked at the cubicle farm, the half-dead fern on the filing cabinet, the whiteboard with three layers of erased marker staining through one another. "We're in a worse thing. We're in a university."

Leela opened her laptop anyway.

They searched `Ruderal Analytics` first and got a landscaping company in Ohio, a weed-management startup in Colorado, and a venture blog using the word metaphorically. June muttered through all of it, impatient enough that the trackpad clicks started sounding like a small mechanical weapon. Then Leela narrowed the search with Lakebridge, then New York business records, then Mohit's full name. On the third try the result appeared.

`RUDERAL ANALYTICS LLC`  
Domestic limited liability company  
Filed: August 14, 2025  
Principal office: Water Street, Lakebridge  
Managing member: Mohit Saran

The office address was a coworking suite above a wine bar Leela had passed a dozen times without remembering it. The filing agent used a service address in Albany. There was no Haviland affiliation anywhere on the page. That omission did more than a logo could have done. The page stripped away every academic euphemism: no translational outreach, no lab-side exploratory shell, just Mohit naming himself and filing the lie where the state would treat it as a structure.

June read the page once, then again. "August," she said.

Leela nodded.

"Before the semester."

"Yeah."

"Before he told all of us the lab had to toughen up because the center bid died."

"Yeah."

June put her hand flat on the desk and leaned into it, not dramatic, just containing force. "So he reorganized the whole lab after he already had a company."

Leela heard the sentence click several prior chapters into alignment inside her own head: the summer loss, the adversarial pairings, the contradictory promises, the abrupt obsession with "robustness" not as research question but as brand. She had known pieces. Knowing the filing date gave the pieces edges.

"He didn't pivot because things got hard," June said. "He pivoted because he already had somewhere else to take the work."

Before Leela could answer, a shadow crossed the printer glass. Ben leaned around the divider holding a pastry box from somewhere far nicer than campus coffee.

"Oh, sorry," he said, already continuing. "I didn't mean to interrupt. The faculty candidate breakfast had leftovers, and I thought people up here might want them before they get murdered by air." He lifted the box a little. Inside, three croissants and something glazed sat on parchment paper. "Also Mohit asked if either of you still had the November sponsor deck, because he wants to make sure nothing from Baltimore is being read out of context."

June's head came up so sharply it made Ben take one step back.

"The what?" she said.

Ben blinked. "The sponsor deck. Or side-meeting deck. I don't know what he called it exactly. He just said there were exploratory materials and he didn't want weird circulation."

He had heard something, or seen something, or Mohit had sent him to sweep the hallway for heat. It did not matter which.

Leela said, carefully, "Why would he ask you that?"

Ben opened and closed the pastry box once, buying himself time with the rustle of parchment. "He didn't, like, deputize me. He just mentioned it when I dropped off the visitor badges. I thought if you had it and there was some confusion, better to clean it up early than let it become a whole thing."

June laughed, low and ugly. "You thought that."

Ben's face changed. The performance fell a fraction and the twenty-four under it showed. "I'm trying to be useful," he said, then grimaced slightly, hearing the sentence land in Mohit's voice instead of his own. "I'm trying not to have everyone act like I'm radioactive every time I open my mouth."

There it was, human and not strategic. The admission sounded needier than slick. He wanted in. He wanted the room to stop punishing him for trying to belong to the wrong person too visibly. In that naked bid for approval, Leela saw how Mohit must have built the habit in him: praise one errand, one forwarded screenshot, one careful hallway update at a time, until reporting ceased to feel like reporting and started feeling like competence.

June did not soften. "Then stop carrying his weather for him."

Ben stared. "What?"

"Nothing," Leela said before June could keep going. "We're working."

Ben lingered there, hurt and uncertain and still somehow eager to recover. "Okay," he said. "Anyway. Pastries are here if you want them."

He set the box on the filing cabinet and left with the same quick, front-loaded walk he brought to every errand, a gait that made him seem always a little late to the adulthood he was auditioning for.

June waited until his footsteps had vanished into the office noise. "He said November sponsor deck."

Leela nodded.

"I never said sponsor. I never said November. Did you?"

"No."

"Then how did he know it wasn't just random Baltimore trash?"

Leela closed the business-records tab and opened nothing in its place. Her coffee had gone lukewarm again. Below them a stairwell door banged and the office printer started its dry mechanical coughing. The whole building sounded exposed.

"He heard enough," she said. "Or Mohit asked enough."

"Same outcome."

Leela's phone buzzed on the desk.

The sound stopped both of them cold.

Then she turned it over.

It was Mohit.

`Good to have you both back. Quick reminder that exploratory external materials tied to ongoing lab work are not for circulation outside approved channels. Easy for context to get lost. Happy to discuss later today if helpful.`

June read it over her shoulder. "There. Done. He felt the temperature change."

Leela looked past the screen to the pastry box Ben had left behind. One croissant had shed a confetti of flakes onto the wax paper. The glaze on the danish had already started to set cloudy in the office air.

"He's not subtle enough to be a mole," June said.

"No."

"He's an app notification with shoes."

Despite everything, Leela almost laughed. June pressed on before the laugh could land and drain the room.

"We can't talk in here anymore," she said. "Not this, not Omar, not anything remotely like a noun."

Leela nodded. Her body had already agreed before her mind finished the sentence. The sixth floor, the basement lab, the coffee alcove, the hallway outside Mohit's office where people used to debrief after meetings like they were still in one shared story: all of it had shifted in a single afternoon from shabby institutional space to instrument.

"We also can't use university storage," she said. "If he starts asking about circulation, he's already thinking about access logs."

June's eyes cut to her. "You think he can see that much?"

Leela thought of the Hades audit trails, of what system administrators could see, of the fact that she no longer had privileges and Mohit still did. "I think we should behave like he can."

"Great." June picked up the notepad page she had tucked into her laptop sleeve at the hotel and flattened it on the desk between them. "Rules, then."

Her pen moved fast.

`No lab Slack.`  
`No Haviland drive.`  
`No talking in Keating unless it's fake.`  
`Tell Omar only in person, off campus.`  
`Keep copies on personal machines.`  
`Assume Ben reports vibe, if not content.`

Under that she wrote, in smaller letters: `Find prior deck versions if they exist.`

Leela said, "We should keep a timeline too. Filing date. Deck creation. Slide export dates. The conference schedule. His missed prep slot."

"Yeah." June handed her the pen. "You do the depressing part."

Leela wrote:

`08/14/25 Ruderal filed`  
`10/02/25 June fig export w/ old label`  
`08/29/25 Leela plot export w/ old legend`  
`11/12/25 deck created 2:14 a.m.`  
`11/12/25 Mohit misses prep / off-program meeting`

The act of writing it by hand, on cheap hotel paper in a sixth-floor alcove that smelled faintly of toner and radiator dust, made the dates feel less like impressions and more like a structure one could lean weight against.

June looked down at the list. "He didn't get sloppy," she said. "He got greedy."

"Greedy looks sloppy after enough timestamps."

June folded the page once, then again. "We should tell Omar tonight."

"At your place?"

"Not mine first. Too obvious. The noodle place on Alder, then upstairs if needed."

Leela nodded. The plan made immediate sense because it assumed the old habits were dead. They would not loop Omar in casually between machines or do hallway check-ins after lab meeting until the right people happened to be around. Everything that had once made the lab feel humane had become a transmission vector.

June snapped her laptop shut. "Come on."

"Where?"

"Outside. Before Professor Helpful comes back with a fresh bulletin."

They took the stairs instead of the elevator. On the landing between third and second, Leela heard voices below and June stopped with one hand on the rail, listening. The building gave them its usual layered sounds: pipe knock, distant laughter, the metal hush of the HVAC turning over. None of it was silence anymore. It was only coverage.

They exited through the side door near the loading dock where the smokers stood in all weather. No one was there now. The air hit hard and damp. Across the alley the brick wall of the next building sweated dark streaks. June tucked her hands into the pockets of her coat and looked back at Keating with plain distrust now. The shabby basement that had once sold itself as communal looked wired.

"I keep thinking about how normal this would've looked a year ago," she said. "Ben hovering, Mohit doing side meetings, deck versions everywhere. I would've just called it bad boundaries and gone home."

Leela zipped her coat higher. "It was bad boundaries."

"Yeah." June looked at her. "And now it's evidence."

What stood between them now was a shared grammar, new and ugly and usable. It had not existed in Baltimore forty-eight hours earlier, when the deck was still a disgusting discovery and the sex had been a different kind of answer to a different kind of question.

Leela said, "If we do this, we do it on purpose. No blurting. No hoping the right person will happen to care."

June's mouth tightened in something that was almost approval. "I know."

Leela waited a beat, then said the harder part. "And if Ben asks, we don't punish him with honesty until we know what we need from him."

June looked back toward the building. "That's a very ugly sentence."

"It's true."

"It's also true that I want to shake him until his fillings fall out."

"I know."

June breathed steam into the wet cold and rubbed it away with the back of her glove. "Fine. We treat him like a leak, not a villain."

"Yeah."

"And Mohit?"

Leela thought of the August filing date, the 2:14 a.m. timestamp, the old exports, and the polite text on her phone that had arrived minutes after Ben's interruption, already dressed in institutional language about context and approved channels. She thought of the rooms Mohit preferred: the office, the conference suite, the committee table, every place where composure could do violence without leaving visible marks. She thought too of the thing she still had not told June, the older proof sitting in the dark behind this newer one like a second foundation.

"Mohit," she said, "we treat him like somebody who's already started building his explanation."

June considered that and nodded. "Okay."

For the first time all day, the answer did not feel like one more postponement. It felt like the beginning of method.

They stood there another moment in the alley cold, not touching. Traffic hissed on the wet street beyond the engineering buildings. Somewhere inside Keating a door alarm chirped and shut off. June pulled the folded note from her pocket, looked at it once more, and then tucked it into the inside compartment of her wallet behind an old MetroCard and three receipts.

"Tonight at seven," she said. "Text me only if you get delayed. Nothing else."

Leela said, "Okay."

June started walking toward the immigrant-commercial strip without waiting to see if Leela followed, confident now in a way that had nothing to do with optimism. It was simply direction. Leela fell in beside her after a step, matching pace.

Behind them, Keating kept humming, fluorescent and server-hot and falsely neutral. In her coat pocket Leela's phone pressed against her palm, warm from Mohit's text and the body heat she had given it. Ahead lay dinner, Omar, dates, copies, whatever name resistance took before it was strong enough to survive daylight. Beneath all of it, unchanged, sat the earlier secret she had never fully surrendered. She could feel its shape even as she moved.

But now there was a list. There were timestamps. There was a filed company with Mohit's name on it and a junior student in an expensive parka carrying messages he did not understand were messages. The paranoia had objects. That did not make the thing smaller. It made it usable.


# Chapter 11

By the second week of January, the Civic Signals Lab smelled of wet wool, hot dust, and the wholesale-store onion bagels Mohit bought whenever he wanted management to pass for care. Nothing they had started before break had changed who got to stand at the front of the room. The basement had the same fluorescent hum it had carried all fall, the same server-closet heat leaking under the metal door, the same whiteboards furred over with old marker ghosts, but the semester had changed its pressure anyway. People's coats stayed on longer. Nobody sat on the salvaged couch until the room told them what kind of meeting it was going to be.

Leela arrived with slush drying in white veins on her boots and found Mohit setting out a paper box of bagels beside a stack of napkins so thin they tore if you looked at them too hard. He had his expensive glasses off and was polishing them with the hem of a dish towel he had probably borrowed from the faculty kitchenette upstairs. He never used the basement paper cups if he could help it. He had brought a real ceramic mug down with him, dark green with the university crest, as if even coffee needed hierarchy.

June was already there, sitting backward on a chair, coat still zipped, one ankle hooked through the rungs. She had trimmed her blunt hair again over break and missed by half a centimeter on the left side, a flaw that only made it look more like June had done it on purpose. She worried a square of nicotine gum out of a pharmacy blister with her thumbnail and glanced at the bagels like they had personally lied to her.

Ben came in behind Leela carrying a cardboard tray of coffees nobody had asked him to get. Snowmelt ran off the hem of his expensive technical jacket. He set the tray down, then crouched to rub a salt line off one careful sneaker with the corner of a receipt before he took his seat, doing it quickly, as if the room might forgive vanity if it looked like hygiene.

Omar arrived last, apologizing though he was only two minutes behind Mohit's calendar invite. He wore a charcoal sweater over a collared shirt crisp enough to look ironed, and he had brought a clementine in his coat pocket. He peeled it as he sat, the rind coming off in one long bright strip that he laid beside his notebook in a neat coil. Fear, on him, usually started in the jaw. Today it had not started yet, but Leela could see where it would go.

Mohit waited until everyone had something in front of them, coffee, gum, notebook, laptop, a cheap paper plate with nothing on it, and then smiled in the managerial way that made warmth sound expense-accountable.

"Happy spring term," he said. "I know nobody believes in that phrase yet, but let's pretend for fifteen minutes."

June bit into the gum. "You brought onions this early. That's not optimism."

A small laugh moved through the room and died quickly. Mohit nodded as if he had permitted exactly the right amount of levity.

"I want to start with realities," he said. "The center bridge money we were hoping to have did not materialize. The department travel pool was tightened over break. Sponsor reimbursement is moving, but not on a timeline I can responsibly promise against. So I'm not going to sell you a fantasy about abundance."

He said abundance like it was a childish idea they had all outgrown. Leela looked at the bagel box. The paper lid had gone translucent in circles where the oil had soaked through.

Mohit rested both hands on the back of an empty chair. "This spring, the lab has money for one student travel slot. One fully supported trip. Registration, hotel, airfare, the ordinary things that suddenly become extraordinary when nobody wants to pay for them. And for students thinking seriously about dissertation timing, I can actively shepherd one priority defense path through committee season. One."

Nobody moved. The fluorescent tubes hissed overhead. In the server closet something gave a higher, thinner whine, then settled.

Ben was the first to speak. He did it too fast, tripping over his own eagerness. "When you say travel slot, do you mean only accepted full papers, or would a workshop count if it was, like, strategically useful?"

Mohit turned to him with a softer face, the one he used when rewarding juniors for saying the question he wanted in the room. "Good. Useful distinction. I mean visibility that compounds. I'm less interested in tourism than signal."

June snorted. "Jesus Christ."

Mohit glanced at her. "Do you want to contribute something more legible than that?"

"I'm just admiring the branding," June said. "One slot, one path. Sounds like a game show for people with bad health insurance."

Ben stared down into his coffee. Omar kept his eyes on the clementine peel.

Mohit did not rise to it. He rarely had to. "You can call it branding if that helps. I call it refusing to lie to you. Scarcity isn't a personal whim. It's the environment. The professional question is what you do inside it."

That was the part that landed, not the one slot or even the defense path, but the little sermon around them. Mohit was good at taking a structure that would have hurt anyway and polishing it until resistance looked naive.

Leela felt the wrist brace on her right hand catch against the seam of her sweater. She had fronted conference costs on her Hudson Federal card before and watched them survive longer than the conferences themselves. Conference travel meant hotel deposits, flight change fees, and dinners that were supposed to be reimbursed and never quite were. The interest had its own memory. The defense path mattered even more: it meant a date, a room, a committee that showed up prepared instead of vaguely hostile, a future in which Lakebridge rent ended on schedule instead of stretching into another year of cheap blinds and radiator insomnia. Until Mohit said one, she had let herself keep using the plural.

Omar cleared his throat. "What counts as strongest public story by your standard? Existing acceptances, or what can be submitted in the next cycle?"

He kept his voice courteous and almost formal, but Leela heard the strain in the careful transitions, each clause laid down with more control than he should have needed for an ordinary question.

"A mix," Mohit said. "Momentum matters. External legibility matters. So does lab citizenship. The people I can argue for are the people whose work looks both strong and socially stabilizing."

June laughed once, without humor. "Socially stabilizing."

Mohit kept going. "We had a messy fall. Some of that was real, some of it was noise, but committees don't reward mess. Sponsors don't reward mess. I need to be able to tell a coherent story about this group."

The word group lay in the room like an accusation nobody could quote back to him later.

Leela asked, "What exactly is a defense path?"

She hated how dry her voice sounded, as if she were asking about a software dependency instead of the next year of her life.

"It means I spend political capital instead of goodwill," Mohit said. "Pre-meetings. Committee management. Timely document movement. Making sure the person in question is not trying to fight six institutional headwinds alone."

His gaze flicked to her and away. The look lasted less than a second and carried exactly the amount of meaning he could later deny.

June said, "So the rest of us get to enjoy the informal path."

"The rest of you get honesty," Mohit said. "Which is more than many labs provide."

Leela thought of the Baltimore hotel room, of June standing over a stolen deck with their figures in it, both of them too angry to sit. Honesty was not the word for it; the word made her want to laugh and then choke on it.

Ben, still trying to recover usefulness, said, "Is there a timeline for deciding? Just so people can plan deliverables."

Mohit nodded approvingly. "By February. Earlier if someone gives me a reason not to wait. And before anyone says fairness, let me be clear: I'm not pitting you against each other. The institution already did that. I'm refusing to hide it under fake family language."

That nearly would have been persuasive if Leela had not seen the pleasure he took in the cleaned-up room. Scarcity might have been real. He was real too.

He turned to Omar. "Your robustness note is closest to travel-legible, if we can tighten the comparison story and get the claims into a form reviewers won't bounce for sloppiness. There is interest there. The sponsor conversations in Baltimore made that very clear."

Omar gave a small nod that was not gratitude so much as submission dressed up to pass inspection.

Mohit looked at June next. "You need recovery and visible steadiness. Not a dramatic swing. Clean benchmark work, clean language, no more avoidable spectacle."

June unwrapped a second piece of gum and put it in her mouth on top of the first. "Love the pastoral tone."

"You can sneer or you can listen," Mohit said. His voice stayed gentle, which made the line hit harder. "I would recommend listening."

Then to Leela. "For you, the question is whether spring reads as consolidation or drift. The Research Integrity noise does not help, even if it remains noise. So I need boring documents and finished prose, not more cleverness."

The room did not get quieter. It got more exact. Ben looked fixedly at his laptop. Omar's fingers paused on the edge of his notebook. June stopped chewing for one beat and then started again.

Leela said, "There hasn't been a finding."

"I didn't say there had," Mohit replied. "I'm talking about committee psychology. You know that."

That was the trap with him. He could say something punitive and make you sound foolish if you objected to the exact wording instead of the use.

He spent the next ten minutes turning the knife by calling it planning. Ben would help with administrative cleanup and literature synthesis. June would stabilize the benchmark branch and produce something sponsor-safe. Leela would get him a revised dissertation chapter outline by Monday and keep herself available for "cross-paper support," a phrase that meant labor without ownership. Omar would prioritize the robustness note. Mohit kept returning to the same committee-room vocabulary until every sentence tasted like printer toner. Nobody touched the bagels until the meeting ended, and then Ben, perhaps because he was still young enough to be hungry through humiliation, tore one in half and ate it standing up.

Leela stayed seated after Mohit dismissed them. The chair under her felt one screw away from collapse. June pushed herself up first.

"One slot," she said, mostly to the room, mostly to herself. "Nothing says collaborative science like a lifeboat drill."

Omar put the clementine segments carefully into a line on the paper plate he still had not used. "He's not wrong about the budget."

June swung her coat on. "No. That's what makes it art."

She left before anyone could answer. Ben followed two minutes later with the tray of empty coffee cups, moving quickly, like he could still win the morning back by being useful in a hallway.

That left Leela and Omar for a few seconds in the stale onion air. Omar gathered the peel into his fist.

"Do you think he already knows?" he asked.

"About what?"

"Who gets it."

Leela looked at him. His beard line was as careful as ever, but his jaw had started at last, a small jump near the hinge every few seconds. "I think he wants us to act like he doesn't."

Omar let out a breath through his nose. It might have been a laugh if the room had been kinder. "Yes," he said. "That sounds right."

He carried his plate and peel to the trash, missed the bagel box on his way out, and came back for it, embarrassed by the miss in a way Leela would not have found embarrassing if she had not already been watching him so closely.

She gathered her laptop slowly. The semester had begun with a vocabulary lesson: there was one slot and one path, and everything else came wrapped in talk about realism and adulthood. By the time she left the basement, she could already feel the phrases settling over the lab's uglier facts like thin snow over old salt.

Outside the basement, Keating's hallways had the overbright vacancy of the first days after break, bulletin boards full of event flyers nobody would attend, industrial carpet still damp at the edges where people had tracked slush in. The sixth-floor office window faced the lake, which had gone from black water to a hard gray plate ringed with dirty snow. The view looked expensive, almost serene, and no one in the room had time to enjoy it properly.

Leela spent an hour opening and closing the revised dissertation chapter file without revising anything. Every paragraph she touched turned back into Mohit's list: boring documents, finished prose, no more cleverness. Her own paper from September sat in her citation folder like a bruise she had decided was healed because looking at it directly would require admitting it was still there. She had let a robustness sentence survive that should have died. It was not a fabricated result, not exactly, but a clean line sanded out of a dirtier truth. She had called it acceptable because the deadline was real, because everyone else's compromises arrived in professional fonts too, because the lab had already changed the meaning of ordinary wrongdoing. The self-exonerating story still worked on her some days. On others it sounded cheap.

At three-thirty, her phone buzzed with a calendar reminder she had set in November: pay credit card minimum by the eighteenth. She dismissed it without opening the balance. The amount existed whether she looked at it or not.

Across from her, Omar had started building figures.

He had moved his monitor to the edge of the desk so he could sit closer, shoulders tucked, one hand on the mouse, the other resting against his mouth. He had changed out of his sweater into a plain blue button-down sometime after lunch. It was rolled carefully at the forearms, not messily. Even under pressure he dressed as if sloppiness might become evidence. A mug of mint tea cooled beside his keyboard. The tea bag string was wrapped around the handle three times so it would not trail in his way.

For an hour he said almost nothing. Files opened and closed. A plot rendered, vanished, rendered again. He wrote two lines in his notebook, crossed them out so cleanly the paper almost tore, and wrote two more. At four-ten his phone lit up with an email preview before he flipped it face down.

`International Students Office: Spring funding verification reminder`

Leela saw only the subject line and wished she had not. Omar did not look up. He dragged a CSV into a plotting script, waited, saw the figure appear, and then said, very quietly, "Fuck."

It was such a rare word from him that it worked like a dropped beaker. Leela turned.

He looked at her for a second, embarrassed to have been heard, then back to the screen. "Sorry."

"What happened?"

"The interesting thing did not happen," he said.

On his monitor a bar chart sat in June's plotting template, clean colors, mean scores, little error lines. Omar's method was supposed to beat the benchmark on the corrupted-slice evaluation that Mohit had singled out. Instead the benchmark sat fractionally above it, ugly in the way small losses always were, too slight for tragedy, too clear for comfort.

"You lost by three tenths," Leela said.

"By averaged seed, yes."

He said averaged like it had personally wronged him. She got up and came around his desk before deciding whether that was kindness or interference.

June had built the plotting template months ago and hard-coded a provenance footer into the bottom margin after a week of mislabeled figures. It was tiny gray text, almost invisible unless you were looking for it. Leela was looking for it before she consciously knew she was. At the bottom of Omar's plot, in eight-point font, was the source path.

`/benchmarks/corrupt/baseline_5seed_mean.csv`

Omar clicked back to the terminal and opened another file. A new chart came up in the same colors with different numbers. The benchmark dropped six points like it had fallen downstairs.

The footer changed.

`/benchmarks/corrupt/baseline_oldtok_smoke_seed2.csv`

Leela said, "That's June's smoke run."

Omar kept his eyes on the screen. "It's the only old-tokenizer baseline with the corruption logs still aligned."

"It trained for half the schedule."

"Yes."

"It was a debugging run."

"Yes."

She waited for an explanation that might restore the ordinary world. He gave her the technical version instead.

"The five-seed average is on the new tokenizer. My adapter was tuned on the old one because the full port broke the augmentation hooks in December. If I compare old tokenizer to new tokenizer, the methods section becomes a referendum on preprocessing instead of the actual result."

Leela leaned one hip against the desk. She could smell the mint tea going tannic. "If you compare against the smoke run and call it the baseline, the methods section becomes a lie."

His hand tightened on the mouse until the knuckles blanched. "I didn't say I would call it the baseline."

"You said nothing yet."

"Exactly."

For a moment neither of them spoke. Down the row, somebody in another cubicle laughed at a video and then immediately lowered the volume. A heating pipe knocked once behind the wall.

Omar opened the figure caption draft. The current line read:

`Comparison against reproduced benchmark on corruption slice.`

He highlighted reproduced and stared at it without deleting.

Leela said, "You can still send Mohit the wash."

He gave a short, incredulous breath. "The wash."

"What would you call it?"

"I would call it accurate."

He looked at her then, finally, all the polish gone for three naked seconds. "And then what? He just held a meeting about one slot and one path. You heard him as well as I did."

"I did."

"If this becomes a wash, it dies. If it dies, I become the person who needed special handling for a year and still couldn't produce a clean public result. That does not stay in the paper column for me. It leaks into funding, timing, every conversation where somebody decides whether delay is a small inconvenience or an actual problem."

He stopped, then started again with more control. "I'm not saying that makes this noble. I'm saying you know exactly how the lab translates softness."

The email subject line still glowed faintly through his phone screen where it lay face down: Spring funding verification reminder. The language was neutral, the voice administrative, and the sentence carried the kind of power that could cost a person the right to remain ordinary.

Leela thought of Mohit's look when he said timely document movement, of her own September paper and the sentence she had let live because the model had looked good in every way except the one that would have ruined the abstract, and of the word reproduced sitting under Omar's cursor like a dare.

"You could rerun the full matched baseline," she said, because it was the clean answer and because she needed to hear whether he would take it.

Omar laughed without amusement. "On what time, on what compute, and with whose goodwill when the queue is already a week deep? By the time it finishes, the story is stale. He knows that. I know that. You know that."

That was also true. The lab had enough machines for ambition and not enough for innocence.

He deleted reproduced at last. The blank space in the caption looked worse than the word had.

Leela said nothing. She could still say smoke run. She could still say do not submit this. She could still send June a screenshot and force the matter into daylight. She imagined each consequence in order. Omar losing the first public chance Mohit had dangled all semester. Mohit calling her obstructive and unstable, the senior student who mistook methodological purity for politics. June maybe backing her, maybe not, and even if she did, what then. Leela would not get a defense date by being principled on someone else's bar chart. The sentence in her own paper sat there too, waiting to be remembered by the wrong person at the right time.

Omar saved the new figure into a folder named `jan_review_clean`.

The word clean made something cold pass through her, not in her body, in the room, like a draft from a cracked window in a building that had none.

He said, more quietly, "If you want to tell him not to use it, tell him."

He did not say please or help me. The cruelty of the moment was that he offered her the power honestly, because both of them knew what using it would mean.

Leela looked back at her own desk, at the dissertation chapter file still open to a paragraph that had not improved all day. She thought of her mother's transfer from November, already dissolved into rent, utilities, and minimums; of the stupid travel slot and the less stupid defense path; and of Mohit's talent for making every moral choice arrive already indexed to somebody else's institutional vulnerability.

"I've got my own work," she said.

It was not an answer to the question he had asked. It was worse. It let the question stand and refused to carry it.

Omar's face changed by almost nothing. He nodded once. "Of course."

She went back to her desk. Behind her, he typed. The keys sounded faster now. After a minute he opened the methods file and began filling the blank where reproduced had been.

June came in around six with a paper sack of fries going soft in grease and cold air. She tossed half the sack onto Leela's desk without asking and stood at Omar's shoulder long enough to clock that he was still on the robustness note.

"Still courting reviewers?" she said.

"Something like that," Omar replied.

She looked at the figure on his screen, then at the bag of fries, deciding which deserved her energy. "Don't use the default blue on the bars. It looks like a bank ad."

Omar changed the color. June took two fries, winced at the temperature, and went downstairs to run a benchmark job before Leela could decide whether relief counted as cowardice.

By eight-thirty the office had thinned to the people who could not afford to leave. Snow along the window ledge reflected parking-lot light back into the glass. Ben came up once to ask if anybody wanted vending-machine pretzels, too anxious to eat his own offer. Omar said no. Leela said nothing. Ben hovered long enough to notice the charged quiet, then retreated with his jacket crackling around him.

At ten, Omar sent the draft to Mohit.

Leela saw the subject line come across the lab thread two minutes later.

`Updated comparison story for morning review`

There was no emoji, no apology, no qualifier, just story. She stared at the word until it stopped looking like English.

The morning review happened back in the basement because Mohit liked the lab more when everyone was forced into the same air. Snowmelt had dried into dirty crescents on the concrete by the door. Somebody had wiped the conference table badly enough that it smelled faintly of lemon cleaner over the usual coffee and fan heat. The bagel box was gone. In its place sat a plastic tub of mini muffins from a grocery store bakery, each one in a paper cup the color of old bruises.

Mohit stood at the far end of the table with his ceramic mug and an expression of brisk gratitude, as if the lab had rallied in exactly the way he had hoped. Ben had already connected the monitor and was clicking too quickly through windows, apologizing every time the wrong one appeared. His hair was still damp from a shower. He had either woken up early to perform competence or had not slept at all.

June came in last with a knit cap pulled low and a strip of nicotine gum tucked into the sleeve of her coat. She took one muffin, peeled the paper off it in perfect clean strips, and did not eat it.

"Let's keep this tight," Mohit said. "I want to lock the narrative before the week fragments."

Leela sat halfway down the table, close enough to the monitor to read the small things, far enough from Mohit to avoid having to watch his face continuously. Omar plugged in his laptop. His shirt today was white, too white for January. He had shaved his beard line even closer than usual. When he clicked to the figure, the room's attention sharpened around it the way people pretended numbers deserved.

The chart looked excellent. It was not beautiful; it was better than beautiful. It looked like the kind of result people stopped touching once it had a caption under it.

Omar spoke without rushing. "This is the corruption-slice comparison after aligning preprocessing with the adapter configuration. Under that matched setup, the intervention remains ahead across all reported categories, with the largest gain in the high-noise bucket."

June's chewing stopped. Leela watched her eyes move, not across the bars, but to the bottom edge of the slide.
