There it was, June's tiny gray provenance footer, the habit of a woman who trusted tools more than people.

`/benchmarks/corrupt/baseline_oldtok_smoke_seed2.csv`

June said, "Why is the smoke run in the footer?"

Ben's hands froze over the keyboard. Omar looked at the slide as if he had not expected it to confess on him.

"It's the only old-tokenizer baseline with aligned corruption logging," he said. "The release mean is on the December preprocessing stack."

June turned to him. "It's also the half-trained debug run from when the memory leak was chewing the A6000s."

"Yes."

"And you're calling that the matched comparison."

"It's matched on tokenizer and logging."

"It's not matched on training schedule."

Mohit lifted one hand, not interrupting yet, just placing process on the table. "Let's separate substantive disagreement from nomenclature."

June gave him a flat look. "That's a nice trick. No."

Omar said, more tightly now, "I can specify the training distinction in the methods."

June tapped the untouched muffin paper against the table. "You can specify it in neon if you want. It's still the weak run."

The old room would have let the sentence stand there and forced somebody to decide whether truth was more important than comfort. The new room waited for hierarchy. Leela could feel it. Ben was staring at her screen as if somebody senior might generate the correct moral vocabulary if he stayed still enough. Mohit turned his head the slightest amount toward her, an invitation shaped exactly like a request for expertise.

"Leela," he said, "technically speaking, is there a clean way to scope this comparison without overclaiming it?"

He had handed her a door and called it neutral.

June looked at her then, not pleading, not warning, just looking. That was worse. Omar did not look at her at all. His attention was fixed on a point just to the right of the monitor, the way people stared when direct gaze might make them shatter their own composure.

In Leela's mouth, the only honest sentence tasted useless. That is a debug run and you know it. The next honest sentence was worse. I have seen this before and kept going. She thought of her own paper, of the line Mohit could pull out later if he needed another story about slippage, about judgment, about senior students who wanted sympathy for compromises they only objected to when somebody else made them, of Omar's phone lighting with that soft administrative reminder, and of what June's vindication would purchase, which might have been satisfaction and not much else.

She heard herself say, "If the caption says old-tokenizer matched baseline and the methods make clear that the only aligned run stopped early, then it's a narrow comparison. Not good, but defensible."

Ben let out a breath and started typing before Mohit had answered, grateful for any sentence that sounded official. June's face barely changed, but she took her hands off the table and folded them in her lap as if the laminate had turned cold. Mohit nodded as though clarity had risen naturally from the room instead of being dragged out of someone.

"Good," he said. "That is the right level of precision."

June barked out one humorless laugh. "Precision."

Mohit ignored it. "Then let us be precise. We're not claiming a universal benchmark win. We're claiming that under the matched preprocessing configuration, the intervention outperforms the available aligned comparator on the corruption slice. That is narrower, cleaner, and much harder for a reviewer to swat away as sloppiness."

He said cleaner without irony. Ben, taking dictation from power, repeated it under his breath while editing the slide deck.

Omar sat very still. "I can add a sentence on the training schedule and archive the exact run metadata."

"Do that," Mohit said. "And make sure the repo naming is less chatty before submission. Nobody needs internal folklore in a public path."

There was the laugh the room might have had in another life. Nobody took it. The file name remained there on the slide, smoke run and all, until Ben updated the export.

June tore the muffin in half and finally ate a bite. "If you're going to use it," she said to no one and everyone, "at least stop calling the thing a reproduced benchmark. That's bullshit."

Ben said, "I can change it to matched baseline."

"Old-tokenizer matched baseline," Leela said.

Her own voice startled her, not because it sounded guilty but because it sounded practiced.

Ben nodded too hard. "Right. Old-tokenizer matched baseline."

June looked at Leela for one second longer than the room required, then leaned forward, put two fingers on Omar's laptop, and moved the cursor to the y-axis label.

"Also your font is too small," she said. "If we're doing this, at least don't make it ugly."

That was how the agreement happened. It did not happen with consent. It happened with labor.

Ben fixed the caption. June increased the axis font and darkened the error bars because she could not bear a sloppy figure even when she hated the story it told. Omar opened the methods file and added the sentence about shortened training schedule in language careful enough to sound transparent and not careful enough to undo the effect. Mohit refined the abstract line so the claim narrowed without losing lift. Leela watched the document populate with euphemism and exactitude, the two oldest academic substances, and then opened the Overleaf draft when Ben could not find the right place for the caption update.

She typed:

`Comparison against an old-tokenizer matched baseline on the corruption slice; the available aligned baseline run used a shortened training schedule, so results should be interpreted as configuration-specific rather than global benchmark replacement.`

It was accurate enough to survive expert scrutiny and inaccurate enough to do the job. It was a sentence built the way institutional shelter was built: just enough truth in the beams that the whole thing could stand.

"Yeah," Ben said, reading over her shoulder. "That reads a lot better."

June said nothing. Omar said, "Thank you," so quietly that it might have been intended for the screen.

Mohit took his mug in both hands and looked around the table with the composed satisfaction of a man who mistook other people's trapped choices for mentorship working. "This," he said, "is what maturity looks like. Not purity. Not melodrama. Constraint management."

June peeled another strip of paper off the muffin cup until it came away in ragged pieces. "You should embroider it on a pillow."

Mohit smiled. "If it gets the paper out the door."

He moved on to other tasks after that, but the meeting had already decided the chapter of the semester that mattered. Travel remained one slot. The defense path remained one path. Nothing had become fairer. The only thing that had changed was that the lab now had a new small public lie it had made together, with enough fingerprints on it that no one could point cleanly from outside the circle.

When the review broke, Ben stayed behind to push the updated figures to the shared folder. He renamed the directory `submission_clean_v2` and then, perhaps sensing what the word clean had started to sound like in the room, changed it to `submission_rev`. June took her coat off the back of the chair and left without her muffin. Omar lingered just long enough to close every window on his laptop before he stood, as if open tabs could testify. Mohit carried his ceramic mug upstairs, where the real cups lived.

Leela remained at the table with the updated draft on her screen. The basement sounded normal again, full of fan noise, fluorescent buzz, and somebody in the far room laughing too loudly at nothing. She put her hands flat on the laminate and felt the tacky residue the lemon cleaner had left. Across the table, June's half-peeled muffin paper sat like evidence from a very small crime.

June came back for her gum wrapper. She saw Leela still there and stopped.

She picked up the wrapper, looked at the caption, and said, "That smoke-run clause is yours. Don't hand it back to me later like we split it."

Leela kept her eyes on the screen. "I know."

"Good." June rolled the wrapper into a hard silver bead between finger and thumb. "I fixed the font. That's a different job."

She left with the bead in her pocket and the half-peeled muffin paper still on the table. The basement door closed behind her with its usual small mechanical thud, and Leela stayed where she was.

Leela saved the file, synced the folder, and closed the laptop. June's work was still visible in the darker error bars and the larger axis labels; Leela's lived in the locked caption beneath them, the sentence that made the smoke run look chosen instead of cornered. The half-peeled muffin paper stayed on the lemon-sticky table, and somewhere on the shared drive Ben had already buried the whole thing under `submission_rev` while the basement fans kept humming. Upstairs, the paragraph she had failed to fix the day before was still waiting in the sixth-floor office.


# Chapter 12

Upstairs, Omar usually entered rooms as if he had already considered the possibility that he was imposing and decided, for everyone else's ease, not to perform the apology aloud. That morning the sixth-floor graduate office got a rougher version of him. The elevator opened. He stepped out with his scarf knotted wrong, one side hanging nearly to his knee, and a clear freezer bag under his arm because the navy accordion folder he used for official documents had split along the bottom seam. Inside the bag, a passport photocopy was bent around a stack of I-20s, and a packet of cardamom biscuits had burst somewhere along the walk over, leaving pale crumbs in the corners like grit.

Leela looked up from a paragraph she had been revising for forty minutes without improving. The radiator by the window had been knocking all morning in a patient, judicial way. Omar crossed to her cubicle too fast, then stopped too hard, as if his body had overshot the version of calm he meant to present.

"Do you have a minute," he said. Then, because even that was too imprecise for what was happening, "No. I need you to look at something now."

He set the bag on the desk. His hands were steady for one second and then not at all. Leela saw the tremor in the right one before he tucked it under his left wrist.

"What happened?"

"My funding letter isn't signed." He took out a stapled checklist printed from International Student Services. Every line had been highlighted in alternating yellow and blue. "The packet has to be complete by four-thirty or they treat the extension as late. They told me that three times in three slightly different sentences, which I assume means they enjoy it."

Leela read the checklist. It was the kind of administrative page universities produced by the thousands, all beige wording and false gentleness, but the requirements were brutally plain when you looked at them long enough. Updated I-20 request. Proof of current funding. Faculty letter on departmental letterhead stating stipend amount, appointment dates, and fee coverage. Advisor signature. Upload before close of business for same-cycle processing. At the bottom, in bold, a warning about delays affecting work authorization and status documentation.

"What's the actual date?" she said.

Omar pulled another page from the bag, this one an email thread printed so many times the toner had started to ghost at the edges. "Today is the last day they will accept the packet without kicking it into late review. They can still review late submissions, but then there is a gap on record and more forms and a conversation I would prefer not to have with the federal government for the rest of my life."

He said federal government with the kind of brittle accuracy that told her he had already spent the morning imagining every possible outcome and ranking them. His jaw kept shifting sideways, a tiny violent movement under the careful beard line. Omar was usually dressed as if a day might suddenly become important in public. Today the collar points of his shirt were uneven. One was folded under.

"When did you send it to Mohit?"

"Monday." He took out the draft letter. "I sent him a version. He changed two words, told me not to say `guaranteed` because nothing's guaranteed, which was charming, and asked me to resend. I resent it. Then he wanted to discuss `broader spring alignment` before signing. Yesterday he was unavailable. Today he's apparently in external meetings until the collapse of empire."

He handed her his phone. Mohit's last email sat on the screen, timestamped 8:07 a.m.

Before I sign anything, I'd like clarity on your judgment and on whether we're aligned about spring priorities. I'm tied up with external meetings until later this afternoon. Let's find a moment once the temperature in the room is lower.

It was exactly Mohit's voice, soft enough to sound embarrassed on behalf of conflict, clean enough that anyone outside the lab would read it as basic managerial caution. Leela felt the click of recognition in her chest. He was not forgetting. He was pricing.

"What room?" she said.

Omar gave a short laugh that had no amusement in it. "I assume the room where I become teachable."

The office around them went on making its ordinary sounds. Somebody at the far end was opening a yogurt with the violence of a person already angry at email. The copy machine beeped twice and then gave up on whatever page it had been trying to swallow. Through the long window the lake was a sheet of dirty pewter and the sky had the packed, low look of more snow.

Leela read the checklist again, slower. The letter needed exact dates. January 15, 2026 through August 31, 2026. It needed the annualized funding figure. It needed tuition and fee remission stated explicitly. It needed a faculty signature. One page, maybe two if someone got decorative, and all of Omar's legal safety for the next stretch of his life had been narrowed to that stupid page and the hours left on the clock.

"Did you call ISSS?" she asked.

"At nine. At ten fifteen. At ten forty-two." He was speaking faster now, losing the commas between thoughts. "The woman I got the second time was kind enough to explain, again, that they cannot issue the updated document until the funding proof is complete, and if it is incomplete after today they have to annotate the file. She said annotate the file in the tone people use for a relative who has started drinking at lunch. I asked whether that meant I was out of status. She said she could not advise me on that question until she saw the file. So now apparently my life is Schrödinger's paperwork."

He put both palms on the desk, leaned over the highlighted sheet, and closed his eyes once. When he opened them, the polish was gone. Not his intelligence. Not even his irony. Just the part of him that usually kept fear from becoming visible to other people.

"I know he's doing this on purpose," he said. "I know because if he wanted a meeting, he could have had a meeting. He wants me waiting. He wants me to hear every hour as him."

Leela thought of chapter after chapter of Mohit's power expressed through apparently minor scheduling decisions, a changed metric, a postponed conversation, a travel slot discussed and then not discussed again. Delay was his cleanest instrument. Delay left no bruise you could photograph. Delay turned everyone else into the person who sounded unstable.

"Come on," she said, already standing. "We're going to Gloria."

Omar blinked. "She can't sign it."

"No. But she can tell us exactly what he thinks he can get away with."

They walked across the quad with Omar carrying the freezer bag tucked under his coat against the wind. The snow at the curb had gone gray and ridged from plows. Leela's right hand hurt inside the wrist brace she had started wearing again after too many weeks of clenching at keyboards and carrying tension there as if the joint itself could solve anything. Omar kept checking his phone every dozen steps, not because a miracle was likely but because waiting without checking would have felt like negligence.

Baines Hall looked as if it had been designed by people who believed glass made authority less cruel. Black window bands. Neutral carpet. The permanent artificial-citrus smell of places where decisions were documented before they were made. Gloria Fisk's assistant waved them in after one look at Omar's face.

Gloria stood when they entered. She had the same immaculate silver hair and practical suit she always did, but when she opened the paper folder in front of her the top sheet was not a memo. It was the day's crossword half-done in blue pen, half the clues filled with the kind of clipped certainty of somebody who solved them between meetings and resented interruptions only on principle. She slid it under a budget spreadsheet before speaking.

"Sit," she said. "Tell me what the exact document is."

Omar sat but did not relax into the chair. He took the checklist from the bag, smoothed it on her desk, and went through the requirement line by line with the meticulousness of a man reciting instructions for his own rescue. He named the funding dates. The annualized stipend amount. The fee remission language. The upload deadline. He did not say please until the end, and then only once.

Gloria read without moving her mouth. "And Professor Saran has the draft letter already?"

"Since Monday," Omar said.

"With the figures?"

"Yes."

"Has he indicated there is any actual funding uncertainty?"

Omar's jaw shifted again. "Not in any way that would survive quotation."

Leela handed over the phone with Mohit's email. Gloria read that too. Her face did not change much, but she set the phone down more carefully than she had picked it up.

"I can remind him that this is time-sensitive," she said.

"I do not need a reminder," Omar said. The formality snapped through his voice like wire. Then he corrected himself, more controlled and somehow more alarming. "I need the signed document filed before the office closes."

Gloria folded her hands. "I understand the urgency."

Leela almost laughed. The sentence landed with the weightlessness of departmental empathy, which was to say none. Omar did not laugh. He was too far gone for that kind of campus comedy.

"Then sign it yourself," Leela said.

Gloria looked at her, not offended, just tired. "I cannot certify lab funding that is under a faculty member's grant authority unless the department is formally reassigning that support, and I cannot do that in an hour and a half. That requires accounts, a revised appointment, and several people none of us want to meet in this context."

"So he gets to do this."

"He gets to control the account he's the principal investigator on," Gloria said. "That's not the same statement, however much it feels like it right now."

"It's exactly the same statement right now," Leela said.

Gloria did not rise to it. That was one of her talents. She absorbed accusation and returned administration.

"If you want this handled as a complaint," she said, looking at Omar now, "we can discuss that. But a complaint does not produce a same-day letter. It produces a file, and files move at the speed files move. I'm trying to keep this in the category where something practical can still happen."

There it was. Not help, exactly. Containment with a sympathetic face.

Omar sat very still for a second, then leaned forward. "What practical thing?"

Gloria turned the checklist toward herself and tapped the relevant lines with the blue pen. "The office does not need a long narrative. They need dates, gross stipend, fee coverage, and confirmation of good standing. If you have an older approved version, the format can be reused. The international office does not care about style. They care about whether the required fields exist and whether the faculty signature is there."

Leela watched the sentence land. Gloria had not given them a solution. She had given them the shape of the lock.

"Do you have an older version?" Gloria asked.

"Of course I do," Omar said. It came out almost offended. "I've got every version. I've got the rejection email from my first visa appointment in Cairo because I needed to remember what exact lie they wanted the second time."

That was the first piece of himself he had offered all morning that had nothing to do with task flow. Leela saw Gloria hear it too. Something softer crossed the older woman's face and then got covered over at once.

"All right," Gloria said. "Send Professor Saran the request again. Copy me. Keep the subject line factual. No accusations. No speculation about motive. If he responds, he responds. If he doesn't, I will note that I asked."

"And if he still doesn't sign?" Leela said.

Gloria capped the pen. "Then we will know more than we know now."

"That's a disgusting sentence."

"It's an accurate sentence." Gloria's own voice had shortened now, the courtesy stripped back to structure. "Do not copy the dean's office. Do not copy International Student Services on an accusation unless you are prepared for the practical result, which is delay. If there is a path to solving this before five, it is narrower than you want and quieter than you deserve."

The folder was still open on her desk. Leela could see a square of crossword grid peeking out from under the budget sheet, one clue unanswered. Not a window. Not moral clarity. Just a little private puzzle interrupted by other people's institutional emergencies.

Gloria reached for the phone. "Forward me the checklist. I will call him."

They left with less than they had come for and more than Gloria would have admitted she had given. In the hallway Omar stopped so abruptly Leela nearly walked into him. His phone had vibrated.

Mohit had answered the email he had just resent with Gloria copied.

I see you've expanded the audience on this. That was unnecessary. I'm still in external meetings. We can speak at 3:30 about the letter and, more importantly, about the broader issue of judgment.

Omar stared at the screen. Then he barked out a laugh so dry it made two undergraduates glance over from the water fountain.

"The broader issue," he said. "Of course. God forbid the narrow issue of whether I remain legally legible."

Leela read the message over his shoulder. There it was, naked now because Gloria's presence had forced him to become slightly more explicit. The signature was a choke collar. He wanted Omar in a room, wanted him frightened before he got relief, wanted whatever promise or submission or silence he had decided the afternoon should extract.

"Do you have the old letter on you?" she said.

Omar looked at her. "Yes."

"Show me."

They went back across the quad more quickly. Snow had started again, not in flakes exactly but in hard white grains that clicked against coat fabric. Omar dug through the freezer bag while they walked and produced a prior funding letter in a plastic sleeve. He had labeled the top with a strip of masking tape in neat block letters: SPRING 2025 - APPROVED. Of course he had. Omar's version of panic still archived itself.

In the sixth-floor office, Leela spread the old letter, the current draft, and Gloria's checklist across the narrow desk surface. The old one had exactly what Gloria said it would: departmental letterhead, one paragraph of reassurance, the dollar figure, the tuition line, Mohit's signature in dark blue script above his typed title. It was amazing how small a weapon could look once formatted correctly.

"This is forgery," Omar said.

"Yes."

"I am aware you know that. I am saying it because one of us should."

Leela kept looking at the page. "Did he ask you for anything specific at three-thirty?"

"Not yet."

"He will."

Omar rubbed a hand over his face. "I know."

He sank into the chair across from her desk and went suddenly, visibly cold. Not metaphorically. The office heat was unreliable and the walk back had dampened his trouser cuffs. He took the packet of broken biscuits out of the freezer bag, stared at it as if he had never seen it before, then set it down unopened.

"I hate that this works," he said. "I hate that all of this works. The letter is one page. The whole architecture is one page. That is the joke."

Leela heard herself say, "Ben uploads half his forms."

Omar looked up sharply. "No."

"He has access to Mohit's office and probably the faculty side of the portal."

"No."

"Then what? You go to the three-thirty meeting, he takes you apart for twenty minutes, and if he feels satisfied maybe he signs before four-thirty and maybe he doesn't."

"That's still less insane than involving Ben."

"It's less degrading," Leela said. "I'm not sure it's less insane."

She already knew where her mind had gone because it had gone there with obscene speed. Ben ran errands for Mohit. Ben formatted slide decks and room requests and whatever else could be disguised as mentorship when passed downward. Ben wanted proximity more than dignity and authorship more than sleep. The lab had spent months learning each other's vulnerabilities as if that were just another research method. This one was simply easier to name.

Omar understood what she was thinking and hated it on sight.

"Absolutely not," he said again, but there was less force in it now because the clock on her computer said 1:47 and time had started eating principle by the minute.

"Do you want the letter filed?"

He looked at the highlighted checklist. At the old signature. At the current draft with Mohit's comments still embedded.

"I want not to need any of this," he said.

"That wasn't one of the options this morning."

Down the hall somebody started laughing too loudly at something on speakerphone, the bright dead sound of people not yet in trouble. Leela picked up the current draft. Mohit had altered almost nothing. He had changed `will receive` to `is expected to receive`, moved the amount down a line, and added one sentence about satisfactory academic progress, as if the money were a reward for character rather than payroll.

She knew this voice. She had spent years helping him refine it in smaller ways, drafting bullet points for recommendation letters, smoothing awkward phrasing in grant materials, listening while he rehearsed compassion into language precise enough to travel. She knew where he placed warmth and where he hid the knife.

"Come on," she said.

Ben was in the basement lab, predictably close to both Mohit's office and the printer that jammed every other day. A mushroom-shaped humidifier puffed beside his monitor because winter heat made his nose bleed, a fact he had once explained to the room in gruesome and unnecessary detail. Today the little plastic cap was glowing green. Ben was collating flyers for some sponsor visit, lining the stacks so precisely they looked ironed.

He straightened when he saw them. "Hey. What's up?"

Leela did not waste time easing into it. "We need Mohit's faculty portal access."

Ben gave a startled laugh. "What?"

"Omar's funding letter hasn't been signed. It has to be uploaded by four-thirty."

The laugh died. His eyes flicked to Omar, then back to Leela. "Okay, but if Mohit hasn't signed it, then I can't just, like, do a fake admin move. That's insane."

"You do admin moves for him all the time."

"Not fake ones."

Omar said, very calm now in the way he became when panic had burned through and left only clean edges, "He's withholding the signature to force a private meeting. The document itself is substantively complete. I need it filed today."

Ben shifted his weight. "I mean, I get that it's bad. I'm not saying it's not bad. I'm saying if he sees I touched the portal, that's directly traceable to me."

There was the real sentence. The problem was not ethics. It was exposure.

Leela tried one more version of decency. "He put you there because he trusts you. Use that for something that isn't pathetic."

Ben flushed. "That's not fair."

"No," Omar said. "It isn't."

Leela had the useless thought that the two of them might join on that, on fairness or injury or any of the old exhausted nouns people used before somebody had to actually do something. Then Ben said, "I've got qualifying review in six weeks."

Leela almost snapped that first-years barely had qualifying review so much as they had a dressed-up check-in, but Ben kept talking, faster now that he had started.

"Mohit keeps saying I need something visible by then," he said. "Not errands. Not being helpful. A real paper line, or summer support becomes a conversation."

The sentence told her what she needed. Ben was not asking whether this was wrong. He was asking what wrong could buy.

"And if this ever gets audited, I'm not just the kid who clicked one box today," he went on. "He had me push a visiting-scholar intake from his office last week and certify two reimbursement forms in December from his login. He said that's normal. If this goes bad, it goes bad like a pattern."

Omar's face changed at that, not surprise exactly. It looked like recognition with one more layer taken off it.

Leela said, "Then stop pretending this is the first clean thing you're about to dirty."

Ben gave a small, wounded laugh. "That's not much of a sales pitch."

"The workshop extension of my robustness paper isn't hypothetical," she said. "I've already got a live draft. You help me now, and you go on it as third author. Not acknowledgments. Author. The reproducibility appendix and evaluation harness go under your name, and I send you the draft tonight."

Omar turned to her so fast his chair scraped the floor. "Leela."

She did not look at him. Ben's whole face changed, not into trust, exactly, but into the bright greedy attention Mohit could summon from him in seconds. It made him look younger and worse.

"Third out of how many?" he said.

"Three."

Ben kept staring. "And you don't use the other part of this against me."

Leela let the silence sit long enough for him to hear what she was choosing not to say. "Help now, and today stays about Omar. Keep stalling, and I stop making that distinction for you."

Ben swallowed. The humidifier puffed once between them like a cartoon cloud. "Could you put the paper part in writing?"

There it was. The full little bastard version of the bargain. Leela felt something cold and efficient settle into place inside her, something she recognized too late because it had been standing next to Mohit all year.

"I'll email you tonight," she said. "You help now."

Ben looked at Omar, maybe hoping for absolution from the person in actual danger. Omar's mouth tightened.

"Do it or don't," he said. "I'm done helping other people feel moral while my life is administrative theater."

That did it. Ben exhaled through his nose, nodded once, and stood.

"His office computer is probably still logged in," he said. "He had me upload a visiting scholar form from there last week. If the session timed out, I can't get through two-factor. If it didn't, maybe. And if anyone asks, I was never in here."

"Maybe is enough," Leela said.

Mohit's office was unlocked because of course it was; people like him believed openness was proof of innocence. The room smelled faintly of cedar and printer toner. His desk was immaculate except for a pile of donor packets clipped with black binder clips and a bowl of the expensive wrapped chocolates he never ate himself. Ben woke the iMac. Haviland's blue login banner flashed, hung long enough to stop all three of them cold, then disappeared into an already-open browser session.

"Still live," Ben said, with equal parts relief and horror.

Leela moved beside him. "Find the draft."

Ben opened the shared drive. There were folders nested inside folders with names that tried and failed to look orderly. `travel_forms`. `donor_followups`. `intl_students`. `misc_admin_old`. Inside `intl_students` Omar's paperwork sat in a little midden of other people's dependencies: `omar_support_spring2025_signed.pdf`, `omar_support_draft_jan2026_v2.docx`, `chen_ds2019_update.pdf`, `fee_remission boilerplate.docx`, `scan from printer 14.pdf`.

"Jesus," Ben said softly.

"Open the draft."

The Word document appeared, already half-written in Mohit's voice because Omar had learned, as all of them had, to ghost the sentence rhythms faculty found easiest to sign. Leela read fast. The end date was still August 15 from some older payroll template, and the health-insurance sentence had vanished entirely. She fixed the dates and put the fee remission back in full. She kept the parts Gloria had effectively approved: amount, appointment period, good standing. She stripped out the little hesitations Mohit had added only to remind Omar who controlled certainty. Then she added back one soft phrase Mohit liked in formal letters because she knew its cadence would matter. I am pleased to confirm became This is to confirm. It was less generous, more official, and closer to him.

"Annualized amount?" she said.

"Thirty-six thousand," Omar answered immediately. "Tuition and mandatory fees covered. Health insurance through the graduate appointment."

He was standing behind her now, leaning so close she could feel the chill still coming off his coat sleeve. His fear had narrowed him into usefulness. He was reading each line as if bad phrasing itself could get him deported.

Ben hovered by the door. "If he checks version history, this is bad."

"If he checks version history," Leela said, "we'll already have gotten what we need."

"That isn't reassuring."

"It wasn't meant to be."

She left the cursor at the blank signature line. It looked accusatory even on Mohit's screen.

Ben said, "There has to be a scan."

There wasn't a tidy `signature_assets` folder, and there was no helpful PNG waiting under a vanity filename. Ben searched `sign`, then `mohit`, then `blue` and got donor headshots, a conference logo, and nothing they could use. Finally he opened the old signed letter. Mohit's signature sat in dark blue above the typed title, flattened into the PDF like part of the page. The room was very quiet except for the fan in the iMac and the muted hiss of the hallway heater.

"Can you pull it off that?" Omar said.

"Maybe."

The first screenshot took half the closing paragraph with it. The second turned to mush when Ben resized it. Then the office phone rang, one abrupt bureaucratic blast that made all three of them stop breathing until it clicked over to voicemail. Ben swore under his breath. Leela took the mouse, zoomed the old PDF to four hundred percent, cropped until only the blue loops remained with a strip of white around them, and pasted it into the draft. On letterhead the rough edges almost disappeared. That was as clean as they were going to get.

Mohit's name dropped onto the page in dark blue script, smooth and practiced, the same stroke Leela had seen on recommendation letters and reimbursement approvals and a dozen other small permissions that added up to adult life in the university. On this page it looked obscene, not because it was false exactly, but because it was true in the wrong direction. They all knew he would have signed eventually if the afternoon had gone the way he wanted.

Leela adjusted the placement by two millimeters. Her hand did not shake until after.

She exported the file as a PDF.

"Open the portal," she said.

The Haviland International Student Services site was not waiting helpfully in a tab. Ben found the faculty dashboard buried under donor calendars and a half-finished recommendation letter. Omar's name sat under Pending Actions with a red status marker beside Funding Verification. Everything else was green. Passport copy received. Enrollment confirmed. Current address verified. Financial documentation pending. A life arranged into check boxes by people who would never have to carry it.

Ben dragged the PDF into the upload field. The site flashed an orange banner: REQUEST TYPE MISMATCH.

"What now?" he said.

Omar leaned in so hard his knuckles hit the desk. Ben had left the request menu on `Initial Sponsorship`, whatever form Mohit had last touched.

"No," Omar said. "Continuing appointment. Spring only. If you send it to initial review, it goes to the wrong queue."

Ben changed the dropdown and uploaded again. The progress bar crawled. Twenty-two percent. Fifty-one. Eighty-nine. Then it stalled.

"Don't do this," Omar said to the screen, very softly, as if soothing an animal.

The progress bar finished. A new field appeared: Certify that the attached document accurately reflects committed support for the period listed.

Ben took his hand off the mouse. "That's the part that's directly on me."

Leela turned to him. "Your ID is already all over his admin trail. This one at least buys you something. Third author. The appendix is yours. Click the box."

He looked sick. The green humidifier glow had followed him only in her mind; here under Mohit's office lights he was just a twenty-four-year-old in an expensive jacket trying to decide what sort of cowardice had the better resale value.

"Third author?" he said.

Leela heard Omar make a sound behind her, not quite disbelief anymore. More like the disgust you gave a street trick you could not afford not to use.

"Yes," she said.

"And not, like, acknowledgments."

"Author."

Ben clicked the box.

Then he clicked Submit.

The page stalled long enough for Leela to hear her own breathing and Omar's and the heater ticking in the wall. Then it reloaded and the red marker beside Funding Verification turned green.

Submitted by faculty recommender at 4:23 p.m.

Omar let out a breath so hard he had to sit down on the visitor chair by the bookcase. Not a graceful lowering. More like his knees had revised themselves without consulting him. He pressed the heel of his hand to one eye and laughed once more, this time thin with shock.

"That might be the ugliest document I've ever loved," he said.

His phone buzzed. Then Leela's. The student-side system had sent an automatic receipt to Omar and a duplicate notification to the faculty email on file. She pictured Mohit somewhere across campus in a donor meeting, feeling his pocket vibrate and knowing before he looked exactly what had happened.

Ben was still standing by the desk. "Could you send the email now?" he asked. "Just so it's concrete."

Leela turned and stared at him. He flushed again, but he did not back down. Of course he didn't. He had learned from the same teacher she had.

"You really want this timestamped in the next thirty seconds," she said.

"I want to know I'm not the only idiot in the room."

That, at least, was honest.

She sat at Mohit's desk because there was nowhere else to do it and opened her own email on the edge of the iMac screen. Her wrist brace brushed the keyboard. The office smelled more strongly of cedar now, or maybe she had stopped noticing everything else. She typed Ben's address. June came to mind immediately, along with the paper that already had too much rot in it and the ease with which authorship had turned into a currency loose enough to tip with.

Then she wrote:

Ben,

Per our conversation, when I prepare the workshop extension of the robustness paper this spring, I will include you as third author and credit you for the reproducibility appendix and evaluation harness updates. I will send the current draft tonight.

Leela

It was explicit, disgusting, and entirely legible. Ben leaned in just enough to verify the line. Omar said nothing. The green status marker stayed green.

She hit send.

In the outbox, the message to Ben sat directly above the automatic receipt confirming that Omar Elmasry's updated funding materials had moved from INCOMPLETE to SUBMITTED. On the screen the two emails looked as if they had been generated by the same office. They had solved the four-thirty problem. What they had actually started would take the rest of the week.


# Chapter 13

By the time Leela found the fourth-floor study room, the library had thinned to the kind of public quiet that made every ordinary sound feel indictable. Snowmelt squealed under her boots. The radiator under the window clicked and sighed. Inside the glass room June had already spread herself across the long table with the force of someone claiming a hospital chair: yellow legal pad open, laptop half charged, coat on the second seat, four folded nicotine-gum wrappers lined in a neat silver row by her wrist.

"You're late," June said without looking up.

"It is 7:04."

"That's what late means when I got here first."

Leela set down her backpack and the cardboard tray of coffees. Her right hand hurt inside the wrist brace. The cup lids had bitten damp rings into her palm. June took one, peeled back the cap, sniffed, and looked offended.

"This is decaf."

"It was the shortest line."

June finally looked at her, took in the brace, the wet hair escaping Leela's clip, whatever lived in her face after the Omar letter week, and let some sharper answer die. "Fine," she said. "We'll suffer in a dignified institutional way."

Omar arrived three minutes later with his coat buttoned to the throat and a clear bakery clamshell balanced on top of his laptop. The clamshell held date bars, cut into exact rectangles and dusted with sugar that had already started to melt in the heat of the room. He set them down with an apologetic little lift of one shoulder.

"The man at Cedar Market has decided I'm no longer allowed to live on coffee," he said. "He was very severe about it."

"Good," June said. "Somebody should be."

His beard line was as careful as ever, but his jaw gave him away before he spoke another word. He sat, took his laptop out, and also, without meaning to, set the clear plastic sleeve of his I-20 packet on the table. He moved it back into his bag almost immediately, like he had exposed an organ.

For a minute they arranged their little evidence lives in silence: June's legal pad, Omar's exported emails and calendar invites, Leela's laptop, a folder of PDFs, a manila envelope she had not decided about, and the list she had made on the walk over with chronology, document index, cover memo, and the names of offices that might pretend not to know one another once something ugly entered the system. Through the glass, undergraduates padded past in socks and slides, carrying their ordinary panic toward exams or hookups or the vending machines downstairs. The room smelled like damp coats and old paper gone warm in the heat.

"Before we start," June said, flattening a page with the side of her hand, "what are we actually making. Because if this turns into three people telling war stories, Gloria's going to tuck it into a folder marked climate and call it a tragedy."

"We need a grievance packet with exhibits," Omar said. "Something that survives first contact with minimization."

"And if possible," Leela said, opening a blank document, "something that doesn't read as a personality dispute between students under stress."

June snorted. "We're students under stress."

"Yes," Leela said. "I would still prefer not to hand them that language."

That, at least, got them moving in the same direction. Leela started a chronology and put the screen where all three of them could see it. June flipped back through her legal pad, dates in the left margin, arrows and stars and greasy thumbprints from months of carrying it around in the same tote as oranges and receipts. Omar pulled up his inbox and began forwarding himself old threads with subject lines so mild they now looked psychotic: Spring planning. Quick follow-up. Notes from our chat. Mohit had always preferred violence that fit inside scheduling.

By eight-thirty the table looked less like panic and more like work. June had pages spread in a fan around her elbows. Omar had built a neat stack of printed emails and weighted the corner with his phone. Leela color-coded the chronology, though she knew June would mock her for it and also rely on it within ten minutes.

"September second," June said. "He tells me not to circulate the benchmark because Leela needs room to build an independent replication. His exact phrase is, quote, don't collapse the discovery sequence."

Leela looked up from the keyboard. "September third, he tells me June's benchmark is still too messy to trust and I should reproduce the evaluation layer myself if I want a stable dissertation chapter."

June barked one hard laugh. "Discovery sequence. Jesus Christ."

Omar scrolled. "September fifth, he writes me that pairwise stress testing will make the lab more professionally mature and that friendship can obscure technical truth."

"Friendship can obscure technical truth," June repeated. "That one should go on his grave."

Leela typed while they talked, trying to keep her fingers ahead of the shape that was emerging. Alone, any one of those lines could still be made to look like advising, eccentric perhaps, slightly manipulative if you were not paid to admire it. Together they clicked into place with a mechanical certainty that made her skin feel too small. The pattern was not that Mohit contradicted himself. It was that he did it on purpose, building each private conversation to lean against another one none of them could hear.

They found the October seminar there too once June stopped flipping pages like she was trying to start a fire and started reading them carefully. Mohit had told Omar to be "fearless about quality thresholds" two days before the talk. He had told June the week before that "some internal heat" might help her tighten the benchmark section. He had told Leela, separately, that the lab needed to stop mistaking kindness for rigor. June read the three notes in a row and then pushed the pad across the table to Leela.

"Take a picture if you want," she said.

The offer stalled Leela's hands over the keyboard. June hated people touching her notes. She had once yanked a shopping list back from Leela with real offense because it happened to be on the reverse side of a page that also held meeting prep. Now she left the whole legal pad between them, palm braced on the cover only long enough to keep herself from taking it back.

"I can just type from it," Leela said.

"Take the picture. My handwriting looks like a medical emergency after page twenty."

Leela did. Her phone camera hovered over June's dates, little arrows, irritated underlinings, the material proof of months June had not spent trusting memory. Up close, the paper smelled faintly of cigarette smoke from before June switched to gum and never admitted it counted as quitting. Leela cropped the image too carefully. Across the table June stayed uncharacteristically still and let her take the time.

Omar pushed the bakery container toward them. "Eat something. Please. I would like to avoid assembling a grievance and a concussion in the same night."

June took a date bar, bit into it, and looked surprised. "These are good."

"Yes," Omar said. "That's why I brought them."

After that the room found its old lab competence again, the version that had once made the place bearable without ever becoming easy. June read dates aloud and swore at Mohit's phrasing. Omar, who normally sounded as if he had been edited by a careful journal, let irritation roughen him into speed. Leela built the packet around them, her screen filling with headings and subheadings and the ugly, almost intimate labor of turning harm into a format an institution might consent to recognize. She kept translating what Mohit had done into institutional nouns: contradictory assignments, misuse of unpublished work, coercive withholding of documentation, leverage through immigration paperwork. She hated the nouns. She also needed them.

By ten the packet had acquired the kind of mass that frightened her. Victory was nowhere in it; any person acting in good faith would have to stop calling the year a misunderstanding. June's legal pad gave them dates. Omar's inbox gave them phrasing. The Baltimore deck gave them one clean theft, stolen figures lifted out of lab work and restaged as Ruderal's private vision. The funding-letter episode gave them leverage, documented and recent. Leela started an exhibit list and June leaned over her shoulder to read it, so close Leela could smell the mint bitterness of the gum and the soap June used when she bought whatever was cheapest in bulk. For one stretched second it felt like Baltimore in reverse, all the same proximity stripped of heat and turned toward work.

"No," June said softly, tapping the screen. "Move the deck before the visa letter. If Gloria wants to play dumb, the theft needs to show up before she gets to say this is just about Omar's paperwork."

"The deck is the cleanest artifact," Leela said.

"Exactly. Let it contaminate the rest."

Omar held himself very still, the way he did when he thought one wrong sentence could shatter a useful arrangement. "She's right," he said. "If the document begins with the visa matter, they can narrow it to process failure. If it begins with document use, then the visa letter becomes pattern rather than exception."

June pointed at him with the date bar. "See. This is why you get pastries."

He almost smiled. "I didn't know the terms of the exchange needed clarification."

Around midnight they started trying to kill the packet themselves. That was June's phrase for it. Assume disbelief. Assume Fisk exhausted and already writing the email in her head about preserving appropriate channels. Assume Mohit calm, offended, immaculate. They kept asking what would survive him and what would not. The library had gone from crowded to cavernous. Somewhere beyond the study room a printer coughed and then jammed. The heating system came on full and turned the air dry enough that the legal-pad pages curled at the corners.

"This paragraph dies instantly," June said, drawing a slash through Leela's draft with the blunt pencil she kept behind her ear. "No offense."

"You're literally crossing it out."

"Because it sounds like a grant application by someone trying not to seem angry."

"I'm trying not to seem angry."

"That's the problem."

Omar took the pencil from June before she could do further damage. "The issue is not anger. The issue is proof density." He read from the screen. "'We were repeatedly placed in conflicting advisory structures that undermined research integrity and created retaliatory conditions.' That's true, but it's still summary. Give them one conflict they can't narrate around."

Leela knew the place where the packet went thin before either of them named it. September sat in the chronology like a bad tooth. It had too many verbs and not enough attachments. She had written that she observed irregular repository duplication and then built the next paragraphs around the consequences because the source itself was missing from the file. October held together. November held together. January held together. September did not.

June saw it a beat later. She tapped the screen with the eraser end of the pencil. "This line. 'Observed irregular repository duplication on lab infrastructure.' Observed by who."

"Me," Leela said.

"How."

Leela kept typing for one extra second, a stupid reflex, then stopped. "Server logs."

June's hand went still. Omar was already standing up with the legal pad scans, muttering something about the library copier flattening the margins into gray mud. He left the room before Leela answered the next question, and she knew later she would understand that as luck and call it strategy.

"And where are the logs," June said.

Leela looked at the manila envelope before she could stop herself.

June followed her eyes. "You've got to be fucking kidding me."

"I've got screenshots," Leela said. "Old ones. Not the raw logs."

"Since when."

The radiator hissed. Outside the glass, a student in a red beanie walked past balancing three books against his chest, fully alive inside some other life.

"September," Leela said.

June did not raise her voice. That made it worse. She took the gum out of her mouth, folded it into a napkin, and pressed the napkin flat with one thumb until the paper tore. "Before Baltimore."

"Yes."

"You had the source and you let me spend two months zooming into deck footers in a hotel room."

"I didn't have the source. I had images taken under inherited admin access I no longer controlled, and by October Research Integrity had already frozen my credentials."

"That's not the same thing as not having it."

"No," Leela said. "It isn't."

There was nothing useful to add. The shame of it was old enough now that it had lost heat and gained structure. She could feel every beam. She had found the cloned directories in September, Mohit's private Ruderal tree blooming where no private company should have existed, student repos copied under a name he had never spoken aloud to them. She had taken screenshots because she did not know what else to do, then closed the window, then told herself she needed more, then told herself she needed clean proof, then told herself another year of drift was materially impossible and a live fight with Mohit would end her before it touched him. She had not said those thoughts out loud at the time because even in her own head they sounded like excuses wearing shoes.

June's chair gave a small complaint as she leaned back. She pressed the torn napkin flat with one finger, doing the arithmetic of damage with the same face she used for broken plumbing. "What else."

Leela almost asked what she meant, almost made her specify it, and hated herself for the instinct before it finished forming.

"If the logs don't go in because they're messy," June said, "fine. But you keep dodging every sentence where your paper should show up. What else."

Leela rubbed the edge of the wrist brace against her thumbnail until the Velcro rasped. "If I use the logs, I need to explain why Mohit had leverage over me when I found them."

June waited.

"The conference paper," Leela said. "The robustness claim did not hold on the actual holdout split. Not cleanly. The aggregate looked good if you pooled the runs and buried the failure mode in an appendix that never happened. He said the trend was still defensible. I knew that was a sentence built to get a submission out the door. I submitted it anyway."

June shut her eyes for one second. When she opened them, there was no wit left in her face at all. "Did you know it was bad when you sent it."

"Yes."

"Did he."

"Yes."

June looked down at the exhibit list, then back at Leela. "So if we put the logs in, he says you're trying to burn him before somebody asks why your paper is dirty."

"He says I exceeded inherited admin authority, mishandled internal data, and produced a compromised result under pressure," Leela said. "And the committee hears my name first because it's on the paper."

"Your name is on the theft too."

"Not formally."

June gave a short, joyless laugh. "There it is."

Leela felt the sentence land exactly where June intended it to, heavy with recognition. There it was: the line she had been walking all year and calling complexity, because complexity sounded smarter and less embarrassing than wanting two incompatible things at once, justice and a dissertation future that still looked salvageable from the outside.

When Omar came back with the scans on a flash drive and toner dust on two fingers, June had already put the gum back in her mouth and the legal pad back under her forearm. He paused in the doorway, sensing the temperature change.

"Did the copier improve your mood," he said carefully.

"No," June said. "We have a hole in September."

Omar set the flash drive down. "Can the hole be filled."

Leela answered before June could. "Not cleanly."

Omar looked from one of them to the other, then chose the packet. It was one of the things she respected and resented in him, the way survival could make a person disciplined enough to postpone every emotional question until after the fire inspection. "Then we decide whether the current version survives," he said. "And if not, we decide what failure mode we prefer."

They kept going because there was nothing else to do. Omar rewrote his statement about the funding letter three times until it stopped sounding grateful and started sounding true. June cut whole paragraphs out of the cover memo and replaced them with one-line specifics brutal enough to breathe: Mohit used unpublished student figures in a private deck. Mohit assigned students to secretly reproduce or undermine one another's work. Mohit delayed time-sensitive immigration paperwork after direct requests. Leela reorganized the exhibits around those claims and watched the packet become sharper and somehow hollower at the same time, a body with its central organ removed so it could pass inspection.

At two-thirty Omar said, "My name stays on it."

June glanced up. "Nobody said otherwise."

"You both thought it."

Leela had thought it, not because she doubted him but because she knew exactly what the university could do with a student on F-1 status who stopped being useful and started being visible.

"If my name is absent," Omar said, each clause clipped cleaner than the one before it, "they will call it hearsay dressed in concern. If my name is present, they may still do that, but at least the lie will require effort."

June chewed once, hard. "Okay."

He nodded and, after a second, added, "Also I'm tired of having the most important fact about my life appear only when it can be used against me."

That was the closest any of them came to a speech all night. It landed because he did not decorate it. He just turned back to the laptop and corrected a date in his statement from January 22 to January 23, because the email had hit at 11:48 p.m. and accuracy still mattered even after a sentence like that.

The library scanner ruined June's handwritten dates no matter what settings they tried. The yellow paper washed into a pale blur. At four-fifteen June swore at the machine loudly enough to earn a shush from the circulation desk, and they gave up on keeping the whole operation inside the library. The cold outside hit like metal. Snow along the quad had gone from white to the color of old receipts. Leela carried the backpack on both shoulders to steady the weight of the laptop, the flash drive, the envelope. Omar walked fast in silence, one hand closed around the strap of his bag. June kept one gloved hand under her armpit and the other around her phone, typing notes to herself with her thumb even while she crossed the icy path.

Keating at that hour smelled like burnt dust and old coffee. The basement lab was dark, but the sixth-floor copy room was lit, and Ben was in it with an overfull sleeve of mini powdered donuts, a department polo under an expensive winter jacket, and one sneaker up on the chair while he re-tied the lace as if the fraying tip had become the central emergency of his morning.

He startled when he saw them and then immediately overcorrected into helpfulness. "Oh. Hey. You're all up early."

"We're not up early," June said. "We're up late."

"Right. Yeah. Sure." Ben dropped his foot to the floor. Powdered sugar marked the knee of his dark pants like chalk dust. "Do you need the copier? Mohit wants sponsor packets for the breakfast thing and this machine is less cursed than the one downstairs."

Leela hated how quickly relief moved through her. Ben had been hovering near every consequential conversation since October, always with some small errand from Mohit and a face too eager to be innocent. Letting him touch the packet was stupid. The library scanner had turned June's dates into yellow soup, though, and this copier's scan-to-email only worked through a department login they did not have. Retyping months of notes before dawn would gut the chronology and introduce fresh places for the university to call them sloppy. "We need clean scans of the notes and one combined PDF of the packet. The library scanner kept washing the dates out."

"Totally. I can unlock scan-to-email. It still runs through my login, but I can route the file to you." Ben was already reaching for his ID badge. "The margin detection on this one is better if you feed from the flatbed, not tray two. Tray two crops like crazy."

June said, "Don't open the preview. Don't save anything here. It goes to Leela and nowhere else."

Ben blinked, then nodded too fast. "Yeah. Obviously."

Omar had already set the legal pad on the counter. Time kept making pragmatists out of them against their preferences, and this one had teeth. Ben tapped in his code, bright and eager and trying much too hard to sound like a person who belonged on the staff side of a doorway.

"Send it where," he said.

"My Haviland address," Leela said. "And nobody else's if the machine gives you options."

"Sure."

His fingers moved fast over the touch screen. For a second the preview of the first page came up large and unmistakable: Graduate grievance packet regarding supervisory misconduct and misuse of student research. June's notes began on page two, followed by the chronology, the deck screenshots, the funding-letter trail. Ben's eyes flicked to the title and away. Leela saw him see it. He said nothing, only fed the next page more carefully than necessary and brushed powdered sugar off the glass with the side of his hand.

"You guys doing some kind of, I don't know, documentation thing?" he asked, too casual to be casual.

June looked at him. "That machine has a mute button, right?"

Ben gave a thin laugh. "I was just trying to help."

"Then help by not narrating."

He flushed, genuinely young for one unguarded second. "Right. Sorry."

The scans came through crisp this time. The dates were legible and the arrows intact. Ben stepped back as if proximity itself might become evidence. When the machine chirped done, he glanced at the screen and frowned.

"Weird," he said. "Because I was the one logged in, it sent the packet to me too. I can delete it."

Leela's whole body wanted to grab the printer tray, the screen, the air between them. She made herself sound bored instead. "Please do."

"Yeah. Of course."

June was watching her, not Ben.

They took the scans and went down to the basement lab because no one wanted to walk back to the library with the packet this close to finished. The room was almost tender in its ugliness at that hour: fluorescent lights humming over abandoned mugs, the salvaged couch with one arm sinking lower than the other, whiteboard ghosts layered into a permanent fog of old ambitions. Leela had once loved that about the place, the evidence that people stayed here long enough to become messy. Now every surface looked like residue.

She opened the packet on the lab desktop because the bigger monitor made it easier to check pagination. June stood at her left shoulder. Omar leaned against the whiteboard, reading the cover memo out loud under his breath, catching dropped words and duplicated dates. Ben's scan confirmation hit Leela's inbox while she merged the files. A second later a text followed.

copier sent the packet to me too btw. deleting now

She typed thanks and hated how much that single word resembled trust.

Then there was only the last decision.

The manila envelope had gone soft at the corners from months in her backpack. She took it out because June was already looking at it, because pretending it did not exist had become stupid, because some part of her had carried it all this way in case proximity did what principle had not. Inside were three folded printouts and one page from her September notebook. The first screenshot showed the Ruderal directory nested inside the lab server tree, timestamped and ugly as a caught hand. The second showed the overnight clone jobs touching student repos one after another. The third was not theft but the thing attached to it, the holdout-split table from her paper, a column of bad numbers she had cut from the final version before submission because Mohit called it noise and she had needed him to be right.

June said, very quietly, "Those go in."

Omar pushed off the whiteboard. "What are those."

Leela kept her eyes on the pages. "Old admin captures. And material related to my paper."

"Would they strengthen the packet," he asked.

"Yes," June said.

Leela answered at the same time. "Not safely."

The fluorescent lights buzzed loud enough to seem personal. Somewhere in the server closet a fan rose and settled.

"If they're real evidence," Omar said, more slowly now, "I don't understand why they aren't attached."

June flattened her palm on the envelope. "Because once they're attached, this stops being a grievance about his pattern and turns into a fight about what he can throw back at her."

A muscle jumped in Omar's jaw. He took in enough from the desk, from June's voice, from Leela's silence. His face closed a fraction. "Is that wrong."

It should have been easier to answer him than June. Instead it was harder, because he asked the question as if there were still such a thing as a correct proportion of self-preservation.

"It's not wrong," Leela said. "It's expensive."

June let air out through her nose, the sound wrecked on the way to a laugh. "Everything is expensive."

Leela laid the first screenshot beside the keyboard. She could attach it. She knew exactly where it would go in the exhibit order, after the deck and before the funding letter, the missing hinge that would turn a plausible complaint into something harder to smother. She laid the second beside it, the clone-job timestamps. Then she added the paper table, with its ugly omitted column. With all three on the desk, the year changed shape. It was no longer an unfortunate pattern or an ambiguity about whether Mohit had merely drifted past ethical boundaries while overworked and visionary and badly supervised himself. With the pages there, he had done it, and she had known, and her own name was soldered to the proof in ways no careful paragraph could undo.

She saw, with horrible clarity, the packet Mohit would make in return. He would put her conference paper on top. Research Integrity was already primed by the earlier complaint under her credentials. Committee members would reach the holdout table before the cloned directories and feel grateful for a version of the story that let them call everything contamination instead of choosing a side. They would talk about her stipend and the defense that had not yet officially been delayed, because March had not happened yet and futures always remained technically intact right up until the email arrived killing them.

"Leela," June said.

Leela clicked the exhibit list, added one line break too many, deleted it, and then, with the cursor still blinking, put the three pages back into the envelope.

It was a small movement. The pages settled on one another, and each fold slid back into the next. She felt the old creases find themselves under her fingers like muscle memory.

June did not speak for several seconds. Omar looked down at the cover memo as if it might change in his hands if he stared hard enough.

"Okay," he said finally, and the word was not agreement. It was merely the sound a person made when there were still tasks left and no authority above them was coming.

Leela attached the scanned notes, the deck screenshots, Omar's statement, the funding-letter trail, the chronology, the cover memo. She addressed the email to Gloria Fisk and the graduate studies grievance address, then copied the ombuds office because June insisted on one witness outside the department and Omar said witnesses mattered more than hope. The subject line looked flimsy no matter how she phrased it: Graduate grievance regarding supervisory misconduct and misuse of student research. She re-read it until the words flattened.

"Send it," June said.

Leela clicked.

For a moment nothing happened. Then the message vanished into Sent. The auto-response arrived fifty-three seconds later, chilled and neutral, thanking them for their submission and promising review according to university procedure. Dawn had begun somewhere outside the basement windows too high to see from here. The fluorescent lights still made everybody look sick.

Leela shut the laptop. Server hum filled the room, along with the cheap wall clock clicking toward six.

She slid the envelope back into the inner sleeve of her backpack.

The gum stopped moving in June's jaw as Leela folded the flap shut, making the decisive pages small enough to disappear again. The complaint now existed in three inboxes and at least two offices. It could hurt them. It could maybe start something. No relief reached June's face. What settled there instead was colder: the knowledge that the version they had just sent was the weakened one, because Leela had wanted a version of justice that still left her something to defend.


# Chapter 14

Back upstairs, the sixth-floor graduate office smelled of wet wool and the hot-dust cough the radiator gave off every time it woke. Outside the long window the lake looked skinned over with dirty pewter. June stood at the sill in her work boots, chewing nicotine gum hard enough that the muscle in her jaw kept ticking. Omar had taken off his coat and folded it over the back of the chair with the same care he used on shirts that cost more than any of them admitted. Leela sat at her desk with the grievance PDF open again, as if another pass through the thing might generate the evidence she had refused to include.

It was still the weakened version. No server logs. No clean line from Mohit's private clones to Ruderal. Their best chance had gone out at dawn in a file that smelled, even to her, of fear.

Ben texted, `Are you all upstairs? Need 5 min. Actually important.`

June looked over when Leela read it aloud. "He writes like a hostage trying to sound casual."

"Maybe he finally found a spine," Omar said.

Nobody believed that, but nobody said not to let him up.

Ben came in carrying cold with him. Sleet shone on the shoulders of his navy shell, and the lift-ticket band from some January ski weekend was still strangling the top loop of his backpack. He had twisted one strap twice around his wrist hard enough to blanch the knuckles. His cheeks were red from the wind. He shut the door with more care than the moment deserved and left his hand on the knob a second too long, as if the metal might still let him back out.

"What happened?" Leela asked.

Ben swallowed. He had a habit, when he was scared, of picking at the skin beside his thumb until it shined wet. He was doing it now.

Leela felt her pulse begin its stupid inventory. Who knew, what had moved, which version of the story would count as official by the end of the hour.

"So I need to say this before it becomes, like, even worse in a way I can't get in front of." He looked from Leela to June to Omar and back, already hunting for the face that might rescue him. "I sent Mohit the draft."

June stopped chewing. "What draft."

Ben gave a tiny helpless laugh. "Come on."

"What draft, Ben."

"The grievance. The PDF. Last night."

The room seemed to contract around the open file on her screen. She had spent months shaving the truth down to something survivable, and survivable had turned soft enough for Ben to pick up barehanded and pass along.

The radiator knocked once behind Leela, metal on metal, like a bad idea gaining confidence.

Omar sat back very slowly. "You forwarded the complaint to the person accused in the complaint."

"I know how that sounds."

"It sounds exact," June said.

Ben took two steps into the room and then seemed to realize that only brought him farther inside what he had done. "You were asking me to verify the thing about Omar's letter upload, and my name was in it, and some of the other stuff was about private meetings I wasn't even there for, and I just kept thinking, if this is going official and he gets it from somebody else first, then it looks like I was sitting on a document accusing my advisor of coercion and data theft and I didn't say anything. That's insane. That's not safe for me either."

"Safe," June repeated. She said it like she had found a dead insect in her coffee.

"I was trying not to get buried," Ben said. "I was trying not to be the freshman idiot who gets pulled into a thing and then everybody acts like obviously I should've known the rules."

"There were no rules," Leela said. Her voice came out flatter than she meant it to. "There was a private draft."

"Which named me. And the portal thing. And the part where the letter got uploaded under Mohit's signature, which, by the way, isn't a small detail, Leela."

There it was: the raw animal understanding that his own fingerprints were on something ugly. Fear had sent him straight to Mohit, the one person in the building who always volunteered to define safety for other people.

"When?" Omar asked.

"Like one fifteen. Maybe one twenty."

"With what note?" Omar said.

Ben blinked. "What?"

"Did you merely attach the file and ruin us passively, or did you add a little essay."

"I said I thought he should know there was a document circulating and that I wasn't sure everything in it was accurate as written." Ben heard himself, winced, and rushed on. "But I also said some of it looked serious. I wasn't trying to whitewash him. I wasn't. I just thought he should see it before it became a whole departmental thing."

June laughed once. It was a good laugh in another universe, quick and mean and almost musical. In this one it made the room smaller.

"You thought he should get first edit on his own indictment."

"That's not fair."

"Nothing about this is fair, you little shit."

Leela should have felt only fury. Instead there was fury and, beneath it, the smaller more humiliating recognition that Ben's logic was not foreign. He had done in twelve hours what she had been doing all year: feeding the worst thing to the person most capable of weaponizing it, because maybe if she moved first into the damage she could control how hard it landed.

June saw something move across her face and turned on her before she could hide it. "Don't."

"I didn't say anything."

"You got that exact expression you get when you're halfway to understanding him."

"I understand the mechanism," Leela said. "I don't forgive it."

"Can one of you just tell me how bad this is?" Ben asked. The need in his voice was naked now; he kept wiping his palm on the side of his quarter-zip and checking his phone screen as if disaster might have revised itself while he wasn't looking. "Procedurally, I mean. Or socially. I need the real score here."

Omar stared at him. "You are standing in the score."

All four laptops made noise at once. June's phone buzzed against the windowsill. Ben looked down first, as if he had been waiting for the gold star to arrive.

The subject line on Leela's screen was: `Concern re: circulation of confidential materials`.

Mohit had sent it to Gloria Fisk. He had cc'd Leela, June, Omar, and Ben.

For one stupid second Leela noticed the civility of the punctuation. No exclamation mark, no panic, no self-defense that could be screened to a lawyer later and called volatile. He had been awake at one in the morning, or awake by one-fifteen, and he had still given the subject line the quiet dignity of a donor update.

She opened it.

Dear Gloria,

I regret having to raise this before we have better facts, but last night I became aware of a draft memorandum circulating among current students that includes selectively excerpted advising conversations, unpublished work product, and a description of what appears to be an unauthorized revision/upload of visa-related funding documentation.

Because several of the underlying situations already involve sensitive compliance and mentoring matters, I am concerned that the circulation itself may now present a separate professional-misconduct issue. I am especially concerned that more junior students may have felt pressure to endorse claims or share private materials they are not in a position to evaluate independently.

Some of the draft's assertions appear to reflect misunderstandings of normal adversarial evaluation procedures inside the lab. Others may be better addressed through standard departmental process than through informal dossier-building that risks further confusion.

My priority is to protect the students, the integrity of their research records, and the department. I am happy to meet individually with anyone involved today so that people can clarify what they have directly observed and what they may have signed onto under stress.

Best,
Mohit

He had taken their night in the library, June's legal pad, Omar's visa panic, Ben's useless conscience, and Leela's own late confession about the ghostwritten letter, and ironed them flat into nouns. `Confidential materials.` `Compliance.` `Professional misconduct.` He had not denied anything important. He had simply rearranged where the danger sat.

June read faster than the others. Leela could tell from the way her mouth hardened, one side first. "Look at that. He found the phrase junior students."

Ben said, "He cc'd Gloria."

"Yes," Omar said. "That's what a counterattack looks like."

Leela kept reading. Ben's forwarded note was still below Mohit's email in the chain, visible if she scrolled. `I thought you should have this. Some of it seems serious and some may be missing context. I didn't want to mishandle it.`

Mishandle it.

Her stomach turned with the blunt physical certainty of bad cafeteria eggs. Mohit's knowledge itself barely registered; men like him always knew before the paperwork did. The violence was the conversion. Their fear had existed for one night in a document that still sounded like people. By breakfast he had turned it into a question of stewardship.

June looked at Ben over the top of her phone. "You didn't forward a memo. You forwarded the only room where anybody said what he did out loud."

Ben had gone pale under the wind burn. "I didn't know he was going to make it about misconduct."

"He didn't make it about misconduct," Omar said. "You handed him misconduct."

That landed. Omar rarely let his voice rise, which made the quiet in it more expensive. Ben opened his mouth, closed it, then said, "Should I reply and say I wasn't pressured?"

June's head snapped toward him. "Don't touch your keyboard."

"I mean it could matter how he framed me."

"Ben," Leela said, and heard how tired she sounded. "If you send another email right now, you're not clarifying anything. You're just giving him more sentences."

Ben nodded too fast. He wanted orders. That was the other sick joke of it. The lab had trained all of them to look for a parent-shaped voice at the exact moment judgment was required.

A second email came in before anybody moved. This one was to June alone, though Leela could see the subject when June's phone lit again on the sill.

`Ten minutes today?`

June picked up the phone. "Fuck."

"From him?" Leela asked.

June did not bother pretending otherwise. "Yes."

Omar said, "Don't go."

June slid the phone into her coat pocket. "I didn't say I was going."

But an hour later, when the wet snow outside had turned to sleet and then to a colorless kind of rain, Leela found June in the fifth-floor corridor outside Mohit's office anyway.

Keating always looked cheapest in February. The cinderblock sweated faintly under the paint. Salt had dried in fans around the building mats. Somebody had left a paper plate with two stale brownie corners on the radiator between faculty doors. June stood under Mohit's office placard with her hands jammed deep in her coat pockets, one heel lifting and dropping against the tile in a tiny metronome she would have denied.

"You really came," Leela said.

June did not turn. "I said I was thinking about it."
