"That isn't the same thing."

"No." June finally looked over. Her hair needed trimming again; one side was beginning to hook against her jaw. "It isn't."

Leela stopped beside the dead ficus outside the copier room. "If you go in there alone, he's going to split hairs until you start agreeing with his nouns."

"He already sent the nouns."

"We can decide our own response first."

"We had our chance at that."

There it was again, the place June could always touch if she wanted the argument to bleed faster. Leela felt the old shame move through her like cold water under a door.

"That isn't fair either," she said.

June gave her a look that could have charred paper. "No? We sent a complaint with the middle cut out because you wanted justice that still let you graduate on time. Now he has the opening he needs, and you want me to perform solidarity like we're still in the clean version of this."

Leela started with precision because that was still her cheapest form of hope. "What I want is for him not to take us apart by identifying, correctly, where each person is most vulnerable and then offering the exact amount of rescue required to make that vulnerability private again."

June barked a laugh. "See, this is why I can't talk to you when you're scared. You start sounding like a grant abstract about your own life."

The office door opened behind them before Leela could answer. Mohit stood there with his reading glasses off in one hand and a glass jar of fennel seeds open on his desk behind him, the sharp sweet smell getting out into the hall. His shirtsleeves were immaculate. He wore the composure of a man mildly irritated that a day had become cluttered.

"June," he said, as if this were an ordinary advising check-in. Then to Leela: "Leela, if you need time with her after, I won't keep you long."

The generosity of that sentence was almost funny.

June pushed off the wall. "Ten minutes."

"Of course."

She went in. Mohit closed the door with the same quiet care Ben had used upstairs, but the old latch did not catch cleanly on the first pull. The door settled with a thin seam of light still showing at the frame.

Leela took three useless steps toward the stairwell and stopped. She did not leave. Mohit's door had the standard wired-glass slit, clouded with old tape ghosts, enough to flatten everyone inside into moving weather. She stood by the copier and watched the red digital clock over the faculty mailboxes turn from 2:11 to 2:12 while the machine beside her kept waking and sleeping with a throat-clear of rollers. At first the office gave her only a murmur, his voice pitched for reassurance, June's lower and harder. Then a chair leg scraped, and Mohit's words came through cleanly enough to bruise.

"I'm not asking you to defend me," he said. "I'm asking you to separate what you directly know from a grievance assembled out of everybody else's panic."

June answered too low for Leela to catch.

"What Gloria will see," Mohit said, "is a student coming off a bad seminar season and attaching herself to claims she can't independently document. That doesn't read as brave. It reads as unstable."

The copier clicked awake again. Through the wired glass Leela saw June go still, one hand braced on the back of the visitor chair. When the machine fell quiet, June was saying, "You really know how to make care sound filthy."

Mohit let the silence do a little work. "I know what institutions hear," he said. "And I know you need a future after this place. If you stop attaching your name to this draft, I can introduce you this week to a model-evaluation team in Brooklyn. Good infrastructure. Small group. No appetite for departmental theater. I can't make that call honestly while you're endorsing material you didn't witness."

June laughed once, flat enough that Leela felt it in her teeth. "So that's the price."

"That's me refusing to let one bad document close a door you'll want open in six months."

"A door you happen to own the key to."

Another scrape. June moved across the narrow strip of glass, and Leela saw her bite down on the inside of her lower lip so hard that when she pulled free there was already a bright wet line there. Mohit said something softer, lost to the copier's rollers, and June answered louder.

"Don't dress this up like you're saving me."

At 2:23 the door opened.

June came out with her face blank in the efficient way people blank it when they do not want to be seen deciding anything yet. The cut inside her lip had marked one corner of her mouth red. Mohit remained inside. He gave Leela a small nod, a colleague's nod, and disappeared back to his desk.

"Walk," June said.

They went down one flight into the concrete stairwell where the air was colder and smelled faintly of bleach. Someone had stubbed out a cigarette on the inside sill weeks ago and the ash still clung there because Keating's custodians did the visible rooms first.

"What did he say?" Leela asked.

June leaned against the railing and looked past Leela at the ash clinging to the stairwell sill. "You heard some of it."

"Not enough."

"Fine. He said the complaint as drafted is a fucking gift to him."

"Because it is."

"Right. Good. Glad we agree on reality."

Leela let that pass. "What else."

"He said if this goes any farther in exactly this form, Gloria's first question is going to be about the letter upload and the private notes, not Ruderal. He said he didn't want Omar exposed on the visa paperwork issue. He said he didn't want your existing compliance mess turned into a second record." June touched the split inside her lip with the tip of her tongue and winced. "'You're too smart to let yourself get cross-contaminated by other people's procedural mistakes. If your name comes off this, I can put you in front of a model-eval team in Brooklyn this week.'"

Leela closed her eyes for a second. Mohit loved a lab metaphor when it let him sound clean.

June wiped the blood from the corner of her mouth onto the heel of her hand. "He made it sound like he's doing me a favor by noticing what I'm actually good at."

"He's trying to buy you."

"He's pricing me," June said.

"No?"

June pressed her thumb into the split in the paper ID bracelet still looped around her key ring. "That's the problem. If he said, Here is cash, please betray your friends, I could spit in his face and leave. He said academia is already going to read me as unstable this year because of the seminar and the internal conflict, and he could put me in a room where none of that matters if I stop attaching my name to claims I can't document myself."

"You can document some of them."

"Some." June looked up at the stairwell window where rain glazed the dirty wire glass. "And some I got from you after you sat on them for months."

That one landed cleanly. Leela had no answer that did not sound like appeal.

June kept going because once she started talking plainly she tended to keep her hand on the bruise until both people had felt it. "He didn't ask me to defend him. He asked me to sort what I saw with my own eyes from what I got folded into because the rest of you wanted the coalition to look bigger than it was."

"That isn't what we wanted."

"What did we want, then?"

"We wanted enough corroboration that he couldn't call any one of us confused."

"He's calling us confused anyway."

Leela gripped the cold rail hard enough that the old wrist brace seam bit her skin through the sweater sleeve. The stairwell amplified every small sound: June's gum wrapper crackling in her pocket, the heavy door above opening and closing, water hitting the metal sill in erratic taps.

"If you take his introduction now," she said, "you're letting him write the story of why you took it."

June rubbed the heel of her hand over her mouth. For a moment the hardness drained out of her and left only a tired, compact cold. "He already writes the story, Lee. He wrote it before Ben ever hit send."

"Then why help him."

June looked at her for a long time. "Because I need a future too."

It was the simplest thing anybody had said all day, and it made argument feel obscene.

Leela tried anyway. "We all do."

"Yes, and some of us are worse placed than others. Omar needs paperwork. You need a defense date and a miracle. I need somebody to look at my CV and not see the girl who got gutted in seminar and then attached herself to a grievance with missing teeth." She pushed off the rail. "He knows exactly which hope to press with each person. That doesn't make the hope fake."

"So you're taking it."

"I'm taking the meeting," June said. "I already took the meeting. I'm taking the introduction if he sends it. I'm not promising him anything else today."

"Today."

"Don't do that."

"Do what."

"Make me your clean loss. You don't get to look at me like I'm the first person here to choose my own skin."

That hurt because it was partly false and mostly true. Leela had been choosing her own skin in increments so small she had mistaken them for reason.

June dragged both hands through her coat pockets as if there might be a spare decision hidden there, came up with only a flattened receipt, and cursed under her breath. "Also, before you ask, no, I don't know if he forwarded the email to anybody else. He said Gloria would want to keep it contained. He said that like it was comfort."

"Are you going to tell Omar?"

"I don't want to tell anybody anything for maybe six hours."

She shouldered past the stairwell door and was gone before Leela decided whether to follow.

Omar was waiting in the basement lab when she got back, standing by the hummed-over whiteboard with his coat on again and an uncapped dry-erase marker in one hand, as if he had meant to write something and then lost the thread. The lab smelled of burnt coffee and old upholstery and the server closet's perpetual furnace breath. Someone had left a cup noodle fork on the couch arm. Ben was nowhere in sight.

"Well?" Omar asked.

"She met him."

"I assumed that from her disappearance."

Leela set her bag down on the desk that used to feel like hers before Research Integrity turned every login into somebody else's property. "He offered her an industry introduction if she disentangles herself."

Omar's expression barely shifted, but he set the marker down so carefully the plastic still clicked against the tray. "Of course."

"He framed it as protecting her from the weaker parts of the complaint."

"Which are weak because you removed the stronger parts."

She flinched. Omar saw it and looked away, not kind enough to spare her but not interested in theatrical cruelty either.

"That's not the whole situation," she said.

"No. It is only the part that matters today."

Leela sat. The desk edge was cold through her jeans. "She hasn't withdrawn. Not yet."

Omar let out a short, ugly breath that was nearly laughter. "What would exact withdrawal look like. A notarized statement."

"Can you not do this right now."

"I can do exactly this right now," he said. He had started snapping the marker cap on and off, on and off, until the sound became its own small alarm. "Because I'm the one whose visa paperwork is now sitting in an email thread labeled possible misconduct. I'm the one whose funding letter becomes an unauthorized upload the moment Mohit wants that language. I am very sorry if the ethical subtlety of June's job lead is emotionally complex for you. I'm trying to calculate whether I need my own counsel."

Leela opened her mouth, shut it, started again. "I know."

"No," Omar said. "You know in the way people know weather. I know in the way I will have to answer a question with dates and signatures if this expands."

His phone buzzed. He checked it and grimaced.

"Who is it?" she asked.

"International students office. Automated reminder about spring verification." He slid the phone back into his pocket with too much force. "Apparently the universe enjoys theme."

Leela wanted to say something useful and found only scraps. That had been the year in miniature: the desire to be exact colliding with the fact that exactness rarely arrived before damage.

"He's triaging," Omar said after a moment, quieter now. "He knows who can be offered prestige, who can be offered escape, who can be offered paperwork, and who can be offered blame."

"And Ben?"

Omar's mouth twisted. "Ben is offered the chance to be the good child."

As if summoned by the sentence, Ben appeared at the lab door ten minutes later, not crossing the threshold. He had lost his jacket somewhere and looked smaller without it, all quarter-zip and coffee stain and the red welt where his backpack strap had dug into one shoulder. He held his phone in both hands like an appliance he had not agreed to operate.

"Can I talk to you?" he asked Leela.

Omar said, "No."

Ben flinched. "I mean just like two minutes."

Leela was tired enough to be cruel, tired enough not to spend the energy. "Outside."

They went into the hall by the vending machines. One of them had been out of order since September and still displayed a cheerful digital `ENJOY`. The floor tile there always felt colder than elsewhere, probably because of the loading dock below.

Ben spoke before she had fully turned toward him. "He asked me to write down how the draft got to me."

Leela stared.

"Not in, like, a formal way. He said just so everybody is accurate about sequence and no one accidentally overstates pressure that wasn't there." Ben rubbed at his forehead. "He said because I'm junior, people may have assumed I were easy to lean on."

`I were.` Even now he was grabbing at older people's language and dropping pieces of it on the floor.

"And are you?" Leela asked.

"What?"

"Easy to lean on."

Ben looked offended for half a second, then miserable. "Apparently."

"You do understand he's making you a witness."

"He said he was trying to protect me."

"From what."

Ben laughed without humor. "From all of you, kind of."

The machine behind them buzzed and clicked, trying to dispense nothing. Leela could see the pulse in Ben's temple. He had not slept. His hair, usually arranged to look accidentally good, stood up crooked over one ear.

"I wasn't trying to betray you," he said. "That sounds pathetic now, but I wasn't. I just needed somebody senior to tell me what was safe."

There it was again, honest enough to be worse than apology.

Leela thought of Mohit's office door, of her own years translating his evasions into mentorship because the alternative cost too much, of Ben at twenty-four believing adulthood was a building with controlled entry and someone inside would eventually wave him through. It would have been easier to hate a snake. It was harder to deal with a boy who kept crawling toward the boot because the boot had a title.

"What do you want from me," she asked.

"Should I send the summary or not."

"To Mohit."

"Or Gloria. Or both. I don't know how this works."

"It works," Leela said, "by him getting more useful to him every time you panic."

Ben went still. The sentence hit. Good.

"But if I don't send anything, then it looks like I'm hiding."

"From him? Or from yourself."

He stared past her at the vending machine glass, at the dusty spiral of peanut-butter crackers nobody ever bought. "I don't know anymore."

Leela believed him. That was the worst part.

"Then stop writing until you do," she said. "Not because I'm asking for loyalty. That ship is gone. Because every sentence you give him becomes his sentence."

Ben nodded, once. "Okay."

He did not move. He wanted absolution still, or at least a tone of voice he could treat as the first plank back to shore.

He said, softer, "Are they going to hate me forever."

Leela was too tired to lie kindly. "I don't know."

He took that like a formal consequence, tucked the phone into his pocket, and went back down the hall toward Mohit's office anyway.

By evening June had not answered three texts.

`Can we talk in person?`

`Did he say anything else about Gloria or RI?`

`I'm not asking you to decide tonight. I just need to know where you are.`

The last one showed delivered, not read. Leela watched the small gray status line longer than the words deserved. Her radiator in graduate housing had started its nightly clang by the time Omar finally texted in the separate thread he and Leela had made after Ben became unsafe.

`I'm not putting anything sensitive in writing now.`

A minute later: `Do not send me more details unless necessary.`

He was right. It still felt like abandonment.

Leela called June once and listened to it ring under the mechanical banging of the radiator, the sound of old pipes making a case against sleep. No voicemail. June had killed that feature months ago after a conference organizer left three passive-aggressive reminders about a poster dimension error. Another private fact, stupidly tender in its uselessness, and Leela hated that even now her mind reached for the scraps of June that had nothing to do with survival.

The next day she saw June only in motion.

First at eleven, crossing the quad in the olive coat she wore when snow turned to rain, tote bag jammed under her arm, head down against the wind off the lake. Leela called her name. June looked up, raised a hand that might have meant wait or stop or simply I heard you, and kept walking.

Then again after three outside the machine room, where June was talking to somebody from systems staff about a broken queue and sounded almost normal, too normal, her voice dry and practical and untouched by the previous twenty-four hours. When Leela got close enough to interrupt, June said, without looking at her, "Don't use university channels for anything about this. Not email. Not Slack. Not text if you can help it."

"That isn't an answer."

"It's the answer I have."

"Are you withdrawing."

The systems guy glanced between them, understood instantly that he was looking at a mess with tuition attached, and retreated through the machine-room door.

June finally faced her. She looked exhausted, eyes rubbed pink at the rims, hair clipped back with the cheap black claw clip she kept losing and rebuying. "I'm trying not to make one stupid decision after another at top speed."

"So that's a yes."

"That's me saying I need room."

"You had room. We made room."

Something tightened in June's face, slight as a paper cut and just as immediate. "Did we."

Leela felt the sting the moment the words left her. June did too. Neither of them apologized. They had long since exhausted apology as a solvent.

"Lee," June said, quieter, and the private register of it hurt more than a snapped line would have. "I can't be the place you come for faith right now."

Then she was gone into the machine room, door slamming shut behind her hard enough to rattle the posted safety goggles.

By Friday, the grievance had already changed shape in the air.

Nobody official said so directly. Gloria's assistant sent a calendared `climate check-in` for the following week. A faculty member Leela barely knew nodded to her in the hall with the careful over-kindness people used around a recent death or a person rumored to be difficult. Ben carried two boxes of sponsor folders into Mohit's office and would not meet her eyes. Omar stopped lingering anywhere public. The lab group chat, once a place for broken code, free food sightings, and late-night cruelty that counted as affection, became a strip of silence with one automated building notice about a steam shutdown on Saturday morning.

Leela texted June once more from the laundromat in the basement of her housing block, because the machines were loud enough to make desperation feel briefly practical.

`If you're out, just say you're out.`

The washers spun gray water behind thick glass. A child down the row was building a fort out of overturned plastic baskets while his mother counted quarters on top of a detergent box. Leela's own sheets were in the smallest machine because the next size up cost an extra dollar fifty she did not want to spend. Her phone sat on top of the warm lid, screen dark.

Silence.

She tried, against judgment, to picture June taking the introduction as logistics instead of betrayal. A train downstate. A clean office with standing desks and people who thought benchmark infrastructure was a product problem instead of a dissertation wound. Paychecks that arrived without being braided to one man's moods. It was easy to picture. That was what made it hurt. Mohit had offered something June could actually use.

By nine the dryers had eaten four dollars and left the fitted sheet still damp at the corners. Leela hauled the laundry upstairs anyway. In the hall outside her apartment someone had propped open a door with a statistics textbook. The radiator inside her unit was clanging again, furious with its own metal lungs.

She set the laundry basket down and checked her phone one last time.

There was a new message, but not from June.

From Ben: `He said not to worry if people seem weird. He said these things get clarified. I know you probably don't want updates. Sorry.`

No question mark. No request for forgiveness. Even shame had not broken his habit of reporting upward and sideways at once.

Leela stared at the text until the screen dimmed.

Then she opened the thread with June and saw only her own messages, stacked one over the other like notes slid under a locked door. The last thing in the conversation from June was three nights earlier, before Ben, before Mohit's email, before this new clean split in the air between people still speaking and people managing. It was about nothing. Eggs. Did Leela need eggs. June had been at the store.

Leela sat on the edge of the bed in her coat. The damp sheet in the basket cooled against her shin. Somewhere down the hall, somebody laughed at a television laugh track, huge and canned and oblivious. Her radiator kept striking the same two notes.

Across the room, the defense folder she'd left on the desk under the blinds waited where she'd dropped it, too orderly to belong to this week.

Mohit had moved first. That was the simple version. The truer one was worse: he had moved into openings they had made for him, and now the only coalition that had felt briefly larger than his office existed mostly as separate silences. Omar protecting paperwork. June protecting a future she could still imagine. Ben protecting the part of himself that still believed proximity to power could be mistaken for safety.

Leela put the phone face down on the mattress and listened to the pipes hammer through the wall like a building trying to warn itself too late.


# Chapter 15

Blue, salted morning leaked through the blinds and across the meeting folder on Leela's desk while the radiator hammered the pipe behind it. The noise had started at six and never settled back into mere noise. She had given up on sleep, shoved the blanket aside, and crossed the studio barefoot, the floorboards cold enough to bite. Her right hand had curled during the night. She pulled the wrist brace tighter with her left, listening to the Velcro rasp in the dark.

The folder held exactly what she had left there: dissertation abstract, chapter summaries, projected timeline, the one-page defense calendar she had built and rebuilt until every week between March and June looked occupied by progress instead of panic. She had printed the calendar on better paper than the rest because the department copier made ordinary sheets feel temporary. If today went the way it was supposed to go, she would email the graduate coordinator by four, hold a room for late April, and stop opening the graduate-housing portal just to stare at the line about summer vacancy. If today went well, the credit-card minimum on the eighteenth would remain what it had been for months, a nuisance rather than a referendum. If today went well, the money her mother had sent in November could stay filed under bridge instead of beginning to look like subsidy.

She boiled water for coffee she did not really want, then drank it because her body no longer recognized the difference between ritual and appetite. The mug warmed her palm through the chipped glaze. She ate half a banana gone soft at the stem and swallowed an antacid that turned to chalk at the back of her tongue. Her phone, faceup beside the sink, gave her nothing from June. The last exchange sat there from two nights earlier: `committee tomorrow at two, unless he moves it again` from Leela, then silence long enough to count as its own sentence.

Omar had written at 7:12.

`Good luck. Text when you're out.`

That was all. It was the correct amount. Anything larger from him would have turned into strategy or apology, and he could not afford either one before noon. She typed `thanks` and deleted it. She typed `Will do` and deleted that too. In the end she locked the phone and put it in her bag unanswered, as if preserving the message in its neat, limited kindness would help.

She dressed the way she always dressed when she needed people to mistake endurance for order: black jeans, clean socks, the good blouse she saved for committee rooms, the navy blazer she had bought for Baltimore and never quite trusted as armor, small gold studs she had not thought about since Diwali. Her hair was too oily to leave loose, so she braided it fast and clipped the tail up. Concealer softened the purple under her eyes without removing it. There were limits to what concealer and ten minutes could do. She slid the folder into her tote, added her laptop, charger, a tube of lip balm, the orange extension cord she carried out of habit, and the protein bar that had been living in the bag long enough to qualify as emergency planning rather than food.

By the time she stepped outside, the sky over Lakebridge had flattened into that white winter color that made every building look overlit from above. The walkway behind the library was striped with old salt and the damp black prints of boots. Wet cold worked itself up through the soles of her shoes. She walked fast enough to heat her face and not fast enough to call it nerves. Keating sat ahead of her in its usual sealed-off ugliness, all concrete confidence and dark windows, as if engineering could keep damp air or consequence from crossing a threshold without permission.

What kept returning was how calm Mohit had been all week, not kind or generous or even conciliatory, just calm. He had replied to her slide deck within nine minutes on Monday with three copyedits and one note about framing the contribution more independently. He had smiled at her in the lab on Tuesday and asked whether she had enough time. Yesterday he had sent `Looking forward to a constructive conversation.` He had not once tried to rattle her, which meant he no longer needed the warm-up.

The elevator to the sixth floor smelled faintly of wet wool and somebody else's breakfast sandwich. Two undergrads got in at three with a plastic prototype between them, a hacked-together drone arm or lamp or something equally sure it would matter. One of them held the thing wrapped in his scarf so the epoxy would not stick to the cardboard box. They looked at Leela's folder and laptop and slid politely to make room. She wondered, not for the first time, how old twenty-two had begun to look. Less like youth every year and more like unspent time.

The committee room was smaller than the word committee deserved. A narrow oval table, six chairs, a wall monitor that never centered anything properly, one window looking onto the lake and doing the room no favors. It smelled faintly of burnt coffee, radiator dust, and the sweet chemical wipe somebody had used on the table. Gloria Fisk was already there with her permanent paper folder and a cardigan the color of old oyster shells under her blazer. Up close, as always, she looked less reassuring than she did in departmental newsletters. Her skin had the flattened fatigue of somebody who had spent the morning turning crises into agenda items.

"Leela," she said. "We're just getting set up."

"Thanks for making time," Leela said, because the room required that kind of sentence before it would allow any other.

Professor Alan Weiss sat two chairs down, uncapping a fountain pen and capping it again as if the meeting had already become a waiting room. The side of his middle finger was stained green from the ink. He taught systems and liked to ask questions that sounded curious until the third clause, when they hardened. Opposite him sat Professor Elena Reyes from information science, who kept tucking the end of a red scarf back under her jacket collar even though the room was overheated. She had set a little tin of ginger lozenges next to her notebook and arranged them in a line neat enough to suggest either discipline or a need not to think with her hands.

Leela plugged in her laptop. The wall monitor mirrored her first slide crooked, then corrected itself. Title, subtitle, her name, date. The simple institutional holiness of it almost made her laugh. She had spent six years converting the most humiliating parts of her life into deck format. Objectives, methods, limitations, next steps. She had once believed that was adulthood.

Mohit came in last.

He did not look rushed. He had his coat folded over one arm and a thick paper packet under the other, cream stock clipped with a black binder clip. His glasses flashed once when he turned toward the window. He apologized for traffic between buildings though he had clearly walked over from the office downstairs, then set the packets in front of each chair with the gentle, distributive competence of a man handing out an agenda for a donor lunch.

"I wanted to circulate a little supplementary context before we start," he said. "Nothing dramatic. I just thought it would help ground the discussion."

Leela looked down at the cover page.

PRE-DEFENSE REVIEW: SUPPLEMENTAL MATERIALS FOR COMMITTEE CONSIDERATION.

Her own name sat underneath in departmental serif.

The room did not move, but something in it tightened. It was too immaculate. That was the first insult. Not even stapled crooked or rushed off the printer. He had tabs on the side: scholarship, provenance, compliance. On the second page she could already see the header from the Research Integrity notice that had suspended her server privileges in October. Under compliance, in bold, the phrase unresolved data-handling concerns.

She had not been asked for a response memo. She had not been warned there would be materials beyond her dissertation summary and slides. She had been told pre-defense, which in Haviland language meant developmental, collegial, a room full of people finding a procedural way to say yes while protecting themselves from blame if your final draft later embarrassed them.

Mohit sat down and gave her the same small smile he used on first-year admits and visitors from industry. It showed no teeth and all the damage.

"Why don't you go ahead," Gloria said.

Leela did.

The first minute was all mechanics. Clicker in left hand because the brace made her right thumb clumsy. Slide advance late by half a beat. Her own voice too dry on the opening sentence. Then the body remembered its cheaper talents. She had been talking her way through rooms for years now, and the skill had outlived almost everything else. She walked them through the dissertation's frame: machine-learning triage systems used by cities and public agencies, the way they failed not in the abstract but at the exact points when demand spiked and language got ugly and human. Storm-week complaint logs. Tenant hotlines. Crisis tags that drifted under stress because people in trouble did not speak in benchmark English. A model could look robust on clean data and still misroute the worst day of somebody's month.

By slide four, her breathing had evened out. She stopped seeing the packet for stretches at a time. She talked about the error taxonomy she had built from cross-city logs, the ablation studies that showed where supposedly resilient models fell apart, the human-review interventions that mattered more than another decimal point of leaderboard performance. She did not oversell. She had learned that if you offered Mohit anything shinier than the truth he would later invoice you for it with interest.

Professor Reyes leaned forward at the slide on multilingual drift. "On the Newark and Bridgeport samples," she said, touching one lozenge tin with a fingertip without opening it, "did the hand-labeled error categories transfer, or did you have to rebuild them city by city?"

"Mostly transfer with ugly edges," Leela said. "The high-level categories held. The trouble was at the level that mattered operationally. People describe a flooded basement differently from a heat outage, and they describe both differently when they're writing on no sleep from a borrowed phone. The model reads that as noise unless you teach it what desperation sounds like in each local corpus."

Reyes gave one short nod and wrote something down.

Weiss asked about generalization across domains, and Leela answered that too. She was good here. That, more than anything, made the room cruel. If she had been messy or shallow, if the work had actually collapsed under a few ordinary questions, she could have hated herself cleanly. Instead she could feel them following her. Even Gloria, who had mastered the administrative expression of listening without promising anything, looked up from the packet and kept looking.

When Leela finished, there was a brief, decent silence, cooler than praise but long enough to let the work sit in front of them as work and not as a student body making requests. She let herself feel one stupid inch of hope.

Mohit folded his hands.

"Thanks, Leela," he said. "That was clear, and I want to say at the outset that I think there is real intelligence in the project. The issue, at least from my side, is not whether you've worked hard. It's whether the dissertation is currently in a shape that the department can responsibly send to defense."

There it was, polished enough to reflect the lights.

Gloria turned one page of the packet with a careful finger. "Why don't you walk us through the concerns as you see them."

Mohit inclined his head, as though he had hoped not to be asked and was prepared anyway.

"There are three related issues," he said. "First, the question of independent scholarly contribution. Several core evaluation components in the current dissertation grew out of shared lab benchmark infrastructure, and the boundaries between Leela's contribution and June Park's earlier systems work are not, in my view, sufficiently legible for a final defense document."

Leela opened her mouth. Gloria lifted a hand, not quite to stop her, just enough to sequence her.

"Let him finish," Gloria said.

"Second," Mohit went on, "there are provenance concerns tied to the data pipeline and repository structure used for portions of chapters two and three. Some of that is reflected in the October compliance matter, which remains unresolved in practical terms even if it has not produced a final finding. Third, I learned only after the fall workshop submission that a robustness claim closely related to the dissertation's second chapter had not replicated cleanly under rerun. Again, I'm not framing this as fraud. I'm framing it as a record that is too unstable, at present, to support a defense date."

He said support a defense date the way other people said support a local business.

Leela could hear the fluorescent hum again. It had dropped out while she was presenting and returned the moment he began translating her year into process nouns.

"I need to answer that now," she said.

Gloria looked at her and gave the smallest nod.

"The October review was not a finding that I mishandled data," Leela said. She heard herself starting in the register she used for faculty, measured enough to be mistaken for calm. "It was a temporary suspension on inherited admin privileges because my credentials were attached to repositories I used to maintain for the lab. Those were shared systems. The review did not conclude that my dissertation data was fabricated or that the analysis here is invalid."

Mohit glanced down at the packet as if checking whether his own document agreed with him.

"I don't believe I said either of those things."

"You said provenance concerns tied to the dissertation as if that's settled."

"I said the record is unstable."

"Because the lab record is unstable," Leela said, and now the clauses started adding themselves. "Which is not the same as my work being unstable. The repository structure was not something I designed for my dissertation. It was inherited infrastructure, and I lost access to the audit trail after the suspension, so if what you're asking for is a more defensible history than the one the lab currently has, that's not something I can produce alone in this room."

Weiss looked up from his green-stained finger. "Can you separate, in writing, what in chapter two depends on the flagged pipeline and what doesn't?"

"Yes," Leela said. "Most of the analysis can be rerun from the mirrored dataset copies and the local experiment logs. The issue isn't whether the results exist. The issue is that the original chain of custody sits inside a lab system I no longer administer."

"And the shared benchmark question?" Reyes asked. She did not sound hostile. That made it worse. Hostility would have given Leela something to push against. "How would you distinguish your contribution from June Park's work in language that would survive external review?"

June's name in the room landed like a thumb on a bruise. Leela felt it before she had language for it.

"June built the benchmark infrastructure layer," she said. "I was never claiming otherwise. My dissertation uses that infrastructure the way everybody in the lab used shared infrastructure. My contribution is the failure taxonomy, the cross-city stress tests, and the intervention analysis built on top of it. Those are not the same project."

Mohit steepled his fingers more tightly. "The problem is that they don't yet read as separate enough. Not for a defense. Maybe for an internal draft conversation, but not for the department's final threshold."

"They read that way because the lab was organized that way," Leela said.

The room quieted a little further. She had not raised her voice. She had, however, allowed a truth in without furniture around it.

Mohit did not flinch. "Say more."

It was the kind of invitation he liked because it could always later be described as generosity.

Leela's hand went into her tote before she had fully decided. Her fingers found the hot edge of her laptop.

"In September I found cloned copies of student repositories under a private directory labeled Ruderal," she said. The words were out now, flat and specific. "My credentials were still attached to part of the admin structure, which is part of why the October review landed on me. The provenance problem didn't appear out of nowhere. It was built."

Gloria straightened. "Leela, are you making a formal allegation about advisor misconduct in this meeting?"

"I'm saying the lab architecture was manipulated in ways that now make my work look contaminated by design."

Weiss leaned forward. "Do you have the underlying logs in the materials before the committee today?"

"No."

Reyes asked, "Were they submitted to Research Integrity or attached for this review?"

"Not for this review," Leela said. "This was supposed to be a pre-defense meeting, not a hearing on why the lab got arranged this way."

Gloria's voice stayed level, which was what made it final. "Then I can't let us turn this into a separate fact-finding process without advance notice and documentation before the committee. If there are additional materials, they need to come through the proper channel."

Mohit kept his eyes on the packet, granting her the courtesy of not looking triumphant. "I don't think it's helpful to collapse a difficult lab history into the narrower question we have to answer today."

With two mild words he turned theft and selective sabotage into something that sounded like ordinary wear in an old building.

The flash of courage that had opened her bag died there under the fluorescent hum and three faculty faces arranging themselves around procedure. She thought of the log export she had kept hidden in September and June's yellow legal pad in an apartment she could not currently walk into. If she kept pushing now, out of order, in defense-mode desperation, Gloria would call it a separate matter, Weiss would ask for commit hashes, Reyes would ask why none of it had been attached beforehand. All reasonable. That was the trap.

"The projects in the lab overlapped by design," she said finally. "Methods, evaluation scaffolds, sometimes data access. That was presented as collaboration and robustness work. It made authorship and independence harder to narrate cleanly after the fact, but it doesn't make the dissertation derivative."

Reyes asked, "Was that overlap documented anywhere in the project plans?"

Leela gave one helpless half-laugh she hated the instant it escaped. "Not in a way that would make this easier."

Mohit shifted one page of the packet toward the center of the table. "This is exactly my concern. We have a talented student whose work has emerged from a messy lab environment. I take responsibility for some of that messiness. I should have clarified boundaries earlier. But because I did not, I think the responsible thing now is to slow down, disentangle the scholarship, and make sure no one is put in a position where a defense becomes vulnerable afterward."

He had made himself sound like the only adult willing to protect her from the consequences of his own architecture.

Gloria's face did not change much, but her voice lost some of its cushioning. "Leela, do you have documentation you wish the committee had before it today and that would materially alter this picture?"

The room narrowed around the question. It was fair in the way institutions loved best: fair if you ignored time.

She thought of the log export on her laptop and the page in June's legal pad that matched Mohit's promises against the server timestamps. She thought of Omar's letter, Ben forwarding the grievance draft like a child running to tell, the night in the library when June had looked at the timeline gap and waited for Leela to step all the way into the truth, and Leela had still chosen the version of courage that left a door open for herself.

If she brought any of that here now, out of order, in defense-mode desperation, Mohit would call it retaliation. Gloria would call it a separate matter. Weiss would ask for dates and commit hashes and who else had seen the files first. Reyes would want to know why none of it had been formally attached to the pre-defense materials. All of those would be reasonable questions. The devastating part was that reasonableness was not on her side anymore.

"I have files and context," she said. "I don't have a packet before the committee."

Mohit looked almost sad for her. That expression on his face had once worked on her like a hand at the back of the neck.

"And that's part of why we should not force a date today," he said softly.

She turned to the others before she could answer him directly and make the room about temperament instead of content.

"The nonreplicating result from the fall workshop was not carried forward unchanged," she said. "I reran those analyses. I narrowed the claim. The dissertation chapter is more conservative than the submission was."

Weiss flipped to a page she had not yet read. "The packet says the revised chapter still relies on the same evaluation slice."

"It relies on the same dataset family."

"With different claim language," Mohit said.

"Because the claim changed."

"After the submission."

"After the rerun."

The exchange would have become transcript if she let it. She forced herself to breathe once before going on.

"What I'm saying," she said, and hated the formal phrase but needed the spine of it, "is that the chapter now makes a narrower and supportable argument about failure concentration under stress conditions. It doesn't present the earlier robustness claim the way the workshop version did. If you want the exact diff between the two, I can send that this afternoon."

Reyes looked at her over the top of the packet. "Could you defend the current version confidently to external readers next month?"

That was the honest question in the room, which was why it hurt the most.

Leela answered honestly. "I could defend the argument. I could also revise it further if the issue is clarity about the benchmark layer. Those are different things."

"But could you defend it cleanly," Reyes said, "with the provenance questions hanging over it?"

Leela looked at the window. The lake beyond it was the color of printer paper left in sunlight too long. She thought of saying yes and letting the lie bloom in front of everyone. She thought of saying the provenance questions were manufactured, which was truer and still unusable. In the space it took to decide, Mohit moved again.

"This is not a referendum on whether Leela is smart enough to defend," he said. "She is. I would not say otherwise. The issue is whether the document is ready for the institutional act of defense. Those are separate questions, and conflating them would be unfair to her."

He had taken her best argument and turned it into a case for delay.

Gloria closed the packet and rested both hands on it. "Let me ask the practical question. If we didn't approve scheduling today, what concrete revisions would you undertake first, Leela?"

Her palms stayed on the closed packet as she said practical, pressing the mess into the version you could minute and circulate.

Leela knew the answer because she had already built it into the better-paper timeline.

"I would rewrite the benchmark framing in chapter one so the shared infrastructure is explicitly bounded and the original contribution is carried by the failure taxonomy and intervention sections," she said. "I would append a data-provenance memo separating the mirrored dataset copies from the lab-admin pipeline that triggered the October review. I would also include the rerun tables from the workshop analysis as an appendix so the narrower claim is transparent."

She was answering well. She could feel it, the terrible competence of it. Each item landed with the hard clarity of somebody who had already spent nights alone preparing to survive damage. She was not flailing. She was telling them exactly how the thing could be fixed.

Weiss tapped his capped pen once on the table. "How long would that take, assuming you can access what you need?"

There was the trap again, tucked into the assumption that access still belonged to her.

"A few weeks for the writing," Leela said. "Longer if access questions slow down the provenance memo."

"And who would verify the disentangling from June's benchmark work?" Gloria asked.

Leela nearly said June. But June had not answered her in days. June had taken Mohit's meeting and then stepped sideways out of the coalition they had built badly in the first place. June was not going to walk into this room and rescue her with a yellow legal pad and an uncomplicated conscience.

"I can document my own commits, drafts, and notebooks," Leela said.

"That is not quite the same thing," Mohit said.

It came out mild enough that a stranger might have heard mentorship in it.

Leela turned to him then. She had been avoiding that direct line, the one between them that could still rearrange the whole room if either of them used it correctly.

"You know the work is not derivative," she said.

His face remained almost tender. "I know the work emerged from collaboration, pressure, and some preventable ambiguity. I also know the department can't schedule a final defense on the basis of what I know privately or feel sympathetically. It has to be legible in the record."

Again he said record as if it had formed itself without hands. She almost laughed. He had built the paperwork and the sequence of damage that now passed for truth. He had cloned repositories under her credentials, forced overlapping assignments, leaned on selective reporting, rewarded the child who leaked back to him, and now sat before three other faculty members explaining that the file had a sad story to tell about Leela's readiness. There was no villainy in his tone to grab onto. That was the art of it. He had translated coercion into governance.

"The record is not a weather system," she said.

Nobody spoke for a beat.

Mohit looked at her as if he wished she had chosen a different sentence. "No," he said. "But it's what the institution can act on."

Gloria intervened before the line could sharpen any further. "I think we're at the point where the committee needs to decide whether it is prepared to approve scheduling now, approve conditionally, or defer. Unless anyone has a final question for Leela on the substance."

Reyes opened the tin at last, took out a ginger lozenge, and then set it back unopened. "I don't think the issue is substance exactly," she said. "I think the issue is that the substance and the surrounding record cannot currently be separated. I'm sorry."

There was no comfort in the apology. It had too much intelligence in it.

Weiss cleared his throat. "I would be uncomfortable sending this to defense with an active compliance shadow and unresolved contribution boundaries. That is not a judgment on the intellectual project. It is a judgment on timing."

The word hung in the room as if it had descended from the ceiling instead of being arranged by hands and signatures, as if it had not already eaten half a decade of her life in small administrative bites.

Gloria looked from face to face and saw, correctly, that nobody needed a secret ballot to become cautious together.

"Then the committee is not prepared to approve scheduling at this time," she said. "Our recommendation will be substantial revision and a reconvened review after the provenance questions are clarified and the independent contribution is more cleanly established."

Leela's hands had gone very still on the table. She could feel the edge of the packet biting the pad of her thumb through one sheet of paper. If she moved too quickly she would either cry or say something that would become a new document by five o'clock.

"What's the timeline for reconvening," she asked, and heard how disciplined she sounded, which made the question lonelier.

Gloria did not give her the kindness of pretending there was one. "I can't give you a date today."

Mohit leaned slightly forward. "Let's take a week, regroup, and build a revision plan."

The phrase hit her harder than the decision. Build a revision plan was what people said when a project slipped scope, not when an adult life broke cleanly along the seam he had been scoring for months.

In the instant after Gloria's sentence, whole adult futures she had been living toward simply disappeared. The April room reservation. The summer move out of graduate housing before the rent laddered again. The postdoc applications she had half drafted under the fantasy that being late by a few years could still be narrated as seriousness once the degree existed. The dentist appointment Meera had nagged her to make. The possibility of telling her mother a real month instead of another evasive update. Even the humiliations had been organized by the assumption of an end. She had accepted debt, delay, Mohit's moods, six years of basement light, because the line still pointed forward. Now the line stopped in a committee room with a view of dirty ice.

"Can the recommendation specify that there is no finding of misconduct attached to the dissertation itself," she said.

Weiss and Reyes both looked at Gloria, not at Mohit, and the tiny shift barely helped.

Gloria chose her wording carefully enough to make each word feel purchased. "The recommendation can state that the committee is deferring on grounds of revision and record clarity. It won't make any finding beyond that."

Which meant every person who heard about it secondhand would supply their own finding anyway.

Mohit nodded as though this were a fair accommodation. "I think that's right."

Leela began sliding her papers into the folder before anybody had formally dismissed her. The motion forced the room to end. Her summary pages had gone damp at one corner from her hand. She could not remember touching water.

"Thank you all," she said. She hated herself for saying it and hated herself more for saying it steadily.

Reyes gave her a look that was not pity exactly. It was closer to professional sorrow, the feeling smart women in institutions learned to substitute for outrage when outrage would not clear a room. Weiss recapped his pen. Gloria said, "We'll follow up in writing." Mohit stood as if to help with the cables.

"I've got it," Leela said.

She packed the charger badly, shoving the orange cord into the tote without wrapping it. Mohit's packet went in too. She did not know why except that leaving it behind felt like consenting to the version of events that had just won.

The hallway outside the room was full of ordinary afternoon life. A graduate student from materials science laughed too loudly near the printer. Somebody had left a half-dead peace lily on the windowsill with a note that said FREE TO GOOD HOME. The copy room door stood open on its humming blue light. Every building liked itself best at the scale where other people's disasters registered as background acoustics.

Leela walked to the bathroom at the end of the corridor because the elevator bank would have people and the stairwell would have too much air. The women's room was empty. The light came on in staggered strips above the mirrors, one fluorescent tube after another finding the courage to admit where she was.

The room smelled of industrial soap and old paper towels baked dry by the vent. One stall door did not quite latch. There was a curl of black thread on the counter by the sink, maybe from somebody's scarf, maybe from the edge seam of her own blazer. She set her bag down hard enough that the packet corners inside thudded against the laminate.

Then her body, which had been willing to behave in public for exactly fifty-three minutes, quit the arrangement.

She bent over the sink and tried to take one careful breath. Another one. The first breath shuddered and came back sharp. The second hitched halfway in. She pressed the heel of her left hand against the counter edge until it hurt. Her right hand, strapped and mostly decorative in moments like this, hung useless at her side.

During the meeting she had been biting the inside of her cheek without noticing. Now she found the torn place with her teeth and bit down deliberately, hard enough to make the pain separate from everything else. It was an ugly, bodily impulse, the kind a body chose when it needed one sharp signal. Skin gave. Warm salt flooded her mouth over the chalk of the antacid she had taken that morning.

She spat into the sink. Pink first, then darker.

The automatic faucet took a second to recognize her hands. Water jumped out too fast, cold enough to sting. She rinsed and spat again. A thin clot clung to the white basin before the stream worried it toward the drain.

Her phone vibrated in the bag. Once. Then again.

She did not look.

She took Mohit's packet out and laid it on the counter because the bag suddenly felt too full of paper, too much like an office she was carrying on her shoulder. The top page was already bent where she had jammed it in. Pending revision and further review, the recommendation line said. A drop of blood fell from her mouth before she could turn away and stippled the words further review. Toner feathered outward in a tiny gray bruise.

The words blurred under the blood, and with them went the last hope that the right phrasing might still save her.

She bit the cheek again. Harder this time. The fresh pain flashed white behind her eyes. When she spat, the sink caught a red string and thinned it to nothing.

Her phone buzzed a third time and went still.

Leela rinsed once more, folded the damp packet shut, and held it under her arm until the paper softened against her blazer. She stayed there facing the mirror while the torn place in her cheek kept filling and emptying, filling and emptying, and the fluorescent lights hummed over the sinks like a machine too small to care what it was preserving.


# Chapter 16

She still had not looked at the phone when the washer lurched into its spin cycle. The laundromat on Alder was hotter than her apartment and louder than the committee room had been, which made it easier. Heat rolled off the dryer banks in a soapy, metallic breath and turned the windows into sweating mirrors. Every pair of boots on the floor carried a white salt tide at the edges. Leela sat on an orange plastic chair with her knees touching the black laundry bag and watched her sheets slap the glass as if the machine were trying to shake an answer out of them.

She had brought the good blouse from the meeting too, because it still smelled faintly of the faculty conference room: burnt coffee, radiator dust, the sweet chemical wipe somebody had used on the table before the ambush. Mohit's packet was folded in the tote with the detergent, the blood on the words further review dried into a gray feathered bruise. She had worn the blouse because it still fit under the blazer she bought for Baltimore and because somewhere in her body a childish part of her believed a committee could be managed by collars. Now the collar points knocked uselessly against the side of the washer. Her defense had not been denied. It had been "postponed indefinitely pending further revision and review," which was worse in the particular way academic language liked to be worse. Denial had a date inside it. Indefinitely was a hallway with fluorescent lighting.

She unlocked her phone to check the time and saw the Hudson Federal reminder at the top of the screen again: minimum payment due on the eighteenth. Under it sat Mohit's email from 7:12 that morning, subject line "Next Steps." He had written three short paragraphs in the careful managerial tone he used when he wanted cruelty to look like structure. He was sorry the meeting had become difficult. He remained committed to helping her identify a constructive path. He thought it would be best if they spoke one on one before she let anxiety distort the opportunities still available to her. She had read the message in bed while the radiator clanged hard enough to make the spoon in the mug on her nightstand tremble. She had not replied. She had dragged the laundry out instead, as if separating darks from lights qualified as a governance model.

The option of leaving had acquired its own practical vocabulary by breakfast. It involved storage bins, sublet groups, and the question of whether the graduate-housing office would let her out before summer if she furnished the room and found a woman willing to take over the lease. Graduate housing took its money at the start of every month whether or not a future still existed to justify it. Leaving also meant deciding whether she could put the books in Meera's garage without Meera looking too directly at the number of them, and whether she could say on the phone to her mother that the program had shifted and she was rethinking timelines, as if six years were a haircut that had grown out badly. The shame in that version was domestic, not spectacular. It lived in luggage, in family WhatsApp replies, and in the fact that Anita had sent her twelve hundred dollars at Diwali and called it nothing, just keep it, just until the next stipend.

The other option sat in Mohit's email. She could ask for the remediation plan, accept that the paper from September would now live forever as proof of her compromised judgment, rerun experiments on access she no longer controlled, apologize in rooms where every apology became data, and perhaps, if she kept her head down long enough, receive a smaller future as a reward for professional maturity, in the form of a later defense, a weaker letter, or a pity introduction to somebody else's postdoc. It was not even impossible. That was the insulting part. The committee had not thrown her off campus. It had left the door open exactly wide enough for her to crawl back through it.

When the dryer buzzed, June texted. "Interview done. If one more man in a quarter zip says mission alignment I might bite him."

A minute later another message came in. "They want another round. Remote this time. Thank fuck."

Leela looked at the spinning socks and wrote, "That sounds promising."

June answered fast. "Promising is not the word. But it pays actual money."

Then, after a pause long enough to imagine June walking through an airport concourse or across some glass lobby full of upholstered optimism, she added, "If you're serious about getting out, I can send recruiter names. These places hire unfinished PhDs all the time."

There was nothing cruel in it. That made it worse. June was not withholding tenderness as punishment. She was already moving in the direction of salary bands, vesting cliffs, offices with windows, and she was decent enough to hold the door open behind her. The sentence did not ask how Leela was. It assumed the relevant question had become where else she could land. Leela typed, "Committee pushed the defense. No date." She deleted it, retyped it, and sent it.

June's reply took longer. "Fuck. Did they put it in writing?"

"Not yet."

"Get the memo before you talk to Mohit. Forward me anything they send."

Then June sent one more message. "And yes, I meant the recruiter thing."

Leela held the phone in both hands until the screen dimmed. She had spent the last three weeks telling herself that once June was done being furious, or done being scared, or done taking whatever Mohit's meeting had offered her, they would return to the part where the two of them could say the true sentence out loud and decide what to do with it. But June's texts came from another weather now. They were helpful, sharp, and already budgeting for departure. Waiting for her to come back emotionally was not a plan. It was a superstition with a charging cable.

She folded everything while it was still hot enough to sting her fingers. The wrist brace had gone through the mesh bag and come out shaped like a small defeated exoskeleton. Her socks steamed faintly in the cold air that leaked every time the front door opened. When she stepped back outside, the tote against her thigh radiated warmth through her coat for half a block and then surrendered. Alder Street was half slush, half grit. The halal grocer had stacked clementine boxes in a tower by the door and covered them with a blue tarp that snapped in the wind. A nail salon window advertised student discounts in letters missing three vowels. Above the strip, campus rose in gray stone and self-esteem.

She cut behind the library toward Keating because there was nowhere else to go with a Sunday and a postponed life. The quad was almost empty. A grounds truck idled by a snowbank, and somewhere a metal flag halyard knocked against a pole with the patience of torture. Without undergraduates moving through it, the campus looked less prestigious and more recently expensive. All the glass had the expression of a place expecting witnesses.

On the sixth floor the shared office smelled of wet wool and printer heat. One radiator hissed under the lake-facing window. Her cubicle still held the stupid archaeology of a nearly finished degree: reviewer comments clipped together with a binder clip shaped like a cat, a dead succulent in a Haviland mug, three conference badges, the defense checklist she had made in January when March had still sounded like a time rather than an injury. She sat down and opened the document called REVISION PLAN.

It contained seven headings, all reasonable in isolation, and they told her to rerun the robustness experiment with a corrected baseline, expand the ethics subsection, clarify the provenance of repository access, and meet with her advisor to define authorship boundaries. The list was her own. She had typed it the night before the committee because she believed, with the embarrassing persistence of an obedient student, that if she arrived with work already broken into subproblems the adults in the room might treat her like a person capable of solving them. Now the headings read like chores assigned to the woman who had lost the case against herself.

She tried to turn them into an email to Gloria Fisk. Dear Professor Fisk, thank you for convening the committee. I would appreciate clarity on the specific milestones required for reconsideration of my defense timeline. She got that far and stopped. Every noun in the sentence was somebody else's noun, especially reconsideration, timeline, and milestones. The university loved a word that could describe a life and a project plan with the same sterile usefulness. If Gloria answered, it would be with process. If Mohit answered, it would be with a meeting. If she followed either path, the full price of staying would be silence about everything that had made the committee version of her possible.

She opened the hidden folder instead. It sat inside three other boring names on her hard drive because hiding had once felt like prudence. Inside were the September logs from hades, exported before Research Integrity cut her access; screenshots from the Ruderal deck in Baltimore; drafts of June's grievance timeline and scanned pages from the yellow legal pad, June's blocky handwriting getting angrier as the semester went on; Ben's forwarded email chain; Omar's funding letter versions; and her own conference paper, the clean draft and the dirty one, along with the rerun results that had refused to reproduce the claim Mohit later used to paint her as ethically suspect. The folder did not flatter anyone. That was partly why she had never given it to anybody in full.

If she exposed the archive as it actually existed, she was not the heroine in it. She was the person who saw the cloned repositories in September and decided not to blow up her life yet. She was the person who padded a result when the deadline narrowed and June needed the same machines. She was the person who ghostwrote part of Omar's funding letter because the clean procedure would have left him out of status. She could tell morally satisfying versions of each choice. She had. The archive disliked those versions. It kept the timestamps.

Outside the window, the lake looked like a sheet of unpolished aluminum. Students crossed the quad below in twos and threes, their scarves blown flat behind them. Leela imagined the three futures available to her with the same exhausted precision she had once applied to debugging. In one, she disappeared without giving the institution anything more to weaponize. In one, she bargained. In one, she turned the whole mess over and let the broken parts of her own record come with it. The third future was the only one that did not require her to keep pretending the first two were forms of control.

At three, Mohit sent a second email. "I know this is painful," it began, which was how he sounded when he wanted to name pain as a managerial condition rather than a fact somebody had produced. She closed the laptop without answering, put the hard drive in her coat pocket, and walked out before she could draft something professionally suicidal like go fuck yourself or professionally useful like thank you, I'd welcome the chance to discuss. The elevator shuddered down six floors. In the lobby, the heat hit her face and then the outside cold erased it.

The walk to North Lake took twenty minutes and felt punitive by the last five. Wind came unobstructed across the frozen water and found every gap in her coat. Salt cracked under her boots on the boards of the footbridge. The ice below had gone from the first damp gray of winter to something thicker and opaque, with old snow blown into ridges like folded paper. A red warning sign was cable-tied to the railing, and the plastic had split through the word THIN so that it looked like a stammer.

She stood halfway across, hands deep in her pockets around the hard drive and the folded printout of Mohit's email, and let the wind force tears out of her eyes. Crying was too organized a word for it. The cold just made the face honest. From there Keating was visible through bare trees, a slab of lit windows at the edge of campus, and beyond it the black-glass administration building where every room managed to sound already transcribed. The university looked coherent from a distance. That was one of its talents.

In model evaluation, a holdout set was the data you kept back so the system could not memorize the answer key and call the trick generalization. She had taught that definition to undergraduates three semesters running. This year nobody had kept anything back. Mohit had trained on their trust, their money problems, June's temper, Omar's visa clock, Ben's hunger to be chosen, Leela's belief that if she stayed reasonable long enough reason would be rewarded. The institution had trained on the rest. If the archive stayed only on her laptop, it would end up like everything else at Haviland: something she could be talked into trimming until it barely accused anyone.

Her mother had left a missed call and a voicemail consisting of two questions and one instruction: had she eaten lunch, and did she know yet about the cousin's engagement weekend in April; also, wear the real boots, not the city ones. Leela listened to it once and put the phone away. The love in Anita's voice had the dense practical weight of cooked rice. It did not tell her what to do with Mohit. It only made leaving feel like admitting that all the waiting at home had been for nothing.

By the time she turned back toward town her face hurt, the kind of clean pain that at least belonged to weather. She did not feel brave. She felt used up. The anger sat low and steady, no longer dramatic enough to mistake for courage. Mohit had already made her compromised. The committee had already entered that compromise into the record. The only remaining question was who would get custody of the full version.

Her apartment was overheated for once. The radiator hammered in bursts like somebody testing pipes with a wrench. She set the tote of laundry on the mattress, opened the laptop on the desk, and worked through the folder without music. Each file demanded a small act of nonvanity. She kept the logs. She kept the rerun notebook that proved the September result was dirty. She kept the draft complaint with her own hedges still visible in tracked changes. She kept the messages where June asked for the full server evidence and Leela said not yet. She kept the upload metadata on Omar's letter. She kept the deck versions with the stolen figures and the cached screenshots in case the PDFs later disappeared. She made a chronology in plain language because there was no point in handing a lawyer a cathedral of filenames and calling it truth.

When everything fit, she compressed the directory, encrypted it, checked the checksum, and stared at the blinking cursor where the archive still needed a name. complaint_final was a joke. misconduct_bundle sounded like something Mohit would send. evidence made her think of courtroom television and clean victims. She typed `holdout_set`, watched the underscore appear like a hinge, and pressed return. The name sat there flat and technical, which was exactly what she needed.

The graduate workers union had circulated counsel's contact information twice that winter, once after the travel-slot fiasco and again when stipends hit late in January. Leela found the email thread, half hidden under campus announcements, and wrote a version of the truth short enough to be sent: I'm a PhD student facing advisor retaliation and research misuse. Documentation is available if helpful. Is someone available today? The reply came eleven minutes later from Marisol Vega. "I'm here till six-thirty. If you want to bring materials in person, do that. Don't send the files over university email."

Marisol's office was over the pharmacy on Alder, up one flight of narrow stairs that smelled of old mop water and cumin from the takeout place next door. A corkboard outside the door held strike photos, rent-rights flyers, and a faded drawing of a tabby cat in a hard hat. Leela arrived with her backpack damp at the shoulders from blown snow and stood for a second with her hand on the knob, aware that handing over the drive would turn the day from contemplation into record.

Marisol Vega was younger than Leela had expected, maybe early forties, with blunt bangs she had clearly cut herself and men's snow boots patched at one toe with a neat strip of silver duct tape. A paper cup of mint tea sat untouched beside her keyboard until a skin formed over it. She moved it out of the way with mild embarrassment only after Leela noticed, as if cold tea were a recurring personal defeat. "Come in," she said. "Shut the door all the way. It sticks."

The office had one real lamp, one filing cabinet, and enough paper for three kinds of trouble. Marisol waited until Leela sat down before pulling a legal pad toward herself. "Start wherever the timeline actually starts," she said. "Not where the university started paying attention."

Leela did, but not elegantly. She started with the server logs in September because that was the first moment the year offered her a knife and she put it back in the drawer. She moved through the adversarial pairings, the conference paper, Baltimore, the grievance, Ben forwarding it, the committee. Twice she found herself drifting into explanatory jargon and had to stop because Marisol's face stayed politely blank whenever the vocabulary got too lab-specific. So she translated. He stole figures. He made scarcity formal. He used my dirty result when it was useful to him. I helped keep Omar enrolled by faking part of a letter. I sat on the logs because I thought I could survive this if I timed it right.

Marisol did not look shocked. She did not look impressed either. She asked dates, file names, who had originals, who knew what, whether Mohit had ever put any of his contradictory instructions in writing, whether June and Omar were willing to corroborate, whether Research Integrity already possessed anything that could later be compared. Her questions had the plain brutality of competence. By the time Leela finished, the story sounded less like a confession and more like a chain of custody.

"Does the archive have the parts that make you look bad?" Marisol asked.

"Yes."

"All of them?"

Leela almost said enough of them. "I think so."

Marisol shook her head once. "I need a less academic answer than that."

Leela felt something mean rise in her throat and burn there. "Yes. The parts where I waited. The fake-clean paper. Omar's letter. The parts where I thought I could manage him."

"Good," Marisol said. "If it's everything, it's useful. If it's almost everything, Haviland will spend six months finding the seam and pull the whole thing apart."

Leela took the drive out of her pocket and set it on the desk. It was small enough to look unserious, black plastic with a scratch along one edge. "I don't know what this gets me," she said.

Marisol glanced at the drive but did not pick it up yet. "It might not get you anything good."

"I noticed."

"It might get you retaliation, or a settlement designed to buy silence, or an investigation so narrow it insults you on purpose. The university's favorite move is to admit a process problem and deny a power problem."

Leela laughed once, ugly and brief. "That sounds right."

"But if what you want is a clean exoneration, this isn't that office," Marisol said. "If what you want is for the documents to survive you losing your nerve, or losing your laptop, or getting talked into revision number twelve, I can help with that."

"Do June and Omar know you're bringing me the full file?" she asked a moment later.

"Not yet."

Marisol tapped the drive with one fingernail and pushed it an inch back across the desk. "Then we slow down. I'm not taking blind custody of a sealed archive that can blow back on two other grad workers before either of them hears it from you."

Leela looked at the drive. "I'm not asking their permission."

"I didn't say permission." Marisol pulled the legal pad closer. "I said notice, and I said chain of custody. Open it. We make a manifest in plain English. Then you tell whichever one you're most tempted to protect by editing."

Something in Leela's chest tightened hard enough to feel medicinal. She plugged the drive into Marisol's desktop and typed the passphrase herself. `holdout_set` mounted on the screen. The folder tree opened down the screen: `server_logs_sept`, `ruderal_deck_baltimore`, `june_timeline_scans`, `omar_letter_versions`, `paper_dirty`, `paper_clean`, `complaint_drafts`, `text_exports`. Her hand moved to the mouse before she had decided anything. She clicked once on `june_timeline_scans` and then on `omar_letter_versions`, feeling the old stupid reflex rear up again, the wish to hand over truth with the teeth filed down.

Marisol watched the cursor stop. "If those are the folders you're suddenly ashamed of," she said, "they're probably the ones that keep the rest from looking curated."

Leela opened a message to June because Omar's name carried too much paperwork inside it and June had already spent the day talking like someone halfway out the door. She typed, "I'm with union counsel. I'm handing over the full archive tonight. Your notebook scans are in it. So are the texts where you asked for the server evidence and I said not yet. I'm telling you before the copy starts."

June replied before Leela could regret sending it. "Is it actually the full thing or the tasteful grad-student version of the full thing?"

Leela typed, "All of it."

Another bubble came back almost at once. "Then keep the ugly parts. Keep my pages. Keep your not yet. Keep the fucking logs. If you clean me up now, he'll own the language again."

The shame that rose in Leela's throat was hot and almost clarifying. She took her hand off the mouse and wrote on Marisol's pad: June's handwritten timeline pages and related text messages documenting Leela's withholding of server evidence. Beneath that she wrote: Ghostwritten funding-letter drafts and upload metadata tied to Omar's visa paperwork. Her handwriting looked pinched and schoolgirl neat.

"Good," Marisol said. "Now I can tell what I'm preserving."

She started the copy only after the list was complete. A progress bar opened, then stalled at twelve percent long enough for Leela to feel her pulse in the bad wrist before the numbers began moving again. Marisol watched the transfer window, then the checksum utility, then the legal pad, as if mistrust were simply another office supply. "Once this is copied," she said, "I'll put one encrypted copy on union storage and one on an offline drive. If you decide next week that this was a terrible idea, you can still decide that. What you won't be able to do is pretend it never existed."

"What do you do with it?" Leela asked.

"Right now? Preserve it. Build the index. Figure out what corroborates what. See which parts matter only if June says yes, which parts matter only if Omar does, and which parts stand on timestamps alone. Then we wait for a setting where they can't reduce it to mentoring style and unfortunate misunderstandings."
