"A public setting."

"Usually," Marisol said. "Power behaves better when it has an audience."

The progress bar reached the end. Marisol verified the checksum once on screen and once on paper, writing the string down in tight block print that made every letter look like it could testify. Leela watched her do it and felt something settle, not relief exactly, more the end of a useless appeal. She had spent months trying to preserve a version of herself that might still deserve uncomplicated sympathy if the facts ever became public. That version did not exist. It had not existed the night she hid the logs, or the deadline week she let the false result stand, or the hour she doctored Omar's letter because the clean procedure was a polite machine for immigration disaster. Wanting a clean alibi had been one more way of staying loyal to the institution's taste in victims.

Marisol labeled a folder HOLDOUT SET / L.K. / MARCH 2026 and slid it into a drawer. "Leela," she said, looking up for the first time in several minutes, "they can already call you compromised. He already did. The committee already stamped it. The difference is whether compromised means documented and contextualized or whether it means whatever version he tells first."

When Leela stood, the room tilted from hunger. Marisol pointed to a basket of granola bars without looking up from her notes. "Take one. Counsel's not your mother, but counsel can still tell when somebody hasn't eaten."

Leela took one because refusing would have felt theatrical. It was peanut butter and stale at the edges. By the time she got downstairs the snow had started again, not dramatic flakes, just the dry mean kind that moved sideways under the streetlights. Her backpack was lighter by almost nothing. Upstairs, Marisol had the checksum on paper, the manifest in plain English, and the copied drive already in a labeled drawer.

Whatever story Haviland later told, `holdout_set` was no longer just a folder Leela could open, rename, or thin out at two in the morning.


# Chapter 17

By nine in the morning the Haviland Innovation Atrium smelled of lemon polish, espresso, and pastry that had been thawed somewhere else. Sun through the glass walls laid a hard white shine across the concrete floor and made the donor banners look more expensive than cloth had any right to look. The room had been built to flatter engineering. Every cord was hidden. Every table edge matched. Even the fingerprints disappeared.

Leela stood behind a retractable wall near the catering entrance with a paper folder tucked under her arm and her laptop open on a high-top table that was supposed to hold sparkling water. Her right wrist ached inside the brace. The folder held twelve printed packets, the public version of what Marisol had checksummed into a drawer in March, because Omar had insisted that if the wireless failed or somebody decided to be brave in theory and useless in practice, paper still counted as technology. Her phone showed three threads open at once: Omar, June, and Nina Solis from the graduate workers union, who had the encrypted archive and instructions to send it wider if all of them lost their nerve at the same time.

June arrived first, coming through the service door with a paper cup of coffee she did not seem to want. Her blunt-cut hair had been hacked shorter again, the line at one side a little crooked. She stopped long enough to flatten the cup between her hands and drop it into the trash before she said, "How many backup plans are we on now."

"Three send paths, two physical packets, and Nina if both of those fail," Leela said. "Four, if you count the timed link."

"That one doesn't count. Timed links are for people who still believe in elegant solutions."

She had nicotine gum in one pocket and her yellow legal pad under the other arm. Leela had not seen the pad in weeks. The sight of it made something tighten and then loosen in the same place. June noticed her looking and shifted the pad against her ribs as if it were not evidence but an ordinary object, grocery list, meeting notes, nothing worth building a future around.

Omar came in a minute later in a navy shirt so crisp it made the atrium's student-volunteer polos look damp. He always dressed more carefully when he was frightened; fear showed first in his jaw and then, if it kept going, in the speed of his hands. Today his hands were quiet. He set his phone on the table between the packets and Leela's laptop.

"I scrubbed the sponsor list against the faculty list," he said. "No duplicates except Gloria and the dean's liaison. The email is drafted. If their network blocks the attachments, I send the drive link. If that fails, I tether."

"And if you don't send anything," June said, not looking at him, "Nina does."

"Yes."

"Good. I like plans that assume character defects."

Ben appeared so suddenly that Leela understood she had been listening for him. He had one of the department's volunteer lanyards on over a pale blue shirt that still held the vertical fold line from the package. Somebody in events had given him a polo to wear and he had draped it over his arm instead, not willing to look that temporary. His sneakers were immaculate. There was a crescent of dried toothpaste near one cuff, as if he had dressed in a hurry and only caught most of himself.

"I came straight over," he said. "I just need to make sure we're still on the same page, because the way June was texting last night made it sound like there was maybe a version where this turns into me being the center of the story, and I did do what you asked, I just can't be the whole public face of it. That was never the agreement."

"Nobody asked for your face," June said.

"That's not what I said."

"It's what I heard."

Leela closed her laptop because the screen glare was making her irritable. "You're not the only release path. That's the point. If you walk out right now, the documents still go."

Ben flinched as if she had slapped him with something harder than the sentence. He looked younger immediately, the way he always did when approval left the room. "I'm here," he said.

"Then stay here," June said. "You don't get points for standing upright."

Omar slid a printed sheet toward Ben. It was the event program with Mohit's speaking slot circled in blue pen and three times written beside it.

"When he starts the Ruderal transition slides, I send," Omar said. "If Gloria or events cuts the projector before then, Leela hands the packets out. If Mohit tries to move this into a side room before there are witnesses, Nina comes forward. If he asks you a direct question, you answer only the question."

Ben stared at the page as though it might become simpler if he refused to blink. "You keep saying Ruderal out loud like that's not insane."

June gave him a flat look. "It stopped being insane when he built a company out of our work."

He turned to Leela. "Are we sure she has to be in this version."

"I'm standing right here," June said.

"I know. I just mean the public piece. Because if he pulls her into it, he's going to make it look personal. He's good at that. He'll make it look like this is about the grievance and Baltimore and all the weird interpersonal stuff, not the documents."

June's face did not change. "You don't know enough to use Baltimore as shorthand."

Leela stepped in before Ben could apologize badly and make the air worse. "The documents go whether June speaks or not."

June looked at her then, properly for the first time that morning. There was less anger in it than there had been in March. That made it more difficult to bear.

"I didn't promise you anything," June said.

"I know."

"He already gave me my off-ramp. Clean little meeting, nice person from industry, whole speech about how I was too smart to tie myself to other people's procedural mistakes. If this turns into him pointing across the room and asking me to help him keep the lie tidy, I want everybody to understand that I know exactly what it costs if I don't."

No one said anything for a moment. Out in the atrium a student volunteer laughed too loudly at something an administrator had not meant as a joke. Plates knocked against one another in the catering kitchen. Through the wall Leela could hear the microphone being tested: one, two, thank you, one, two.

Ben swallowed. "I'm here," he said again, softer this time, as if saying it to himself.

Leela thought: this was what trust had become. Not faith. Not repair. An engineering solution to mutual fear. Assume every person in the room might freeze, defect, vanish, decide the institution was too large and their own life too small. Build anyway.

"All right," she said. "Then nobody is indispensable. That's the only reason this works."

June barked a laugh without humor. "Bleak. Accurate. Fine."

Omar nodded once. "Then we should take our places."

He picked up his phone. Ben picked up the volunteer polo as though he had just remembered he needed a costume. June tore a fresh piece of nicotine gum from the foil and tucked the wrapper into her back pocket instead of throwing it away. Leela slid three of the packets into her tote and felt the paper weight settle against her hip. It felt absurdly small. Months of fear, theft, shame, the archive she had named `holdout_set` on a winter afternoon with numb fingers and a heater rattling in her apartment, reduced to toner and staples.

"One more thing," Ben said, stopping at the door.

Nobody encouraged him.

"If this works," he said, "what actually happens to us."

June pushed the door open. "If you wanted a reassuring meeting, you should've stayed with him."

She went out into the light.

Leela followed more slowly. There was no answer better than that.

* * *

By ten-thirty the atrium had filled with the exact mix of people Haviland liked to photograph: men in soft blazers without ties, women in excellent shoes, trustees wearing badges as if they had invented sponsorship, faculty who had remembered to become genial for one morning, undergraduates standing beside poster boards with the fixed smiles of students who had been told opportunity might walk by in loafers. On the far wall, three large screens looped the same department reel: drone footage of campus, a robot hand sorting colored blocks, a line of code scrolling in blue light for no reason except that somebody in communications thought code looked like progress.

Leela stood near a pillar wrapped in pale oak slats and held a paper plate she had no intention of eating from. The mini quiche on it had gone leathery under the heat lamp. She could feel the packets in her tote whenever someone brushed past her. Every accidental touch made her think security had arrived, though there was no reason for security yet. Her body had been preparing for official punishment for so long that it did not know what to do with an hour in which she was the one who had set the timing.

Across the room, Omar took up position beside a student poster about municipal data governance that nobody had read twice. He looked attentive in the ordinary way: one hand in his pocket, the other curled around his phone, listening to a trustee say something about responsible innovation. Only Leela could see how hard he was holding still.

June stayed near the coffee station at the back. From a distance she could have been mistaken for someone helping with logistics, compact and unsmiling and dressed for utility rather than display. The legal pad was gone from sight. That frightened Leela more than if June had held it up like a weapon. It meant June had not chosen yet whether it was evidence or just paper.

Near the glass doors, Nina stood under a donor banner in a borrowed blazer over her rain shell, visitor badge clipped crooked, looking like she was trying to pass for somebody's late-arriving policy guest. Leela let herself look at her only once. That was enough.

Ben moved through the room with a box of presentation clickers and adapters, trying so hard to look useful that usefulness itself started to look suspicious. He laughed when people laughed at him. He bent too quickly when anyone older asked for something. Once, as he crossed in front of Leela, he mouthed, still okay? without slowing down. She did not answer.

Then Mohit took the stage.

He did not look hurried. He never did. His sleeves were rolled once to the forearm, exact and unrumpled. His expensive glasses caught the light and made the pause before his first sentence feel practiced rather than vain. The opening slide behind him showed an aerial map of a floodplain rendered in Haviland blue and white, the title in thin clean font: CIVIC ROBUSTNESS IN THE WILD.

"Thank you for spending your morning with us," he said, and the trustees in front flattened their programs on their knees while a sponsor near the aisle tipped her face toward the stage, already willing to be persuaded. "We're trying to build systems that survive contact with reality. That sounds obvious, but most machine learning still treats the world as a laboratory that ought to behave."

A few people smiled.

"Our lab has spent the year asking a harder question. What would it mean to train both models and researchers not for ideal conditions, but for adversarial ones? Not for consensus, but for the public mess we actually inhabit?"

At the coffee station June's mouth thinned to a line. A trustee near the front was already nodding. Adversarial, public mess: the language that made strategy sound like courage and cruelty sound like pedagogy. Leela felt the old disgust rise with the older, more humiliating recognition underneath it. He was good at this. He had always been good at it. If she had met him only here, only in this room with the lake light turning the glass walls into a soft gleam and the trustees nodding before the end of his sentences, she would have liked him too.

He moved through the first slides with the fluent pace of someone who understood exactly how long a room could be asked to admire a graph. He thanked students without naming them. He thanked sponsors by name. He used care language and market language in alternating turns, a braid he had spent years perfecting.

"One of the things we've learned," he said, "is that resilience cannot be outsourced to goodwill. You have to design for friction. You have to invite critique early enough that it can still improve the work."

At the back of the room June shifted her weight from one foot to the other. Omar glanced down at his phone once and looked back up.

Mohit advanced to a slide about translational pathways, the phrase glowing in white over a chart of pilot partnerships. He spoke about local government collaborations, licensing possibilities, the moral duty to move tools out of the lab and into institutions that could use them. He did not say Ruderal yet. He did not need to. The sponsors at the center tables had already put on their bright, acquisitive faces, the expression money wore in academia before lunch.

Omar pressed his thumb to the screen.

Nothing changed immediately. The projector still glowed. Mohit's voice kept its even warmth. But ten seconds later a trustee in the front row frowned at his phone. A sponsor beside him did the same. Then someone near the back opened a laptop. Leela heard the thin flock of new-message chimes go off around the atrium, each one small enough to be deniable and together loud enough to alter the air.

Mohit kept talking for one more sentence. "What we're showing today is not just technical performance, but a culture of disciplined mutual challenge-"

He saw Gloria Fisk, halfway to the stage, reading from her phone with her mouth set in a line that meant process was no longer hypothetical.

His pause was almost invisible. That was part of the skill.

"It appears," he said lightly, "that some of you have received an internal packet stripped of context. Since transparency is obviously a value in this room, let me address that directly before rumor has a chance to do what rumor does."

A sponsor lowered her phone. The dean's liaison let his packet rest against his thigh instead of opening it. Of course they did.

"Doctoral mentorship in a fast-moving lab produces partial documents," he said. "You're seeing pieces of red-team exercises, contingent funding discussions, and draft planning notes. We deliberately pressure-test one another's work here. We also discuss scarce resources honestly. If isolated emails now look contradictory, that's because students experience the same evolving situation from different vantage points and private coaching rarely reads well once selectively forwarded."

He smiled, not broad enough to become defensive, just enough to suggest mild regret at everyone else's confusion.

"I would ask that you not mistake academic rigor for misconduct simply because somebody has discovered that screenshots travel faster than trust."

Leela watched the reassembly happen in real time: the donors relieved to hear there was a reasonable adult story available; the faculty calculating how ugly it would be if the unreasonable version proved true; the administrators reaching with their eyes for the nearest smaller, more containable interpretation. Even she felt it, for one hateful second, the desire to let his version win because his version preserved the shape of the world.

Gloria reached the edge of the stage. "Mohit," she said quietly, but the microphone caught it anyway. "Perhaps we should pause here."

"Of course," he said. Then, turning back to the room with that same composed generosity: "I'm happy to answer brief questions now and move the rest into a more appropriate setting. There's nothing here that can't be explained."

That line nearly did it. Leela saw shoulders lower. A man from a foundation board slipped his phone back into his jacket. One of the student volunteers took a dutiful step toward the sponsor cluster as if the whole thing might still be managed into a coffee break.

Leela set her plate on the nearest high-top before the quiche could slide off. Grease streaked her thumb. She pulled the top packet from her tote, crossed into the aisle, and handed it to the woman in the cream blazer with the green scarf.

"Before this gets reduced to a private mentoring dispute," she said, louder than she meant to, "page four is my ablation table. It's from an unsubmitted November run. The orange condition is wrong because it's the draft version from before I fixed it. Page seven has the clone path from the lab server into Professor Saran's Ruderal folder. That's why we printed these."

She gave the next packet to the dean's liaison, then held a third out toward a trustee who accepted it because refusing would have been more conspicuous than taking it.

"If you want a smaller room after that, fine," Leela said. Her voice sounded steadier than the inside of her ribs. "But not before the people already holding this know what they're being asked to ignore."

The woman in the green scarf flipped to page four and stopped touching her neck.

"Professor Saran," she said, eyes still on the paper, "was that work licensed to Ruderal Analytics or not."

Ruderal Analytics cut through the atrium cleaner than any accusation could have.

Mohit turned to her as if grateful for the chance to be precise. "Ruderal was an exploratory translational vehicle. The deck in circulation was a draft mock-up assembled from approved aggregate results to test external interest. Under ordinary university policy, materials developed inside the lab fall under institutional research governance. No student-owned intellectual property was transferred. Anybody familiar with early-stage research translation will understand how ordinary that is."

He said it smoothly enough that ordinary almost sounded factual. The phrase university policy landed exactly where he had aimed it. One of the faculty sponsors lowered his packet a few inches. Gloria lifted a calming hand.

"We are not adjudicating an ownership dispute in the atrium," Gloria said. "Mohit, Leela, June, and Ben, come with me. We can review the documents upstairs with the appropriate offices."

He looked past Gloria toward June beside the coffee urn before anyone moved.

"June," he said, gentle as a mentor checking whether a student wanted to add nuance. "You were part of some of those translational conversations. If it'd be helpful, you can clarify that these were mock-up materials, not a production claim on anyone's dissertation."

Two trustees who had been half turning toward the corridor stopped with their programs still folded in their hands. Near the coffee urn, somebody set down a cup too hard. The spoon inside rang once and kept trembling.

June put her coffee down on the floor. She wiped her hand once on the side seam of her pants. When she spoke her voice was so flat with fear that it sounded calm.

"No," she said. "I can't clarify that, because it isn't true."

June stepped away from the coffee station and into the open aisle between the sponsor tables. She did not hurry. Leela thought, wildly, absurdly, of all the times June had walked across the lab with a plate of takeout balanced on one hand, swearing at benchmarks and bad code and people who called anything lightweight when somebody else had to carry it. Now she moved like somebody carrying something that would break her if she gripped it wrong.

"I found `ruderal_pitch_v3.pdf` in Baltimore in November," June said. "Not in some hypothetical future deck. In a real folder, on a real laptop, while you were off taking side meetings during the conference you were supposed to be at with us."

Nobody interrupted her.

"Slide fourteen used my evaluation figure and Leela's ablation table from a run we hadn't submitted anywhere yet. I know it was ours because the orange condition still had my bad label on it. I mistyped the seed, got the wrong shoulder on the bar, fixed it later, and the wrong version is still in that deck. That's not approved aggregate anything. That's a stolen draft."

Her hand went into the pocket with the nicotine gum and came back out with a folded sheet torn from the yellow legal pad.

"And since he likes context so much, here is some. November sixteenth, Baltimore, 5:40 p.m., outside Ballroom C, he tells me translational visibility matters more than authorship timing and that I should trust him to place the work correctly once the market is clearer. February nineteenth, in his office after Ben forwarded the grievance, he offers me an introduction if I stop attaching myself to this. Those are my notes, because he changed the story every week and I got tired of pretending memory was enough."

She looked at Mohit then. Not pleading. Not furious. Just direct in the way she only got when she was done spending language on style.

"Don't use me to clean it up."

For the first time since taking the stage, he seemed to misjudge the faces in front of him by a fraction. It was barely visible. A tightening at the corners of his mouth. A new stillness around the eyes.

"June," he said, "I think you're collapsing several separate conversations."

"No," she said. "I'm finally lining them up."

He turned quickly, not to her but to the rest of them, trying to make her line sound emotional and his sound structural.

"This is exactly what I mean by partial records. A student under stress may reasonably overread a draft diligence exercise, especially when personal grievances are already attached to it."

Gloria took another step toward the aisle. "All right. Enough. We are not finishing this here. Relevant parties, with me now."

Two of the student volunteers started drifting forward again, relieved by the return of instructions. Ben did not move. He stood by the projector cart with the spare clickers in one hand and the volunteer polo balled under the other arm, pale under the atrium light.

"Ben," Leela said.

He looked at her the way people looked at stairwells during fire alarms, as if he might still be told there was a better exit.

"Tell them where he had you bring the hard copies."

His mouth opened. Closed. The woman in the green scarf held her packet half-folded. Gloria kept one hand lifted and still. Even the volunteers stopped drifting. Under that silence, he finally spoke.

"I printed it," he said, the second word cracking. "In November. Before Baltimore was over, actually. You emailed it to me and told me to bring hard copies to your hotel for a breakfast thing with two people from industry. I thought there was permission. You said there was permission."

Mohit's head turned toward him with almost paternal disbelief. "Ben, slow down. You're describing a sponsor breakfast. Preliminary decks get shown in translational meetings all the time. That is not the same as transferring student rights."

"It had the Ruderal logo already on it." Ben was talking too fast now, words bumping into one another. "I remember because I thought the logo looked kind of bad and I asked if somebody in design was fixing it and you said not to worry about the logo, worry about getting the color copies because investors hate grayscale. That's what you said."

The woman in the green scarf folded the packet shut with a neat, offended precision. "If this deck went to outside diligence before title was clear," she said, "our counsel will need written provenance before anything connected to Ruderal moves another inch."

That sentence froze the volunteers better than Gloria had. Nobody went upstairs.

Nina was already moving before she spoke, her bent visitor badge flashing once under the lights as she came in from the back. "I represent the graduate workers union," she said. "The best next step is preservation on the record, in front of the witnesses who have already received aligned documents. Not a side conversation that produces a newer, cleaner story."

Gloria stared at her. "This is not a union matter."

"Graduate labor and supervisory misconduct in a funded lab are exactly a union matter."

Leela watched Gloria absorb the sponsor, the faculty, the students, the packets now open in three different sets of hands, June's legal pad, Ben's face gone waxy with panic, Mohit's expression collapsing inward toward pure calculation. She watched the arithmetic finish.

When Gloria spoke again, the cushioning had dropped out of her voice.

"All right," she said. "Because disputed student work appears to have been circulated externally, and because preservation has to start now, effective immediately Professor Saran is on administrative leave pending formal investigation. You are not to contact students about lab business, access Keating systems, or remove research materials from your office until counsel and OIT complete preservation. Nobody deletes anything. Nobody edits anything. If you have documents relevant to this matter, you send them to my office and to Research Integrity by close of business."

The microphone carried every word into the glass and back.

Someone near the catering table let out a little breath that sounded almost like a laugh and then covered it with a cough. One of the trustees took his badge off and folded the lanyard in half, because apparently even shock could become manners.

Mohit did not raise his voice. That would have been unlike him, and anyway he did not need it.

"Gloria," he said, "you're making a procedural decision in the middle of a coordinated retaliation campaign."

"Then you can make that argument after the records are preserved."

"You are rewarding theft of internal communications."

"I'm stopping you from supervising students this afternoon. The rest can wait."

His eyes moved once across the room and landed on Leela. Not for long. Long enough. She could not read the expression fully because he was too practiced, but she recognized its structure: calculation under insult, the immediate search for survivable lines of control. For a second she had the deranging thought that he was already advising himself through it.

Then Gloria gestured to the dean's liaison and an IT staffer who had materialized near the stage, and the spell broke for everyone at once. The open house became an event again, but now it was an event that had failed in public. Sponsors clustered. Faculty performed concern. The undergraduates by the posters stared openly because nobody had yet taught them the adult version of not seeing.

June bent, picked her coffee up from the floor, and threw it away.

* * *

By noon the atrium smelled less like pastries and more like cold eggs and panic. Catering staff cleared plates nobody had touched. Somebody from communications had turned the department reel off, leaving the far screens dark and reflective, which only made the room look more like an aquarium after the pump failed.

Leela ended up in the hallway outside the basement lab with Omar, June, and Ben while an IT woman in a mustard cardigan photographed the lock on Mohit's office. She had a tiny bandage around one finger and kept tucking a loose braid behind her ear with the back of her hand so she would not smear the adhesive. The legal hold notices looked flimsy for what they had just done.

The lab itself still smelled the same: burnt coffee, hot dust, old couch fabric, the server closet breathing its dry mechanical heat into the room as if nothing in the world had changed. Whiteboard marker ghosts layered the walls. An abandoned ramen cup sat beside the printer. Somebody had left three clementines in a bowl to rot into leather. The place had survived too many semesters by making squalor look temporary. Today it looked mortal.

Ben leaned against the door frame, pale under the fluorescent wash. He had finally put on the volunteer polo, maybe because he needed the extra layer, maybe because once everything fell apart he wanted to resemble the harmless version of himself again.

"So what happens to the papers," he asked. "I mean the actual in-progress ones. The workshop submission, the journal revision, all of that. Because he's on all of them."

No one answered right away.

An email hit Leela's phone. Then another. She opened the first one and felt the familiar administrative nausea spread through her ribs.

All Civic Signals Lab repositories are hereby placed under preservation hold. No modifications may be made pending review.

The second was from the department office.

All external communications regarding sponsor partnerships, conference travel, recommendation requests, and submission timelines for Civic Signals affiliates should be directed to the chair until interim supervision is assigned.

It was astonishing how quickly consequence learned to write itself in bulletproof institutional prose.

"That," June said, reading over Leela's shoulder, "answers the paper question."

Omar had his own phone out. He was scrolling too fast, then backing up and reading again, the way he did when he wanted panic to behave like analysis.

"My summer funding letter names him as PI," he said. "If grants accounting freezes the account, International Students won't care that today was ethically clarifying."

June leaned against one of the stained lab couches and looked up at the ceiling. "Jesus."

"No," Omar said, with a dry, exhausted precision that was almost a joke. "Unfortunately just the university."

Ben laughed once because he was scared, then stopped because nobody joined him.

Leela moved farther into the room. The couch arm under her hand had gone shiny with years of sleeves, spilled food, anxious waiting. In the first weeks of the year the lab had still possessed the shabby cooperative feeling of a place people used hard and forgave. A rice cooker under somebody's desk. Shared takeout. Emergency chargers. Code screens left open while one person explained a proof to another with the fervor of the undercaffeinated. Mohit had poisoned that long before today. Even so, watching the institution finally name him dangerous enough for paperwork did not return the room to innocence. It turned the room into a scene.

June pushed herself off the couch. "That intro is dead."

She said it almost casually. The sentence took a second to land.

Leela looked at her. "Probably."

"Good," June said, then shook her head. "No. Not good. Just clean, finally."

Her voice had none of the brightness she used when she wanted to make a line look easy. She pulled another piece of nicotine gum out and stared at the empty foil sleeve before crumpling it. "He looked right at me and thought I would help him tell it. That was the part that got me. Not even the deck. Not even Baltimore. That look."

Leela knew exactly which look she meant. The soft one. The intimate managerial one. The version of trust that only ran downward.

"I know," she said.

June gave a short nod as if that was enough and maybe all they could safely afford.

Ben had taken the volunteer polo back off. He was worrying one sleeve between his fingers until the cloth twisted. "Am I completely fucked," he asked.

June laughed this time, but there was no meanness in it. Only fatigue. "That's a huge question, Ben."

"I mean program-wise."

Omar looked up from his phone. "You mean career-wise, advisor-wise, and morally, but you're selecting the smallest category because it feels most answerable."

"Okay," Ben said. "Yes. That."

No one rescued him. Leela could see that he wanted rescue anyway, wanted the older people in the room to tell him he had finally done one redeeming thing and would be graded on the curve. He had been raised, she thought not without bitterness, inside systems that always had another adult farther up the staircase. Today the staircase ended in fluorescent light and a legal hold notice.

"I don't know," she said.

It was the truest thing available. It landed on him harder than blame would have.

Another message arrived, this one from Gloria's office requesting that all current advisees and affiliates remain available for interviews over the next seventy-two hours and refrain from discussing the matter with external press or sponsors. Press. The word would have seemed melodramatic yesterday. Now it sat on her screen like an overdue bill.

Ben read the same email on his phone and swore under his breath, the profanity awkward in his mouth. "My parents are going to see something somewhere and call me. They Google me. They Google all of this."

"That's not the worst thing that's about to happen," June said, but not cruelly.

Omar set his phone down on the table with more care than the object required. "I need to figure out by this afternoon whether somebody else can sign for summer funding before the office closes. If the answer is no, I can't wait for Gloria to feel principled on the right timeline."

He was already turning toward logistics because if he did not, fear would turn him inside out. Leela respected him for it and hated that the story kept rewarding that kind of speed.

"I'll come with you to Gloria," she said.

He nodded once. "Thank you."

June rubbed a thumb over the spiral edge of the legal pad. "I've got interviews next week."

"I know," Leela said.

"I'm not saying that for sympathy."

"I know."

"I might still take the first decent offer and get the fuck out."

"That would be rational."

June snorted. "You really know how to sweet-talk a girl."

There was an old rhythm buried in that line, not restored, not safe, but briefly audible. Leela felt it like a bruise pressed lightly. She almost smiled. Almost.

The IT woman in the mustard cardigan came to the lab door. "I need everyone to understand the server closet is under preservation too," she said. "Please don't badge in there unless one of us is with you. Also, if this mini fridge belongs to students and not the lab, label it now, because facilities is asking embarrassing questions."

She said it with the embarrassed practicality of somebody who had entered the day expecting hardware tickets and now had to improvise around scandal and yogurt. June, despite herself, barked out a laugh.

"Second shelf is mine," she said. "If anybody throws out the kimchi, I'll create a separate incident."

The IT woman nodded solemnly. "Noted."

When she left, the room went quiet again except for the fans in the closet and the fluorescent hum that meant silence in Keating was never silence, only electricity pretending.

Leela sat at the edge of one of the desks because her knees had started to feel unreliable. Through the narrow basement window the day had gone gray, spring light turning thin and mean over the service road. She thought of her credit-card balance. Of graduate housing. Of the postponed defense that was still postponed, Mohit on leave or not. Administrative leave would not rewind March. It would not make the committee forget the version of her work he had already seeded into them. The institution had finally believed enough to stop him in public and still had not promised any of them a life after the stop.

That, she thought, was the most faithful part. Not the fall. The bill.

June moved to stand beside her. Close enough that Leela could smell coffee and stale mint from the gum.

"Don't thank me," June said.

"I wasn't going to."

"Good."

Another beat passed.

"You can later," June said.

Leela laughed then, unexpectedly and without elegance, the sound scraping on the way out. It hurt a little. It also helped.

Omar gathered his folder, already preparing to go upstairs to Gloria before the office corridor filled with faculty advising one another how shocked to sound. Ben stayed where he was, staring at the lab as if an adult might still emerge from the server closet and explain the next semester to him. June tore the used top sheet off her legal pad and folded it smaller, once, then again, until it disappeared into her fist.

Around them the Civic Signals Lab remained stubbornly itself for a few more minutes: the dead marker smell, the old upholstery, the server heat breathing out of the closet, the ugly little ecosystem that had fed them and used them both. Then the red light on the badge reader at Mohit's office blinked from green to solid red. Even Ben stopped twisting the polo. Nobody reached for the office door again.

They had stopped him. That part was true.

The rest of it was true too: the summer funding that might vanish, the papers stranded mid-sentence, the recommendation network gone radioactive, the degree timelines already broken, the simple fact that there was no clean institution waiting behind the dirty man. The lab had finally stopped belonging to him by becoming a place that did not belong to any of them, only to the offices upstairs already translating damage into procedure.


# Chapter 18

The offices upstairs had translated the damage into a memorandum. It called the result a change in supervisory circumstances.

Leela read the phrase three times because the first two had not felt insulting enough. The PDF sat open on her laptop beside the stripped-down dissertation, both windows full of tracked changes and institutional blue headers, both pretending language could make scale disappear. Change in supervisory circumstances. As if Mohit had taken a sabbatical. As if he had developed a polite rash and not spent a year turning his students into weapons against one another, then funneled their work into a startup deck with his soft little mentor's smile still warm on it.

Her studio was hot in the stale, boxed-in way graduate housing got hot once the radiators finally shut up and the building remembered it had no idea what to do with summer. The cheap blinds clicked against the frame. A fan pushed the air from one side of the room to the other without improving it. On the mattress behind her sat three stacks of papers she kept meaning to sort: committee forms, credit-card statements, and the dissertation drafts from before her project had been reduced to something survivable.

The current file was one hundred sixty-eight pages if she counted the bibliography and the appendix she no longer respected. In January it had been two hundred forty-two. The missing pages had not vanished cleanly. She had cut away the benchmark chapter whose figures had shown up, translated and polished, in `ruderal_pitch_v3.pdf`. She had cut the section with the robustness result that had not replicated cleanly and had looked so beautiful in a conference template that she had nearly believed it herself. She had cut every paragraph that still carried Mohit's phrasing in the joints. What remained was individually attributable work, which was the department's phrase for the part of the wreckage they could process without having to confess how the wreckage had been built.

The memo listed the conditions of her provisional submission:

1. revised committee composition
2. narrowed research claims
3. external review to follow defense scheduling
4. no use of disputed laboratory materials beyond already archived public artifacts

Disputed laboratory materials was another good one. The year had apparently been composed of materials and circumstances, weather systems and office supplies, not Omar in the graduate office with both hands flat on the desk because if he picked up his phone too fast it shook, not June in the Baltimore bathroom with conference makeup gone gray under fluorescent light, saying say it plain, if he stole it then say stole, and later, hours later, June's hand over Leela's mouth on the hotel-room floor because the walls were thin and wanting anything that badly felt louder than grief, not the smell of the basement lab after midnight, hot dust and old upholstery and the sweet rot of coffee left too long on a warmer, all of it mixed with the intimate panic of realizing the room where you had spent six years had been training you to accept smaller and smaller humiliations as ordinary.

She highlighted the phrase external review to follow defense scheduling and replaced follow with inform. It was useless and made her feel better for six seconds.

Her phone buzzed with a calendar reminder: Baines Hall, 10:00 a.m., Fisk.

The walk across campus took eight minutes if she cut behind the library. June sun had reached that early point where it looked generous from a distance and mean once it hit concrete. Students in commencement leftovers still moved through the quad with their families, all of them carrying flower paper and shiny envelopes. Leela felt wrong among them in the way she had felt wrong among undergraduates since she was twenty-six. Too old for ceremony, too unfinished for pride, carrying not flowers but a manila folder with the memo, the revised title page, and a printout of the Montrose State College posting Gloria had forwarded the week before with the subject line Worth considering.

Baines Hall smelled like artificial citrus and vacuumed carpet. It always did. The building had the acoustic quality of a place already preparing its own transcript.

Gloria Fisk was in the conference room with her paper folder and a coffee so weak it looked medicinal. Her silver hair was perfect from ten feet away and more tired up close. Today she had added a yellow butterscotch candy in a twist of cellophane, set beside her legal pad as if she had packed herself a treat for surviving a meeting she did not want.

"You found the place," Gloria said.

"I have actually been summoned here before."

Gloria gave a quick breath that might have been a laugh if she had trusted the room more. "Fair enough. Sit."

Leela sat. The chair was upholstered in the exact fabric Haviland must have bought by the acre in 1998 and never questioned again. Gloria opened the folder, arranged the papers with small precise taps, and for one second looked less like a chair and more like a woman trying to keep paper from replacing blood entirely.

"I've reviewed the revised draft," she said. "This is workable."

Workable was the little promotion from circumstances and materials. Leela folded her hands under the table so she would not touch her face.

"That's not the same as good," Leela said.

"No," Gloria said. "It isn't. But it is defensible, which is what the degree requires at this point."

At this point was another administrative miracle. It packed a whole year into four words and left the damage outside.

Gloria pushed the memo across. "You will submit under substitute supervision. Professor Halliday has agreed to chair the committee on a provisional basis. The defense itself will likely be late summer. Possibly early fall, depending on external review timing."

"And the review?"

Gloria looked at the papers instead of at Leela. "Continues."

"That's an elegant verb for not saying anything."

"It is the accurate verb."

Leela almost said accurate according to whom, but Gloria was tired in a way she recognized from mirrors, and the question would not improve the answer. Through the glass wall she could see an administrator walking past with a box of catered sandwiches from some event already better funded than the end of her degree.

Gloria took off her glasses, polished them on the hem of a handkerchief, and put them back on. "I want to be direct about one thing. The final report is not going to use the words you would use."

"Which words are those?"

"You know which words."

She meant words like stole, threatened, and coerced, words that would also have had to include the fact that Mohit built a private company out of student labor while teaching them to call the arrangement realism. Leela let the list pass through her without speaking it. There was no pleasure in making Gloria hear it.

"The current findings language is about disclosure failures, authorship misrepresentation, and inappropriate entrepreneurial overlap." Gloria said the phrase as if she had not authored it but could not deny custody. "There may also be findings on advising conduct."

June would have laughed out loud. Omar would have become so precise his sentences would start clicking at the joints. Leela only stared at the candy on the table and thought entrepreneurial overlap sounded like two strollers bumping on a sidewalk.

"He cloned our repositories," she said.

"I know."

"He used my credentials to route part of it."

"I know."

"He held Omar's visa letter until it was almost too late."

For the first time Gloria's face tightened in something less administrative than annoyance. "I know what happened. Knowing and writing a report are not the same function."

There it was, finally, the private register slipping through with all the padding stripped off. It landed harder because it was true.

Leela sat back. The chair made a sound like a cough. "So what's the report for?"

Gloria's eyes moved toward the door, then back to Leela. "Containment, if you want the vulgar answer. Process, if you want the donor-safe one. A record, if you want the answer I can say without hating myself."

The room went quiet except for the vent. Leela had not expected honesty from Gloria and disliked the brief urge to reward it.

"All right," she said.

Gloria nodded once and turned a page. "The Montrose position is still open."

The words landed harder than anything about the report, not because they were cruel but because they were practical. The one-year teaching fellowship at Montrose State College was in New York but not near anything that would count as leverage. It meant four courses a semester, advising, committee service if needed, and a salary just high enough to be called humane by people who had not graded eighty first-year coding assignments in a week. Gloria had sent the ad with two sentences: They need someone quickly. You would be good at this.

"I spoke with the chair there," Gloria said. "Not at length. He has a son with celiac disease and spent ten minutes describing bread to me, so I can vouch for nothing except that he is a real person. But they can move fast. If you want it, I believe the offer will be formal by this afternoon."

Leela blinked. The detail about the bread was so unnecessary it made the whole thing feel more real, which was probably why Gloria had included it. Or maybe Gloria had simply been unable to help herself. Administrators also wanted proof that the people at the other end of their phone calls had kitchens.

"Why?" Leela asked.

"Why can they move fast?"

"Why did you call them?"

Gloria held her gaze. "Because your advisor cannot write a letter. Because this department can offer sympathy more easily than bridge funding. Because you need an income in August whether the external review has found its preferred nouns or not. Choose whichever answer offends you least."

Leela looked down at the Montrose printout in her folder. She had circled the salary last week and then written `Hudson Federal 18th` in the margin like a joke so bad only she would understand it. Four thousand eight hundred dollars of card debt did not care whether her future felt narratively satisfying. Graduate housing ended thirty days after a successful defense or sixty days after loss of student status, depending on which office she asked and how badly they wanted her gone. A one-year fellowship was not what she had thought six years in a PhD was for. It was, however, a thing with health insurance and a date certain.

"I'd have to move."

"Yes."

"I'd be teaching intro Python to nineteen-year-olds who still think Git is a social problem."

Gloria's mouth twitched. "Almost certainly."

"And machine learning ethics to business majors."

"Probably that too."

Leela thought of Mohit telling her, years ago, that she had a research temperament. He had said it with such proprietary warmth that she had carried the sentence around like a reference letter before learning that he handed different versions of the same sentence to whoever needed feeding. Research temperament. Now she had a one-year teaching fellowship at a state college she had never once imagined while constructing the grand theoretical life she had supposedly been building in a basement without windows.

"You'd be good at it," Gloria said again, and this time Leela heard the other clause inside it: it is also what there is.

She nodded. "Tell them yes."

Gloria wrote the word on her pad. No ceremony. No congratulations. A brief scratched yes, then a line under it. "Good. The formal offer should come today. Sign the submission memo before you leave."

Leela signed in three places, beside Candidate, Provisional committee approval, and Acknowledgment of revised scope. The university pen dragged at the loops in her name. By the time she slid the papers back across the table she felt neither relief nor defeat, only the blunt metabolic drop that came when a decision stopped being hypothetical and started billing you.

"For what it's worth," Gloria said, stacking the pages, "smaller isn't always lesser."

Leela stood. "That's a line you should workshop before giving it to students."

"I wasn't planning to put it in a brochure."

"Good."

Gloria picked up the butterscotch candy and turned it once between her fingers. "No. Probably not."

Outside Baines, Leela stood in the heat and checked her phone. There was already an email from Montrose Human Resources with the subject line Visiting Teaching Fellowship Offer Letter. She opened the PDF in the patchy shade of a campus maple while a family nearby took graduation photos in matching linen. The offer listed salary, start date, courses assigned as needed by departmental demand, the contingency of degree completion or documented ABD status, and benefits beginning September 1. A draft teaching load sat behind it in a second attachment: Intro Python, Data Ethics for Managers, and a lab section nobody had mentioned in the posting.

Before she reached the signature page, Meera called. If Leela let it ring out, Meera would call again, and then Anita would call with the worry already fully cooked. She answered.

"Tell me how bad it is," Meera said. There was a car door thump and then the sealed quiet of a parked vehicle. "I have seven minutes before a crown prep."

"I can submit."

"That's not what I asked."

Leela pressed her shoulder against the tree. Bark snagged lightly at her shirt. "Substitute chair. External review still going. Defense maybe late summer. Maybe fall."

A beat of silence. "In March you told Amma June was paperwork."

"I know what I told her."

"I'm not scoring you. I'm trying to stop her from telling three aunties you'll be done by July."

That landed exactly where it should. Leela watched a little boy in a rented cap kick at the roots around the tree while his mother kept trying to hand him flowers for the photo.

"Gloria also got me a job," she said. "Montrose. One year."

"What kind of one year?"

"Four courses a semester. Probably a lab. Forty-four."

Meera let out a breath through her nose. "Do they give you moving money?"

"No."

"Housing?"

"I don't know yet."

"Then find out today. And if the first paycheck is late or they want a deposit you can't cover, tell me before you put it on a card."

Leela stared at the salary line on the PDF. "You make it sound very dignified."

"I make it sound like rent." Meera's voice softened without getting prettier. "Do you want it, or do you need it?"

"I need August not to arrive empty."

"Fine. Then take it and call it what it is." A rustle on the line, keys maybe. "I'll tell Amma the defense moved again. You tell her about Montrose after you've eaten something."

Leela closed her eyes. "Okay."

"And Leela."

"What?"

"I'm sorry this is what they left you with."

The sentence was so plain it got past her defenses before she could do anything intelligent with it. "Yeah," she said. "Me too."

When the call ended, the offer portal had timed out. She logged back in, entered the temporary code again, and clicked accept while the tree's shadow shivered across the screen. The fellowship began, not with rescue or conviction, but with a checkbox and a salary thin enough to count in her head.

An hour later Montrose sent a faculty ID setup link, a payroll packet, and the same draft fall schedule. That was how the fellowship continued, not with a clean escape but with logins.

Back in her studio she opened the dissertation portal. The site had confetti-colored icons and a banner that read Congratulations on this milestone. Leela laughed once, out loud, a short ugly sound that made the room feel smaller. She uploaded the PDF, the memo, the scope statement, the committee revision form. She checked a box confirming that the work was her own to the extent described in accompanying materials, which was apparently how Haviland preferred to phrase a year of theft, panic, silence, and reciprocal contamination.

When the progress bar hit one hundred percent, she expected something bodily, maybe nausea or tears or the simple animal drop of terror. Instead she noticed the fan clicking every seventh rotation and the sharp imprint of the desk edge against her forearm. The body was always a little humiliating that way. It refused the big theater and delivered a minor detail.

Submission complete, the portal said.

Below that: Thank you for your contribution to the university's scholarly community.

Her phone buzzed.

June: you alive

Another message before Leela answered.

June: last tuesday dinner before i go become a corporate evaluation ghoul

June: bring beer or contempt, i have enough plates for exactly neither

Leela typed `I can do both` and sent it before she had time to decide whether the line sounded too much like their old selves.

* * *

June's apartment looked as if somebody had tried to move out and then gotten distracted by the problem of having once lived there. Half the books were boxed and half still leaned above the folding table. The good knives were wrapped in a dish towel on the counter with PACK LAST written across the towel in black marker. A roll of packing tape hung from June's wrist like a bracelet she hated. Her blunt-cut hair had fresh uneven edges, which meant she had trimmed it herself in a bathroom mirror and not cared if the back came out wrong.

"You actually brought both," she said when Leela held up the beer and the takeout bag.

"I respect the brief."

June took the beer, popped the cardboard top, and called over her shoulder, "Door's open if that's Omar. If it's a murderer, tell him to wait until after dumplings."

It was Omar. He came in with a white bakery box balanced on one hand and looked, as usual, as if he had dressed carefully in order to argue with the day's humiliations before they could begin. Even in June's heat-trap kitchen he still had the collar buttoned on his shirt. The box had gone soft with syrup in one corner.

"The baklava suffered in transit," he said. "You will have to accept it as a moral rather than structural offering."

June snorted. "Perfect. That's also what the university gave us."

Omar set the box down, glanced around the apartment at the open cartons and missing chairs, and for one raw second looked too young for his careful beard. "You're really leaving."

"Tomorrow morning." June pulled a nicotine-gum blister pack out of her pocket with the same motion someone else might have used for cigarettes. "If I stay any longer, Haviland will figure out a way to award me a certificate in resilience."

"Frame it," Leela said. "Hang it next to your employee badge."

"I'm not getting a badge. I'm getting a laptop and a Slack invitation to a company with a name like ScalarForge or moralpanic.ai. I told you, it's bleak."

It was not called either of those things. It was called Mercer Metrics, which sounded like a consulting firm built entirely out of beige carpet. June had described the job over text as model evaluation for companies too embarrassed to admit they had built wrappers around other people's systems. The pay was good enough to say yes quickly and not so good that the yes felt clean.

They ate on paper plates because the real plates were already boxed. June had packed the tablecloth but not the cheap folding table itself, as if the table had earned more loyalty by being ugly in public. The dumplings sweated through their containers. Outside the open window somebody on Alder Street was revving a motorcycle with the lonely commitment of a man who badly wanted witnesses.

June cracked a beer and slid another to Leela. "You first. Did the empire let you submit your corpse dissertation?"

"I submitted."

"And?"

Leela shrugged. "Now I wait for them to decide whether survival counts as scholarship."

"It never has before."

"Gloria got me the Montrose job."

June stopped halfway through peeling the paper off a pair of chopsticks. "Seriously."

"Seriously."

"Jesus. Four-four?"

"Four-four."

"That's witness protection with office hours."

Omar laughed, then looked guilty for enjoying it and laughed harder. "I'm sorry. That is very cruel and also exactly right."

"It's fine," Leela said. "They also might give me first-year ethics for computer science."

"Even funnier."

"I know."

June leaned back in her chair and looked at Leela for a long second, the joke thinning around the edges. "You taking it?"

"I already took it. Meera called while I was signing and asked whether forty-four thousand dollars came with moving money."

"Did it?"

"No."

"Then they dared you to live on it and called it a fellowship," June said.

"It's also income," Omar said.

"I know," Leela said. "I took it because August keeps happening."

"Good." June tore open the nicotine gum. "Pride is a luxury product. We don't stock it here."

Omar wiped syrup from his thumb with a napkin. "Gloria finally sorted out my status too. Or perhaps sorted is generous. There's a path now, though it still feels like stepping into air."

Leela looked at him. "What kind of path?"

"Provisional co-advising with Rina Patel in applied math, if the dean approves summer bridge funding. If not, they are prepared to discuss what they keep calling an alternative completion option."

"Terminal master's exit," June translated.

"Yes." He smiled without humor. "I appreciate how our institution always chooses the phrase least likely to alarm a donor at lunch."

"What did the visa office say?"

Omar took a drink before answering. "They said my documentation is currently sufficient. The word opened in the middle of the sentence like a trapdoor."

The word sat there between the baklava box and the sweating dumplings while soy sauce soaked through one corner of Leela's paper plate and touched the table. She slid a napkin underneath and thought of Omar carrying an entire future on language that flimsy.

June reached behind her and tugged a stapled packet from one of the boxes. "Speaking of language crimes. Gloria sent me the preliminary findings summary."

"Of course she did," Leela said.

"I think as a treat."

June handed the packet across. The first page was full of headings and restrained nouns. Findings regarding disclosure practices. Findings regarding supervisory climate. Findings regarding external commercial entanglement. Leela read three paragraphs and had to put it down.

"Supervisory climate," June said. "Like the lab got mold."

"Climate is a useful word," Omar said. "It suggests weather. It lets everyone behave as though they merely forgot an umbrella."

Leela found the line about inappropriate entrepreneurial overlap and felt actual laughter rise in her throat, ugly and involuntary. "Overlap. He stole our work and the report sounds like two circles on a whiteboard touching by accident."

"Don't forget advising conduct," June said. "Maybe they'll put him in timeout and ask him to complete a module."

"With reflection questions," Omar said. "Name a time when a student's immigration status was not an appropriate mentoring lever."

That got all three of them. The laughter went longer than the joke deserved because the alternative was the report itself. June bent over the table, coughing into her elbow. Omar took off his glasses to wipe his eyes. Leela laughed until the room blurred and then hated the blur because she did not want to cry in June's half-packed kitchen with dumpling grease on her hands.

When the laughter died, June tapped the packet flat against the table.

"I don't know what I expected," she said. "Not justice. I'm not twelve. But this thing doesn't even have enough nouns to be insulting properly."

"It can't use the nouns," Leela said. "If it did, they'd have to explain how long everybody watched."

June looked at her with the blunt attention she used when jokes were over, direct enough that Leela had to keep going.

"They'd have to explain why I sat on the logs," she said. "Why you took the meeting. Why Omar touched the baseline. Why Ben kept hovering until hovering became forwarding. The clean version is that Mohit did it to us. The dirty version is that he built a room where every bad choice came with an invoice."

Omar's jaw shifted, that tiny sign that fear and pride used the same muscles on him. "The report has no use for the dirty version."

"No," Leela said. "And I don't know what to do with the part that stays."

June got up, crossed to the counter, and came back with her yellow legal pad, the one she had been carrying through the grievance months like a second spine. The cover was bent, the top page scarred with pen pressure from older notes. She put it beside the packet, then looked at Leela.

"We decide that tonight," she said. "Before I go. What happens to `holdout_set`?"

Leela had brought the drive in her bag without telling herself she had decided anything. It was small enough to fit in her fist, matte black, no bigger than an old phone. Inside it sat the logs, the deck versions, Omar's letter, June's notes, the contradictory project briefs, and enough timestamps to make an administrator break into hives. Also inside it sat their own uglier residues, the parts the official report had no use for because institutions liked victims best when they came pre-separated from compromise.

She set the drive on the table.

"The lawyer still has a copy," Omar said.

"I know."

"Then we could delete ours."

June's expression flattened. "Do you want to?"

Omar thought. He always did. Even now, with beer on the table and syrup drying at the corner of the bakery box, he gave the question full intellectual dignity before he answered. "Some days, yes. I know forgetting isn't possible. I'm just tired of evidence as a way of living."

Leela felt that in her back teeth. The months of folders and hashes and forwarding chains, the careful naming of files so panic did not eat chronology, the sense that even friendship now required metadata.

"But?" June said.

"But if we delete it," Omar said, "then the report becomes the whole available memory. And the report is a coward."

The room went still in the specific way only June's kitchen could manage: Alder Street suddenly quiet, the refrigerator clicking on, the packing tape tacky against June's wrist when she lifted her beer.

"I don't want to send it wider yet," Leela said. "It isn't because I'm noble. The next people who get it will flatten it in a different direction. They'll want one monster and three deserving students and maybe a cautionary op-ed. That's not what happened either."

"No," June said. "It isn't."

She was looking at the drive, not at Leela. The side of her thumb still had a little ridge of adhesive from the tape. Leela remembered that thumb inside her mouth in Baltimore and then, immediately after, remembered June not answering three of her calls in March. The body did not sort chronology into what was useful.

"I'm not asking for a retroactive happy ending," June said, still looking at the table. "For us, I mean."

Omar looked politely away.

Leela let out a breath she had been holding for months under different names. "Good. I don't have one."

June nodded once. "Okay."

The simplicity of it hurt more than a fight would have, precisely because nothing closed. The truth between them had finally gotten small enough to carry without performance. They had wanted each other. They had also failed each other in sequence and in detail. Both things would remain true in Brooklyn, in Montrose, in whatever office Omar ended up pleading his future inside.

June picked up the drive and turned it over in her hand. "We keep calling it `holdout_set`, right?"

"I think so," Omar said. "The name still fits."

"Say why," June said.

He gave her an annoyed look that was mostly cover for thought. "Because a holdout set is the portion you don't let the model train on if you actually want to know whether it understands anything. You keep something back. Otherwise it only learns the house answers."

June set the drive down again and looked at Leela.

"And this?" she said.

Leela looked from the drive to the packet from Gloria to the legal pad with its dented cardboard cover. "This is your pad, Omar's letter, my logs, the deck versions, the cloned commits with my login on them."

June smiled, quick and humorless. "Gross. Correct, but gross."

"My specialty."

"No argument."

Omar reached for the legal pad and tore off a clean page. "Then we keep it privately. Encrypted. With all three of us."

"Not Ben?" June asked.

It was the first time his name had entered the room. Leela thought of Ben's expensive winter jackets, his appalled little-boy face at demo day when events had finally outrun audition. He had sent an apology the length of a fellowship statement the week before. She had read the first six lines and archived it.

"I don't know," she said.

"He should not have sole custody of a houseplant," June said. "I'm not giving him this."

"Agreed," Omar said. "No offense to houseplants."

The joke landed lightly and stayed light, which was maybe the best available mercy.

Leela unzipped her bag and took out two more drives. June looked up, surprised.

"I made copies yesterday," Leela said. "Same volume, same checksum. I thought maybe if I had them ready we wouldn't have to stage indecision."

"Jesus," June said softly.

"What?"

"Nothing. That's just the most you thing I've ever heard."

Omar took one drive and weighed it in his palm as if surprised by the lack of heft. "Almost no mass at all."

"Yeah," June said. "Like a keycard or a dongle. Tiny little thing, and your whole year is fucked."

They wrote down the recovery instructions separately, not the passphrase itself, just the route to it: union lawyer if one of us disappears, do not put in email, do not store in cloud notes, do not get sentimental and open it drunk. June added the last line and underlined it twice. Omar folded his page with absurd neatness and tucked it into his wallet behind his transit card. Leela slid hers into the Montrose offer folder, which felt bleak but appropriately honest. Her future job and the evidence of her ruined one were in the same pocket.

By the time they finished, the takeout had gone cold and the room had darkened toward evening. June switched on the kitchen light. It made the apartment look temporarily more itself, the old Tuesday-dinner version with too many people and not enough chairs, before the boxes ruined the illusion again.

"I should say something wise because I'm leaving," June said.

"Please don't," Leela said.

"Fair. Then something practical. Send me your Montrose address when you have it. If you let your undergrads bully you into making Discord channels, I'll come upstate and put you down."

"I would deserve it."

June looked at Omar. "And you. If they try to sell you the terminal master's as a favor, call me before you sign anything."

"Why?"

"Because I would enjoy being professionally rude to them."

Omar's smile this time was real. "Thank you. I will treasure the offer and hopefully never use it."

He stood first. Of course he did. He had an office to return to, an inbox capable of ruining a night, a whole provisional future balanced on other people's calendar invites. At the door he turned back, fingers already on the frame.

"For what it's worth," he said, careful and unsentimental, "I'm glad the year did not end with the university being the only witness."

Then he was gone, footsteps quick on the stairs.

Leela helped June tape one more box because leaving right away would have made the room too conscious of itself. The box was full of cables, three chipped mugs, and a pan June refused to abandon though its handle wobbled and one side had burned permanently black.

"You could buy a new one," Leela said.

"I could. This one knows me."

Leela pressed the tape down. "That's disgusting."

"Yeah."

June set the box by the door and leaned against it for a second. Up close she smelled like tape adhesive, dish soap, and the peppermint bite of nicotine gum. It was not the hotel and not the lab. It was something current and unromantic and therefore harder to sentimentalize.

"I'm glad you took the job," June said.

"I'm glad you took yours."

"No, you're not."

"No," Leela said. "But I'm glad you got out."

June looked at her long enough to make lying impossible in either direction. "You too," she said. "Sort of."

That was the shape of it. Not absolution. Not a reopened wound. A damaged sentence with enough grammar left to stand.

When Leela left, the night air on Alder Street had gone soft and city-warm. She walked uphill with the beer buzz already fading, one copy of `holdout_set` in her bag beside the Montrose paperwork, the edge of the drive tapping now and then against the folder clip through the canvas. Behind her, June's apartment windows stayed lit while boxes shifted in shadow. Ahead, campus rose in its familiar blocks and institutional facades, all of it still standing.

Soon enough the findings would flatten into paper: Gloria's report, a personnel action, a committee binder slid into some cool metal drawer that smelled faintly of dust and toner. Advising conduct. Entrepreneurial overlap. Revised scope. Small enough for a tab.

The drive clicked against her keys when she crossed the broken stretch of sidewalk by the bus stop. It weighed almost nothing. She kept checking for it anyway, thumb hard over the little rectangle through the canvas, as if something that light could still vanish before she got it home.
